תלמוד ירושלמי שקלים
William Davidson Edition - English
https://korenpub.co.il/products/vol-8-shekalim

Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<sup><b>[2a]</b></sup> <strong>Halakha 1 · MISHNA</strong>  <b>On the first of Adar</b> the court <b>proclaims concerning</b> the collection of <b>shekels,</b> i.e., the yearly half-shekel contribution to the Temple treasury made by each adult male for the purpose of buying communal offerings. <b>And</b> they also proclaim <b>with regard to</b> the obligation to uproot forbidden mixtures of <b>diverse kinds</b> of food crops in gardens and fields. <b>And on the fifteenth</b> day <b>of the</b> month of Adar, <b>the Scroll [<i>Megilla</i>]</b> of Esther <b>is read in the cities [<i>kerakim</i>]</b> surrounded by walls from the time of Joshua. <b>And they</b> also <b>repair the roads</b> that were damaged in the winter, <b>and the streets, and the cisterns. And</b> at that time <b>they perform all that is necessary for public welfare. And they</b> also <b>mark the</b> Jewish <b>gravesites</b> anew, so that people would know their location and avoid ritual impurity, as the previous markers may have eroded during the rainy season. <b>And</b> agents of the court <b>also go out</b> to inspect the fields <b>for diverse kinds</b> of food crops, to determine whether or not the farmers had in fact uprooted these seeds after the proclamation on the first of the month. If the agents of the court found that these diverse kinds had not been uprooted, they themselves would uproot them.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that the court would issue a proclamation concerning the new shekels on the first of Adar. The Gemara asks: <b>And why</b> specifically <b>on the first of Adar?</b> The Gemara answers: This was done <b>in order that Jews would bring their shekels</b> to the designated Temple chamber <b>in the proper time,</b> as the shekels had to be collected before the beginning of Nisan each year. <b>And</b> this would ensure that the <b>collection</b> of <b>the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber</b> would be <b>collected from the new</b> shekels <b>at its proper time,</b> which is <b>on the first of</b> the month of <b>Nisan,</b> i.e., the beginning of the Temple year. After that date all communal offerings must be purchased from the new shekels. <b>And Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak said:</b> The <b>collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber</b> was performed each year on the same date as <b>its first time, as it is written: “And it came to pass in the first month in the second year, on the first day of the month, that the Tabernacle was reared up”</b> (Exodus 40:17). <b>And</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>was taught about</b> this verse: <b>On the day that the Tabernacle was erected, on that</b> very <b>day the funds were collected.</b> The first of Nisan was thereafter permanently established as the date for the collection of the chamber. <b>Rabbi Tavi</b> said that <b>Rabbi Yoshiya</b> said <b>in the name of</b> Rabbi <b>Kahana: It is stated here,</b> with regard to the additional offerings of the New Moon: “This is the burnt-offering of every New Moon throughout <b>the months of</b> the year” (Numbers 28:14), <b>and it is stated there,</b> concerning the months of the year: “This month shall be to you the beginning of months; it shall be the first of <b>the months of</b> the year to you” (Exodus 12:2). <b>Just as “the months of” stated there are counted only from Nisan,</b> the first month of the year, <b>so</b> too, <b>“the months of” that is stated here,</b> with regard to the new shekels, <b>are counted only from Nisan.</b> <b>Rabbi Yona said: Rabbi Tavi left</b> aside <b>the first</b> clause <b>of</b> this <b><i>baraita</i>, and read</b> only <b>its latter</b> clause, in which it is stated that the months of the year are counted from Nisan. However, this does not prove that the first of Nisan is the date after which communal offerings must be purchased with the new shekels. <b>This is not as it was taught,</b> as this is the full text of the <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“This is the burnt-offering of a new month on its month,</b> throughout the months of the year” (Numbers 28:14). One <b>might</b> have thought that a person <b>should contribute</b> a half-shekel <b>each and every month.</b> Therefore <b>the verse states: “of a new month on its month, throughout the months,”</b> from which it is inferred: <b>In one</b> particular <b>month</b> a person <b>contributes</b> his half-shekel <b>for all the months of the year.</b> The <i>baraita</i> continues: One <b>might</b> have thought that a person can contribute his half-shekel <b>in any month he wants.</b> Therefore, <b>it is stated here,</b> with regard to the additional offerings of the New Moon: <b>“The months of”</b> (Numbers 28:14), <b>and it is stated there,</b> with regard to the months of the year: <b>“The months of”</b> (Exodus 12:2). <b>Just as</b> the phrase <b>“the months of,” which is stated there, is counted only from Nisan,</b> the first month of the year, <b>so</b> too, the phrase <b>“the months of,” which is stated here,</b> with regard to the offerings, <b>is counted only from Nisan.</b>
 § The mishna taught that on the first of Adar the court would issue a proclamation concerning the new shekels. The Gemara asks: <b>What does it</b> mean that <b>they proclaim? Rav Huna said: They announce</b> publicly that everyone is obligated to donate the half-shekel. <b>As you say</b> in quoting the verse: <b>“And they made a proclamation throughout Judea and Jerusalem,</b> to bring in for the Lord the tax that Moses, the servant of God, laid upon Israel in the wilderness” (II Chronicles 24:9). The tax of Moses is the half-shekel that the Israelites were commanded to donate.
<b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Megilla</i> 1:4): <b>The only</b> halakhic difference <b>between First Adar and Second Adar</b> is <b>the recitation of the Megilla and the gifts to the poor,</b> which are mitzvot of Purim, which is celebrated only in Second Adar. The Gemara comments: <b>Rabbi Simon</b> says <b>in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi: The proclamations about the new shekels and</b> about <b>diverse kinds</b> are <b>also between them,</b> i.e., are performed in Second Adar and not First Adar. The Gemara comments further: <b>Rabbi Ḥelbo and Rav Huna</b> said that <b>Rav</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥiyya the Great</b> as follows: <b>All fulfill</b> the obligation to read the Megilla <b>on the fourteenth</b> of Adar, <b>which is</b> the fixed <b>time of its reading</b> for most people, although there are other times in which particular groups can read the Megilla. <b>Rabbi Yosei said: And that is so;</b> the proclamations about the shekels and about diverse kinds were certainly issued in Second Adar. Rabbi Yosei explains: <b>Didn’t they say</b> that the court would <b>proclaim about</b> the new <b>shekels</b> on the first of Adar <b>in order that Jews would bring their shekels in the proper time,</b> by the first of Nisan? <b>And if you say</b> that in a leap year the court issues this declaration <b>in First Adar, until then there are</b> still <b>sixty days of the year</b> before the first of Nisan. With so much time remaining, people might be lax and neglect to donate their half-shekels. Rabbi Yosei continues: Furthermore, <b>didn’t they say</b> that on the fifteenth of Adar the agents of the court <b>would also go out</b> to examine the fields <b>for diverse kinds</b> of food crops, to determine whether the farmers had in fact uprooted these plants, and to do so themselves if those farmers had neglected their duty. Was this <b>not</b> performed on this date <b>in order that the plants would be perceptible</b> at that time? The court agents must have gone out near springtime, as otherwise they would have been unable to detect the presence of diverse kinds. And <b>if you say</b> that this task is performed <b>in First Adar, until then</b> the new crops <b>are</b> too <b>small</b> to be seen. Consequently, they must have waited until Second Adar, when the crops were visible.
The Gemara comments: In light of the statement that the proclamation concerning the new shekels was to ensure that the coins would arrive in the Temple at the proper time, <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya asked</b> the following rhetorical question: <b>If that is so,</b> then with regard to <b>the residents of Babylonia,</b> who live at a distance of several months’ travel from Jerusalem, the court should <b>proclaim about the</b> new <b>shekels at the beginning of winter. Didn’t the court</b> make the proclamation <b>in order that the Jews would bring their shekels in the proper time, and</b> thus the <b>collection of the</b> Temple <b>treasury chamber</b> will <b>be collected from the new shekels at its appropriate time, on the first of Nisan?</b> It therefore would be necessary to issue the proclamation earlier for distant locations. <b>Rabbi Ulla raised a difficulty in the presence of Rabbi Mana:</b> Rabbi Ḥizkiya’s statement indicates that in each place the proclamation should be issued in accordance with the time it takes for the shekels to reach Jerusalem from there. <b>But we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shekalim</i> 3:1): <b>On three occasions in the year</b> the shekels were <b>collected from the chamber,</b> for the purchase of offerings: <b>Half</b> a month before <b>[<i>biferos</i>]</b> the festival of <b>Passover, half</b> a month before the festival of <b><i>Shavuot</i>, and half</b> a month before the festival of <b><i>Sukkot</i>.</b> Rabbi Ulla continued and <b>said to</b> Rabbi Mana in explanation: In light of this mishna, <b>shouldn’t we say</b> that the proclamation for shekels was issued in all places on the same date, the first of Adar, whereas the ceremony of the collection of the Temple treasury chamber was performed at three different times, in accordance with the arrival of the shekels to the Temple from different locations: With regard to <b>those who are near</b> Jerusalem, whose shekels arrived before the first of Nisan, the collection ceremony for their coins is <b>half</b> a month before <b>Passover,</b> while the ceremony for <b>those who are farther</b> away is <b>half</b> a month before <b><i>Shavuot</i></b>; <b>and</b> the ceremony for <b>those who are</b> even <b>farther</b> away <b>than they are</b> is <b>half</b> a month before <b><i>Sukkot</i>.</b> <sup><b>[2b]</b></sup> Rabbi Mana <b>said to</b> Rabbi Ulla: The different collections of the chamber do not reflect the different times of the shekels’ arrival, but rather <b>the entire</b> collection of shekels <b>arrives at one</b> time, by the first of Nisan, and in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Ḥizkiya. <b>And why</b> then did the Sages <b>say</b> that the money is collected from the chamber <b>on three occasions</b> in the year? <b>In order to publicize [<i>pumbei</i>] the matter,</b> that everyone is obligated to donate half-shekels for the purchase of communal offerings.
§ <b>Rabbi Yehuda bar Pazi</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi: <b>Can we read</b> the following verses <b>and not be afraid?</b> On the one hand, when the Jewish people were asked to donate <b>for</b> the <b>good</b> purpose of the construction of the Tabernacle, the verse states: “And they came, both men and women, <b>as many as were willinghearted,</b> and brought…an offering of gold to the Lord” (Exodus 35:22). This indicates that only the generous among the people brought donations. On the other hand, when the Jews were asked to donate <b>for</b> the <b>evil</b> purpose of the Golden Calf, it states that not only the willinghearted but: <b>“And all the people broke off</b> the golden rings which were in their ears, and brought them unto Aaron” (Exodus 32:3). A similar idea is found with regard to the people’s initiatives. When they initiated <b>for good,</b> at Sinai, it states: <b>“And Moses brought forth the people</b> out of the camp to meet God; and they stood at the nether part of the mount” (Exodus 19:17). This verse indicates that they did not venture forth on their own initiative, but only upon Moses’ instruction. In contrast, when it is <b>for evil,</b> the people took the initiative, as the verse states with regard to sending the spies: <b>“And you came near to me every one of you,</b> and said: Let us send men before us, that they may search the land for us, and bring us back word of the way by which we must go up, and the cities unto which we shall come” (Deuteronomy 1:22). Another example: <b>For good,</b> with regard to the song at the Red Sea after the Israelites were rescued from the Egyptians, it is stated: <b>“Then sang Moses and the children of Israel</b> this song unto the Lord, and spoke, saying: I will sing unto the Lord, for He is highly exalted; the horse and his rider He has thrown into the sea” (Exodus 15:1). They did not start singing of their own accord, but merely followed Moses’ lead. Yet <b>for evil,</b> after the spies delivered their report upon their return from Eretz Yisrael, the verse states: <b>“And all the congregation lifted up</b> their voice, and cried; and the people wept that night” (Numbers 14:1). <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said:</b> When he reproves the Jews, the prophet also notes their greater willingness to perform evil than good: “I said: Surely you will fear Me, you will receive correction; so her dwelling shall not be cut off, despite all that I have visited upon her; <b>but they rose early</b> and <b>corrupted</b> all their doings” (Zephaniah 3:7), which indicates that <b>every</b> act of <b>corruption that</b> the Jews <b>would perform, they would perform it early</b> in the day. In contrast, only the vigilant arise early to fulfill mitzvot. In reference to the previous statement that the Jews donated to both the Tabernacle and the Golden Calf, <b>Rabbi Abba bar Aḥa said: You cannot discern the</b> true <b>nature</b> of this people, as donations <b>are requested</b> for the Golden <b>Calf and they give;</b> and later, donations <b>are requested for the Tabernacle, and they</b> also <b>give.</b> In this regard, <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina taught this <i>baraita</i>.</b> The verse states: <b>“And you shall make an Ark cover of pure gold”</b> (Exodus 25:17). <b>Let the gold of the Ark cover come and atone for the gold of the Calf.</b>
The Gemara returns to the issue of the collection of the shekels. <b>Rabbi Ḥaggai</b> said <b>in the name of Rabban Shimon bar Naḥman: Three collections are stated in this passage</b> (Exodus 25:1–3): <b>The collection for the sockets,</b> in which the beams of the Tabernacle building were placed; <b>the collection of shekels</b> for communal offerings; <b>and the collection</b> for the building <b>of the Tabernacle.</b> When the verse states: <b>“Speak to the children of Israel, that they take for Me an offering”</b> (Exodus 25:2), <b>this is the collection of sockets. “Of every man whose heart makes him willing, you shall take My offering”</b> (Exodus 25:2); <b>this is the collection of shekels. “And this is the offering which you shall take from them”</b> (Exodus 25:3); <b>this is the collection of the Tabernacle.</b> The Gemara elaborates: <b>The collection of the Tabernacle</b> goes <b>to the Tabernacle,</b> and <b>whatever</b> the authorities <b>want to do</b> with this money for the purpose of the Tabernacle <b>they</b> may <b>do. The collection of shekels</b> is <b>for</b> the communal <b>offering; whatever they want to do</b> with this money for the acquisition of the various communal offerings, <b>they</b> may <b>do.</b> Every Jewish male participates in this collection, <b>in order that they all have an equal</b> share in the communal offerings. As for <b>the collection of the sockets,</b> it is brought <b>for</b> the making of <b>sockets.</b> In this case the verse states: <b>“The rich shall not give more, and the poor shall not give less”</b> (Exodus 30:15). Every male from age twenty upward donated the fixed sum of a half-shekel, which was used for the making of the sockets. <b>Rabbi Avun said: In this passage as well, three collections are stated: “Half a shekel for an offering to the Lord”</b> (Exodus 30:13); <b>“Shall give the offering of the Lord”</b> (Exodus 30:14); <b>“They give the offering of the Lord”</b> (Exodus 30:15).
§ The mishna taught that <b>on the fifteenth</b> day <b>of the</b> month of Adar <b>the Scroll</b> of Esther <b>is read in cities.</b> The Gemara asks: But how can this be so? <b>Didn’t Rabbi Ḥelbo</b> say citing <b>Rav Huna</b> citing <b>Rav, in the name of Rabbi Ḥiyya the Great: All fulfill</b> the obligation to read the Megilla <b>on the fourteenth</b> of Adar, <b>which is</b> the fixed <b>time of its recitation.</b> This ruling apparently includes even the inhabitants of walled cities. The Gemara answers: This statement does <b>not come</b> to establish the correct date for reciting the Megilla, <b>but to teach you that all the</b> other <b>mitzvot that are practiced</b> in a leap year <b>in Second Adar are not practiced in First Adar.</b> The Gemara relates: <b>Rabbi Yosei and Rabbi Aḥa were sitting</b> together. <b>Rabbi Yosei said to Rabbi Aḥa:</b> The statement that all fulfill their obligation on the fourteenth indeed means that all may read the Megilla on the fourteenth. Yet <b>it is reasonable</b> to say so <b>only after the fact,</b> i.e., if they neglected to do so on the fifteenth, the inhabitants of a walled city have fulfilled their obligation by reading on the fourteenth. <b>However,</b> the residents of walled cities <b>may not</b> read the Megilla on the fourteenth <b><i>ab initio</i>.</b> Rabbi Aḥa raised an objection to Rabbi Yosei. <b>But wasn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In <b>a place where they were accustomed to read</b> the Megilla on <b>two days,</b> due to doubt over whether or not that city was surrounded by a wall in the days of Joshua (e.g., Tiberias; see <i>Megilla</i> 5b), <b>they read it</b> on <b>two days?</b> Yet you claim that even in a place where the Megilla must definitely be read on the fifteenth, if it was read on the fourteenth the residents are not obligated to read it again. Rabbi Yosei <b>said to him: I too hold like that,</b> i.e., that residents of a walled city should read the Megilla on the fifteenth, even when they had already read it on the fourteenth. Rabbi Yosei did not explain how Rabbi Ḥelbo’s statement that all fulfill their obligation on the fourteenth fits with the <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rabbi Mana said: And this is so;</b> there is no contradiction from the <i>baraita</i>. <b>If, after reciting</b> the Megilla on the fourteenth of Adar, one wants <b>to read it again on the fifteenth, perhaps we do not listen to him,</b> i.e., allow him? <b>If you say so, you have turned out to have directly uprooted</b> the proper <b>time</b> for the recitation of the Megilla in <b>walled cities,</b> which is the fifteenth of Adar. Therefore, it is appropriate for one who has already read on the fourteenth to read again on the fifteenth.
<b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says:</b> All the <b>mitzvot that are practiced in Second Adar are not practiced in the First</b> Adar, <b>apart from</b> the prohibitions against <b>eulogy and fasting</b> on Purim, <b>which</b> apply <b>equally in both this</b> month and <b>that</b> one. It is prohibited to give a eulogy or to fast on the fourteenth and fifteenth of First Adar. <b>Rabbi Ba</b> said, citing <b>Rabbi Yirmeya in the name of Rav,</b> and similarly, <b>Rabbi Simon</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel.</b> <b>Rav Huna the Great from Tzippori said: Rabbi Ḥanina instituted the custom in Tzippori in accordance with this</b> opinion <b>of Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel.</b> The Gemara infers: Rav Huna <b>did not say</b> that according to Rabbi Ḥanina the <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with the opinion of Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel, i.e., as a definitive ruling; <b>rather,</b> he meant that Rabbi Ḥanina merely <b>instituted the custom</b> in practice, even though it is not the definitive <b><i>halakha</i>.</b>
 <b>However, with regard to the issue</b> of dating legal <b>documents,</b> the following practice is followed. One <b>writes First Adar,</b> and in <b>Second Adar, simply</b> Adar. <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> In <b>First Adar</b> <sup><b>[3a]</b></sup> one writes <b>simply:</b> Adar, whereas in <b>Second Adar</b> one writes: <b>Second</b> Adar.
§ The mishna taught that on the fifteenth of Adar <b>they repaired the roads</b> that were damaged in the winter, <b>and the streets, and the cisterns. And they did all that was necessary for public welfare.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>These are</b> the matters <b>necessary for public welfare: They judge monetary cases, capital cases,</b> and <b>cases</b> that involve the punishment <b>of lashes. And</b> the court also <b>redeems valuations, consecrations</b> of articles for Temple or priestly use, <b>and consecrations</b> for Temple maintenance or as offerings. <b>And they give the <i>sota</i> to drink, and they burn the</b> red <b>heifer,</b> to use its ashes for the ritual purification of those rendered impure by contact with the dead, <b>and they break the neck of the heifer whose neck is broken, and they pierce the ear of a Hebrew slave, and they render the leper ritually pure. And they remove the locks</b> that were placed <b>over the water</b> cisterns during the winter, as this water was for public use in the summer, <b>and they do not replace them</b> until the winter.
 <b>We learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>there</b> that although it is prohibited to perform unnecessary work on the intermediate days of a Festival, during these days <b>one</b> may <b>water an irrigated field, and one</b> may <b>mark the graves</b> by painting lime <b>on</b> them. The Gemara asks: But <b>didn’t</b> the court <b>already mark</b> the graves <b>in Adar,</b> as stated in the mishna here? Why was it necessary to mark them again during the Festival? The Gemara answers: It can <b>be explained</b> as referring to a situation <b>where a downpour rained down</b> after the graves were initially marked in Adar, <b>and washed away</b> the previous markings.
 The mishna further taught that on the fifteenth of Adar, agents of the court would <b>also go out</b> to inspect the fields <b>for diverse kinds</b> of food crops. The Gemara asks: But <b>didn’t they already go out in</b> the beginning of <b>Adar</b> to examine the fields for diverse kinds, as the mishna previously stated? The Gemara answers: It can <b>be explained that the year was late [<i>afila</i>],</b> i.e., the crops had not yet sprouted by the beginning of Adar, <b>and the plants are not perceptible</b> until later.
§ The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> is the obligation of <b>marking</b> graves derived? <b>Rabbi Berekhya</b> said in the name of <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar bat Ya’akov in the name of Rabbi Ḥunya of Berat Ḥavrin, Rabbi Yosei said it</b> was <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Aḥa, in the name of Rabbi Ḥunya of Berat Ḥavrin,</b> while <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya</b> and <b>Rabbi Uziel, son of Rabbi Ḥunya of Berat Ḥavrin,</b> said it <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥunya of Berat Ḥavrin.</b> These Sages said that the proof is from a verse: “And the leper in whom the plague is, his clothes shall be rent, and the hair of his head shall go loose, and he shall cover his upper lip, <b>and shall cry: Unclean, unclean”</b> (Leviticus 13:45). The Gemara explains: This teaches that one must take measures <b>in order that the ritually impure</b> object, in this case the leper, <b>calls to you verbally,</b> as it were, <b>and tells you: Separate</b> yourself from it. Just as the leper warns everyone that he is ritually impure, one must likewise mark graves to warn passersby of their impure status. <b>Rabbi Ila in the name of Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥman</b> cited a different verse in this regard: <b>“And when they that pass through shall pass through the land, and anyone sees a man’s bone, then shall he set up a sign by it,</b> till the buriers have buried it in the valley of Hamon-gog” (Ezekiel 39:15). This verse explicitly states that there is a need to mark graves. The Gemara adds that further <i>halakhot</i> can be derived from this verse. <b>“Bone”: from here</b> it is derived <b>that one marks bones. “A man”: From here</b> it is derived <b>that one marks</b> a <b>spine and skull</b> that were found. <b>“Then shall he set up”: From here</b> it is derived <b>that one marks</b> the sign of ritual impurity <b>on a fixed stone,</b> for <b>if you say</b> that one may mark <b>on a detached stone, that very</b> stone is likely to <b>move</b> from that spot, <b>and</b> it will incorrectly mark <b>another place</b> as <b>ritually impure.</b> It is therefore necessary to use a stone that will not be moved. The verse further states: <b>“By it”:</b> This is referring <b>to a place of ritual purity.</b> In other words, the marking should not be placed on the spot of ritual impurity itself, but in a nearby, ritually pure location. <b>“A sign”: From here</b> we learn <b>of</b> the obligation to <b>mark.</b>
It was further taught with regard to the same issue: <b>And</b> if one <b>found one marked stone, even though one should not establish it in this manner,</b> i.e., one should not mark a place of ritual impurity with a single stone <i>ab initio</i>, but with two stones in order to delineate the extent of the impurity, nevertheless, <b>one who covers it,</b> i.e., places some part of his body in the space above it, is rendered <b>ritually impure.</b> In such a case <b>I say,</b> i.e., one ought to assume: There was <b>a dead</b> body <b>marked</b> here, <b>and it was located underneath</b> this stone. However, if <b>there were two</b> marked stones, <b>the one who covers</b> them with part of his body remains <b>ritually pure,</b> as the source of impurity is typically located between, not underneath, the two markers. <b>And</b> consequently, if he covered the space <b>between</b> the stones, he is <b>ritually impure.</b> <b>And if there were plow marks between</b> these two stones, <b>they are</b> considered <b>like isolated stones</b> with regard to this <i>halakha</i>. Therefore, if he covered the area <b>between</b> the stones with part of his body he is <b>ritually pure, and</b> if he covered the space <b>surrounding</b> the area where they are set he is <b>impure,</b> as it can be assumed there was a source of impurity under each stone.
<b>It was taught</b> in a different <i>baraita</i> that <b>one does not mark on</b> the location <b>of the flesh</b> of a corpse without any bones. Why not? <b>Perhaps the flesh has decomposed,</b> leaving less than the minimum size that confers ritual impurity by covering, which is an olive-bulk. If the mark is left there, the spot will permanently be treated as ritually impure. <b>Rabbi Yusta bar Shunem wondered in the presence of Rabbi Mana: And doesn’t he thereby render pure items ritually impure retroactively?</b> If the flesh is not marked at all, pure items might pass over the spot before the flesh has decayed, rendering them ritually impure. <b>He said to him: It is preferable that</b> people <b>are harmed by it temporarily,</b> while the flesh is still there, <b>and are not harmed by it permanently,</b> by wrongly thinking that their pure foods contracted ritual impurity from this source.

Halakhah 2

 <strong>Halakha 2 · MISHNA</strong>  At the end of the last mishna it was stated that the court sends out agents on the fifteenth of Adar to inspect whether the owners of fields had indeed uprooted any diverse kinds of crops as instructed. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: At first</b> those agents <b>would uproot</b> the diverse kinds of crops <b>and cast them in front of</b> the owners of the fields. <b>When</b> the number of <b>transgressors</b> who would not uproot diverse kinds in their fields <b>increased,</b> and the Sages saw that this tactic was ineffective, <b>they would cast</b> the uprooted crops <b>onto the roads.</b> Ultimately, <b>they instituted that the entire field should be declared ownerless.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to what <b>Rabbi Yehuda said</b> in the mishna, i.e., that there were three stages of the Sages’ decree, the Gemara elaborates as it was <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: At first</b> the agents of the court <b>would uproot</b> the diverse kinds of crops <b>and cast them in front of</b> the owners, <b>and</b> those owners <b>would doubly rejoice. One</b> reason for their happiness was <b>that</b> others <b>were weeding their fields</b> for them, <b>and the</b> other reason was <b>that they would derive benefit from</b> these <b>diverse kinds,</b> which they would feed to their animals. The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>When</b> the number of <b>transgressors</b> who maintained diverse kinds in their fields <b>increased, they would cast</b> the uprooted crops <b>onto the roads,</b> so that passersby should trample on them and render them unfit for any use. <b>Even so,</b> the owners still <b>rejoiced that</b> those agents <b>were weeding their fields.</b> Therefore, the Sages <b>instituted that the entire field should be declared ownerless,</b> which would deter people from maintaining diverse kinds in their fields.
The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>that property declared ownerless by the court is</b> in fact <b>ownerless?</b> As the mishna states that the court rendered the fields ownerless, clearly they had the power to do so. What is the source for their having this power? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is written,</b> with regard to the proclamation of Ezra and his court to all the people to gather in Jerusalem, where he instructed those who had married foreign women to divorce them: <b>“And that whosoever came not within three days, according to the counsel of the princes and the elders, all his substance should be forfeited, and himself separated from the congregation of the captivity”</b> (Ezra 10:8). This shows that the court has the power to render ownerless the property of anyone who violates its commands. The Gemara further asks: <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> this ownerless field <b>is exempt from tithes,</b> like a field rendered ownerless by its owner? <b>Rabbi Yonatan, son of Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Aḥa, learned</b> the <i>halakha</i> of this case <b>from this</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>One</b> may <b>not intercalate the year,</b> by adding the month of Second Adar, <b>neither in the Sabbatical Year nor in the year after the Sabbatical Year. And if they</b> transgressed and <b>intercalated it,</b> the year is <b>intercalated.</b> Rabbi Yonatan explains how exemption from tithes of a field that was declared ownerless by the court can be derived from this <i>baraita</i>: <b>And</b> yet with regard to that <b>one month that he adds,</b> i.e., the month to intercalate the year, which is added to the Sabbatical Year, <b>aren’t</b> the crops harvested in that additional month <b>exempt from tithes?</b> These crops are Sabbatical produce and as such exempt from tithes, but that status is only a result of the decree of the court to extend the year. This proves that the court has the power to render a field exempt from tithes. The Gemara continues to analyze the <i>baraita</i>. <b>Until here</b> it is clear why <b>the Sabbatical Year</b> should not be intercalated, as it extends the prohibitions of the Sabbatical year. However, <b>what is</b> the reason that <b>the year after the Sabbatical Year</b> should not be intercalated? <b>Rabbi Bun said:</b> The reason is so <b>as not to increase</b> the period <b>of the prohibition of new grain.</b> In the year after the seventh year people are still relying on the produce of the sixth year until the new crop sprouts in the spring of the eighth year. Since the food supply is liable to run out, the sooner the new grain can be eaten, the better. It is only permitted after the bringing of the <i>omer</i> offering on the sixteenth of Nisan, and therefore it is preferable not to extend the year, which would delay the beginning of Nisan. <b>Rabbi Ze’eira</b> says <b>in the name of Rabbi Elazar: That which you say,</b> that one may not intercalate the Sabbatical Year or the following year, applied <b>until</b> the time <b>that Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>permitted</b> one <b>to import vegetables from outside Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>to Eretz</b> Yisrael. Beforehand there was a danger of insufficient food, and the court needed to refrain from lengthening either year. <b>However,</b> from the time <b>when Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>permitted one to import vegetables from outside Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>to Eretz</b> Yisrael, and there was enough food for all, <b>the Sabbatical Year is</b> like <b>other years of the Sabbatical cycle,</b> i.e., it is permitted to intercalate any year.
<b>It was taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i> that <b>one</b> may <b>not intercalate</b> the year following the Sabbatical Year. <b>Rabbi Mana said: This</b> matter <b>which you say:</b> It applied <b>at first, when</b> the seasons of <b>the years</b> proceeded in <b>their proper manner,</b> i.e., the new grain was ripe in time and could be eaten immediately after the sixteenth of Nisan. <b>However, now, when</b> the seasons of <b>the years do not</b> proceed in <b>their proper manner,</b> and the grain is not ready until later anyway, there is no problem delaying Nisan, so <b>the Sabbatical Year is</b> like <b>other years of the Sabbatical cycle.</b> The Gemara adds: <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the court <b>of the house of Rabban Gamliel intercalated</b> the year <b>immediately after the Sabbatical Year,</b> in the first month of that year. Rabbi Yonatan previously proved from the court’s ability to extend the Sabbatical year that the court has the power to declare property ownerless, to the extent that its produce is exempt from tithes. With regard to this, <b>Rabbi Avun said: If</b> your proof is <b>from this, you cannot learn anything from it.</b> Rabbi Avun explains: The Torah says: <b>“Observe [guard] the month of the spring,</b> and keep the Passover unto the Lord your God; for in the month of the spring the Lord your God brought you forth out of Egypt by night” (Deuteronomy 16:1). This means that one should <b>guard it</b> so <b>that</b> the month of Nisan <b>arrives in the</b> spring of the <b>new</b> year, i.e., in Nisan of the lunar year, which is roughly eleven days shorter than the solar year. If the festival of Passover is scheduled to arrive too early, due to the difference between the solar and lunar years, that year must be intercalated. Since the intercalation of the year is a Torah requirement, the extra month added to a Sabbatical Year is exempt from tithes by Torah law. Therefore, the extension of the year by intercalation is not merely a power of the court, and therefore cannot function as a source for the court’s power to declare property ownerless and exempt it from tithes.
<b>But this</b> other mishna (<i>Pe’a</i> 5:1) <b>does state this</b> <i>halakha</i>, that a field declared ownerless by the court is exempt from tithes. With regard to <b>a pile</b> of grain <b>under which</b> gleanings <b>have not been gathered</b> by the poor, who are entitled to the lone stalks that fall during a harvest, <b>all</b> the kernels <b>that are touching the ground belong to the poor. And</b> about this mishna <b>Rabbi Ami said</b> <sup><b>[3b]</b></sup> <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish: It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai,</b> who maintain that it is possible to declare an article ownerless exclusively with respect to the poor (see <i>Pe’a</i> 6:1). In defense of the attribution of the mishna to Beit Shammai the Gemara continues: <b>For if</b> the mishna is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Hillel,</b> who maintain that the court cannot declare something ownerless only for the poor, then the <b>poor would</b> be allowed to <b>eat</b> the kernels touching the ground only after <b>tithing.</b> <b>And Rabbi Yosei said to him,</b> Rabbi Ami: <b>We learned</b> a different explanation whereby it is not necessary to attribute the mishna to Beit Shammai, i.e., <b>that everyone agrees</b> that this produce <b>is exempt from tithes because</b> the Sages imposed <b>a fine [<i>kenasa</i>]</b> on one who was careless enough to pile his grain on top of gleanings. He forfeits any stalks touching the ground, as they are rendered ownerless, and ownerless food is exempt from tithes. Since some of the kernels touching the ground are from the pile, not the gleanings, the ruling that they are all exempt from tithes depends upon the Sages’ power to declare them ownerless. If the mishna in <i>Pe’a</i> is understood in accordance with Rabbi Yosei’s opinion, it serves as evidence that the Sages can in fact declare property ownerless to the extent that its produce is exempt from tithes.

Halakhah 3

<strong>Halakha 3 · MISHNA</strong>  <b>On the fifteenth of</b> Adar, money changers would <b>sit</b> at <b>tables</b> set up <b>in</b> the rest of <b>the country,</b> outside the Temple, to handle the collection of shekels. <b>On the twenty-fifth of</b> Adar, the money changers <b>sat in the Temple. From</b> the time <b>when</b> the money changers <b>sat in the Temple,</b> the court <b>began to seize collateral</b> from those who had yet to donate the half-shekel. <b>From whom did they seize collateral?</b> From <b>Levites, Israelites, converts, and emancipated slaves. However,</b> they did <b>not</b> seize collateral from <b>women, slaves, or minors. And any minor whose father began</b> one year <b>to contribute</b> a half-shekel <b>on his behalf,</b> despite the fact that he was not obligated to do so, <b>he may not cease to do so</b> in subsequent years. The court <b>does not seize collateral from priests,</b> although they are legally obligated to give a half-shekel like all other Jews, <b>because of the ways of peace.</b> The mishna goes on to explain the status of priests with regard to the contribution of the half-shekel. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said</b> that <b>ben Bukhri testified in Yavne: Any priest who contributes</b> the half-shekel <b>is not</b> considered <b>a sinner,</b> despite the fact that he is not obligated to do so. Rabbi Yehuda added that <b>Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai said to</b> ben Bukhri: <b>Not so; rather, any priest who does not contribute</b> the half-shekel is considered <b>a sinner,</b> as they are obligated like all other Jews. <b>However,</b> Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai continued, <b>the priests</b> who do not contribute <b>interpret this verse to their own</b> advantage: <b>“And every meal-offering of the priest shall be wholly made to smoke; it shall not be eaten”</b> (Leviticus 6:16). Those priests claim as follows: <b>Since the <i>omer</i></b> offering <b>and the two loaves,</b> i.e., the public offering of two loaves from the new wheat brought on the festival of <i>Shavuot</i>, <b>and the shewbread</b> placed on the sacred table in the Sanctuary each Shabbat, which are all meal-offerings, are <b>ours,</b> then if we contribute shekels we will have partial ownership of these communal offerings, as they are purchased with the shekels. <b>How,</b> then, <b>can</b> they <b>be eaten?</b> They ought to be regarded as priests’ meal-offerings, which must be wholly burnt. But since these offerings are eaten, the priests concluded that they are not obligated to contribute the half-shekel. This argument does not, however, take into account the fact that communal offerings belong to the public, which is understood as its own entity, and are not regarded as shared offerings of all who contribute to the public purse.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that the court <b>does not seize collateral from minors</b> for the half-shekel. The Gemara infers: This indicates <b>that</b> with regard to <b>claiming</b> the half-shekel from minors, the court does <b>claim</b> the money. The Gemara adds: <b>This</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>that you say,</b> i.e., that the court does not claim the money, applies <b>to</b> a young person <b>who has grown two</b> pubic <b>hairs</b> and therefore is legally an adult in other respects, but is not yet twenty years old. Before he is twenty he is obligated to donate the half-shekel only by rabbinic law, and for that the court does not seize collateral. <b>However, if he did not grow two hairs,</b> the <i>halakha</i> does <b>not</b> apply <b>to this</b> case and he has no obligation at all to contribute a half-shekel. The Gemara continues: <b>And</b> as for <b>seizing collateral,</b> the court <b>does not seize collateral</b> from a minor, <b>even if he grew two hairs,</b> until the age of twenty.
 The mishna taught that no collateral is taken from priests because of the ways of peace. The Gemara adds that <b>this is</b> how the <i>halakha</i> of this <b>mishna</b> is taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One</b> may <b>not seize collateral from priests because of the way of honor,</b> i.e., priests must be treated with respect. This is also the meaning of the ways of peace mentioned in the mishna.
The mishna further taught that <b>Rabbi Yehuda said</b> that ben Bukhri <b>testified</b> that any priest who contributes the half-shekel is not considered a sinner, to which Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai retorted that any priest who does not contribute is a sinner. <b>Rabbi Berekhya said: Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Zakkai’s source</b> is the following verse: <b>“This [<i>zeh</i>] they shall give,</b> everyone who passes among them that are numbered, half a shekel after the shekel of the Sanctuary…” (Exodus 30:13). The word <i>zeh</i> has a numerical value of twelve, from which it is derived: <b>Twelve tribes shall give,</b> including the tribe of Levi, of which the priests are members. <b>Rabbi Tavi</b> said <b>in the name of Rav Hamnuna</b> that <b>the Rabbis respond in this way to Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who quoted Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Zakkai’s criticism of ben Bukhri’s opinion: The <i>halakha</i> is that <b>the sin-offering of an individual,</b> which for some reason cannot be sacrificed, must be left to <b>die,</b> by confining the animal in an enclosure and withholding food and drink from it. However, <b>a communal sin-offering</b> is <b>not</b> left to <b>die (</b>see <i>Temura</i> 16a). A similar distinction applies to meal-offerings: <b>The meal-offering of an individual may be entirely sacrificed</b> on the altar, e.g., when it belongs to a priest. <b>But a communal meal-offering,</b> i.e., the <i>omer</i>, the two loaves, and the shewbread, all of which come from the collection of shekels, <b>is not entirely sacrificed,</b> but is always eaten. If the priests are obligated to contribute the half-shekel, these meal-offerings will partly belong to them, and it is prohibited to eat the meal-offering of a priest. The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> this is <b>difficult.</b> How could this question be presented against the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda? Can <b>one raise a difficulty against a person</b> from <b>a matter with which he does not agree? As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Yoma</i> 62b) <b>that a communal sin-offering</b> is <b>not</b> left to <b>die,</b> and yet <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: It is</b> left to <b>die.</b> According to Rabbi Yehuda there is no distinction between a communal sin-offering and an individual sin-offering in this regard. If so, there should likewise be no difference between communal and individual meal-offerings, and it should be possible to sacrifice the communal meal-offerings in their entirety. The Gemara adds: <b>And he,</b> Rabbi Yehuda, <b>responds to</b> the difficulty of the Rabbis against his opinion: <b>Isn’t this</b> half-shekel given by a priest <b>an individual donation?</b> Since he is not obligated to donate the half-shekel, the contribution of a priest may be considered an individual donation, which separates it from the money of the rest of the community. For that reason, the communal offerings are not considered the property of the priests. <b>And</b> the Rabbis <b>respond to him that since</b> the priest’s half-shekel <b>has been handed over to the community, it is</b> considered part of the <b>communal donation.</b> The Gemara notes that there is another dispute of <i>tanna’im</i> along the same lines as this one. <b>It is written:</b> “This they shall give, <b>everyone who passes among them that are numbered,</b> half a shekel after the shekel of the Sanctuary” (Exodus 30:13). <b>Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Neḥemya</b> disputed the meaning of this verse. <b>One</b> of them <b>said</b> that the verse means that <b>all who passed through the</b> Red <b>Sea</b> must <b>give</b> a half-shekel, i.e., the entire Jewish people. <b>And the other said</b> that <b>all who passed</b> before Moshe <b>to be numbered</b> must <b>give</b> the donation, whereas those who were not counted with the rest of the Jewish people, i.e., priests and Levites, need not provide a half-shekel. The Gemara comments: <b>The one who said</b> that <b>all who passed through the</b> Red <b>Sea</b> must <b>give</b> a half-shekel <b>supports</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Zakkai,</b> who maintains that the priests must also contribute, whereas <b>the one who said</b> that <b>all who passed to be numbered</b> must <b>give</b> the donation <b>supports</b> the opinion <b>of ben Bukhri.</b>

Halakhah 4

<strong>Halakha 4 · MISHNA</strong>  <b>Although</b> the Sages <b>said,</b> as stated in the previous mishna, that the court <b>does not seize collateral</b> from <b>women, slaves, and minors,</b> as they are not obligated to contribute, <b>however, if they contributed a shekel</b> of their own accord, the Temple treasurers <b>accept from them.</b> Conversely, in the case of <b>a gentile or a Samaritan [<i>Kuti</i>] who contributed a shekel</b> to participate in the communal offerings, <b>they do not accept</b> it <b>from them. And</b> likewise, <b>they do not accept from</b> a gentile or a Samaritan <b>pairs</b> of birds sacrificed in the purification ritual of <b>a <i>zav</i>, pairs</b> of birds <b>of a <i>zava</i>, or pairs</b> of birds <b>of a woman who gave birth,</b> all of which are brought for ritual purification, <b>or sin-offerings or guilt-offerings.</b> <b>This is the principle:</b> With regard to <b>anything that can be</b> brought to the altar <b>as a vow or as a free-will offering,</b> the priests <b>accept</b> it <b>from</b> gentiles and Samaritans, and with regard to <b>anything that cannot be</b> brought <b>as a vow or as a free-will offering, they</b> may <b>not accept</b> it <b>from them. And this</b> principle <b>was similarly articulated by Ezra,</b> when he recorded the Jewish leadership’s rejection of the Samaritans’ request to assist the Jews in the construction of the Second Temple, <b>as it is stated:</b> “But Zerubbabel, and Joshua, and the rest of the heads of fathers’ houses of Israel, said unto them: <b>You have nothing to do with us to build a house unto our God;</b> but we ourselves together will build unto the Lord the God of Israel, as King Cyrus the king of Persia has commanded us” (Ezra 4:3). <sup><b>[4a]</b></sup> The mishna states another <i>halakha</i>: <b>And these are</b> the people <b>who are obligated</b> in <b>the premium [<i>kalbon</i>],</b> a small sum added to the half-shekel collected: <b>Levites, Israelites, converts, and emancipated</b> Canaanite <b>slaves, but not priests, women,</b> Canaanite <b>slaves, or minors.</b> <b>One who contributes</b> a half-shekel <b>on behalf of a priest, on behalf of a woman, on behalf of a slave,</b> or <b>on behalf of a child, is exempt</b> from the premium, as they are exempt. <b>But if he contributed on his own behalf and on behalf of another,</b> i.e., he contributed one whole shekel to discharge both his own obligation and that of someone else, <b>he is obligated in one premium. Rabbi Meir says:</b> He must pay <b>two premiums.</b> The mishna further states: <b>One who gives</b> the collection agent <b>a <i>sela</i>,</b> i.e., a whole shekel, <b>and takes a shekel,</b> i.e., a half-shekel, as change <b>is obligated</b> in <b>two premiums.</b> <b>One who contributes</b> a half-shekel <b>on behalf of a poor</b> person, <b>on behalf of his neighbor, or on behalf of a resident of his city is exempt</b> from the premium. The Sages did not obligate in the premium those who use their own money to fulfill the obligation of another. <b>But if one loaned them</b> a half-shekel, rather than paying it on their behalf, <b>he is obligated</b> to pay the premium. Since the recipients of the loan must repay the money, it is as though the half-shekel were paid from their property rather than the lender’s. <b>Partnered brothers,</b> who have fully divided among themselves their late father’s assets, and <b>who,</b> if they jointly pay a whole shekel from those assets to discharge both of their obligations, <b>are obligated in the premium</b> like any other two private individuals, <b>are exempt from the animal tithe</b> for the livestock they inherited. Since they have completely divided between them all inherited assets, they are considered purchasers of the livestock, and a purchaser is exempt from the animal tithe. <b>But when</b> they have not completely divided the assets, and <b>they are</b> therefore <b>obligated in the animal tithe,</b> as the livestock is considered in their father’s possession, <b>they are exempt from the premium</b> for their joint payment, as in the case of one who pays on behalf of another. <b>And how much is a premium? A silver <i>ma’a</i>.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say:</b> It is only <b>half a <i>ma’a</i>.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that <b>although they said</b> that the assets of minors are not seized as collateral for the half-shekel payment, nevertheless, their money is accepted if they contribute it. The Gemara asks: The ruling that their money is accepted indicates that <b>with regard to claiming</b> their contribution, it <b>is not claimed</b> <i>ab initio</i>. However, the mishna’s statement <b>here,</b> that their property is not seized as collateral, indicates that <b>you say</b> that <b>it is claimed, and</b> yet <b>here,</b> at the end of the mishna, <b>you say</b> that <b>we don’t claim</b> the money. The Gemara answers that there are two different types of minors: <b>Here,</b> the statement of the mishna from which it can be inferred that the half-shekel is claimed from a minor is referring <b>to</b> one <b>who has two</b> pubic <b>hairs.</b> As the obligation to contribute the shekel is only fully in force at the age of twenty, a thirteen-year-old boy who has two pubic hairs can be referred to as a minor, even though he is considered an adult for other matters. <b>And here,</b> where it is stated that the half-shekel is not claimed at all from a minor, it is referring <b>to</b> a boy <b>who does not yet have two</b> pubic <b>hairs.</b>
 § The mishna taught: The half-shekel is not accepted from <b>a gentile or a Samaritan,</b> nor are they allowed to bring other types of obligatory offerings. <b>Rabbi Ba said:</b> The mishna’s ruling that both half-shekels and obligatory offerings are not accepted is referring to Samaritans, and it <b>can be explained according to</b> the opinion of <b>the one who said that a Samaritan is</b> considered <b>like a gentile. As</b> the Sages <b>disagreed</b> with regard to this matter: <b>A Samaritan is like a gentile;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says: A Samaritan is like a Jew in all matters.</b>
<b>Rabbi Elazar said:</b> No, the statement of the <b>mishna</b> concerning obligatory offerings applies only <b>to gentiles, as</b> in this instance the mishna is <b>not</b> referring <b>to Samaritans.</b> The <i>halakha</i> with regard to Samaritans on this matter is subject to the general disagreement as to whether they have the status of Jews or gentiles. <b>And</b> indeed <b>it was taught likewise</b> in a <i>baraita</i>. It is written: “When a man of you brings an offering” (Leviticus 1:2). The phrase <b>“a man”</b> comes <b>to include converts,</b> as the verse does not specify the children of Israel. <b>“Of you,”</b> a restricting phrase, comes <b>to exclude apostates,</b> who have partially or entirely left Judaism. Hence, the Samaritans, who are classified as converts, bring obligatory offerings. The Gemara wonders: It appears that <b>the mishna disagrees with Rabbi Elazar,</b> as we learned: <b>Pairs</b> of birds offered in the purification ritual <b>of a <i>zav</i>, pairs</b> of birds <b>of a <i>zava</i>, or pairs</b> of birds <b>of a woman who gave birth are not accepted from</b> a gentile or a Samaritan. <b>But</b> this statement of the mishna is problematic: <b>Are there pairs of birds</b> offered in the purification ritual <b>of a <i>zav</i> or of a <i>zava</i> among the gentiles?</b> The <i>halakhot</i> of purification from ritual impurity do not apply to gentiles. The Gemara explains how Rabbi Elazar understands the mishna: <b>Rather,</b> it must be that <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna, concerning the half-shekels, applies <b>to gentiles, and the latter clause,</b> which deals with obligatory offerings, is referring <b>to Samaritans.</b> The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>it is so; the first clause</b> of the mishna is referring <b>to gentiles, and the latter clause</b> is referring <b>to Samaritans.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said</b> with regard to the Samaritans: <b>Initially,</b> during the construction of the Temple, <b>neither a specific article,</b> i.e., any item meant to be left intact, <b>nor a nonspecific article,</b> e.g., silver or a material that is incorporated into the structure and is not distinct, <b>is accepted from</b> gentiles or Samaritans. <b>Subsequently,</b> once the construction has been completed, <b>a nonspecific article is accepted from them, but a specific article is not accepted from them. Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish said: Whether</b> it is <b>initially,</b> during the construction of the Temple, <b>or subsequently, neither a specific article nor a nonspecific article is accepted from them.</b> The Gemara wonders: <b>A <i>baraita</i></b> apparently <b>disagrees with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan: Consecrated</b> property <b>and donations for the maintenance of the Temple are not accepted from them.</b> This statement does not distinguish between the initial period of construction of the Temple and afterward, or between specific and nonspecific articles. The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yoḥanan would <b>resolve it,</b> and explain that the <i>baraita</i> indeed addresses the <i>halakha</i> of donations <b>both at the start and at the end</b> of the construction of the Temple, <b>but only</b> with regard to <b>specific articles.</b> Therefore, the <i>baraita</i> does not contradict the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan. It was stated above that <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish said: Whether</b> it is <b>initially,</b> during the construction of the Temple, <b>or subsequently, neither a specific article nor a nonspecific article is accepted from them.</b> The Gemara wonders: <b>A <i>baraita</i></b> apparently <b>disagrees with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish, as it was taught</b> (see <i>Arakhin</i> 5b): <b>All agree that</b> gentiles may <b>vow and be the subject of a vow.</b> A gentile who vows to donate his own value to the Temple and a Jew who vows to donate the value of a gentile must donate that sum to the Temple maintenance fund. This shows that pledges are accepted from gentiles, which contradicts the opinion of Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish. The Gemara answers: Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish would <b>resolve it</b> by explaining that this <i>baraita</i> is referring to not the Temple maintenance but a vow to bring <b>a burnt-offering,</b> which all agree a gentile may do. The Gemara asks: <b>It works out well</b> that gentiles may <b>vow,</b> as this can be interpreted as speaking of <b>a burnt-offering.</b> However, the statement that gentiles can <b>be the subject of a vow,</b> if it is not referring to his value, cannot be dealing with the imposition of an obligation on a gentile to bring <b>a burnt-offering,</b> which would be the reverse case of a regular vow to bring an offering oneself. The reason is that a Jew has no means of forcing a gentile to bring an offering. The Gemara answers: <b>No;</b> this is not the correct interpretation of the mishna. <b>Rather,</b> the mishna is referring to a case <b>when a Jew said:</b> It is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to donate <b>a burnt-offering, and a gentile heard and said: What this</b> person <b>said</b> is likewise incumbent <b>upon me.</b> Since the gentile did not vow independently but tied his statement to that of someone else, it is considered as though a vow were uttered in reference to him. The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if the mishna is referring to a burnt-offering, <b>doesn’t</b> the gentile <b>bring libations with it?</b> Burnt-offerings must be accompanied by libations. <b>And</b> the <b>surplus</b> money dedicated to libations <b>goes toward</b> the payment for the <b>sacred vessels.</b> It would therefore <b>turn out</b> that the gentile <b>brings a specific article.</b> <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Rabbi Bun responded: But didn’t we learn</b> (see <i>Arakhin</i> 1:2), that according to Rabbi Meir, gentiles can <b>be valuated and</b> may <b>valuate</b> to the Temple the fixed sum of others, as prescribed by the Torah (Leviticus 27:1–8). The same question can be posed here: <b>Doesn’t</b> the sum valuated <b>go toward the Temple maintenance?</b> The gentile might end up donating a specific article. Rather, the explanation is <b>as they say there:</b> When a gentile pledges money to the Temple, <b>he intends it for Heaven,</b> for God in general, rather than for any specific use in the Temple, <b>and</b> the pledge <b>comes into the maintenance of the Temple</b> fund <b>on its own.</b> In other words, these items cannot be considered a direct gift of a specific article. <b>You</b> can <b>likewise say even here,</b> with regard to the surplus money from the libation of a gentile’s burnt-offering, that when he brings money for the libations, <b>he intends it for Heaven,</b> <sup><b>[4b]</b></sup> <b>and</b> the funds <b>come into the Temple maintenance</b> fund <b>on their own.</b> Consequently, it is permitted to accept the libations of a burnt-offering from a gentile, as the remaining money was never specifically designated for this purpose. <b>What does Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish do with this?</b> How can he reconcile his opinion that even nonspecific articles are not accepted from gentiles with the ruling of this mishna? He <b>explains that</b> the Temple construction was restricted exclusively to Jews, as the book of Ezra states concerning the Samaritans: <b>“You have nothing to do with us to build a house for our God;</b> but we ourselves together will build for the Lord” (Ezra 4:3). <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya said that Rabbi Simon asked,</b> i.e., suggested: <b>Now,</b> since it has been concluded from the verse in Ezra that contributions are not accepted from gentiles for the maintenance of the Temple, it can be inferred from another verse that even contributions <b>toward the aqueduct,</b> which brought water to Jerusalem or <b>toward the walls of the city and its towers, are not accepted from them. As it is written that</b> Nehemiah said to Sanballat and the other gentile representatives: “The God of heaven, He will prosper us; therefore we His servants will arise and build; <b>but you have no portion,</b> nor right, nor memorial in Jerusalem” (Nehemiah 2:20).
§ There is a dispute in the mishna with regard to one who contributes a whole shekel on behalf of himself and another. The first <i>tanna</i> maintains that he is obligated in one premium, while Rabbi Meir rules that he must pay two premiums. The Gemara quotes a <b><i>baraita</i></b> that clarifies the reason <b>for Rabbi Meir’s</b> opinion: <b>As Rabbi Meir said: Just as</b> one’s <b>shekel is</b> mandated by the <b>Torah, so</b> too, <b>his premium is</b> mandated by the <b>Torah. Rabbi Meir</b> therefore <b>holds, with regard to</b> one <b>who gives</b> exactly <b>a whole shekel,</b> half for himself and half for another, <b>that he is obligated in</b> one <b>premium</b> for each half. <b>As Rabbi Meir said:</b> When Moses was instructed in the <i>halakhot</i> of the shekel contribution, <b>the Holy One, Blessed be He, took out a kind of coin of fire from under His Throne of Glory and showed it to Moses and said to him: “This they shall give”</b> (Exodus 30:13), i.e., <b>like this</b> in volume and weight <b>they shall give</b> of metal. Since not all weights of coins are equal, and some coins might be slightly smaller than the one shown to Moses, it is therefore necessary by Torah law to add a premium.
The mishna stated that <b>one who gives a <i>sela</i>,</b> i.e., a whole shekel, <b>and takes a half-shekel</b> as change <b>is obligated</b> to pay <b>two premiums. Rabbi Elazar said: This is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir, as it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>These are obligated in a premium, etc.</b> One who gives a <i>sela</i> and takes a shekel is obligated, according to the opinion of the Rabbis, to pay <b>one premium. But Rabbi Meir says:</b> He must give <b>two premiums.</b> <b>And Rav said:</b> No, this ruling of the mishna <b>is a statement</b> accepted by <b>all, as</b> everyone agrees with that which <b>Rabbi Meir said:</b> One who hands over a whole shekel and receives half back must give <b>one</b> premium for the <b>shekel that he gives</b> to the money changer, <b>and one</b> premium for the <b>shekel that he receives</b> as change, and a third premium as the <b>one mandated by the Torah</b> to make up the requisite weight. The Gemara adds: This means that <b>according to</b> the opinion <b>of Rav there are three premiums.</b> In support of this claim, the Gemara relates: When <b>Rabbi Yirmeya came,</b> he said explicitly that <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak</b> said <b>in the name of Rav: There are three premiums: One</b> for the <b>shekel that he gives, and one</b> for the <b>shekel that he receives, and one</b> that applies <b>by Torah law.</b>
§ The mishna stated that a certain type of partnership between brothers renders them obligated in the premium and exempt from the animal tithe, while another form of partnership renders them obligated in the animal tithe and exempt from the premium. The Gemara elaborates: The case of <b>partnered brothers who are obligated in the premium and exempt from the animal tithe</b> is referring to <b>when they divided</b> up the estate of their deceased father <b>and</b> afterward <b>again established a partnership.</b> They are therefore partners in the normal sense, rather than co-owners of the original inherited estate, and regular partners are obligated in the premium and exempt from the animal tithe. The Torah specifically exempts partners from the animal tithe (see <i>Bekhorot</i> 56b). Conversely, the <i>halakha</i> <b>that they are obligated in the animal tithe and are exempt from the premium</b> is referring <b>to</b> brothers <b>who have not divided</b> their father’s estate between them at all. It is therefore considered as though their father were still the sole owner of the estate and is contributing the shekel for the pair of them. As stated earlier in the mishna, one who contributes his own money on behalf of another is exempt from a premium. Meanwhile, as the estate is under a single ownership, the animal tithe must be separated from the livestock.
<b>Rabbi Elazar said: And this is</b> the <i>halakha</i> only <b>when they divided kids for goats and goats for kids,</b> i.e., when the two brothers inherited both kids and goats, and yet one took all the goats and the other all the kids. Since each originally inherited both species, this division is tantamount to one brother trading his goats for the other’s kids. When they again become partners, they are like strangers who join together. Consequently, they are exempt from the animal tithe. <b>However, if they divided kids for kids and goats for goats,</b> so that each takes some of both types in the normal manner, the <i>halakha</i> is that the group of animals each brother takes <b>is</b> considered <b>his share from the outset,</b> and no transaction between them has occurred. Consequently, when they reestablished the partnership, the estate reverted to its original status as a single entity, and therefore the animal tithe must be separated. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> No, <b>even if they divided</b> the estate in the manner of <b>kids for kids and goats for goats, they are</b> considered <b>like purchasers.</b> It is as though each brother has sold his share to the other, <b>and</b> they are therefore <b>exempt from the</b> animal <b>tithe, as we learned</b> in a mishna <b>there,</b> in <i>Bekhorot</i> (9:3): <b>That which is bought or given to</b> the owner <b>as a gift is exempt from the animal tithe.</b> The mishna spoke of two cases, one in which brothers are obligated in the animal tithe and exempt from the premium, and the other in which they are obligated in the premium and exempt from the animal tithe. In this regard, <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised a dilemma: And why do we not say</b> that there are also <b>times when they are obligated in both of them,</b> <sup><b>[5a]</b></sup> <b>and</b> there are <b>times when they are exempt from both of them? How is that?</b> If <b>they divided the property but did not divide the animals, they are obligated in both.</b> The brothers are considered regular partners with regard to the property, and they are therefore obligated in the premium. However, the livestock is still regarded as under the single ownership of their father’s estate, and they are therefore obligated in the animal tithe. Conversely, if <b>they divided the animals</b> between them <b>but did not divide the property, they are exempt from both.</b> With regard to the animals, the brothers are considered regular partners and are therefore exempt from the animal tithe. The rest of the property remains under unified ownership, and they are therefore exempt from the premium as well. In this regard, <b>Rabbi Mana said: This</b> statement <b>you,</b> Rabbi Yirmeya, <b>are saying,</b> that brothers who divided the property but not the animals are obligated in a premium, applies only <b>in</b> a case <b>where the animals were not the majority</b> of the property. <b>However, if the animals were the majority</b> of the property, <b>they are</b> considered the <b>principal property.</b> In this case, if the brothers have not divided the animals, even if they have divided the rest of the assets, it is as though they have not divided the estate at all, and they are therefore exempt from the premium. The mishna stated that if the brothers have not divided their inheritance between them, they are obligated in the animal tithe and exempt from the premium. <b>Rabbi Avin said</b> that <b>Rabbi Shimi raised a dilemma: Because you have</b> been stringent and <b>made them as one man with regard to the animal tithe,</b> by treating the estate as though it were under single ownership, will <b>you</b> also be lenient with them and <b>exempt them from the premium?</b> In this case too the stringent ruling should be accepted, and they should be treated as the owners of separate entities, who are obligated in the premium. Rabbi Avin <b>said to</b> Rabbi Shimi in response: Is <b>it not different?</b> There is a distinction here: <b>As he,</b> one of the brothers, <b>gives one whole <i>sela</i></b> from the estate on behalf of both of them, and as the inheritance has not yet been divided, the coin is still considered the legal property of the father. Consequently, they are exempt from the premium, despite the fact that this results in a leniency. The Gemara asks: According to the ruling that the undivided estate is considered under single ownership, <b>now</b> one could claim that <b>even if</b> the brothers <b>divided</b> the inheritance <b>and</b> subsequently <b>reestablished it again,</b> the inheritance should revert to its original state as their father’s property, and they should be <b>obligated in the animal tithe and exempt from the premium. And</b> yet <b>we learned</b> in the mishna that in this case <b>they are obligated in the premium and exempt from the animal tithe.</b> <b>Rabbi Ba</b> answered <b>in the name of Abba bar Rav Huna: It is</b> the same with <b>two brothers who inherited their father,</b> as <b>is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>two brothers-in-law who inherited their father-in-law.</b> If a father had only two daughters and their husbands inherited him, proceeded to divide up the inheritance, and later formed a partnership, they are considered like regular partners, who are obligated in a premium but exempt from the animal tithe. The same <i>halakha</i> applies to two sons.
The Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the additional premium: <b>To where would the premiums fall,</b> i.e., what was done with them? <b>Rabbi Meir says:</b> They would add them <b>to the shekels</b> themselves, for the daily and additional offerings. <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> They would be allocated <b>to free-will offerings,</b> i.e., the money would go toward the burnt-offerings that were sacrificed when the altar was idle. <b>Rabbi Shimon Shezuri says:</b> They would use the premiums to buy <b>gold plating, and</b> this served as <b>covering for the</b> inner walls of the <b>house of the Holy of Holies. Ben Azzai says: The money changers</b> responsible for collecting the shekels <b>would take them as their payment</b> for their services, as they had to take time off from their regular work. <b>And some say</b> that the premiums were spent <b>on transportation expenses,</b> i.e., the cost of moving the shekels from their collection sites to Jerusalem. 

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>Halakha 1 · MISHNA</strong> When people who live far from Jerusalem wish to send to Jerusalem the shekels that have been levied from their community, <b>they</b> may <b>combine</b> their <b>shekels</b> and exchange them <b>for darics [<i>darkonot</i>],</b> which are large gold coins, <b>due to the burden of the way.</b> Instead of carrying large amounts of shekels, the agents who deliver the funds will bring a much lighter burden of gold coins with them. The mishna adds: <b>Just as there were</b> collection <b>horns in the Temple</b> to receive the half-shekel contributions, <b>so too there were</b> collection <b>horns in the rest of the country,</b> i.e., areas outside of Jerusalem. The local inhabitants placed their half-shekels in these horns, which were later brought to Jerusalem. § With regard to <b>the residents of a town who sent their shekels</b> to the Temple <b>and they were stolen</b> from the agent on the way <b>or were lost, if</b> the <b>collection</b> of the chamber <b>had</b> already <b>been collected</b> before these shekels arrived, the agents must <b>take the oath</b> of a bailee <b>to the treasurers [<i>gizbarin</i>].</b> After the collection of the chamber, all the shekels that have been contributed become the property of the Temple, so the Temple treasurers who are in charge of this property become the opposing litigants of the agents. <b>If</b> the ceremony has not yet been performed and the contributions have <b>not</b> yet been collected into the baskets, the shekels are considered the property of the residents of the town, and therefore the agents must <b>take an oath to</b> absolve themselves to <b>the residents of the town.</b> Since those shekels are still considered the property of the residents of the town because the shekels never reached the Temple, they have not fulfilled their obligation. Therefore, <b>the residents of the town</b> must <b>contribute</b> other shekels <b>in their place. If,</b> after the residents of the town contributed other shekels, the original shekels <b>were found or the thieves returned them,</b> both <b>these</b> original shekels <b>and those</b> newly contributed ones have the status of consecrated <b>shekels</b> and belong to the Temple. However, <b>they do not count for the following year.</b> The people cannot claim that since they contributed twice in one year they are exempt from contributing the next year.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It is taught in the mishna that residents of a town can exchange their half-shekels with gold darics in order to ease their burden on the way to Jerusalem. The Gemara asks: If the goal is to ease the agents’ burden by combining the coins into a more valuable commodity, <b>let them make them,</b> i.e., exchange them <b>with, gems [<i>margaliot</i>],</b> which are more valuable than darics and much lighter to carry. The Gemara answers: They do not do this due to concern <b>lest the</b> price of the <b>gems decrease,</b> since, like all commodities, their price can decrease <b>and the</b> Temple treasury of <b>consecrated</b> property <b>will lose.</b> <b>Like that which we learned</b> in a mishna <b>there,</b> in tractate <i>Bekhorot</i>: <b>All of them,</b> all items that can be redeemed, such as different types of consecrated items and the firstborn male who is redeemed from the priest, <b>may be redeemed with money or with an equivalent value of money</b> in commodities, <b>except for shekels,</b> which may be redeemed only with money. The question remains: Why is it that one <b>may not redeem</b> the shekels <b>with vessels</b> or any other type of equivalent value of money in commodities? <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak said</b> that it is due to concern <b>lest the price of vessels decreases and the</b> Temple treasury of <b>consecrated</b> property <b>will lose.</b> This is not the case with money, whose price remains fixed. <b>So too here,</b> they do not exchange shekels with gems out of concern <b>lest the</b> price of the <b>gems decreases and the</b> Temple treasury of <b>consecrated</b> property <b>will lose.</b>
§ It is taught in the mishna that just as there were collection horns in the Temple to receive the shekels, there were also collection horns in the areas outside Jerusalem. The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna</b> refers <b>to new shekels,</b> which are contributed for the new year commencing in Nisan. <b>However, with regard to old shekels,</b> which are brought by people who did not contribute in the previous year and are only now bringing their contribution for that year, the mishna is <b>not</b> addressing <b>this.</b> The collection horns for old shekels were located only in the Temple and not in other places. <b>And it was taught similarly</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Collection horns for <b>old shekels</b> exist <b>in the Temple,</b> but there <b>are no</b> collection horns for <b>old shekels in the rest of the country.</b> It is taught in the mishna that if the shekels were lost or stolen from the agent on his way to Jerusalem, he must take the oath of a bailee and is exempted. The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna</b> is referring <b>to</b> a case where the agent was <b>an unpaid bailee. However, with regard to a paid bailee,</b> it is <b>not</b> addressing this sort of case, since only an unpaid bailee is exempt through his oath from payment for loss or theft. <b>Rabbi Abba said: Even if you say</b> that our mishna is referring <b>to a paid bailee,</b> he is still exempt through oath, since the term <b>stolen</b> used in the mishna is referring to <b>armed robbers [<i>listim</i>],</b> and such a case is deemed an unavoidable accident for which even a paid bailee is exempt. The term <b>lost</b> means <b>like one whose ship sank in the sea;</b> he was saved, but the money he brought with him belonging to the residents of the town was lost with the ship. This too is deemed an unavoidable accident, for which even a paid bailee is exempt. It is taught in the mishna that if the shekels were stolen or lost by the agent after the collection of the chamber, the agent takes an oath to the Temple treasurer to prove that he was not negligent. <b>Rabbi Yustai, son of Rabbi Simon, said</b> that the mishna is <b>in accordance with the one who says that they</b> perform the <b>collection</b> of the chamber with the intention that the ceremony apply to money <b>that has</b> already <b>been gathered</b> but has not yet been brought to the Temple, <b>as well as to</b> money <b>that will be gathered in the future.</b> Therefore, any money that was in the agent’s hands at the time of the collection is considered the property of the Temple treasury. <b>However, according to the one who said that they do not</b> perform the <b>collection</b> of the chamber with the intention to include money <b>that has already been gathered</b> but has not yet reached the Temple, nor money <b>that will be gathered in the future,</b> but rather only money that is already in the Temple, <b>not with</b> regard to <b>this</b> did the mishna say that the agents take an oath to the Temple treasurers and are exempt. Since these shekels did not yet reach the Temple, it is as though they were not contributed at all, and all those whose shekels were lost or stolen must contribute another half-shekel in their place.
§ It is taught in the mishna that if <b>the residents of the town sent their shekels</b> and they were stolen or lost, if the collection of the chamber had not yet been collected, the agents take an oath to the residents of the town; and if they have already been collected, they take an oath to the Temple treasurers. <b>Rabbi Elazar said:</b> The mishna is in accordance with the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon, as Rabbi Shimon says</b> that <b>consecrated items for which one is responsible</b> are considered <b>like his own property.</b> If a person consecrates an item as a vow, he takes on himself the responsibility to replace it if it is lost or stolen. Rabbi Shimon considers this responsibility to be a type of ownership, and therefore the agent who lost it must take an oath to the person who consecrated the lost item, who remains in some sense the owner. Similarly, the agent who lost shekels must swear to the residents of the town. The Rabbis who disagree with Rabbi Shimon maintain that consecrated items belong to the Temple, and therefore no oath is relevant, in accordance with the principle that one does not take an oath on consecrated items. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> disagreed with R. Elazar and <b>said:</b> The mishna <b>is in accordance with all</b> opinions. It is not limited to the opinion of Rabbi Shimon, <b>due to</b> the fact that it is <b>a</b> rabbinically <b>instituted oath</b> not required by Torah law. Since people may claim that they have no responsibility for these items, the Sages instituted taking an oath on consecrated items as well. The Gemara asks: <b>According to the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> who holds that the oath mentioned in the mishna is a rabbinic ordinance, <b>it works out well</b> that if the shekels were stolen or lost by the agent after the collection of the Temple chamber was completed, the agent <b>takes an oath to the</b> Temple <b>treasurers. And if not,</b> if they were stolen or lost before the collections were collected, he <b>takes an oath to the residents of the town.
