And the residents of the town give other shekels</b> <sup><b>[5b]</b></sup> <b>in their place.</b> Although by Torah law one does not take an oath on consecrated items belonging to the Temple treasury, and therefore there is no need to take an oath to the Temple treasurers, this oath is required <b>due to</b> the fact that it is a rabbinically <b>instituted oath,</b> as the Sages required the agent to take an oath as the bailee of the consecrated property that was deposited with him. <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar,</b> there is a difficulty. It is reasonable that when the shekels are lost before the collection of the chamber ceremony, the agents must <b>take an oath to the residents of the town,</b> as <b>this is</b> in accordance with the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon.</b> As long as the collection of the chamber has not taken place, the residents of the town are held responsible for their shekels, since they are considered to be their property. But why does the agent <b>take an oath to the</b> Temple <b>treasurers</b> if the shekels are lost after the ceremony? With regard to the treasurers, <b>what is their purpose</b> in this discussion? One does not take an oath on consecrated items. The Gemara answers: The mishna means that the agents <b>take an oath to the residents of the town in the presence of the</b> Temple <b>treasurers so that</b> the treasurers will <b>not suspect</b> the residents of the town of not having given their shekels. <b>Alternatively,</b> even if the town residents are considered to be honest people who would not be suspected of withholding their shekels, the agents must take an oath in the presence of the Temple treasurers <b>so that</b> the treasurers <b>will not regard</b> the residents to be <b>negligent</b> people who failed to properly watch over the shekels. Rabbi Yoḥanan said that the oath mentioned in the mishna is a rabbinic ordinance. The Gemara comments: According to this opinion, the mishna’s statement that if the shekels were lost before the collection of the chamber ceremony, the agents must take an oath to the residents of the town applies <b>even if the residents of the town took upon themselves to pay</b> other shekels in place of the first ones that were lost or stolen. In such a case, the residents relinquished their right to an oath from the agent, but since it is in reality consecrated property, there is still a rabbinic ordinance that the agent <b>cannot discharge</b> his obligations toward <b>consecrated</b> property <b>without an oath.</b>
Until this point the Gemara has dealt with a situation where the shekels were lost or stolen from the agent. With regard to a case where one <b>set aside his shekel and</b> subsequently <b>lost it</b> before the collection of the chamber ceremony took place, <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> Since the collection of the chamber had not yet taken place, the shekel is considered to be the property of the donor, and <b>he is held responsible for it until he hands it to the</b> Temple <b>treasurer.</b> Therefore, he must replace the lost shekel. <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish says: Consecrated items are</b> considered to be <b>in the possession of the Temple</b> treasury <b>wherever they are,</b> whether or not they have actually reached the hands of the Temple treasurer. Therefore, the donor is not responsible for replacing the lost shekel. The Gemara asks: <b>The mishna disagrees with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish,</b> as it teaches that <b>the</b> agents who lost the shekels <b>take an oath to the residents of the town, and the residents of the town contribute</b> shekels <b>in place of those</b> lost. Apparently, as long as the funds have not yet been collected, the shekels are considered to be in the possession of their owners. The Gemara answers: <b>Isn’t this also because</b> it is <b>a</b> rabbinically <b>instituted oath?</b> This oath is not by Torah law, but rather instituted by the Sages to encourage people to look after the shekels until they are brought to the Temple. According to Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish, no oath is required by Torah law because the shekels belong to the Temple.
It is taught in the mishna that if the shekels were stolen or lost by the agents and the residents of the town set aside other shekels as required, and then the first shekels were found or returned by the thief, both sets are considered to be consecrated shekels. <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The first ones are allocated to</b> the collection of <b>the new shekels,</b> from which the sacrifices of the coming year will be brought, and <b>the second ones are allocated to</b> the collection of <b>old shekels,</b> which are used to repair and maintain the Temple. The Gemara asks: <b>Which are the first ones and which are the second ones?</b> Are the first ones those that were lost, since they were contributed first, or are the first ones those that were contributed in place of the lost ones, and they are deemed so because they reached the Temple first? <b>Rabbi Pineḥas, son of Rabbi Ḥiyya, and Rabbi Abba Mari</b> disagreed. <b>One of</b> them <b>said</b> that the first shekels are <b>those that the residents of the town first sent to the Temple, and the other one said</b> that <b>those that reached the hands of the</b> Temple <b>treasurers first</b> are considered the first shekels.

Halakhah 2

<strong>Halakha 2 · MISHNA</strong> With regard to <b>one who gives his shekel to his fellow to contribute on his behalf</b> by placing it in the collection horn for him, <b>and</b> the fellow instead <b>contributed it for himself, if</b> at the time that he placed the shekel in the collection horn <b>the collection</b> of the chamber <b>had been collected,</b> the fellow is guilty of <b>misuse of consecrated property.</b> When they perform the collection of the chamber, the treasurers also have in mind the shekels that have been contributed but are not yet in the possession of the Temple treasury, so that all those who have contributed shekels will have a part in the communal sacrifices. Therefore, when the agent gives this shekel for himself, he is considered to be deriving benefit from a consecrated item and is guilty of unintentional misuse of consecrated property. With regard to <b>one who</b> mistakenly <b>contributes his shekel from consecrated</b> money, <b>and</b> then <b>the collection of</b> the chamber <b>was collected and an animal</b> purchased with those funds <b>was sacrificed</b> as a communal offering, he is guilty of <b>misuse of consecrated property</b> once the animal has been offered. This is because at that point the money used to purchase the animal is transferred to non-sacred status. However, before that point, merely contributing consecrated money is not considered misuse. If one mistakenly contributed his shekel from money used to redeem the fruits of the <b>second tithe or from money</b> from the permitted sale of produce grown during the <b>Sabbatical Year, he must eat</b> non-sacred fruits besides the ones he already possesses, <b>corresponding to</b> the value of the shekel, and he must treat them with the sanctity of second tithes or Sabbatical Year fruits.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It is taught in the mishna that <b>one who contributes</b> his shekel from consecrated money, when the collection of the chamber ceremony takes place and the animal has been offered, he is guilty of misuse of consecrated property. The Gemara records two different versions of this <i>halakha</i>. <b>We learned in the mishna: If the animal was offered,</b> indicating that if the animal had not been offered, even if the collection of the chamber ceremony has taken place, he has not misused consecrated property. <b>The school of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>taught: If the collection</b> of the chamber <b>had been collected,</b> indicating that he is guilty of misuse of consecrated property even before the animal was offered. The Gemara asks: <b>Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> from Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s school who taught: <b>If the collection had been collected? It is Rabbi Shimon, as Rabbi Shimon said:</b> When one sold items to the Temple treasurers for use in communal sacrifices, such as fine flour for meal-offerings or wine for libations, <b>he would immediately receive his money</b> from the treasurers, <b>and the priests,</b> who <b>are vigilant</b> with regard to mitzvot, would ensure that the relevant items would not become disqualified. Since the items sold became consecrated right away, the money became non-sacred at the time of the purchase; there was no need to wait until the items were actually offered. Similarly with regard to the shekels, the moment the Temple treasury purchases an animal for communal sacrifices, the shekels used become non-sacred and the person who contributed his shekel from consecrated money is guilty of misuse of consecrated items. The mishna taught that if one gave his shekel to an agent to place into the collection horns and the agent placed it in for himself, if the collection of the chamber ceremony has taken place, the agent has misused consecrated property. The Gemara comments: <b>This is difficult. If one steals his fellow’s burnt-offering, and slaughtered it without specifying</b> for whom he was offering it, <b>isn’t the unspecified</b> offering considered to be <b>in the name of the original owners, and</b> doesn’t it <b>atone</b> for them? The same principle should apply here. Since the treasurer performs the ceremony of the collection of the chamber without having anyone particular in mind, this shekel should be attributed to the one to whom the shekel belonged and not to the one who stole the shekel for himself. If the agent receives no benefit from it, why is he considered to have misused consecrated property? <b>Rabbi Yudan said: It should be explained</b> as referring to a case <b>where</b> this shekel was a <b>particular</b> coin that stood out among the other coins in the collection basket, and the Temple treasurer noticed it and removed this coin in the name of the agent, just as the members <b>of the house of Rabban Gamliel</b> were accustomed to do. When one brought a half-shekel from the house of Rabban Gamliel to the Temple, <b>he would intentionally push it into the basket</b> in such a way that the treasurer would notice it and place it among the collected half-shekels. The Gemara comments why it must be the case that the person who illicitly contributed the shekel in his own name is liable for misuse of consecrated funds only if that particular coin is identifiably among those collected. Otherwise, wouldn’t one <b>be concerned and say that perhaps</b> the half-shekel given was not used to purchase offerings and instead <b>fell among the remaining</b> contributions in the chamber? And if this is the case, <b>is there misuse of consecrated property with</b> regard to the <b>remaining</b> contributions? According to the majority of the Sages, one is not liable for misuse of consecrated property for the remaining funds of the Temple treasury chamber. Should one <b>rather</b> explain the mishna <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir? As Rabbi Meir says: One is</b> liable for <b>misuse of consecrated property with regard to the remaining</b> contributions of the chamber. The <i>halakha</i> is clearly not in accordance with R. Meir, as the majority rule otherwise, and the mishna does not indicate that it is reflecting a minority opinion. Therefore, <b>this too</b> is reasonable only if the mishna is addressing a noticeable coin like <b>the particular</b> coin <b>of the house of Rabban Gamliel, which</b> the treasurer <b>would intentionally collect in his name.</b> It is certain that it was included in the contributions collected for offerings, and therefore the agent is liable for misuse of consecrated property.
 The Gemara asks: <b>How did</b> the agent who placed the coin in the basket for himself <b>benefit,</b> that he should be liable for misuse? His goal was to perform a mitzva, and there is a principle that mitzvot were not given to derive benefit from them. We do not consider actions performed to fulfill a mitzva as personally beneficial to those who performed them. <b>Rabbi Avin said in the name of the Rabbis from there,</b> i.e., from Babylonia: <b>Since the court is liable to seize collateral from him and does not seize collateral from him,</b> it is <b>as if he benefited</b> personally from his action.
The mishna seems to indicate that one who brings his half-shekel <b>from second-tithe</b> money has fulfilled his obligation, although he must eat non-sacred fruits in place of the second-tithe money. The Gemara asks: Since the money was already consecrated for a different purpose, it is not possible for a new sanctity to extend to it, <b>as it is written: “But the firstling which is born first to God among animals, no man shall sanctify it”</b> (Leviticus 27:26), and the Sages learn from the wording of this verse: <b>That which is already sanctified, no</b> other <b>sanctity can extend to it.</b> If so, <b>what does he do</b> so that the second-tithe money he contributed should count as his half-shekel? The Gemara answers: <b>He brings a <i>sela</i>,</b> i.e., a coin <b>of non-sacred money, and says: The second-tithe money,</b> already contributed, <b>wherever it may be, should be redeemed upon this <i>sela</i>. That <i>sela</i> assumes the status of second tithe,</b> which is transferred from the half-shekel that had been placed in the collection horn. The rest, i.e., the original contribution, <b>becomes</b> consecrated with the sanctity of <b>shekels.</b>

Halakhah 3

<sup><b>[6a]</b></sup> <strong>Halakha 3 · MISHNA</strong> With regard to <b>one who gathers together</b> small <b>coins and said: These are for my shekel,</b> and subsequently discovered that it amounted to more than a half-shekel. <b>Beit Shammai say: The leftover</b> coins are placed in the collection horn designated for <b>a free-will offering,</b> as the money is consecrated property but it does not have the status of a shekel. <b>Beit Hillel say: The leftover</b> money <b>is non-sacred</b> property since, <i>ab initio</i>, he had in mind to consecrate a half-shekel and no more. An item that was consecrated by mistake does not have the status of consecrated property. However, if he originally said: I am gathering together this money so <b>that I will bring my shekel from these, they agree that the leftover</b> money <b>is non-sacred</b> property. If one who was obligated to bring a sin-offering gathered together coins and said: <b>These are for my sin-offering,</b> then if he had accumulated more than was needed, <b>they agree that the leftover</b> money must be designated as <b>a free-will offering.</b> However, if he originally said: <b>That I will bring my sin-offering from these, they agree that the leftover</b> money <b>is non-sacred</b> property. <b>Rabbi Shimon said: What is the difference between shekels and a sin-offering?</b> Why do Beit Hillel say that the leftover money is non-sacred property in the case of shekels, while with regard to a sin-offering they say that the leftover money is consecrated for a free-will offering? <b>Rather,</b> the issue is <b>that shekels have a fixed</b> value, a half-shekel and no more. Therefore, there is a clear amount beyond which one did not intend the money to become consecrated property. However, <b>a sin-offering has no fixed</b> value. Since the entire sum that one collected could have been used to purchase a sin-offering, whatever he didn’t use must at least be designated for a free-will offering. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: Even for shekels there is no</b> real <b>fixed</b> value. <b>For when the Jewish people ascended from the exile, they would contribute darics,</b> which are Median coins worth two shekels by Torah law. They brought these coins with them and would give a half of one to fulfill their half-shekel obligation. Later on, when the Median Empire was dissolved, <b>they reverted to contributing</b> with <b>a <i>sela</i>,</b> a silver coin of equal weight to the the shekel mentioned in the Torah. People would contribute a half-<i>sela</i> for their half-shekel requirement. When the value of this currency changed later on, <b>they reverted to contributing</b> with <b>a <i>tiva</i>,</b> a different coin which is worth a half-shekel. Some people <b>wished to contribute</b> only <b>dinars,</b> which are half the value of the <b><i>tiva</i>,</b> i.e., one quarter shekel in value. The Sages <b>refused to accept it</b> and required them to contribute at least the half-shekel mentioned in the Torah. Nevertheless, it is clear that the obligation of contributing shekels does not have a fixed value. <b>Rabbi Shimon said</b> in response: <b>Even so,</b> despite the fact that during different periods there were different amounts used to fulfill the obligation of the half-shekel, <b>everyone has equal standing,</b> i.e., at any particular time, everyone contributes the same amount. Therefore, any sum collected beyond that amount was not intended to be consecrated. <b>However, a sin-offering</b> has no fixed amount whatsoever; <b>this</b> person may <b>bring</b> an animal <b>worth a <i>sela</i>, and that one</b> may <b>bring</b> one <b>worth two, and this one</b> may <b>bring</b> one <b>worth three.</b> Therefore, it cannot be supposed that there was no intention to consecrate the whole sum.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It is taught in the mishna: <b>One who</b> gathers together coins and says: These are for my shekel, if he finds that he has more than a half-shekel, then according to the opinion of Beit Shammai, the remainder is designated for a free-will offering, and according to Beit Hillel the remainder is non-sacred property. <b>Rabbi Yosei</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Elazar: With regard to what do they disagree? With regard</b> to <b>one who gathers coin by coin [<i>peroterot</i>],</b> adding small coins bit by bit until they amount to a large sum. It is assumed that he intended to contribute only a half-shekel but did not pay attention to the fact that a larger sum had accumulated. According to Beit Hillel, an item that was consecrated by mistake does not become consecrated, and thus the leftover money is non-sacred property. <b>However, with</b> regard to <b>one who</b> takes a stack of coins and <b>says: These are for my shekel, everyone agrees that</b> he must have intended to consecrate the entire sum. Therefore, the <b>leftover</b> coins <b>are</b> designated for <b>a free-will offering.</b> <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya and Rabbi Beiva</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Elazar: With regard to what do they disagree? When one gathers coin by coin.</b> In that case Beit Shammai maintain that the leftover money is designated for a free-will offering. Since they hold that an item consecrated by mistake is in fact consecrated, all the money that he accumulated becomes consecrated property and must be designated as a free-will offering. However, with regard to <b>one who</b> takes a stack of coins and <b>says: These are for my shekel, everyone agrees that the leftover</b> coins <b>are non-sacred</b> property. Even Beit Shammai agree that he did not intend to consecrate a sum of money so beyond the requirement of the half-shekel <i>ab initio</i>. <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya said: The mishna supports</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Beivai, as we learned in the mishna</b> that <b>Rabbi Shimon said: What is the difference between</b> the leftover money from the <b>half-shekel</b> contribution, which is non-sacred property, <b>and</b> the leftover money for <b>sin-offerings,</b> which is designated for a free-will offering, <b>other than that shekels have a fixed</b> value <b>and a sin-offering has no fixed</b> value? The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> case <b>are we addressing? If</b> Rabbi Shimon is addressing the case <b>of one who</b> collects money in small amounts and <b>says</b> before he starts: <b>I will bring my shekel from these</b> coins, <b>everyone agrees that the leftover</b> coins <b>are non-sacred</b> property. <b>And if</b> he is addressing the case <b>of one who says: I will bring my sin-offering from</b> these coins, here too, <b>everyone agrees that the leftover</b> money <b>is non-sacred</b> property, as explained in the mishna. <b>Rather, this is the case we are addressing: when one</b> takes a stack of coins and <b>says: These are for my shekel.</b> Rabbi Shimon holds that with regard to <b>shekels, since their fixed</b> value <b>is</b> explicitly <b>from the Torah, the leftover</b> coins <b>are non-sacred</b> property, because even when he held all the money he intended only to take the value of a half-shekel from it. However, with regard to <b>a sin-offering, since it has no fixed value from the Torah,</b> as each person brings an animal of whatever value he wants, it is possible that one intended to bring an animal worth the entire value of the coins he took <i>ab initio</i>. Therefore, <b>the leftover</b> money <b>is</b> consecrated <b>for a free-will offering.</b> This is a proof for Rabbi Beivai’s opinion that when one takes a stack of coins and says: These are for my shekel, everyone agrees that the leftover money is non-sacred property. Rabbi Yosei understood the mishna such that when he says: These are for my shekel, everyone agrees that the leftover money is consecrated property. The Gemara asks: <b>What does Rabbi Yosei, in the name of Rabbi Elazar, do with this</b> proof? The Gemara answers: <b>He explains</b> Rabbi Shimon’s opinion in the mishna as <b>referring to one who collects coin by coin, and</b> it is <b>in accordance</b> only <b>with</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Hillel.</b> The Gemara challenges Rabbi Yosei’s version of Rabbi Elazar’s opinion: <b>Didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna: <b>The leftover shekels are non-sacred</b> property? This implies that in all situations where one consecrates shekels, including those where one said: These are for my shekel, the leftovers are non-sacred, which is in accordance with Rabbi Beivai’s explanation. The Gemara answers: There is no such implication. Rabbi Yosei <b>explained it as</b> limited to the case of <b>one who collects coin by coin, and</b> it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Hillel.</b> In such a case, the leftover money is non-sacred property.
The Gemara discusses a different issue. When a person <b>sets aside his shekel and thought</b> at that point <b>that he is obligated</b> to contribute it, and <b>it turns out that he is not obligated</b> to contribute it, as he had contributed it already, the shekel that he separated <b>is not consecrated,</b> just as other mistakenly consecrated items do not become consecrated. With regard to <b>one who sets aside two</b> shekels simultaneously <b>and thought that he is obligated</b> to contribute <b>two,</b> one for the current year and one for the previous year, <b>and it turns out that he is obligated</b> to contribute <b>only one,</b> as he had in fact contributed the previous year’s shekel, <b>what do you do with the second one</b> that was mistakenly set aside? The Gemara answers. <b>Let us hear</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>from this</b> <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one set aside his sin-offering,</b> and at that point <b>he thought that he was liable,</b> as he had unwittingly sinned in such a manner requiring him to bring a sin-offering, and <b>it turns out that he was not liable,</b> the animal <b>is not consecrated.</b> If <b>one set aside two</b> animals <b>and thought</b> at the time <b>that he</b> had unwittingly transgressed two prohibitions and <b>was</b> therefore <b>liable</b> to sacrifice <b>two</b> sin-offerings, and <b>it turns out that he was liable</b> to sacrifice <b>only one, what do you do with that second</b> animal? It must <b>rather be left to graze.</b> On the one hand, it is consecrated with the sanctity of a sin-offering, but on the other hand, since the person who consecrated it is not obligated in another sin-offering, it cannot be sacrificed. Therefore, it must be left to graze until it develops a blemish and is disqualified from being offered. It can then be sold, with the proceeds used to purchase free-will offerings. <b>So too, these</b> mistakenly set aside half-shekels, i.e., in the case of one who set aside two half-shekels and in the end was obligated only to contribute one, <b>are</b> designated <b>for a free-will offering.</b> The Gemara rejects this comparison between the case of the extra shekel and the case of the extra sin-offerings. <b>Here,</b> in the case of extra shekels, <b>how can you say: These</b> are designated for a free-will offering? The mishna distinguishes between the leftover money when collecting shekels, which is considered non-sacred property, and the leftover money when collecting for a sin-offering, which is considered consecrated for a free-will offering.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that even the half-shekel does not have a fixed value, as over the course of history the exact sum of a half-shekel varied. Rabbi Yehuda then describes the coins used for the half-shekel during various periods. The Gemara first explains the mishna’s terminology. <b>Darics are</b> golden <b>dinars.</b> The phrase: <b>They reverted to contributing</b> with <b>a <i>sela</i>,</b> is <b>as it sounds;</b> i.e., the equivalent of the shekel mentioned in the Torah, which is four silver dinar. The phrase: <b>They reverted to contributing</b> with <b>a <i>tiva</i>,</b> refers to a <b>half-<i>sela</i></b> coin, the equivalent of two silver dinar. The phrase: <b>They wished to contribute</b> only <b>dinars,</b> refers to <b>a karat,</b> i.e., a quarter of a <i>sela</i>, or one dinar. The mishna continues: <b>They refused to accept it from them.</b> The Sages did not agree to accept dinars in place of the half-shekel. From where did the Sages derive this? <b>From this</b> verse: <b>“We made ordinances for us, to charge ourselves yearly with the third part of a shekel for the service of the House of our God”</b> (Nehemiah 10:33). The third of a shekel cited in this verse is one-third of a daric, the prevalent coin of the time. Until that time, the custom was to contribute a half of the prevalent coin of the time. The Sages of that generation were concerned that over time the value of the prevalent coin would be reduced to the extent that people would no longer be contributing the value of the half-shekel of the Torah. The verse reports that they therefore set a minimum for the collection, which is the precedent for the Sages of the mishna not accepting the request to lower the fixed amount to a dinar. Once this verse is mentioned, the Gemara discusses other <i>halakhot</i> that are derived from it. <b>Rabbi Ḥilkiya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Aḥa:</b> This verse is about the mitzva of charity. The term <i>shelishit</i>, one-third, used in this verse contains superfluous letters, as it could have been written <i>shelish</i> and maintained its meaning. <b>From here</b> one may derive <b>that a person must donate his shekel three times a year,</b> meaning that he must give a shekel to charity three times a year. One may also derive <b>from here</b> that <b>one</b> may <b>not burden the community</b> by asking them for charity <b>more than three times a year. Rabbi Avin said: From here,</b> there is also support for the fact that the baskets into which the collection of the chamber was collected were <b>three <i>se’a</i></b> in size; and <b>from here,</b> there is a hint to the <b>three collection baskets</b> and the <b>three collections</b> of the chamber during the year.
<b>It is written: “This they shall give, everyone who passes among them that are numbered,</b> half a shekel after the shekel of the Sanctuary” (Exodus 30:13). <b>Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Neḥemya</b> disagree with regard to the reason for the mitzva. <b>One said: Because they sinned</b> with the Golden Calf <b>at the midpoint of the day, they should give a half-shekel. And one said: Because they sinned at the sixth hour of the day, they should give a half-shekel, whose sum is</b> equal to <b>six <i>garmisin</i>,</b> a small coin that was prevalent in that period. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua of the house of Rabbi Neḥemya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Zakkai: Because</b> the Jewish people <b>transgressed the Ten Commandments</b> at the time of the Golden Calf, <b>each one</b> of them <b>shall give ten <i>gera</i>,</b> which equals a half-shekel, as it is written: “This they shall give, half a shekel after the shekel of the Sanctuary; the shekel is twenty <i>gera</i>” (Exodus 30:13). Since the Jewish people violated the first commandment, it was as if they transgressed all ten (<i>Sifrei</i>, <i>Shelaḥ</i>). The Gemara cites an additional reason for the obligation to give a half-shekel: It atones for the sin of the sale of Joseph. First, it introduces this topic. <b>Rabbi Berekhya</b> and <b>Rabbi Levi</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish: Since</b> the sons of Jacob <b>sold</b> Joseph, <b>the firstborn of Rachel, for twenty silver</b> dinar, the nation was commanded that <b>each of them</b> must <b>redeem his firstborn son with twenty silver</b> dinar, which is five <i>sela</i>, as there are four dinar in a <i>sela</i>. The Gemara returns to the subject of the obligation to contribute a half-shekel: <b>Rabbi Pineḥas</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Levi: Since</b> ten of Jacob’s sons <b>sold</b> Joseph, <b>Rachel’s firstborn, for twenty silver</b> dinars, <b>each of them received</b> two dinars, which amounts to <b>a <i>tiva</i></b> or a half-<i>sela</i>; <b>therefore, each and every</b> man <b>must give a <i>tiva</i> for his shekel</b> every year.

Halakhah 4

<sup><b>[6b]</b></sup> <strong>Halakha 4 · MISHNA</strong> <b>The leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what was set aside for <b>shekels is non-sacred</b> property, in accordance with the opinion of Beit Hillel in the previous mishna. The mishna now discusses similar cases for other sacred items: However, with regard to <b>the leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what one set aside to purchase <b>the tenth of an ephah</b> of fine flour for a meal-offering and <b>the leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what one set aside to purchase offerings that he is liable to sacrifice due to ritual impurity or a sin, such as <b>the pairs</b> of birds <b>of a <i>zav</i>, the pairs</b> of birds <b>of a <i>zava</i>, and the pairs</b> of birds <b>of a woman after childbirth, sin-offerings,</b> or <b>guilt-offerings,</b> in these cases, <b>its leftover</b> money must be used for <b>free-will offerings</b> that are offered as repletion of the altar, i.e., burnt-offerings sacrificed at times when the altar was idle. <b>This is the principle: Whatever</b> money <b>is designated for a sin-offering or for a guilt-offering, its leftover</b> money must be used for <b>a free-will offering. The leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what one set aside to purchase <b>a burnt-offering</b> that he owes, due to a vow or to volunteering, must be used <b>for</b> another <b>burnt-offering</b> that he will bring in the future. <b>The leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what one set aside to purchase fine flour for <b>a meal-offering</b> must be used <b>for</b> another <b>meal-offering. The leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what one set aside to purchase <b>a peace-offering</b> must be used <b>for</b> another <b>peace-offering.</b> <b>The leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what one set aside to purchase a lamb for his <b>Paschal lamb</b> is not used for another Paschal lamb, such as for the following year. Rather, it is used <b>for</b> purchasing <b>a peace-offering. The leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what one set aside to purchase sacrifices for a number of <b>nazirites</b> must be used to purchase sacrifices <b>for</b> other <b>nazirites. The leftover</b> money <b>from</b> what a single <b>nazirite</b> set aside for his own offering must be used <b>for a free-will offering.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Yosei said: While I was still there,</b> in Babylonia, <b>I heard the voice of Rav Yehuda ask</b> his teacher <b>Shmuel: If one set aside his shekel and died</b> before he contributed it to the Temple treasurer, what is to be done with this money? Shmuel <b>said to him: It</b> must be <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>free-will offerings.</b> Rabbi Yosei asked: If a High Priest set aside money to purchase fine flour for his meal-offering and then discovered that he had separated more than was necessary, what should be done with <b>the leftover</b> money from <b>his tenth of an ephah?</b> Shmuel answered that this is a matter of dispute, as <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said that he</b> must <b>cast it into the Dead Sea</b> and dispose of it there. <b>Rabbi Elazar said it</b> must be <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>free-will offerings.</b> The Gemara comments. <b>The mishna disagrees with Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> as it is taught: <b>The leftover</b> money <b>from the shekel is non-sacred</b> property. However, with regard to <b>the leftover</b> money <b>from the tenth of an ephah, the leftover</b> money <b>from the pairs</b> of birds <b>of a <i>zav</i> and</b> from <b>the pairs</b> of birds <b>of a <i>zava</i>, the leftover</b> money <b>from the pairs</b> of birds <b>of a woman after childbirth,</b> from <b>sin-offerings and guilt-offerings,</b> all of <b>this leftover</b> money must be used <b>for free-will offerings.</b> The mishna did not distinguish between different types of meal-offerings, so the High Priest’s meal-offering must also be included. However, according to the mishna, its leftover money is used for a free-will offering and not cast into the Dead Sea, as Rabbi Yoḥanan requires. The Gemara asks: <b>What does Rabbi Yoḥanan do with</b> the mishna? The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>explains</b> that <b>it</b> is not referring to the leftover money of a High Priest’s meal-offering. Rather, it is referring to <b>the leftover</b> money <b>of the tenth of an ephah of the meal-offering of a sinner</b> among the <b>entire Jewish people.</b> Everyone agrees that the leftover money of this type of meal-offering must be used for a free-will offering; however, the leftover money of a High Priest’s meal-offering is cast into the Dead Sea.
<b>Rabbi Yosei said: On this</b> point <b>Abba bar Ba raised</b> a difficulty. <b>As they,</b> the Sages of Babylonia, <b>said: From where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> an animal that was designated for use as <b>the Paschal lamb,</b> <i>ab initio</i>, and was ultimately slaughtered before its proper time as a peace-offering is valid, as it <b>is transformed and designated as a peace-offering? The verse states: “If his offering for the sacrifice of the peace-offering is from the flock”</b> (Leviticus 3:6). This verse indicates that <b>any</b> offering <b>that</b> comes <b>from the flock,</b> i.e., sheep or goats, may <b>come as a peace-offering.</b> The verse is referring to a Paschal lamb, about which it is written: “You shall take it from the sheep or from the goats” (Exodus 12:5), and it must refer to one that was offered before its proper time; however, if it were offered on the eve of Passover as a peace-offering it would clearly be an invalid. Rabbi Abba bar Ba <b>raised an objection</b> with regard to this source: <b>A burnt-offering</b> also comes <b>from the flock,</b> and yet its leftover money is not used for a peace-offering; it is used for a burnt-offering. The Gemara answers: The verse is referring to <b>items that</b> may <b>come only from the flock;</b> this <b>excludes burnt-offerings, which</b> may <b>come even from cattle,</b> as it is written: “From the cattle or from the flock you may bring your offering” (Leviticus 1:2). Rabbi Abba bar Ba <b>raised</b> another <b>objection: A guilt-offering</b> comes only from the flock, and the mishna taught that its leftover money is used for a free-will offering. However, according to this explanation, it too should be able to be sacrificed as a peace-offering. <b>Rabbi Bun bar Kahana</b> said that the phrase <b>“From the flock”</b> indicates <b>that which comes from all</b> kinds in <b>the flock,</b> i.e., sheep and goats. This <b>excludes a guilt-offering, which</b> may <b>come from only rams.</b> The Gemara challenges Rabbi Bun. <b>In every</b> other <b>place you say that</b> the word <b>from</b> comes <b>to exclude</b> items from a particular category, <b>and here you say</b> that the word <b>from</b> comes <b>to include</b> the Paschal lamb, as “from” indicates all kinds of animals that can be considered part of a flock. The Gemara answers. <b>Rabbi Mana said:</b> In <b>this</b> verse <b>too, “from”</b> comes <b>to exclude.</b> One <b>exclusion</b> is that the Paschal lamb <b>may not come</b> from a lamb that is <b>two years old,</b> but rather from a lamb that is in its first year. There is an additional <b>exclusion</b> that a Paschal lamb <b>may not come</b> from a <b>female</b> animal, but only from a male. Accordingly, Rabbi Bun bar Kahana’s explanation that the verse: “If his offering is from the flock” is referring to the Paschal lamb, as it may come from any of the animals of the flock, is not based on the word “from” but rather on the superfluous letter <i>heh</i>, which adds the definite article to the phrase “the flock,” as opposed to the verse simply writing “a flock.” Rabbi Mana adds: <b>And</b> furthermore, even if Rabbi Bun bar Kahana would interpret the verse “If his offering is from the flock” <b>with regard to a guilt-offering</b> and not to a Paschal lamb, the word <b>“from”</b> would <b>also</b> come <b>to exclude.</b> It would teach <b>that</b> guilt-offerings may <b>come only from rams</b> and not from goats or female sheep. Rabbi Abba bar Ba <b>raised</b> another <b>objection</b> on the source of the Babylonian Sages. <b>Isn’t it written: “If his offering is from the flock, whether from the sheep or from the goats for a burnt-offering”</b> (Leviticus 1:10)? According to your opinion, this verse could just as easily be interpreted to indicate that in the case of any offering that comes from sheep or from goats, the leftover money must be used for a burnt-offering. However, <b>if so, the leftover</b> money <b>of the Paschal lamb,</b> which may come from sheep or from goats, must then <b>come as a burnt-offering</b> and not as a peace-offering. What indication is there that the concerning a peace-offering is the preferred one? The Gemara answers. <b>Rabbi Avun said: An item that is</b> designated <b>for eating,</b> e.g., the Paschal lamb, which is eaten by its owners, may be <b>transformed into</b> another <b>item that is</b> designated <b>for eating,</b> e.g., a peace-offering. However, <b>an item that is</b> designated <b>for eating,</b> like the Paschal lamb, may <b>not</b> be <b>transformed into another item that is not</b> designated <b>for eating,</b> such as the burnt-offering, which is completely consumed on the altar. <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Rabbi Bun said</b> another answer: The status of <b>offerings of minor sanctity</b> may <b>be transformed and designated as</b> other <b>sacrifices of minor sanctity.</b> Therefore, the Paschal lamb, which is of minor sanctity, can be transformed and designated as a peace-offering, which is also of minor sanctity. However, the status of <b>offerings of minor sanctity</b> may <b>not be transformed and designated as offerings of the most sacred order,</b> such as a burnt-offering. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: On this</b> point, <b>Rabbi Ḥanina raised</b> an issue <b>that they,</b> the Sages of Babylonia, <b>said</b> that the status of <b>a Paschal lamb</b> that is not offered at the proper time <b>is not transformed and designated as a peace-offering, unless it was slaughtered as a peace-offering;</b> but if it was slaughtered for another offering, such as a burnt-offering, it is disqualified. However, <b>I say that even if he slaughtered it as a burnt-offering,</b> it may be transformed and designated as a peace-offering. <b>Rabbi Ila said: The reason,</b> i.e., the source, for the opinion <b>of</b> Rabbi Ḥanina quoted by <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> is the redundancy in the verse, as it is written: <b>“If his offering for the sacrifice of the peace-offering is from the flock”</b> (Leviticus 3:6). It could have simply written: If his offering of the peace-offering is from the flock. The extra phrase “for the sacrifice” indicates that a Paschal lamb slaughtered as <b>any</b> kind of <b>offering,</b> including a burnt-offering, <b>it comes</b> to be <b>a peace-offering.</b> The Gemara asks: According to this opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan, may a Paschal lamb that was sacrificed as a burnt-offering be <b>transformed</b> and designated as a peace-offering even it was slaughtered with <b>a disqualifying intention?</b> If, during one of the services involved in an offering’s sacrifice, i.e., slaughter, receiving the blood, bringing it to the altar, or sprinkling it on the altar, the priest or the one bringing the offering entertains the thought of performing any of the other services or eating the offering at a time that is unfit, the offering is thereby invalidated [<i>piggul</i>]. The Gemara asks: <b>How was it done?</b> If <b>one slaughtered</b> the Paschal lamb <b>as a burnt-offering in order to sprinkle the blood the following day, in any case,</b> whether it is transformed and designated as a burnt-offering or as a peace-offering <b>it is disqualified.</b> The intention to sprinkle the blood at an improper time disqualifies the offering, so it does not matter whether it is transformed and designated as a peace-offering or not. The issue is whether one who eats an olive-bulk from the meat of this offering is liable to receive <i>karet</i>, like one who eats <i>piggul</i> meat, or whether he is not liable, like one who eats a regular disqualified offering. The principle is that an offering becomes <i>piggul</i> only when one slaughters an otherwise valid offering with an intention that disqualifies it. However, when the offering is disqualified for some other reason, the improper intention does not render the offering <i>piggul</i>. Therefore, <b>if you say</b> that even if one offers the Paschal lamb with <b>a disqualifying intention,</b> such as to sprinkle the blood the following day, <b>it is transformed</b> and designated as a peace-offering, and if not for the disqualifying intention it would be a valid offering, it is deemed <b><i>piggul</i></b> and one who eats the meat is liable to receive <i>karet</i>. However, <b>if you say</b> that when the Paschal lamb is offered with <b>a disqualifying intention,</b> it <b>is not transformed</b> and designated as a peace-offering, since it remains a Paschal lamb and it was sacrificed at the wrong time, then it is simply a disqualified offering, but not one that is <b><i>piggul</i>.</b> In that case, one who eats the meat is not liable to receive <i>karet</i>. This question remains unresolved.
If one slaughters the Paschal lamb on Passover eve as a peace-offering, before the proper time for the Paschal lamb, it is transformed and designated as a peace-offering, and it is valid. However, if one slaughters it as a Paschal lamb, then it is disqualified, since it is not the proper time. The Gemara asks: What is the <i>halakha</i> if one slaughters it <b>for itself and not for itself,</b> meaning that at the beginning of the act of slaughtering one intends to slaughter it as a Paschal lamb, but at its conclusion he does so as a peace-offering, and it is not on Passover eve but <b>during the rest of the days of the year?</b> <b>Rabbi Bun bar Ḥiyya</b> said <b>in the name of Shmuel bar Abba:</b> During the rest of the year it has no name; it is not a Paschal lamb, as it is not its proper time, nor is it a peace-offering, as he did not consecrate it for that purpose <i>ab initio</i>. Despite this, if one slaughters it without specifying for which offering he is doing so, it becomes a peace-offering. <b>Since it has no name</b> in that case, then, by the same reasoning, even if one begins to slaughter it as a Paschal lamb, <b>it becomes as one who slaughters it for itself,</b> a Paschal lamb, <b>and not for itself,</b> a peace-offering, <b>silently,</b> without specifying his intention, <b>and it is valid</b> as a peace-offering. Just as total silence determines that it is meant to be for a peace-offering, so too, the explicit change of intention at the end of the slaughtering is sufficient to accomplish the same. One of the disciples <b>said to</b> Rabbi Bun: <b>If this is so,</b> that even when one begins to slaughter the animal with specific intent as a Paschal lamb it is transformed and designated as a peace-offering, then <b>even if one slaughters it for itself in order to sprinkle its blood not for itself,</b> but rather for a peace-offering, <b>it should become, from the first moment, as if he slaughtered it for itself and not for itself silently, and it too should be valid</b> as a peace-offering. <b>Rabbi Abba Mari said: What, shall we say,</b> i.e., is it so clear, that when one slaughters for itself and not for itself <b>silently,</b> without specifying his intention to sprinkle the blood for a peace-offering, that it is considered a valid peace-offering? <b>Or,</b> perhaps, <b>shall we say</b> that even when one slaughters silently with the intent to sprinkle its blood for a peace-offering, it is as if he specified that he is slaughtering for the Paschal lamb, in which case it is transformed and designated as a peace-offering and it is <b>disqualified?</b> The difference is that in the previous case there is not one entire part of the service that is completed for the Paschal lamb. However, in this case, the slaughtering is completed. It is only the intent with regard to a later stage, i.e., the sprinkling of the blood, that could possibly transform it and designate it as a peace-offering. That is not a sufficiently compelling logical extension.
The mishna taught: <b>The leftover</b> money gathered by the <b>nazirite</b> beyond what he needs for his offerings must be used <b>for free-will offerings.</b> On this point, <b>Rav Ḥisda</b> <sup><b>[7a]</b></sup> <b>said: That</b> is only <b>when the</b> nazirite’s <b>sin-offering was sacrificed last,</b> after he offered his burnt-offering and his peace-offering. In that case, any extra leftover money must be used for a free-will offering, as taught earlier in the mishna. <b>However, if</b> he already brought his burnt-offering and sin-offering, and <b>the peace-offering was sacrificed last, the leftover</b> money that he set aside for his sacrifices must be used for <b>a peace-offering,</b> as with all leftover funds of peace-offerings. <b>Rabbi Ze’eira said:</b> This is not so. Rather, <b>even if the peace-offering was sacrificed last, there is one</b> consistent <b><i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai <b>with regard to the nazirite, that the leftover</b> money from that which he set aside for his nazirite offerings must be used for <b>free-will offerings.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>A <i>baraita</i> supports this one and a</b> different <b><i>baraita</i> supports that one. One <i>baraita</i> supports</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ze’eira,</b> as it was taught in a mishna (<i>Me’ila</i> 11b) that in the case of a nazirite who dies after he had set aside a lump sum for all his sacrifices, the money is to be used for free-will burnt-offerings. The <i>baraita</i> elaborates: <b>Which</b> coins <b>are</b> considered <b>unspecified coins? Any</b> money <b>that has money mixed in it for sin-offerings</b> whose owners have <b>died,</b> as when he set aside this money he specified that it would be used for all of his sacrifices, including the sin-offering. <b>And even if he had set aside the money for sin-offerings from</b> the rest of the money designated for the offerings, all the money <b>is</b> considered <b>unspecified money.</b> Once the nazirite dies, the money that he set aside is used for free-will offerings. Apparently, this applies even if he brought the peace-offering last. Therefore, this is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Ze’eira. <b>A <i>baraita</i> supports</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Ḥisda,</b> as it was taught with regard to a nazirite who set aside money for his sacrifices and did not initially specify which coins were designated for which sacrifice, and then took some of that money and said: <b>This</b> money <b>is</b> designated <b>for my sin-offering, and the rest is</b> designated <b>for the rest of my nazirite</b> offerings, <b>and he died</b> before actually purchasing the offerings. The money designated for the sin-offering must be cast into the Dead Sea. With regard to the rest of the money, half must be used for burnt-offerings and half for peace-offerings. If one used the money that was designated for peace-offerings and burnt-offerings for his own purposes <b>he is guilty of misuse of consecrated property</b> only if he used <b>all the money,</b> as that certainly included the money designated for a burnt-offering. However, <b>he is not guilty of misuse of consecrated property</b> if he used only <b>some of it,</b> as it is possible that he used only the portion of the money that was meant for peace-offerings; one who uses that money is not guilty of misuse of consecrated property, since the meat of a peace-offering may be eaten by the owner (<i>Me’ila</i> 7b). Since the author of this <i>baraita</i> <b>did not say: If he died the money</b> must be <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>free-will</b> burnt-offerings, but rather that they are to be used for both burnt-offerings and peace-offerings, it seems that he holds in accordance with Rav Ḥisda; once the nazirite separated the money for his sin-offering from the rest of the money that he set aside, the leftover amount may be used for a peace-offering. This is not in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Ze’eira, who holds that all the money must be used for free-will burnt-offerings.
Since the Gemara mentioned the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the leftover money of a nazirite’s offerings, it discusses the leftover portion of his meal-offering as well. <b>Rav Ḥisda said:</b> With regard to <b>the leftover</b> portion <b>of a nazirite’s loaves,</b> i.e., if the nazirite set aside fine flour for his meal-offering and found that he had set aside more than was necessary, the rest of the fine flour must be <b>left to spoil.</b> Similarly, if he set aside money for his meal-offering and then found that he had set aside more than was necessary, he must cast the leftover money in the Dead Sea. <b>Rabbi Yosei said: And</b> Rav Ḥisda <b>is right. You cannot offer it on its own,</b> as <b>loaves are never brought</b> as an offering <b>on their own;</b> they are offered only along with a sheep as a burnt-offering or along with a ram as a peace-offering. <b>You cannot offer it with</b> the burnt-offering or peace-offering belonging to <b>another nazirite,</b> as <b>no</b> offering of a <b>nazirite is offered without loaves;</b> when the other nazirite took his vow upon himself, he obligated himself to bring his offerings along with his meal-offerings from his own funds, and he has no need for this leftover fine flour. <b>Therefore, one must say</b> that <b>the leftover</b> portion <b>of a nazirite’s loaves</b> must be <b>left to spoil,</b> as it has no other use. § When a nazirite set aside wine or money to purchase wine for his libations and there was more than necessary, the students of the study hall <b>thought to say</b> that the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the leftover portion of <b>his loaves and</b> the <i>halakha</i> of the <b>leftover</b> portion <b>of his libations is the same,</b> and that the leftover portion of libations must also be left to spoil. <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Rabbi Bun</b> said: <b>The leftover</b> portion <b>of libations</b> is different, as <b>it is an offering of the most sacred order</b> and therefore must be <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>free-will offerings.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Rabbi Bun,</b> it becomes apparent that <b>three</b> Rabbis all said <b>the same</b> thing: <b>Shmuel, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Ḥisda</b> with regard to the leftover portion of libations; <b>Rav Ḥisda</b> himself; <b>and Rabbi Elazar</b> all agree that the leftover portion of offerings of the most sacred order must be allocated to communal free-will offerings. The Gemara elaborates: The opinion of <b>Rav Ḥisda</b> is evident from <b>that which we have said.</b> Rabbi Yosei bar Rabbi Bun’s opinion that the leftover portion of libations must be allocated for free-will offerings is based on Rav Ḥisda’s opinion that only the leftover portion of a peace-offering is to be destroyed, but not the leftover portion of libations. This is due to the fact that they are offerings of the most sacred order. The opinion of <b>Shmuel</b> is evident, <b>as Rabbi Yosei said: While I was still there,</b> in Babylonia, <b>I heard the voice of Rabbi Yehuda ask</b> his teacher <b>Shmuel: If one set aside his shekel and died</b> before he contributed it to the Temple treasury, what is to be done with this money? Shmuel <b>said to him: It</b> must be <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>free-will offerings.</b> These shekels were used to purchase communal burnt-offerings, which are of the most sacred order. Therefore, it seems that the allocation of these shekels follows the same principle as do the offerings intended to be purchased with the shekels and the leftover money must be allocated for free-will offerings. The opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> is evident from that which he <b>says</b> with regard to <b>the leftover</b> money <b>from</b> the High Priest’s <b>tenth of an ephah.</b> The High Priest would offer a tenth of an ephah of fine flour made into griddle-cakes daily, half in the morning and half in the evening. When the High Priest died during the day, half would be left over. The <i>amora’im</i> disputed what should be done with the leftovers: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: He</b> must <b>cast it into the Dead Sea. Rabbi Elazar says: It</b> must be <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>free-will offerings,</b> as it is an offering of the most sacred order.

Halakhah 5

 <strong>Halakha 5 · MISHNA</strong> <b>The leftover</b> money collected <b>for</b> freeing unspecified <b>captives</b> must be allocated <b>to</b> freeing <b>captives. The leftover</b> money collected <b>for</b> freeing <b>a</b> specific <b>captive</b> is given as a gift <b>to that captive. The leftover</b> money collected as charity <b>for the poor</b> must be allocated <b>to the poor. The leftover</b> money collected for <b>a</b> specific <b>poor</b> person is given as a gift <b>to that poor</b> person. <b>The leftover</b> money collected <b>for</b> burying <b>the dead</b> must be allocated to burying <b>the dead. The leftover</b> money collected to bury or provide burial shrouds <b>for a</b> particular <b>deceased</b> person is given <b>to his heirs. Rabbi Meir says:</b> It is uncertain what should be done, and therefore <b>the leftover</b> money <b>for the deceased should be placed</b> in a safe place <b>until Elijah comes</b> and teaches what should be done. <b>Rabbi Natan says:</b> With <b>the leftover</b> money collected <b>for a deceased</b> person they <b>build a monument [<i>nefesh</i>] on his grave for him.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to a case where the community <b>collected money</b> to finance the expenses of burying a deceased person <b>with the presumption that he did not have</b> money in his estate to cover these expenses and <b>it was</b> subsequently <b>found that he had</b> money, <b>Rabbi Yirmeya thought to say</b> that the <i>halakha</i> should be in accordance with the first <i>tanna</i> in the mishna, that <b>the leftover</b> money <b>for a deceased</b> person is given as a gift <b>to his heirs.</b> There is no difference whether only a portion of the money was needed and a portion was left, or whether there was no need at all for the money and all of it now remains. <b>Rabbi Idi of Ḥutra said to him: Bring yourself</b> [<b><i>hagga atzmekha</i></b>] to consider the matter and you will see that the two cases are not similar, as surely the townspeople <b>intended</b> to contribute their money <b>only for him,</b> for the deceased. They do not mind if the heirs receive any extra money, as it is difficult to collect precisely the right amount for the burial. However, had they known that their money would not be used for the burial at all, and that the entire amount would be given to the heirs, they presumably would not have initially agreed to contribute. Rabbi Yirmeya <b>said</b> in response <b>to</b> Rabbi Idi of Ḥutra: <b>I didn’t say</b> that <b>this</b> was the definitive <i>halakha</i>, as I merely suggested what I think should be done. However, <b>from where do you</b> derive the distinction you are making? The Gemara leaves the issue unresolved.
§ <b>It was taught in the name of Rabbi Natan:</b> With <b>the leftover</b> money that was collected <b>for</b> burying <b>a deceased</b> person <b>a monument is built on his grave, and</b> wine is bought for <b>spraying over his bier</b> to make a pleasant odor. The mishna teaches that the leftover money for freeing a specific captive or for the support of a particular poor person is given to that person. Similarly, <b>it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One</b> may <b>not redeem a captive with</b> money that was collected for <b>another captive.</b> Similarly, <b>one</b> may <b>not</b> purchase <b>a garment for</b> one poor person with charity <b>collected</b> to to purchase <b>a garment</b> for a different poor person. Nonetheless, <b>one does not protest against the leaders [<i>parnasim</i>]</b> of the community <b>about this</b> if they choose to do so in exigent circumstances. After discussing building a monument for a deceased person, the Gemara adds that <b>it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says: One does not construct monuments</b> for the graves <b>of righteous people.</b> The purpose of a monument is to remember the dead person, and Torah scholars do not need a monument, as <b>their words</b> of Torah that continue to be taught <b>are their memorial.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan was walking while leaning on</b> the shoulder of <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, and Rabbi Eliezer was watching him and hiding from him.</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said: This Babylonian has done two</b> improper <b>things to me. One, he didn’t inquire after my welfare;</b> and <b>another, he is hiding</b> from me, as though he doesn’t want to speak with me. <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Idi</b> tried to placate Rabbi Yoḥanan and <b>said to him: This is the custom among them,</b> i.e., among Babylonians. <b>The small,</b> less prominent people <b>do not inquire after the welfare of great,</b> prominent <b>people;</b> they only respond to them. That is the reason he did not inquire after your welfare. <b>Their custom is to fulfill</b> that which is written in the verse: <b>“The young men saw me and hid themselves, and the aged rose up and stood”</b> (Job 29:8). When Rabbi Ya’akov bar Idi saw that Rabbi Yoḥanan was not mollified, <b>he said to him: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to passing in front of the Adura statue?</b> Does that fall into the category of giving honor to idolatry? Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him: What honor are you giving it</b> by merely passing in front of it? <b>You could pass in front of it and blind its eyes,</b> meaning that you could pass in front of it and insult it. Rabbi Ya’akov bar Idi <b>said to him:</b> If so, <b>Rabbi Eliezer was right not to pass in front of you,</b> since if he had passed in front of you but maintained the custom of Babylonians not to inquire after your welfare, it would have been a slight to your honor. Rabbi Yoḥanan continued: <b>That Babylonian did something else</b> wrong, in <b>that he did not say a <i>halakha</i> in my name,</b> as he repeated without attribution something I taught him. <b>Rabbi Ami and Rabbi Asi entered.</b> In order to appease Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>they said to him: Rabbi, there was an incident</b> that occurred <b>in the synagogue of the weavers [<i>tarsiyyim</i>],</b> where they were discussing the halakhic status of <b>a door bolt,</b> a vertical bar that is affixed to the door in order to push into the ground, <b>which has a knob [<i>gelustera</i>] protruding at its top.</b> <sup><b>[7b]</b></sup> <b>Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yosei disagreed</b> about this issue <b>until</b> it reached a point <b>that they tore up a Torah scroll</b> in their anger. Before the Gemara resumes the story, it asks: <b>Could it enter your mind that</b> these Sages tore up a Torah scroll? <b>Rather,</b> it means that as each of them pulled it in his own direction, <b>a Torah scroll was torn.</b> The Gemara returns to the story told by Rabbi Ami and Rabbi Asi: <b>There was a certain old man,</b> one of the Sages, <b>named Rabbi Yosei ben Kisma,</b> and <b>he said: I will be surprised if this synagogue does not become a house of idolatry,</b> since the Sages say that whoever is angry should be in your eyes like an idol worshipper. The Gemara relates that although Rabbi Ami and Rabbi Asi thought that Rabbi Yoḥanan would be appeased after hearing this story disparaging anger between Torah scholars, Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>responded and said: This is</b> an incident <b>between colleagues.</b> How can you compare that to my situation, as Rabbi Eliezer is my disciple? <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Idi entered before</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>and said to him</b> that <b>it is written: “As God commanded Moses His servant, so did Moses command Joshua</b> and so did Joshua; he left nothing undone from all that God had commanded Moses” (Joshua 11:15). From here it is evident that Joshua taught the Jewish people all of the Torah that he learned from Moses. <b>Is it possible</b> to conceive <b>that</b> with <b>every statement that Joshua made while sitting and expounding to the Jewish people</b> he would diligently <b>say: Thus said Moses?”</b> This does not seem plausible. <b>Rather, Joshua would sit and expound, and everyone knew that it is the Torah of Moses. Similarly, you,</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan, should know that <b>Eliezer, your disciple, is sitting and expounding before his own disciples, and</b> although he does not say so explicitly, <b>everyone knows that it is your Torah. Rabbi Yoḥanan said to</b> Rabbi Ami and Rabbi Asi, who had tried unsuccessfully to appease him: <b>Why is it that you do not know how to appease like ben Idi our colleague?</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the reason that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan was so</b> insistent <b>that</b> people <b>say the <i>halakha</i> in his name?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Since even King David entreated God with regard to this</b> issue <b>to have mercy</b> on him, <b>as it is stated: “I will dwell in Your tent forever; I will take refuge in the covert of Your wings, Selah”</b> (Psalms 61:5). <b>Did David imagine that he would live and endure forever? Rather, this is what David said before the Holy One, Blessed be He: Master of the Universe,</b> may <b>I merit that my words will be said in my name in synagogues and study halls,</b> and through this I will attain perpetual life for myself. The Gemara adds that <b>Shimon ben Nezira said in the name of Rabbi Yitzḥak: Every Torah scholar from whose mouth people quote a matter of <i>halakha</i> in this world,</b> even after his death, <b>his lips move</b> along <b>with it in the grave, as it is stated: “And your palate is like the best wine…moving gently the lips of those that sleep”</b> (Song of Songs 7:10). <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>a mass of</b> heated <b>grapes, once a person places his finger on them,</b> they <b>immediately</b> issue <b>froth,</b> as the wine bubbles up and moves around, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>the lips of the righteous, when people quote matters of <i>halakha</i> from the mouths of the righteous, their lips move with them in the grave.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What pleasure is there for</b> a righteous person when his lips move in the grave? Shimon <b>bar Nezira said:</b> He derives pleasure <b>like one who drinks spiced wine. Rabbi Yitzḥak said:</b> His pleasure is <b>like that of one who drinks aged wine. Even</b> after <b>he drinks it, the taste</b> of the wine remains <b>in his mouth.</b> Similarly, when a Torah scholar’s teachings are quoted in his name in this world, his soul in the Garden of Eden enjoys the feeling.
<b>Rabbi Giddel said: One who quotes a teaching in the name of the one who said it should see</b> in his mind’s eye <b>the author of the teaching as if he</b> were <b>standing opposite him</b> at that moment, <b>as it is stated: “Surely man walks as a semblance”</b> (Psalms 39:7). When one thinks of another by quoting the Torah he taught, one should visualize the image or semblance of that person. On a related note, the Gemara continues. <b>It is written: “Most men will proclaim every man his own goodness”</b> (Proverbs 20:6). <b>This is</b> referring to <b>all</b> other <b>people,</b> meaning that most people will quote another person’s Torah thoughts without attributing them to their author. However, the verse continues: <b>“But a faithful man who can find?”</b> That is, who is faithful and accurate in reporting others’ teachings? <b>This is</b> referring to <b>Rabbi Ze’eira,</b> who took great care to maintain the accuracy of the tradition. <b>As Rabbi Ze’eira said: We need not concern ourselves with the traditions of Rav Sheshet, as he is an open-eyed man,</b> a euphemism for a blind person. Since he could not see his master’s face while studying with him, it is possible that he did not report his master’s words accurately. The Gemara relates another incident that illustrates Rabbi Ze’eira’s concern with the accurate conveyance of tradition. <b>Rabbi Ze’eira said to Rabbi Asi: Did the Rabbi,</b> i.e., Rabbi Asi, <b>know bar Petayya, that you quote teachings in his name?</b> Rabbi Asi <b>said to him:</b> I heard them from <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> who <b>said them in his name. Rabbi Ze’eira said to Rabbi Asi: Did the Rabbi,</b> i.e., Rabbi Asi, <b>know Rav, that you quote teachings in his name? He said to him:</b> I heard them from <b>Rabbi Adda bar Ahava,</b> who <b>said them in his name.</b>
Having mentioned a verse in which David expressed a wish that after his death people would say teachings in his name in this world, the Gemara cites other requests by David. <b>There is no generation without scoffers. In David’s generation, what would the ridiculers of his generation do,</b> when they heard that David was not permitted to build the Temple and only his son Solomon would merit to build it? <b>They would go near David’s windows and say to him: David, David, when will the Temple be built? When will</b> we be able to say: <b>“Let us go to the House of the Lord”</b> (Psalms 122:1) as David himself had requested? <b>And David would respond</b> with a kind of oath: <b>Even though they intend to anger me, may</b> evil <b>come upon me if I do not rejoice in their words,</b> as I too want the Temple to be built soon, even if it means that I must leave this world before my time, <b>as it is written: “I rejoiced when they said to me: Let us go to the House of the Lord”</b> (Psalms 122:1). When David said this, God answered him: <b>“When your days are fulfilled and you will sleep with your fathers”</b> (II Samuel 7:12). This verse indicates that this is what <b>the Holy One, Blessed be He, said to David: I reckon full days for you and not deficient days,</b> i.e., I will not deduct any days from your life. <b>Won’t Solomon, your son, build the Temple only in order to sacrifice communal offerings? The righteousness and justice that you perform are more pleasant to me than offerings, as it is stated: “To perform righteousness and justice is more acceptable to the Lord than an offering”</b> (Proverbs 21:3). 

Chapter 3



Halakhah 1

<strong>Halakha 1 · MISHNA</strong> <b>On three occasions during the year the</b> ceremony of the <b>collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber is performed.</b> During the ceremony, a priest enters the treasury chamber with three containers, lifts up [<i>torem</i>] some of the coins, and places them in the containers. These funds, known as the collection of the chamber, are used to purchase animals for communal offerings and other needs of the Temple. These three occasions are: <b>Half a month,</b> fifteen days, <b>before Passover,</b> on the day before the first of the month of Nisan; <b>half a month before <i>Shavuot</i>,</b> on or around the twentieth of Iyar; <b>half a month before the festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i>, on the day before Rosh HaShana. <b>These</b> three days <b>are</b> also <b>the due dates</b> that were established by the Sages <b>for</b> the setting aside of <b>animal tithes.</b> On each of these days one is obligated to tithe the animals that were born during the intervening period, and it is prohibited for him to eat or sell them until he does so. <b>This is the statement of Rabbi Akiva.</b> <b>Ben Azzai says</b> that the dates established by the Sages for the setting aside of animal tithes are <b>the twenty-ninth of Adar, the first of Sivan, and the twenty-ninth of Av.</b> <b>Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Shimon say</b> that the dates for the animal tithes are <b>the first of Nisan, the first of Sivan, and the twenty-ninth of Elul.</b> <b>And why did</b> Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Shimon <b>say the twenty-ninth of Elul and not the first of Tishrei,</b> as they said the first of Nisan and Sivan? <b>Because</b> the first of Tishrei <b>is</b> the <b>festival</b> of Rosh HaShana, <b>and it is not permitted to tithe on a Festival. Therefore,</b> the Sages <b>advanced</b> the day of tithing the animals born over the course of the summer <b>to the twenty-ninth of Elul.</b>
 <strong>GEMARA:</strong> It was taught in the mishna that funds are collected from the Temple treasury chamber three times a year: Before Passover, before <i>Shavuot</i>, and before <i>Sukkot</i>. The term preceding the name of the Festival that is used in the mishna is <i>bifros</i>. With regard to this word, <b>Rabbi Abbahu said: Wherever it is taught to us</b> in the mishna using the term <b><i>peras</i>,</b> which is from the same root as <i>bifros</i>, it means <b>half.</b> In this case it is referring to <b>half of the thirty-day</b> period <b>preceding</b> each of <b>the Festivals, when</b> lectures <b>on the <i>halakhot</i> of</b> the upcoming Festival <b>are delivered.</b>
The mishna continues: <b>And these</b> three days when funds were collected from the Temple treasury chamber <b>are</b> also <b>the due dates</b> for animal tithes. With regard to this <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> Why did the Sages choose these specific times as the due dates for animal tithes? These dates were chosen <b>because they</b> mark <b>the birthing periods</b> of animals. Some animals give birth before Passover, others deliver only before <i>Shavuot</i>, and yet others have their young between <i>Shavuot</i> and <i>Sukkot</i>. The Sages fixed the tithing times to correspond to the periods during which animals usually give birth. <b>Rabbi Aḥa and Rabbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> offered a different reason: <b>So that animals will be</b> readily <b>available for those going up</b> to Jerusalem <b>for the pilgrim Festivals.</b> The animals are tithed shortly before each of the Festivals so that there should be ample numbers of non-sacred animals ready to be sold for food and offerings to those on their way to Jerusalem. <b>Rabbi Yudan said:</b> The reason is <b>so that one should not come to</b> violate <b>the prohibition against delaying</b> an offering that he was obligated to bring to the Temple (Deuteronomy 23:22). If animal tithes would be due only after the Festival, three Festivals might pass without him bringing his animal tithes to Jerusalem to be offered, and therefore he would violate this prohibition. The Sages, therefore, established these days as the due dates for animal tithes so that they should serve as a reminder of the times by which the tithes must be brought to the Temple. <b>Rabbi Yosei said: Anyone who procrastinates</b> and fails to tithe <b>his untithed</b> animals at the proper time <b>transgresses the prohibition against delaying</b> bringing his offerings. <sup><b>[8a]</b></sup> The dispute in the mishna with regard to the third due date for animal tithes stems from a disagreement concerning the date of the new year for animal tithes. The Gemara now cites the various opinions about this matter. <b>We learn elsewhere</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>(</b><i>Rosh Hashana</i> 7b<b>): Rabbi Meir says: On the first of Elul</b> it <b>is the new year for animal tithes.</b> All the animals born prior to that date belong to the previous tithe year and are tithed together, whereas those born after that date belong to the next tithe year. <b>Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Shimon say:</b> The new year for animal tithes is <b>on the first of Tishrei. Ben Azzai says:</b> The animals that are <b>born in</b> the month of <b>Elul are tithed by themselves,</b> separate from those born before the first of Elul and from those born afterward, which belong to the next tithe year. With regard to Rabbi Meir’s opinion that the new year for animal tithes is on the first of Elul, <b>Rabbi Ḥuna said: The reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Meir</b> is that he maintains that the new year for animal tithes begins on the first of Nisan, and the year to which an animal is assigned is determined not by its birth date, but by the date of its conception. Since the period of gestation of small livestock is five months (see <i>Bekhorot</i> 8a), <b>up to that time,</b> the first of Elul, <b>they conclude giving birth from the old,</b> i.e., from the animals that conceived before the first of Nisan. <b>From here on,</b> from the first of Elul, they begin giving birth from <b>the new,</b> i.e., from the animals that conceived after the first of Nisan. The Gemara explains the opinion of Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Shimon, who maintain that the new year for animal tithes is on the first of Tishrei. <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Rabbi Bun</b> said <b>in the name of Rav Ḥuna</b> that <b>the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Shimon</b> is as follows. The verse states: “The flocks are clothed in the meadows, and the valleys are wrapped in grain; they shout for joy, they also sing” (Psalms 65:14). <b>“The flocks are clothed in the meadows,”</b> meaning the sheep have donned extra flesh, because they have conceived; <b>these are</b> the animals <b>that conceive early</b> in Adar and deliver by the first of Elul. <b>“And the valleys are wrapped in grain”: These are</b> the animals <b>that conceive late</b> in Nisan, when the grain has already grown and is clearly recognizable, and deliver only by the first of Tishrei. <b>“They shout for joy, they also sing”:</b> All the animals, those that are born early and those that are born late, become friends, <b>and they all enter the pen</b> together in order <b>to be tithed.</b> The Gemara proceeds to explain the opinion of ben Azzai. <b>Ben Azzai said: Since</b> the date of the new year for animal tithes is in doubt, as there are <b>those</b> who <b>say thus,</b> that it is the first of Elul, <b>and</b> there are <b>those</b> who <b>say thus,</b> that it is the first of Tishrei, the animals born in Elul cannot be tithed with animals born in another month, as perhaps they were born in a different tithing year, and one may not tithe animals from the new year for animals from the old year, or vice versa. Therefore, the animals that are <b>born in</b> the month of <b>Elul are tithed by themselves.</b> <b>How so?</b> If <b>five</b> animals <b>were born in Av and five in Elul</b> of the same year <b>and five in Tishrei</b> of the following year, <b>they do not join</b> together for tithing. Those born in Elul join neither with those born in Av nor with those born in Tishrei, as perhaps they were born in a different tithing year. All the more so those born in Av do not join with those born in Tishrei, as they were certainly not born in the same tithing year. If, however, <b>five</b> animals <b>were born in Tishrei and five in Av</b> of the same year, <b>they join</b> for tithing. The Gemara asks: <b>Does ben Azzai propose an intermediate position between the statements of his disciples?</b> These Sages being members of the next generation, one would have expected him to examine the reasoning behind their respective opinions and decide in favor of one of them. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya and Rabbi Meyasha came</b> and said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shmuel bar Rabbi Yitzḥak</b> to explain this difficulty: Ben Azzai did not rule in favor of either of his disciples because the matter about which they disagreed was the subject of an earlier dispute. For <b>the fathers of the world,</b> i.e., the great scholars of the previous generation, <b>had</b> already <b>differed on</b> this matter, and since ben Azzai was unable to decide between them, he adopted an intermediate position. The Gemara clarifies this assertion: <b>Who are these fathers of the world? Rabbi Yona taught in the presence of Rabbi Yirmeya:</b> This is referring to <b>Rabbi Yishmael and Rabbi Akiva.</b> The Gemara notes: <b>That is to say</b> that the wording of ben Azzai’s statement: Since there are those who say thus, and there are those who say thus, indicates that <b>ben Azzai was</b> both <b>a colleague and a disciple of Rabbi Akiva.</b> Although he frequently appears as his disciple, he actually reached the level of a disciple-colleague. The Gemara asks: How is this evident? <b>If you say</b> that Rabbi Akiva was only his <b>teacher, is there anyone who says about his teacher: Since</b> there are <b>those</b> who <b>say thus, and</b> there are <b>those</b> who <b>say thus?</b> This wording implies that ben Azzai related to the disputants as equals, and this would have been inappropriate had ben Azzai only been Rabbi Akiva’s disciple, and so he must also have been his disciple-colleague. <b>Rabbi Avun in the name of Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak learned</b> that ben Azzai was a disciple-colleague of Rabbi Akiva <b>from the following:</b> After bringing two cases about which Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel disagree, the Mishna brings another case where Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel agree but Rabbi Akiva disagrees with the two of them. The Mishna then states that <b>ben Azzai said to</b> Rabbi Akiva: <b>We are</b> already sufficiently <b>troubled by the cases about which</b> Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel <b>disagree, and you come</b> now <b>to dispute</b> that <b>about which they agree. That is to say</b> that <b>ben Azzai was</b> both <b>a colleague and a disciple of Rabbi Akiva.</b> For <b>if you say</b> that Rabbi Akiva was only <b>his teacher, is there anyone who says to his teacher: And you come</b> now <b>to dispute</b> that <b>about which they agree?</b> Rather, infer from here that ben Azzai was also Rabbi Akiva’s colleague.
§ The Gemara returns to the discussion of the new year for animal tithes. <b>We learned elsewhere</b> in a mishna (<i>Bekhorot</i> 57b): <b>All</b> animals <b>that were born from the first of Tishrei until the twenty-ninth of Elul</b> of the same year <b>join</b> together for tithing. If <b>five</b> animals were born <b>before Rosh HaShana and five</b> were born <b>after Rosh HaShana they do not join</b> together for tithing.
