If <b>five</b> animals were born <b>before</b> one of the three annual <b>due dates</b> for animal tithes, <b>and five</b> were born <b>after</b> those <b>due dates, they join</b> together. The due dates do not separate the animals into different groups, but merely establish the times when it is a mitzva to tithe them. <b>Rabbi Yosei said: This is to say</b> that <b>they did not make the animal tithe like</b> the tithe of fruit, whose obligation is determined by the time of the beginning of <b>the formation of the fruit,</b> as the parallel for animals would be the time of conception. They <b>also</b> did <b>not</b> make animal tithe <b>like</b> the tithe of grains and olives, whose obligation is determined by the time that those plants reach <b>a third</b> of their growth, as the parallel for animals would be the time that they are fit to be brought as offerings. The Gemara explains: <b>If you say</b> that the determining stage for animal tithes is <b>like</b> that for fruit, i.e., parallel to the time of the beginning of <b>the formation of the fruit, let</b> the mishna <b>teach</b> as follows: <b>All</b> animals <b>that were conceived from the first of Tishrei until the twenty-ninth of Elul</b> join together for tithing. And <b>if you say</b> that the determining stage for animal tithes is <b>like</b> that for grains and olives, parallel to the time that the latter reach <b>a third</b> of their growth, <b>let it teach</b> as follows: <b>All</b> animals <b>that were born before the twenty-second of Elul,</b> seven days before Rosh HaShana, join together for tithing. But animals born after that date are not fit to be brought as offerings that year, because an animal is not fit for the altar until it is eight days old. Therefore, they do not join together for tithing with the animals that were born earlier and are fit to be brought as offerings. Rather, the determining stage for animal tithes is the time of the animal’s birth. <b>Rabbi Shammai</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Beivai, son of Rabbi Ḥiyya:</b> This is not so, as in fact <b>they made</b> the animal tithe <b>like</b> the tithe of grains and olives, the obligation with regard to which is determined by the time that those plants reach <b>a third</b> of their growth. The mishna where it is taught that all animals born from the first of Tishrei until the twenty-ninth of Elul join together for tithing was <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, as Rabbi Shimon said:</b> An animal that is <b>lacking time,</b> i.e., it is less than eight days old, even though it cannot yet be brought as an offering, nevertheless <b>enters the pen to be tithed.</b> When <b>Rabbi Mana</b> heard this explanation he <b>arose</b> and discussed it <b>with Rabbi Shammai. He said to him:</b> Here <b>you say one thing,</b> i.e., that the mishna was taught in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Shimon, whereas <b>we learned elsewhere in the</b> mishna that <b>ben Azzai said:</b> The animals that are <b>born in</b> the month of <b>Elul are tithed by themselves</b> (<i>Bekhorot</i> 57b). Does he <b>not</b> refer to those born all month long, <b>even those that were born before the twenty-ninth of Elul</b> and will not yet be eight days old before Rosh HaShana? Now <b>can you say that ben Azzai’s</b> opinion <b>is in accordance with</b> the sole dissenting opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon and not with</b> the majority opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who maintain that an animal that is less than eight days old does not enter the pen to be tithed? Rather, it is reasonable to say that he does not disagree with the Rabbis, but he understands that even the Rabbis agree that an animal that was born on the twenty-ninth of Elul is tithed together with all the other animals that were born in Elul, even though it can be tithed in Tishrei only after it is eight days old. This indicates that the determining stage for animal tithes is the time of the animal’s birth. And how are the animals born at the end of Elul tithed according to the various opinions? Just <b>as you say according to</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis,</b> who maintain that the animals born from the first of Tishrei until the twenty-ninth of Elul of the same year are tithed together, as the new year for animal tithes is the first of Tishrei; still, those animals that were born during the last eight days of the year and cannot be tithed at the tithing period at the end of Elul because they are less than eight days old, <b>one puts aside until the next year, and they are tithed</b> together <b>with</b> other animals <b>of their year,</b> i.e., the previous year. <b>So you can say according to</b> the opinion <b>of ben Azzai:</b> Those animals that were born during the last eight days of the month of Elul, which one cannot tithe at the tithing period at the end of Elul since they are less than eight days old, <b>he puts aside until the next due date</b> in the coming Nisan, <b>and they are tithed</b> together <b>with</b> the untithed animals <b>that were born</b> at any time during <b>Elul</b> of the previous year. <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya said: This is to say</b> that because it was concluded that even though an animal cannot be tithed until it is eight days old, its tithing year is determined by the day of its birth, we can infer from this that <b>the days during which a firstborn is lacking time,</b> i.e., when it is not yet eight days old, <b>count for</b> the reckoning <b>toward its</b> first <b>year.</b> The firstborn should be offered, or if it is blemished, slaughtered and eaten, during the first year of its life so that one does not mistakenly benefit from its wool or its labor. The year is calculated not from the eighth day of its life when it is fit to be offered, but rather from the day of its birth. <b>Rabbi Mana said in the name of Rabbi Yona: My father learned this from the following</b> verse: <b>“All the firstling males that are born</b> of your herd and of your flock <b>you shall sanctify</b> to the Lord your God” (Deuteronomy 15:19). <b>How</b> is <b>this</b> verse to be understood? Seeing that a firstborn animal is sanctified from birth, why need one sanctify it? Rather, the verse comes to teach that <b>from the time of its birth you</b> begin to <b>count its</b> first <b>year</b> during which it should be eaten, and not from its eighth day when it becomes fit to be offered.

Halakhah 2

<strong>Halakha 2 · MISHNA</strong> The funds <b>are collected from the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber with three baskets, each</b> measuring <b>three <i>se’a</i>. On</b> the baskets <b>is written,</b> respectively, <b><i>alef</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>gimmel</i>,</b> based on the order in which the baskets are filled, to indicate from which basket coins should be taken to buy sacrifices. The coins were used in the order of their collection. <b>Rabbi Yishmael says:</b> The letters <b>written on them</b> were <b>in Greek, <i>alfa</i>, <i>beta</i>, <i>gamma</i>.</b> <b>The one who collects</b> the funds from the chamber <b>must not enter while wearing a cuffed garment</b> [<b><i>ḥafut</i></b>], <b>and not with a shoe, and not with a sandal, and not with phylacteries, and not with an amulet,</b> since all of these have places into which money can be inserted. The concern is that <b>perhaps</b> the one collecting the funds <b>will</b> one day <b>become poor, and</b> people <b>will say</b> that it is <b>because of the sin of</b> stealing the shekels of <b>the chamber</b> that <b>he became poor,</b> as they will suspect that he stole money and hid it in those places. <b>Or perhaps he will become rich and</b> people <b>will say</b> that <b>he became rich from</b> stealing <b>the funds of the chamber,</b> even though he did not actually do so. Even though one should not suspect someone of stealing consecrated shekels, the one collecting the funds from the chamber must nevertheless take these precautions, <b>as a person must appear justified before people just as he must appear justified before the Omnipresent [<i>HaMakom</i>], and it is stated: “And you shall be guiltless before the Lord and before Israel”</b> (Numbers 32:22). From here it may be inferred that it is not enough to be innocent before God; one must also be innocent before the Jewish people. Even in situations where there is little concern that one may commit a sin, the proper course is to remain above any possible suspicion of misconduct. <b>And the verse states: “So shall you find grace and good understanding in the sight of God and man”</b> (Proverbs 3:4).
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that funds are withdrawn from the Temple treasury chamber and collected into three baskets measuring three <i>se’a</i> each. The Gemara records another <i>halakha</i> that is learned from this. <b>We learned elsewhere</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 126b): On Shabbat <b>one may remove even four or five baskets</b> of straw or produce if the place they occupy is needed for guests or for students in the study hall. <b>Rabbi Ze’eira asked Rabbi Yoshiya: What is the measure of the baskets</b> that are permitted to be moved because the place that they occupy is needed? Rabbi Yoshiya <b>said to him:</b> <sup><b>[8b]</b></sup> <b>Let us learn</b> the measure <b>that is not clearly stated</b> with respect to the removal of baskets on Shabbat, <b>from</b> the measure <b>that is clearly stated</b> with respect to the collection of funds from the Temple treasury chamber. <b>For it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The funds are collected from</b> the Temple treasury <b>chamber with three baskets each</b> measuring <b>three <i>se’a</i>, which are nine <i>se’a</i></b> altogether, <b>which are twenty-seven <i>se’a</i></b> in total, as the collection of the chamber ceremony is performed on three different occasions over the course of the year. <b>And on</b> the baskets <b>is written,</b> respectively, <b><i>alef</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>gimmel</i>.</b> The same <i>baraita</i> explains: <b>Why was <i>alef</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>gimmel</i> written on the baskets? To indicate</b> which basket contained the shekels that were collected first. Therefore, the treasurer of the chamber would <b>supply himself</b> with shekels <b>from the first</b> basket, which was labeled <i>alef</i>, <b>before</b> taking the shekels from <b>the second</b> basket, which was labeled <i>beit</i>, <b>and from the second</b> basket <b>before</b> taking from <b>the third</b> basket, which was labeled <i>gimmel</i>. Rabbi Yoshiya’s answer to the original question is now clear: Just as the basket that was used to collect funds from the Temple treasury chamber, the measure that was clearly stated, measured three <i>se’a</i>, so too, the basket that may be removed from its place on Shabbat, the measure that was not clearly stated, measures three <i>se’a</i>.
§ The Gemara brings another instance of a measure that is not clearly stated being learned from a measure that is clearly stated. <b>We learned elsewhere</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 76b): <b>One who carries out</b> undiluted <b>wine</b> from a private domain to a public domain or vice versa is liable only if he carries <b>as much as</b> is needed <b>for the dilution of the cup [<i>mezigat hakos</i>].</b> On this topic <b>Rabbi Ze’eira asked Rabbi Yoshiya: What is the measure of the cup</b> referred to in the mishna? Rabbi Yoshiya <b>said to him: Let us learn</b> the measure of the cup that is not <b>clearly stated</b> here <b>from</b> the measure of a cup that is <b>clearly stated</b> elsewhere. <b>As Rabbi Ḥiyya taught:</b> The <b>four cups</b> of wine <b>that</b> the Sages <b>said</b> one is obligated to drink on the first night of Passover together <b>contain a quarter-</b><i>log</i> <b>of</b> raw <b>Italian wine.</b> Each cup of diluted wine must be a quarter-<i>log</i>. Since the proper ratio with regard to diluted wine is three parts water to one part raw wine, each cup of diluted wine contains three quarters of a quarter-<i>log</i> of water and one quarter of a quarter-<i>log</i> of raw wine. Therefore, the four cups together contain one quarter-<i>log</i>. From here it may be learned that one is liable on Shabbat for carrying out raw wine in the measure of one quarter of a quarter-<i>log</i>. <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Avin said in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> That mishna dealing with the measure of wine for which one is liable if he carried it on Shabbat was taught <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda. For it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> addressing the prohibition against carrying on Shabbat: One who carries <b>water</b> on Shabbat from a private domain to a public domain or vice versa is liable if he carries <b>as much as</b> constitutes <b>a</b> single <b>swallow. Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> He is liable if he carries <b>as much as</b> is required for <b>rubbing eye salve [<i>kilor</i>].</b> One who carries undiluted <b>wine</b> on Shabbat is liable if he carries <b>as much as</b> constitutes <b>a</b> single <b>swallow. Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> He is liable if he carries <b>as much as</b> is needed <b>to be diluted in a cup.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>For what measure</b> is one liable if he carries wine that is already <b>diluted</b> from a private domain to a public domain on Shabbat or vice versa? <b>Let us learn it from this</b> <i>baraita</i>: One who carries <b>water</b> on Shabbat from one domain to another is liable if he carries <b>as much as</b> constitutes <b>a</b> single <b>swallow. Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> He is liable if he carries <b>as much as</b> is required for <b>rubbing eye salve.</b> One who carries undiluted <b>wine</b> on Shabbat is liable if he carries <b>as much as</b> constitutes <b>a</b> single <b>swallow. Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> He is liable if he carries <b>as much as</b> is needed <b>for the dilution of a cup. That is to say</b> that the measure for liability in the case of <b>diluted wine</b> is <b>as much as is in one diluted cup.</b> On the topic of the four cups of wine that are drunk on the first night of Passover, the Gemara asks: <b>What is the measure of</b> this <b>cup? Rabbi Avin said: One and a quarter <i>titreton</i>,</b> which is roughly equivalent to a quarter-<i>log</i>. The <i>log</i> was no longer a common measure, so Rabbi Avin offers its rough equivalent, using a more familiar one.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the ruling? May one <b>drink</b> all four cups <b>at once</b> [<b><i>bekherekh eḥad</i></b>], without interruption, or must there be some sort of interval between them? The Gemara answers: <b>From</b> the fact <b>that Rabbi Mana said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yosei said</b> with regard to <b>the <i>hallel</i></b> that is recited on the first night of Passover, that <b>if one heard it in the synagogue</b> from the prayer leader, <b>he has fulfilled his obligation</b> to recite <i>hallel</i> and need not repeat it at home. <b>That is to say</b> that <b>if one drank</b> all four cups <b>at once</b> without interruption, <b>he has fulfilled his obligation.</b> One who heard <i>hallel</i> in the synagogue may omit the part of the <i>hallel</i> that is recited between the third and fourth cups, as he has already fulfilled his obligation. Doing so leaves no interval between these cups, yet Rabbi Yosei did not regard this as a problem, indicating that one is not required to have some sort of interval between the cups. The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to drinking</b> the four cups of wine little by little, <b>with interruptions?</b> The Gemara answers: When the Sages <b>said that one must drink</b> four cups of wine, <b>didn’t</b> they institute <b>that he must drink</b> them, and <b>not that he should become intoxicated</b> from drinking them? Therefore, <b>if he drank them</b> little by little, <b>with intervals, he too</b> is acting in accordance with the will of the Sages, as he <b>is not becoming intoxicated,</b> and therefore he need not drink the entire quarter-<i>log</i> at once. The Gemara further asks: <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to fulfilling one’s obligation</b> to drink four cups of wine on the first night of Passover <b>with wine</b> produced from grapes that grew during <b>the Sabbatical Year? Rav Oshaya taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One fulfills his obligation</b> with regard to the four cups <b>with wine of the Sabbatical Year.</b> The Gemara raises another question: <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to fulfilling one’s obligation</b> to drink the four cups <b>with spiced wine?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>From</b> the fact that <b>bar Kappara taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>spiced wine is</b> treated <b>like wine, that is to say, one fulfills his obligation</b> with regard to the four cups <b>with spiced wine,</b> as it is wine. The Gemara asks yet another question: <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to fulfilling one’s obligation</b> to drink the four cups <b>with diluted wine?</b> The Gemara answers <b>from what Rav Ḥiyya taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>the four cups</b> of wine <b>that</b> the Sages <b>said</b> one must drink on the first night of Passover, <b>one fulfills his obligation whether</b> he drinks them <b>undiluted or diluted, provided that they have the taste and appearance of wine.</b> Undiluted wine should not be so strong that it doesn’t taste like ordinary wine, and diluted wine must preserve the taste and appearance of wine even after the water is added. Having mentioned the desired appearance of the wine after it has been diluted, the Gemara notes that <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said: It is a mitzva to fulfill one’s obligation</b> with regard to the four cups <b>with red wine, as it is stated: “Look not upon the wine when it is red, when it gives its color in the cup,</b> when it glides down smoothly” (Proverbs 23:31). Having already taught that spiced wine is fit for the four cups, the Gemara cites what <b>we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Cooked wine is like spiced wine.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the <i>halakha</i> with regard <b>to fulfilling one’s obligation</b> to drink the four cups <b>with cooked wine? Rabbi Yona said: One fulfills his obligation with cooked wine.</b> The Gemara notes that <b>Rabbi Yona</b> conforms <b>to his</b> own <b>reasoning, since when Rabbi Yona would drink four cups of wine for Passover he would</b> have to <b>wrap his head</b> with a bandage <b>until the festival</b> of <i>Shavuot</i>, in order to relieve the headache that the wine caused him. This being the case, he permitted using weaker, cooked wine for the four cups.
 The Gemara relates that it once happened that <b>a certain</b> Roman <b>matron</b> [<b><i>matronita</i></b>] <b>saw that</b> Rabbi Yona’s <b>face was shining. She said: Old man, old man, one of three things must apply to you. You are either drunk with wine, or</b> else <b>you lend money at interest,</b> and owing to your comfortable income your face shines, <b>or</b> else <b>you raise pigs,</b> which provides you with large profits for little work. <b>He</b> cursed her and <b>said to her: Let despair come upon that woman, as none of these three things apply to me. Rather,</b> my face shines <b>because my learning is with me</b> and it lights my face, <b>as this is</b> what is <b>written: “A man’s wisdom makes his face shine”</b> (Ecclesiastes 8:1).
 The Gemara further relates that <b>Rabbi Abbahu</b> once <b>came to Tiberias,</b> to the school of Rabbi Yoḥanan. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan’s students saw that</b> Rabbi Abbahu’s <b>face was shining. The students said to Rabbi Yoḥanan: Rabbi Abbahu has found a treasure.</b> When Rabbi Abbahu <b>came before</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan, Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him: What new</b> words of <b>Torah did you hear? He said to him:</b> I heard <b>an ancient <i>Tosefta</i>,</b> which was new to me. When Rabbi Yoḥanan heard Rabbi Abbahu’s answer, <b>he applied to him</b> the verse: <b>“A man’s wisdom makes his face shine.”</b>
§ <b>Rabbi Ḥanin said: A <i>log</i> by Torah law is</b> equal in volume to <b>the old eighth with which</b> they would measure the <b>fish brine [<i>morayesa</i>] in Tzippori. Rabbi Yona said</b> to him: Even though the measuring instruments have changed and different ones are in use today, <b>I am familiar with that</b> measure, <b>as</b> it was <b>with that</b> measure that <b>the members of Rabbi Yannai’s household measured honey.</b> <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: A quarter-<i>log</i> is equal to <b>half of a Tiberian eighth.</b> <sup><b>[9a]</b></sup> This is a measure that was used in the past, but no longer in the present. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: This is our</b> measure today and it is not so old. The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> does the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>call</b> this measure <b>old when it was still</b> being used <b>in his day?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Some say that</b> this measure <b>was</b> originally <b>small and it was made larger, and some say</b> that after it was enlarged <b>it was</b> again <b>made smaller, but not as small as it had been</b> originally. Since this measure was not of the same size at all times, the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> was precise when he said that a quarter-<i>log</i> is equal to the old measure of one-eighth. So too, Rabbi Yoḥanan was precise when he said that this was the measure used in his day. The Gemara asks: In terms of the measures currently in use, <b>what is the measure of a cup</b> that holds a quarter-<i>log</i>? <b>Rabbi Yosei in the name of Rabbi Yosei ben Pazi and Rabbi Yosei bar Beivai in the name of Rabbi Shmuel</b> said: <b>Two fingers</b> long <b>by two fingers</b> wide <b>by the height of one and a half fingers and one-third of a finger.</b> The Gemara previously discussed the measure of wine that determines liability if one carries it from one domain to another on Shabbat. There, the Gemara discussed wine in liquid form; here, the Gemara adds a parallel ruling with regard to congealed wine. <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The measure that determines liability for carrying from one domain to another <b>congealed</b> wine is <b>an olive-bulk;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Natan.</b> <b>The Rabbis of Caesarea and Rabbi Yosei bar Beivai</b> said <b>in the name of Shmuel:</b> These words of <b>Rabbi Natan follow</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon</b> in a mishna (<i>Shabbat</i> 76b). <b>Just as Rabbi Shimon said</b> there: The measure that determines liability for all liquids, including wine, is <b>a quarter-</b><i>log</i>, <b>so Rabbi Natan said:</b> When wine is in liquid form, the measure that determines liability for carrying it from one domain to another is <b>a quarter-</b><i>log</i>; <b>after it has congealed</b> one is liable only if he carries out <b>an olive-bulk,</b> which is equivalent to a quarter-<i>log</i> of wine after it has congealed.
§ On a similar matter, <b>Rabbi Simon</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi: An incident</b> is related <b>with regard to the mule of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>that died</b> of a wound, <b>and</b> the Sages <b>deemed its blood ritually pure,</b> i.e., free of the impurity <b>of an</b> unslaughtered <b>animal carcass.</b> They said that the blood of a carcass does not impart ritual impurity as does the carcass itself. <b>And Rabbi Eliezer asked Rabbi Simon,</b> who had reported this ruling: <b>Up to how much</b> blood from an animal carcass does not render one ritually impure? Did they deem it pure because it was less than a quarter-<i>log</i>, but had it been more than a quarter-<i>log</i> they would have deemed it impure? Rabbi Simon <b>paid</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>no attention</b> and did not answer him, so Rabbi Eliezer went <b>and asked Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi, who said to him</b> as follows: <b>Up to a quarter-</b><i>log</i> of blood is <b>pure; more than a quarter-</b><i>log</i> of blood is <b>impure. Rabbi Elazar was displeased that Rabbi Simon had not</b> fully <b>reported the teaching to him,</b> so that he had to go to Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi to ask about it. <b>Rav Beivai was sitting</b> and <b>teaching this case</b> of the mule from Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s household, in which the Sages ruled that the blood of a carcass does not render one impure. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Kahana</b> wished to clarify the issue and <b>said to him: Up to a quarter-</b><i>log</i> of blood <b>is pure,</b> but <b>more than that is impure?</b> Or did the Sages rule that the blood of a carcass is never ritually impure, whatever the measure may be? The Gemara relates: In response to Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Kahana’s question, Rav Beivai <b>kicked him. Rabbi Zerika said to him,</b> i.e., to Rav Beivai: <b>You kicked him because he asked you a question? He said to him: I kicked him because my mind was unsettled,</b> and not because he did anything wrong. Rav Beivai tries to explain his conduct: <b>As Rabbi Ḥanan said:</b> “And your life shall hang in doubt before you; and you shall fear night and day, and shall have no assurance of your life” (Deuteronomy 28:66). This verse in the passage of rebuke refers to three increasingly harsh levels of poverty: <b>“And your life shall hang in doubt before you”; this is one who buys wheat for a year,</b> who has no financial security with regard to the following year. <b>“And you shall fear night and day”; this is one who buys</b> small amounts of wheat <b>from a vendor,</b> with the attendant concern that he might not have enough for the morrow. <b>“And you shall have no assurance of your life”; this is one who buys</b> bread <b>from the baker</b> [<b><i>paltor</i></b>] and cannot afford to buy wheat in advance to assure even one future meal. Rabbi Beivai concluded: <b>And</b> I am in the harshest state, as <b>I rely on the baker.</b> For this reason I did not have the presence of mind to respond appropriately to Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Kahana’s question. The Gemara asks: <b>What then</b> is the halakhic ruling regarding the ritual impurity of the blood of an animal carcass? In response, the Gemara quotes a mishna (<i>Eduyyot</i> 8:1): <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Petora testified that the blood of an</b> unslaughtered <b>animal carcass is ritually pure,</b> which implies that it is ritually pure regardless of the amount, even more than a quarter-<i>log</i>. The Gemara rejects this answer: <b>What</b> does the mishna mean by <b>ritually pure?</b> That such blood is <b>ritually pure</b> in that it does not <b>render</b> an item <b>susceptible</b> to impurity. Even though blood is one of the seven liquids that render an item susceptible to ritual impurity, the blood of an animal carcass is not deemed blood for this purpose. <b>But as for imparting ritual impurity</b> by itself, the blood <b>imparts ritual impurity,</b> as does the carcass itself. The Gemara asks: How can the words of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Petora be understood in this way? Didn’t <b>we learn elsewhere in a mishna</b> (<i>Makhshirin</i> 6:5): <b>The blood of a creeping animal is like its flesh; it imparts ritual impurity</b> to items with which it comes into contact, but <b>it does not render</b> an item <b>susceptible</b> to impurity? <b>And we have nothing</b> else <b>like it,</b> i.e., there is no other liquid that imparts ritual impurity but does not render food susceptible to impurity. The implication is that the blood of an animal carcass would either both impart impurity and render an item susceptible to impurity or neither. The Gemara answers: When the mishna said that we have nothing else like a creeping animal, it meant that we have nothing else like it with regard to the measure required for its blood to confer impurity; the measure of a creeping animal’s blood is the same for both: <b>The measure</b> required for <b>its imparting ritual impurity</b> is such <b>that its blood imparts ritual impurity like its flesh,</b> i.e., in the measure of a lentil. This is not the case with an unslaughtered animal carcass, the flesh of which imparts ritual impurity in the measure of an olive-bulk, while its blood imparts impurity in the measure of a quarter-<i>log</i>. Consequently, there is no contradiction between the mishna in tractate <i>Makhshirin</i> and the testimony of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Petora. <b>Rabbi Yosei said: Two <i>amora’im</i> disagree about this</b> matter. <b>One said</b> a quarter-<i>log</i> of this blood renders one <b>ritually impure, and one said</b> that even after contact with this blood, one remains <b>ritually pure. The one who said</b> it is <b>impure follows</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> in a mishna in <i>Eduyyot</i> 5:1. Rabbi Yehuda maintains there that this issue is the subject of a dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel. The <i>halakha</i> would follow the opinion of Beit Hillel, which is that carcass blood does confer impurity. <b>And the one who said ritually pure</b> holds <b>like</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Petora,</b> who testified (<i>Eduyyot</i> 8:1) that the blood of an animal carcass is ritually pure. <b>Rav Avduma of the descenders,</b> i.e., travelers from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, bringing with them the Torah taught in Eretz Yisrael, <b>said to him: And it is right.</b> That which you explained is correct, <b>as Rabbi Yehuda was the halakhic authority for the house of the <i>Nasi</i>.</b> Rabbi Yehuda ruled then that the blood of the dead mule was pure only because there was less than a quarter-<i>log</i> of it.
§ The Mishna taught that the one who collects the funds from the Temple treasury chamber must not enter the chamber wearing clothes that could arouse suspicion, as <b>perhaps</b> one day <b>he will become poor and</b> people <b>will say</b> that he was reduced to poverty because he had stolen shekels from the chamber. <b>Rabbi Yishmael taught</b> a similar <i>halakha</i> in a <i>baraita</i>: One who is <b>curly haired must not collect</b> funds from the Temple treasury chamber <b>because of the suspicion</b> that he will hide shekels from the chamber in his curls. <b>It was taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i>: <b>The</b> Temple <b>treasurers would untangle [<i>mefaspesin</i>] the matted locks</b> of the one who collected the funds from the chamber after he exited the chamber, to ascertain that no coins were hidden in his hair. <b>It was taught</b> in yet another <i>baraita</i> that deals with a similar suspicion: <b>They would converse with</b> the one collecting the funds from the chamber <b>from the time he entered</b> the chamber <b>until the time he exited</b> it, so that he should not be able to hide money from the chamber in his mouth. The Gemara asks: Why not <b>let him fill his mouth with water,</b> so that it would be impossible for him to insert money into his mouth, and then he would not have to speak? <b>Rabbi Tanḥuma said:</b> He cannot fill his mouth with water <b>because of the blessing</b> that he must recite before collecting the funds from the chamber.
§ <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥman</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yonatan: We find in the Torah, in the Prophets, and in the Writings that a person must appear justified before people as he must appear justified before the Omnipresent. From where in the Torah</b> is this derived? <b>As it is written: “And you shall be guiltless before the Lord and before Israel”</b> (Numbers 32:22). <b>From where in the Prophets</b> is this derived? <b>As it is written: “The mighty One, God, the Lord, He knows, and Israel shall know”</b> (Joshua 22:22). <b>From where in the Writings</b> is this derived? <b>As it is written: “So shall you find grace and good understanding in the sight of God and man</b> (Proverbs 3:4). <b>Gamliel Zuga asked Rabbi Yosei bar Rabbi Bun: Which of these</b> verses <b>is the clearest of all the</b> sources that one must be blameless even in the eyes of man? The three proof texts say more or less the same thing; which is the most unambiguous of the three? Rabbi Yosei bar Rabbi Bun <b>said to him:</b> The verse <b>“And you shall be guiltless before the Lord and before Israel”</b> is the most unequivocal of all.

Halakhah 3

<strong>Halakha 3 · MISHNA</strong> In order to indicate the importance that was attached to the ceremony of the collection of the Temple treasury chamber, the mishna relates that the members <b>of the house of Rabban Gamliel</b> desired that their shekels be the ones collected from the chamber and used for the purchase of the communal offerings. Each of them <b>would</b> therefore come to the Temple specifically on the day of the ceremony of the collection of the chamber, <b>enter</b> the chamber <b>with his shekel between his fingers, and toss it in front of the one collecting</b> the money so that he would see it and place it in the basket containing the money to be taken out of the chamber. Understanding what was happening, <b>the one collecting</b> the money from the chamber <b>would purposely push</b> this shekel <b>into the basket,</b> so that it would later be used to buy communal offerings. <b>The one collecting</b> the funds from the chamber may <b>not</b> begin to <b>collect</b> the money <b>until he asks</b> the Temple treasurers three times: <b>Shall I collect</b> the funds, <b>and they say to him: Collect</b> them, <b>collect</b> them, <b>collect</b> them, <b>three times.</b> The coins were stored in the Temple treasury in three large baskets, each measuring nine <i>se’a</i>. In the collection of the chamber ceremony, coins were removed from these baskets and placed in smaller baskets of three <i>se’a</i> each that were marked with letters (see the previous mishna on <i>daf</i> 8a). After <b>he collected the</b> funds <b>from the first</b> large basket and put them into one of the smaller baskets labeled with the letter <i>alef</i>, <b>he</b> immediately <b>covered with a leather cover</b> the large basket from which he had removed the money. After collecting funds <b>from the second</b> large basket, <b>he covered</b> it <b>with a leather cover</b> as well. But after collecting funds from <b>the third</b> large basket, <b>he did not cover</b> it. The mishna asks: <b>Why did he cover</b> the first two baskets? In order to mark them as already having had funds collected from them. In this way, there was no concern that <b>perhaps he would forget and</b> once again <b>collect</b> funds <b>from</b> a basket from which funds <b>had</b> already <b>been collected.</b> The mishna specifies the intent of the one collecting the funds from the baskets as he does so: <b>He collected</b> funds <b>from the first</b> basket <b>on behalf of</b> the people living in <b>Eretz Yisrael;</b> from <b>the second</b> basket <b>on behalf of</b> the people living in <b>the cities near</b> Eretz Yisrael; and from <b>the third</b> basket <b>on behalf of</b> the people living in <b>Babylonia, and on behalf of</b> the people living in <b>Media, and on behalf of</b> <sup><b>[9b]</b></sup> the people living in <b>the distant countries.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It was taught in the mishna that the members <b>of the house of Rabban Gamliel</b> would arrange that their shekels be the ones collected from the chamber and used for the purchase of the communal offerings. The Gemara asks: <b>If there were two piles</b> of produce, and <b>one separated <i>teruma</i> from one</b> pile <b>on behalf of the other, hasn’t he exempted</b> with this <i>teruma</i> <b>the other</b> pile as well? When tithing produce, it is not necessary to separate <i>teruma</i> from each pile. It suffices to set aside an appropriate measure of <i>teruma</i> from one of them, and this counts as <i>teruma</i> for the other as well. Here too, the shekels are collected from the chamber not only on behalf of those who contributed those specific shekels, but even for those whose shekels were not collected. If so, why did the members of the house of Rabban Gamliel make such efforts to ensure that their shekels be the ones collected from the chamber? The Gemara answers: Even so, <b>they</b> got <b>satisfaction</b> from the fact <b>that the</b> communal <b>offering was necessarily brought from their</b> shekels <b>first.</b> For this reason, they exerted themselves to ensure that their shekels were collected from the chamber.
The Gemara records several <i>halakhot</i> dealing with the collection of funds from the Temple treasury chamber. <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If the one collecting the funds from the chamber mistakenly <b>removed the leather covers</b> that he had placed on the large baskets, so that now he doesn’t know from which baskets shekels have already been collected and from which they have not yet been collected, <b>all</b> the shekels in the uncovered baskets <b>take on the status of the remainder</b> of the chamber. <b>It was taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i>: <b>The third</b> collection of funds from the Temple treasury chamber that took place half a month before <i>Sukkot</i> <b>was the most abundant of all, as it contained</b> both <b><i>isteriot</i>,</b> small coins <b>of gold, and darics,</b> large coins <b>of gold.</b>
<b>It was taught</b> in yet another <i>baraita</i>: <b>He collected</b> funds <b>from the first</b> basket <b>on behalf of</b> the people living in <b>Eretz Yisrael and on behalf of all of Israel;</b> from <b>the second</b> basket <b>on behalf of</b> the people living in <b>the cities [<i>kerakim</i>] near</b> Eretz Yisrael <b>and on behalf of all of Israel; and</b> from <b>the third</b> basket <b>on behalf of</b> the people living in <b>Babylonia and Media, and on behalf of</b> those who dwelled in <b>the distant countries, and on behalf of all of Israel.</b> The Gemara discusses the order in which the funds are taken from the baskets. <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: After shekels <b>were</b> first <b>taken from the first</b> basket labeled with an <i>alef</i>, <b>although</b> shekels still <b>remain in the first</b> basket, shekels <b>are taken from the second</b> basket labeled with a <i>beit</i>. After shekels <b>were taken from the second</b> basket, <b>although</b> shekels still <b>remain in the second basket,</b> shekels <b>are taken from the third</b> basket labeled with a <i>gimmel</i>. If <b>the third</b> basket <b>is completely emptied, he goes back to</b> take from <b>the second</b> basket. If <b>the second</b> basket <b>is completely emptied, he goes back to</b> take from <b>the first</b> basket. If <b>all three</b> baskets <b>are completely emptied, he goes back</b> to the chamber <b>and takes</b> from <b>the shekels</b> that have arrived since the last time shekels were collected from the chamber. He does not take from the shekels that remained in the chamber at the previous ceremony of collection into the baskets, as those coins acquired at that point the status of leftover shekels, which do not have sanctity. <b>Rabbi Meir says: He goes back to</b> take from <b>the leftover</b> shekels. <b>Because Rabbi Meir said: The <i>halakhot</i> of misusing</b> consecrated property <b>apply</b> even <b>to the leftover shekels,</b> as they remain in their sanctified state. Why so? <b>Perhaps they will be needed in the end,</b> since if the shekels collected from the chamber are depleted, the leftover can be used for the purchase of communal offerings.
§ Since the previous mishna deals with the obligation to be guiltless even in the eyes of men and this mishna speaks of the alacrity displayed by the members of the house of Rabban Gamliel when they gave their shekels, the Gemara brings a <i>baraita</i> dealing with worthy traits, including the trait of alacrity: <b>And so Rabbi Pineḥas ben Yair would say: Alacrity</b> in the proper performance of the mitzvot <b>leads to cleanliness</b> of the soul, so that one will not sin. <b>Cleanliness</b> of the soul and refraining from all sin <b>leads to purity,</b> so that one purifies his soul from his previous sins. <b>Purity leads to holiness. Holiness leads to humility,</b> as one recognizes his lowliness. <b>Humility leads to fear of sin,</b> because when one recognizes his inferiority, he becomes more fearful of sin and is careful to avoid temptation. <b>Fear of sin leads to piety,</b> as one begins to impose upon himself stringencies beyond the letter of the law. <b>Piety leads to the holy spirit,</b> because when one acts in a manner that goes beyond the letter of the law, Heaven acts with him in a way that is not natural to man, and informs him of the secrets of the Torah through divine inspiration. <b>The holy spirit leads to the resurrection of the dead,</b> because the spirit of holiness and purity that descend upon him enter the bones of the deceased and resurrect them. <b>The resurrection of the dead</b> that will precede the arrival of the Messiah <b>leads to</b> the coming of the Prophet <b>Elijah, of blessed memory,</b> who will herald the upcoming redemption. The Gemara adduces proof texts for each of the previous statements: <b>Alacrity leads to cleanliness</b> of the soul, <b>as it is written: “And when he has made an end of atoning”</b> (Leviticus 16:20). “He has made an end” denotes alacrity; since one hastens to bring the process to conclusion, he achieves atonement, which cleanses the souls of sinners of their iniquities. <b>Cleanliness leads to purity, as it is written</b> with regard to the offering that a woman brings after her days of impurity and purity that follow childbirth (Leviticus 12): <b>“And the priest shall make atonement for her, and she shall be pure”</b> (Leviticus 12:8). Once she finishes the process of her atonement, that is to say, once she cleanses herself of sin, she reaches purity. <b>Purity leads to holiness, as it is written</b> with regard to the High Priest’s sprinkling of the blood of his bull and goat offerings on the golden altar on Yom Kippur: <b>“And he shall purify it, and hallow it”</b> (Leviticus 16:19), teaching that purity is followed by holiness. <b>Holiness leads to humility, as it is written: “For thus says the High and Lofty One that inhabits eternity, Whose name is Holy: I dwell on high and in a holy place, yet with him also that is of a contrite and humble spirit”</b> (Isaiah 57:15). God’s holiness is drawn to a contrite and humble spirit. <b>Humility leads to fear of sin, as it is written: “The reward of humility is the fear of the Lord”</b> (Proverbs 22:4). <b>Fear of sin leads to piety, as it is written: “But the loving-kindness [<i>ḥesed</i>] of the Lord is from everlasting to everlasting upon those who fear Him”</b> (Psalms 103:17). The recognition of God’s loving-kindness indicates piety [<i>ḥasidut</i>], which results from fear of God. <b>Piety leads to the holy spirit, as it is written: “Then You spoke in vision to Your pious ones”</b> (Psalms 89:20). <b>The holy spirit leads to the resurrection of the dead, as it is written: “And I shall put My spirit in you, and you shall live”</b> (Ezekiel 37:14). <b>The resurrection of the dead leads to</b> the coming of the Prophet <b>Elijah, of blessed memory, as it is written: “Behold, I will send you Elijah the Prophet before the coming of the great and terrible day of the Lord”</b> (Malachi 3:23). The day referred to here is the day of the resurrection of the dead. The Gemara concludes the chapter with a different discussion of virtues: <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in the name of Rabbi Meir: Anyone who</b> lives <b>permanently in Eretz Yisrael, and speaks the holy tongue, and eats the fruits of</b> Eretz Yisrael <b>in purity, and recites <i>Shema</i> in the morning and in the evening, will receive the tidings</b> and assurances <b>that he is one</b> who has merited <b>of the World-to-Come.</b> 

Chapter 4



Halakhah 1

<strong>Halakha 1 · MISHNA</strong> At certain times of the year, half-shekels that had been donated to the Temple and stored in a chamber in the Temple were collected in order to be used for various purposes. The mishna asks: <b>The collection</b> of half-shekels, <b>what would they do with it? They</b> would <b>purchase</b> animals for the <b>daily offerings,</b> which were offered each morning and afternoon; <b>and</b> for <b>the additional offerings,</b> which were offered on Shabbat, the New Moon, and Festivals; <b>and</b> wine for <b>their libations;</b> barley for <b>the <i>omer</i></b> meal-offering; <b>and</b> wheat for both the <b>two loaves</b> offered on Shavuot <b>and the shewbread; and</b> animals for <b>all the communal offerings.</b> § The <b>guards of</b> the <b><i>sefiḥin</i>,</b> grain that grew without being purposely planted, <b>during the Sabbatical Year,</b> ensured that people did not take this ownerless grain, so that it remained available to be used for the <i>omer</i> and the offering of the two loaves. They <b>collect their wages from the collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber. Rabbi Yosei says: One who</b> so <b>desires</b> may <b>even volunteer</b> his services and guard the grain as <b>an unpaid bailee.</b> The Rabbis <b>said to him: Even you</b> must <b>say that</b> the <i>omer</i> and the two loaves <b>come only</b> <sup><b>[10a]</b></sup> <b>from communal</b> funds and not from any one individual. If one were to volunteer his services, he would acquire the grain for himself by guarding it and transporting it to the Temple. In that case, these offerings would have come from an individual. So that the offerings come solely from communal funds, the guards must receive payment from the half-shekels removed from the chamber.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna mentions a dispute about whether an individual may volunteer his services and guard the grain to be used for the <i>omer</i> and the two loaves. This dispute depends on whether something owned or donated by an individual may be brought as a communal offering. With regard to this issue, the Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> related to a mishna (<i>Ta’anit</i> 26a). The mishna lists days on which special offerings were brought, including wood donated on a regular basis by particular families for use on the altar. This time period was referred to as the time of the wood of the priests and of the nation. It was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Why was the time of the wood of the priests and of the nation fit to be counted</b> in the mishna when any individual may donate wood at any time? What makes these families and these times unique? The <i>baraita</i> explains: <b>Rather, at the time that the Jewish people ascended from the</b> Babylonian <b>exile</b> and returned to Eretz Yisrael with Ezra, <b>they did not find wood in the</b> Temple <b>chamber</b> of wood for the altar, due to a lack of funds in the Temple treasury. <b>And these</b> families <b>took the initiative and donated wood from their own</b> property <b>and gave it to the community; and they offered communal offerings with it. And the prophets among them stipulated with them that,</b> in the future, <b>even</b> if <b>the chamber were full of wood,</b> if <b>these</b> families <b>took the initiative and donated wood from their own</b> property, the <b>offerings would be brought</b> using <b>only theirs first.</b> Consequently, the <i>baraita</i> indicates that communal offerings may come from individual funds. <b>Rabbi Aḥa said: This</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, as Rabbi Yosei says: One who</b> so <b>desires</b> may <b>even volunteer</b> his services and guard the grain as <b>an unpaid bailee.</b> However, according to the opinion of the Rabbis, which maintains that one may not donate his services, an individual may not donate the wood for the pyre on the altar. <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> an <i>amora</i>, said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ila: That</b> <i>baraita</i> is <b>a statement</b> accepted by <b>all.</b> If so, <b>with regard to what do</b> the Rabbis and Rabbi Yosei, the <i>tanna</i>, <b>disagree? With regard to the offering itself. However,</b> with regard to items that merely <b>facilitate an offering, everyone agrees that an individual offering</b> may be <b>changed to a communal offering,</b> i.e., that an individual may donate items that facilitate the sacrifice of a communal offering. The Gemara raises a similar issue. <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>a woman who made a tunic,</b> one of the priestly vestments, <b>for her son</b> to wear while he serves in the Temple, the tunic <b>is valid.</b> However, since the priestly vestments must come from communal funds, the priest may use his mother’s tunic in the Temple only <b>provided that she</b> completely <b>transfers</b> ownership of the tunic <b>to the community.</b> <b>Rabbi Aḥa said:</b> This <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, as Rabbi Yosei says: One who</b> so <b>desires</b> may <b>donate even</b> his services and guard the grain as <b>an unpaid watchman. Rabbi Yosei,</b> an <i>amora</i>, said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ila: That</b> <i>baraita</i> is <b>a statement</b> accepted by <b>all.</b> If so, <b>with regard to what do</b> the Rabbis and Rabbi Yosei <b>disagree? With regard to the offering itself. However,</b> with regard to items that merely <b>facilitate an offering, everyone agrees that an individual offering</b> may be <b>changed to a communal offering.</b> <b>A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with</b> Rabbi Aḥa’s explanation of the dispute between <b>Rabbi Yosei</b> and the Rabbis: <b>Those days</b> of family celebrations, on the occasion of donating wood for the altar, <b>are observed</b> both <b>during the time</b> when one can bring an <b>offering,</b> i.e., when the Temple stands, <b>and during the time</b> when <b>no offering</b> can be brought, i.e., when there is no Temple; nevertheless the family still holds a celebration on those dates. <b>Rabbi Yosei says: They are observed only during the time of offering.</b> This <i>baraita</i> demonstrates that even the Rabbis, who disagree with Rabbi Yosei, admit that individuals can donate wood for the pyre, which is why they celebrated this event during the time of the Temple. The Gemara cites <b>an additional</b> source with regard to <b>this</b> topic, <b>as it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Tzadok, said: We were among the descendants of Senaah, son of</b> the tribe of <b>Benjamin,</b> a family that donated wood to the Temple on the tenth of Av. <b>And</b> one year, <b>the Ninth of Av occurred on Shabbat, and we postponed</b> the fast <b>until the conclusion of Shabbat,</b> in accordance with the <i>halakha</i>. <b>And we fasted but did not complete</b> the fast, due to our family celebration. Apparently, the family continued to commemorate the offering of wood even after the destruction of the Temple, as there was no fast of the Ninth of Av while the Temple stood. Had the original donation of wood been only for individual offerings, the celebration of that donation would not have continued after the destruction of the Temple. Therefore, this <i>baraita</i> indicates that Rabbi Ila’s understanding is correct, and even the Rabbis agree that with regard to items that facilitate an offering, an individual offering may be changed into a communal offering.
§ The mishna teaches that the collection of the Temple treasury was used to purchase the necessary items for <b>the <i>omer</i>, and the two loaves, and the shewbread, and all communal offerings,</b> as well as for the salary of those guarding the <i>sefiḥin</i>. <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Menaḥot</i> 83b): <b>All</b> the grain for <b>individual and communal offerings</b> may <b>come</b> both <b>from Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>and from outside</b> of <b>Eretz</b> Yisrael. It may come both <b>from new</b> grain that grew after the offering of the <i>omer</i> the previous year <b>and from old</b> grain, <b>except for the <i>omer</i></b> itself <b>and the two loaves, which</b> may <b>come only from new</b> grain <b>and from Eretz</b> Yisrael. <b>Rav Ḥuna</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yirmeya:</b> The mishna is in accordance with the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yishmael, as Rabbi Yishmael says: The <i>omer</i> may not come from Syria</b> or any other location outside of Eretz Yisrael. The Gemara cites a similar discussion. <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Kelim</i> 1:6): <b>There are ten</b> levels <b>of sanctity. Eretz Yisrael is more sanctified than all</b> other <b>lands. And</b> through <b>what</b> <i>halakha</i> is <b>its sanctity</b> expressed? It is evident in the <i>halakha</i> <b>that they</b> must <b>bring from it the <i>omer</i>, the first fruits, and the two loaves, which may not be brought from</b> the produce of <b>any other lands. Rabbi Ḥiyya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yirmeya: This</b> mishna <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yishmael, as Rabbi Yishmael said: The <i>omer</i> may not come from Syria</b> or any other location outside of Eretz Yisrael. Similarly, <b>we learned</b> elsewhere in a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 1:4) that <b>Rabbi Yishmael says</b> that the verse “In plowing time and in harvest you shall rest” (Exodus 34:21) is not referring to the prohibition against farming the land during the Sabbatical Year, as the Rabbis explain. Rather, it is referring to the prohibition against performing labor on Shabbat. The reason that the verse mentions these two particular forms of labor is to teach that <b>just as</b> the <b>plowing</b> that is prohibited on Shabbat is an otherwise <b>voluntary</b> act, as plowing is never required by the Torah, <b>so too,</b> the <b>harvesting</b> that is prohibited on Shabbat <b>is voluntary. The harvesting of the <i>omer</i> is excluded</b> from the prohibition, <b>as it is a mitzva.</b> The barley for the <i>omer</i> must be harvested on the sixteenth of Nisan, even if it occurs on Shabbat. <b>Rabbi Yishmael</b> is consistent with <b>his</b> own <b>opinion</b> in this matter, <b>as</b> it is <b>Rabbi Yishmael who said: The <i>omer</i> may not come from Syria</b> or any other location outside of Eretz Yisrael. This is <b>consistent with his opinion</b> stated elsewhere, <b>as he said: The harvesting of the <i>omer</i> is excluded</b> from the prohibition on Shabbat, <b>as it is a mitzva.</b> Since Rabbi Yishmael requires that the <i>omer</i> come from Eretz Yisrael, it must be permitted to harvest it during the Sabbatical Year, despite the fact that harvesting is generally prohibited during the Sabbatical Year. This is consistent with Rabbi Yishmael’s opinion that the <i>omer</i> is excluded from the category of prohibited labor on Shabbat because harvesting it is a mitzva; the same holds true for the Sabbatical Year as well. The Gemara asks with regard to the mishna: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught: The guards of <i>sefiḥin</i> during the Sabbatical Year collect their wages from the collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber? It is Rabbi Yishmael.</b> It is apparent from the mishna that the <i>omer</i> must come from Eretz Yisrael; otherwise, there would be no need to hire guards to ensure that there be barley available to use, as it could be imported from outside Eretz Yisrael. <b>Rabbi Yosei said:</b> The mishna is <b>a statement</b> accepted <b>by all,</b> even by those who hold that the <i>omer</i> may be brought from outside of Eretz Yisrael. According to this opinion, if <b>they could not find</b> barley <b>in Syria</b> or anywhere else outside of Eretz Yisrael, <b>they would bring</b> the <i>omer</i> <b>from <i>sefiḥin</i> that were in Eretz Yisrael.</b> Therefore, the guards were still necessary. Since it is permitted to harvest the <i>omer</i> even during the Sabbatical Year, the Gemara asks: With regard to <b>that <i>omer</i>,</b> if it becomes clear that there are no <i>sefiḥin</i> in a particular Sabbatical Year, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> concerning <b>planting</b> it <b><i>ab initio</i>?</b> When <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Adda</b> heard this question, <b>he asked, in the presence of Rabbi Mana:</b> In general, a handful of a meal-offering is burned on the altar, and the remainder of the meal-offering may be eaten by the priests. If the remainder may not be eaten by the priests, the entire offering is invalid. In the case under discussion, if one were to plant barley during the Sabbatical Year, <b>would it not be</b> comparable to the case of <b>a handful</b> of a meal-offering whose <b>remainder may not be eaten,</b> as it is prohibited to eat produce that is planted during the Sabbatical Year? Rabbi Mana <b>said to him:</b> The <i>omer</i> is nonetheless valid, as <b>it becomes like the five</b> offerings <b>that</b> may be <b>brought in</b> a state of <b>ritual impurity but may not be eaten in</b> a state of <b>impurity.</b> There is a mishna in tractate <i>Pesaḥim</i> (76b) that lists five communal offerings that are offered in a state of impurity under certain circumstances; however, they may not be eaten in a state of impurity. Similarly, the <i>omer</i> that is brought from barley that was planted during the Sabbatical Year is valid, despite the fact that after the handful is burned on the altar, the remainder of the meal mixture may not be eaten.
§ The Gemara asks: <b>How does</b> the treasurer <b>do</b> this, i.e., pay the salaries to the guards and harvesters from the chamber collection? The money in the Temple treasury is consecrated property, and consecrated property may be desacralized only through redemption (<i>Arakhin</i> 33a). The Gemara answers: <b>He takes money from the money changer and gives</b> that money as payment <b>to the harvesters and to the guards before the <i>omer</i> is offered.</b> Once the time for offering the <i>omer</i> arrives, <b>he brings money from the collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber and desacralizes</b> it by transferring its sanctity <b>onto</b> the barley that is to be used for the <i>omer</i>. This money now becomes unconsecrated and is used to repay the money changer. The Gemara asks: <b>Is this acceptable?</b> The monetary value of the <i>omer</i>, which is used to desacralize the funds, is far less than the amount paid to the harvesters and guards. The Gemara answers. <b>Rabbi Aḥa</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ba: Whatever</b> the treasurer <b>gives</b> them as payment <b>is</b> considered to be <b>its monetary value from the beginning</b> of the process, even if the barley is worth considerably less on the open market. On a similar note, <b>it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: It is <b>even so with</b> regard to <b>stoneworkers,</b> who hew stones from mountains and cut them for use in the Temple. They too receive their salary from the Temple treasury. <b>How does</b> the treasurer <b>do</b> this? <b>He takes money</b> as a loan <b>from a money changer and gives</b> it as payment <b>to the ledgemen and to the stonecutters before</b> the stone <b>is placed on the row [<i>dimos</i>]</b> that is being built. <b>Once it is placed on the row, he brings money from the collection of the chamber and desacralizes it onto</b> the stones. In this way, the stones become consecrated and the money becomes unconsecrated. That money is then used to repay the money changer. The Gemara asks: <b>Is this acceptable?</b> The workers are paid for all their labor, yet some of the stones become broken and unfit for use. Therefore, the amount of money spent by the treasury is more than the actual value of the stones placed on the structure of the Temple. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Bun,</b> said <b>in the name of Shmuel: Whatever</b> the treasurer <b>gives</b> them as payment <b>is</b> considered to be <b>the monetary value</b> of the stones <b>from the beginning.</b> The value of the stones that are used includes the value of the stones that broke during the process.

Halakhah 2

<sup><b>[10b]</b></sup> <strong>Halakha 2 · MISHNA</strong> <b>The</b> red <b>heifer, the scapegoat, and the strip of crimson</b> wool used in the process of burning the red heifer all <b>come from the collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber,</b> despite the fact that they are not sacrificial offerings. The same is true for <b>the ramp</b> built from the Temple Mount to the location on the Mount of Olives, where they would slaughter the red <b>heifer; the ramp</b> built to lead the <b>scapegoat</b> out of the city; <b>the strip</b> of crimson wool <b>that was</b> tied <b>between its horns;</b> any repairs required for <b>the aqueduct</b> that ran through the Temple courtyard and <b>the walls of the city and its towers; and</b> for <b>all the needs of the city,</b> such as street repairs, security, and the like. All of these <b>come from the remains of the chamber,</b> i.e., from the money that remained in the chamber after the three collections of money were taken to use for communal offerings. <b>Abba Shaul says: The High Priests construct the ramp</b> for <b>the</b> red <b>heifer from their own</b> funds. <b>What would they do with the leftover remains of the chamber</b> after all the items mentioned above had been attended to? <b>They</b> would <b>purchase wine, oil, and fine flour</b> and sell them to those who needed them for their private offerings. <b>And the profit</b> from these sales would go <b>to consecrated</b> property, i.e., to the Temple treasury; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yishmael. Rabbi Akiva says: One may not generate profit by</b> selling <b>consecrated</b> property, <b>neither</b> may one profit <b>from</b> funds set aside <b>for the poor.</b> <b>What would they do with the leftover</b> funds <b>of the collection</b> that had not been spent on communal offerings? They would purchase <b>golden plates</b> as a <b>coating for the</b> walls and floor of the <b>Holy of Holies. Rabbi Yishmael says: The leftover produce</b> was used to purchase the <b>repletion of the altar,</b> i.e., burnt-offerings sacrificed at times when the altar was idle. <b>The leftover</b> funds <b>of the collection</b> were used <b>to</b> purchase <b>sacred vessels.</b> <b>Rabbi Akiva says: The leftover</b> funds <b>of the collection</b> were used to purchase the animals used <b>for the repletion of the altar,</b> since they had originally been collected for offerings. <b>The leftover libations</b> were used <b>to</b> purchase <b>sacred vessels. Rabbi Ḥananya, the deputy [<i>segan</i>] High Priest, says: The leftover libations</b> were used to purchase animals for <b>the repletion of the altar,</b> while <b>the leftover</b> funds <b>of the collection</b> were used <b>to</b> purchase <b>sacred vessels.</b> Both <b>this</b> Sage, Rabbi Akiva, and <b>that</b> Sage, Rabbi Ḥananya, <b>did not agree</b> with Rabbi Yishmael’s opinion <b>with regard to the</b> leftover produce.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> Since the mishna mentions both the <b>ramp</b> used to transport the red <b>heifer</b> and the strip of crimson wool, the Gemara relates that <b>Rabbi Yishmael, son of Naḥman,</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yonatan: There are three strips</b> of crimson, each of which is a different weight. That <b>of the scapegoat is</b> the weight <b>of a <i>sela</i>,</b> as it had to be divided into two, one to tie between the scapegoat’s horns and one to tie on a rock. It is sufficient for the strip of crimson wool used in the purification <b>of a leper</b> to weigh <b>a shekel,</b> half of a <i>sela</i>, as it is not divided in half. The strip of crimson wool that was used in the process <b>of</b> burning the red <b>heifer is of two <i>sela</i>.</b> In order to sink into the mass of burning material, it had to be the heaviest of strips. <b>Rabbi Ḥunya</b> from the city <b>of Berat Ḥavrin</b> said that <b>Rabbi Ba, son of Zavda,</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Ḥalafta:</b> The crimson strip <b>of the</b> red <b>heifer is of two and a half <i>sela</i>. Some expressed</b> his statement using the <b>language: Of ten <i>zuz</i>.</b> This is the equivalent of two and a half <i>sela</i>; however, it is important to quote one’s teacher using the identical language that his teacher used.
§ The Gemara cites additional opinions with regard to the items that are purchased with the remains of the chamber. <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shmuel: The Torah scholars who teach the priests the <i>halakhot</i> of slaughtering, the <i>halakhot</i> of collecting</b> the blood of an offering in a sacred vessel, and <b>the <i>halakhot</i> of sprinkling</b> the blood on the altar <b>collect their wages from the collection of the chamber. Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Redifa</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Imi: Inspectors of blemishes of consecrated</b> animals <b>collect their wages from the collection of the chamber. Rabbi Aḥa</b> said that <b>Rabbi Tanḥum bar Ḥiyya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Simlai: The proofreaders of the</b> Torah <b>scroll</b> that was kept in <b>the</b> Temple <b>courtyard collect their wages from the collection of the chamber.</b> <b>Giddel, son of Binyamin,</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Asi: The two</b> chief <b>judges</b> in Jerusalem, who establish fines and adjudicate cases of <b>thefts, collect their wages from the collection of the chamber. Shmuel says: The women who weave the curtain</b> that separates the Temple Sanctuary from the Holy of Holies <b>collect their wages from the collection of the chamber. Rav Ḥuna said:</b> They collect their wages <b>from the collection for Temple maintenance.</b> With regard to <b>what do they argue? Shmuel considers it like an offering,</b> as the blood of certain offerings is sprinkled on the curtain. <b>Rav Ḥuna considers it like</b> part of <b>the building,</b> and funds for the building itself come from the collection for Temple maintenance. <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda Gudgadot taught: The</b> funds for the <b>incense and all communal offerings come from the collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber. The</b> funds for the <b>golden altar,</b> located inside the Sanctuary and on which the incense was offered, <b>and all sacred vessels come from</b> the <b>leftover money</b> of that set aside for the <b>libations. The</b> funds for <b>the altar of burnt-offering,</b> which was located outside the Sanctuary and on which most offerings were burned; <b>and</b> for <b>the Sanctuary</b> itself; <b>and the</b> various <b>courtyards come from the remains of the chamber.</b> Funds for whatever was <b>outside the courtyards come from</b> the money stored in <b>the chamber for Temple maintenance.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Wasn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that one who derives benefit from <b>the stones of the altar, the Sanctuary, or the courtyards</b> thereby <b>misuses</b> consecrated property? Therefore, if one does so unwittingly, he is liable to repay to the Temple treasury the value of the loss he caused or benefit he gained, in addition to a fine of one-fifth of that amount, and he must bring a guilt-offering. <b>And is there misuse</b> of consecrated property <b>with regard to</b> items purchased with the <b>remains</b> of the chamber? The Gemara answers: <b>Rather,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir, as Rabbi Meir says: One misuses</b> consecrated property even if he derives benefit from items purchased <b>with the remains</b> of the chamber. <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya said</b> in response to this suggestion: <b>Did Rabbi Meir say</b> this statement <b>other than within its year?</b> Even Rabbi Meir agrees that one violates the prohibition against misusing consecrated property by deriving benefit from the remains of the chamber only within the year for which those coins were donated. Since the funds could still be used for communal offerings if needed during that year, the prohibition applies. <b>But here, we are dealing with</b> funds that are <b>outside the year</b> of their collection, as the remains of the chamber were not used to purchase stones until after the year was over. Therefore, the <i>baraita</i> is not in accordance with Rabbi Meir, and the challenge to Rabbi Yehuda Gudgadot remains.
Having cited Rabbi Yehuda Gudgadot, referred to now as Rabbi Yehuda Gudganiyot, the Gemara cites another of his rulings. <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda Gudganiyot taught:</b> If <b>the table and the candelabrum and the altars and the curtain</b> are incomplete or are not in their proper location, <b>they prevent offerings</b> from being brought; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. The Rabbis say: The only</b> items <b>that prevent the offering</b> from being brought <b>are the</b> water <b>basin and the base alone,</b> as the Temple rite may not be performed if the basin and its base are not in place. However, <b>didn’t Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yosei ben Ḥanina both say: Wherever it is written: Opposite,</b> in describing the proper location for one of the sacred vessels, its absence <b>prevents</b> offerings from being brought. For example, with regard to the candelabrum, the verse states: “And the candelabrum opposite the table” (Exodus 26:35). Wherever the verse states: <b>Side,</b> the absence of the vessel <b>does not prevent</b> offerings from being brought. For example, with regard to the table, the verse states: “And you shall place the table on the south side” (Exodus 26:35). <b>And Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥman said in the name of Rabbi Yonatan: Even</b> absence of a vessel whose location is described with the <b>side, prevents</b> offerings from being brought. <b>And Rabbi Ila said in the name of</b> <sup><b>[11a]</b></sup> <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥman: And even</b> correct <b>placement, is necessary</b> for offerings to be valid. According to this view, if the candelabrum, either of the two altars, the table, the curtain, or the basin is invalid or not in its proper location, sacrificial offerings are not accepted. All of the <i>amora’im</i> cited here apparently disagree with the opinion of the Rabbis cited in the <i>baraita</i>, who maintain that the only vessels that invalidate offerings are the basin and its base. <b>Rabbi Ḥanina says:</b> There is no disagreement between these <i>amora’im</i> and the Rabbis. <b>Here,</b> the statements of the <i>amora’im</i> are <b>with regard to the rites that are</b> performed <b>inside</b> the Temple, such as bringing the incense, lighting the candelabrum, placing the shewbread on the table, and sprinkling blood on the curtain. The vessels located inside the Temple are essential for the validity of these rites. However, <b>here,</b> the statement of the Rabbis is <b>with regard to the rites that are</b> performed <b>outside</b> of the Sanctuary, i.e., in the courtyard, such as the sacrificing of most offerings. Only the absence of the basin or its base invalidates these rites.
§ We learned in the mishna that, according to Abba Shaul, the High Priests would pay for the ramp of the red heifer from their own funds. With regard to this issue, <b>Rabbi Ḥanina said: There was great haughtiness among the High Priests, as they would spend more than sixty talents of gold on it.</b> This expenditure was unnecessary, <b>as the</b> previous <b>ramp of the heifer was</b> still <b>standing. But not one of</b> the High Priests <b>would take out his heifer on his fellow’s ramp. Rather, he would demolish it and build</b> a new one <b>from his own</b> funds. <b>Rabbi Ulla raised an objection in the presence of Rabbi Mana: Wasn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Shimon HaTzaddik performed</b> the rites of <b>two</b> red <b>heifers,</b> and <b>the ramp on which he took out this</b> one <b>he did not</b> use again to <b>take out that</b> other one? <b>Is it possible for you to say Shimon HaTzaddik was haughty? What, then,</b> is the reason that he constructed a new ramp? It is <b>due to a higher</b> standard that was established <b>with regard to the</b> ceremony of the red <b>heifer; it is</b> due to a higher level of <b>honor [<i>silsul</i>] accorded to</b> the ceremony of <b>the red heifer.</b> <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Projections and walls would extend</b> from the ramp <b>from here and from there,</b> i.e., on either side, <b>in order that the priests would not peek</b> out over the edge of the ramp <b>and become ritually impure</b> by leaning their bodies over a grave without any interposition.
§ We learned in the mishna that according to Rabbi Yishmael, the leftover remains of the chamber were used to purchase wine, oil, and fine flour. These items were sold to individuals who needed them for their private offerings, and the proceeds went to the Temple treasury. Whereas <b>Rabbi Akiva says: One may not generate profit</b> by selling consecrated property or by using funds set aside for the poor. The Gemara explains that Rabbi Akiva prohibited doing business with consecrated funds only when there was a risk of loss. <b>However, if</b> the seller of the wine, oil, or fine flour <b>wanted</b> to agree that any <b>loss</b> from the subsequent sale of these items would be <b>his, while</b> any <b>profit</b> gained would go <b>to</b> the Temple treasury of <b>consecrated</b> property, it is <b>permitted.</b> The potential profit or loss would result from fluctuations in the market price of these items. An example <b>like this</b> can be found in an incident involving <b>bar Zemina. Funds</b> belonging to <b>orphans were deposited with him. He came and asked Rabbi Mana</b> if he was permitted to invest these funds. Rabbi Mana <b>said to him: If you want</b> to agree <b>that</b> any <b>loss</b> will be <b>yours and</b> any <b>profit</b> will belong to <b>both of you</b> to share, then <b>it is permitted. Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Adda</b> had <b>funds</b> belonging to <b>orphans deposited with him and he did so;</b> he accepted all losses and split the profits with the orphans.
§ It was taught in the mishna that according to Rabbi Yishmael, the <b>leftover produce</b> was used to purchase the repletion of the altar, i.e., burnt-offerings sacrificed at times when the altar was idle. This leftover produce discussed in <b>the mishna is</b> referring to the profits earned, in accordance with the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yishmael</b> cited earlier, <b>as Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Yosef explained the mishna</b> as follows: The <b>leftover produce</b> mentioned by Rabbi Yishmael is a reference to his earlier statement that <b>the profits</b> from the sales of wine, oil, and flour went <b>to</b> the Temple treasury of <b>consecrated</b> property. Regarding the term <b>leftover libations</b> mentioned by Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Ḥananya, the deputy High Priest, <b>this</b> is referring to the <b>fourth <i>se’a</i>.</b> If the treasurer of the Temple paid in advance for a year’s worth of wine, oil, and flour, and the price of these goods later rose, the seller was required to provide the goods at the original, lower price. The treasury then sold these items at the higher price to individuals who needed them for offerings. For example, if the treasurer paid for flour at the price of four <i>se’a</i> for one <i>sela</i>, and the price then rose to three <i>se’a</i> for one <i>sela</i>, the seller would have to provide four <i>se’a</i> for one <i>sela</i>. This fourth <i>se’a</i> was sold, and the profit from this sale was given to the Temple. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan explained the mishna</b> as follows: <b>The leftover produce, this</b> is referring to <b>the fourth <i>se’a</i>. The leftover libations,</b> this is referring <b>to the overfill [<i>beirutzin</i>]</b> of the substance when measured. When one sells a certain measure of wine, oil, or flour to the Temple treasury, he is required to fill the measuring vessel above the rim, so that he provides more than the exact measurement. However, when the Temple treasurer sells these products, he must be exact in the measurements. The difference between these two amounts is referred to in the mishna as the leftover libations. The Gemara asks: <b>And is Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Yosef not</b> of the opinion that the Temple profits from <b>the overfill?</b> Why, then, does he omit any reference to it in his explanation of the mishna? <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya said: That which befalls the fourth <i>se’a</i> befalls the overfill</b> as well. Both fall under the same category of leftover libations. It works out well <b>according to the opinion of Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Yosef,</b> which maintains that the leftover produce was sold for profit by the Temple treasury: <b>One may not generate profit</b> by selling <b>consecrated</b> property, <b>and not even with</b> funds set aside <b>for the poor,</b> and <b>therefore</b> the mishna concludes: Both <b>this</b> Sage <b>and that</b> Sage, Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Ḥananya, <b>did not agree</b> with Rabbi Yishmael’s opinion <b>with regard to the</b> leftover produce. However, <b>in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> which maintains that the leftover produce is referring to the profit gained from the fourth <i>se’a</i>, <b>it is difficult. Didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna that if the treasurer paid for a product at the price of four units per <i>sela</i>, and at the time it was delivered the price had risen and <b>stood at three</b> units per <i>sela</i>, the seller must still <b>provide</b> the produce at the original price <b>of four</b> units per <i>sela</i>, leaving the Temple treasury with an extra unit from which to profit? Apparently, all agree that the treasury may profit in this manner. However, <b>we learned</b> in the mishna that <b>this</b> Sage <b>and that</b> Sage, Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Ḥananya, <b>did not agree</b> with Rabbi Yishmael’s opinion <b>with regard to</b> the leftover <b>produce.</b> The Gemara answers: Everyone agrees that the fourth <i>se’a</i> may be sold on behalf of the Temple treasury. When the mishna says that Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Ḥananya did not agree with regard to the leftover produce, it means that they <b>did not agree</b> that the profits <b>from the produce</b> should be used <b>for</b> purchasing <b>the repletion of the altar. However, they did agree</b> that they may be used <b>for</b> purchasing <b>sacred vessels.</b> It appears that all agree that profits from the overfill of the measures are used to purchase sacred vessels. The Gemara asks: <b>Until here,</b> it is reasonable to say that the <b>overfill</b> from <b>communal</b> offerings is used to purchase sacred vessels. However, is it possible to say that the sacred vessels could be purchased <b>even with</b> the <b>overfill of individual</b> offerings? <b>Wouldn’t it turn out that the sacred vessels had come from an individual,</b> which is not permitted? The Gemara answers that it is <b>like that which we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>a woman who made a tunic,</b> one of the priestly vestments, <b>for her son</b> to wear while he serves in the Temple, the tunic <b>is valid.</b> However, since the priestly vestments must come from communal funds, the priest may only use his mother’s tunic in the Temple <b>provided that she</b> completely <b>transfers</b> ownership of the tunic <b>to the community.</b> Similarly, if the owner donates the overfill to the public, it is permissible to use the profit to purchase sacred vessels. <b>Until now,</b> the discussion has concerned <b>liquid overfill,</b> e.g., wine or oil. Since the overfill was originally within the confines of the sacred vessel, it attained consecrated status. Is the same true <b>even for dry overfill,</b> such as fine flour, in which the overfill was piled on top of the already full vessel? If the overfill was never actually within the confines of the vessel, is it consecrated?
