The Gemara answers: The <i>halakha</i> <b>is in accordance with that which we learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Menaḥot</i> 79a): In the case of <b>the libations,</b> which include fine flour, <b>that were sanctified in a</b> sacred <b>vessel and,</b> subsequently, <b>the offering</b> they were meant to accompany <b>is found to be disqualified, if there is another offering there</b> that requires libations, <b>they</b> may be <b>sacrificed with it. And if not,</b> and <b>they are left overnight, they become disqualified by</b> being <b>left overnight.</b> Since the mishna in <i>Menaḥot</i> does not distinguish between liquid libations and fine flour, the Gemara understands that no distinction should be drawn with regard to the overfill; even dry overfill is consecrated and can be used for the purchase of sacred vessels.

Halakhah 3

 <strong>Halakha 3 · MISHNA</strong> <b>The leftover incense</b> from one year could not be used the following year, as it had been purchased with the shekels collected for the previous year. <b>What would be done with it</b> in order to make it usable? The Temple treasurers <b>would set aside</b> an amount <b>of it</b> equal to the value of <b>the wages of the artisans</b> who worked in the Temple. <b>They would</b> then <b>desacralize</b> that incense by transferring its sanctity <b>to the money</b> owed to <b>the artisans. They would</b> then <b>give</b> the incense <b>to the artisans as their wages.</b> Finally, <b>they would return and buy</b> back the incense from the artisans with funds <b>from the new collection</b> of shekels. <b>If the new</b> funds <b>come on time,</b> i.e., by the beginning of Nisan, <b>they purchase</b> the incense with funds <b>from the new collection</b> of shekels. <b>And if not,</b> they may still purchase it <b>from the old</b> collection, and it is valid.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> We learned in the mishna that the leftover incense was desacralized with money that the Temple treasury owed the artisans. The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t it found</b> that this process involves <b>consecrated property being desacralized with consecrated property?</b> The artisans were paid with money from the Temple treasury, which was also consecrated, yet consecrated property can be desacralized only by transferring its sanctity to unconsecrated property. The Gemara answers that the incense was desacralized with this money after the money itself had been desacralized. <b>How does</b> the treasurer of the Temple appointed to this task <b>do</b> so? <b>Rabbi Shimon bar Bisna said: He brings money</b> from the treasury <b>and desacralizes it onto the building</b> that the artisans have constructed, which is not yet sanctified. <sup><b>[11b]</b></sup> <b>He</b> then <b>brings the incense and desacralizes</b> it by transferring its sanctity <b>onto</b> the money. The Temple treasurers then <b>give</b> the incense <b>to the artisans as their wages.</b> Finally, they buy back the incense with the funds from the new collection, as taught in the mishna. <b>That money</b> that was used to desacralize the incense, which is now consecrated, <b>what should be done with it? Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: I say</b> that <b>it should be given to the house of Garmu and to the house of Avtinas, who were experts in the preparation of the incense and in the production of the shewbread.</b> The producers of the incense and the shewbread receive their payment from the collection of the Temple treasury chamber. <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak said: And this is</b> true only <b>when</b> the treasury <b>owes them money from</b> work done <b>previously,</b> but it should not be given to them as advance payment for work they will do in the future, as the wages for work they do from the month of Nisan and on should come from the collection of the new year. <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Ba asked:</b> If the Temple treasury <b>did not owe</b> these artisans <b>money from</b> work done <b>previously,</b> what would be done with the money? <b>Rabbi Ba came</b> and said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥiyya,</b> who said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yosei:</b> The treasury uses the money to buy offerings <b>with which they replete the altar.</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Ba accepted this answer. <b>Rabbi Ba bar Kohen asked in the presence of Rabbi Yosei: Is the opinion of Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Ba reversed? There,</b> at first, <b>he needed</b> clarification with regard to this question, <b>and here it is obvious to him</b> that one may use the money to pay for the repletion of the altar. The answer to the question was given by Rabbi Ba in the name of Rabbi Ḥiyya, and that same Rabbi Ḥiyya asked the question. The Gemara explains: <b>That</b> about <b>which he needed</b> clarification <b>is</b> whether the money used to desacralize the incense may be used to purchase <b>sacred vessels. That which was obvious to him is</b> that the money may be used to buy offerings for the <b>repletion of the altar.</b>
Rabbi Ḥiyya’s initial dilemma with regard to purchasing sacred vessels with the money used to desacralize incense reflects his indecision concerning the dispute about <b>which</b> two <i>amora’im</i> <b>disagreed:</b> If one <b>prepared</b> incense <b>in a non-sacred</b> vessel, <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, said: It is disqualified</b> for use in the Temple, as incense must be consecrated by being placed in a sacred vessel. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: It is valid</b> for use, as there is no requirement that incense be placed in a sacred vessel during its preparation. According to the opinion of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, all incense is sacred on the level of a sacred vessel. Consequently, the money exchanged for it will have the same level of sanctity, and it can be used to purchase sacred vessels. However, according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi, it is possible to have prepared incense that has not yet been placed in a sacred vessel. The money exchanged for such incense may not be used to purchase sanctified vessels, as it does not have the appropriate level of sanctity. <b>What is the source</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina?</b> The verse states about incense: <b>“It is holy”</b> (see Exodus 30:37). This teaches <b>that its creation,</b> i.e., its preparation, must take place in <b>a sacred</b> vessel, so that it has elevated sanctified status from its inception. <b>What is the source</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi? “It is holy,”</b> meaning <b>that it must come from the collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber,</b> i.e., from the public and not from an individual. However, once it is purchased from communal funds, it may be prepared even in a non-sacred vessel. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Bun, said:</b> The opinion <b>of Rabbi Yosei ben Ḥanina goes in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Shmuel. And</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan.</b> <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shekalim</i> 12a): There is a tannaitic disagreement concerning <b>one who consecrates</b> all of <b>his possessions, and among them there are items</b> that are <b>suitable for</b> use as <b>communal offerings. Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> identified the possession the mishna is referring to and <b>said: Incense.</b> Apparently, he maintained that it is possible for one to be in possession of incense that has not yet been consecrated. Therefore, he must also maintain that incense that was prepared in a non-sacred vessel is valid for use, in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi. <b>Rabbi Hoshaya</b> said: The mishna <b>should be explained</b> as referring specifically <b>to an artisan of the house of Avtinas, who would receive incense as his wages.</b> The incense was prepared in a sacred vessel, but it could come into his possession after being desacralized. <b>And</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yosei ben Ḥanina is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Shmuel, as Rav Huna said in the name of Shmuel:</b> With regard to the <b>mortar,</b> within which they crushed the ingredients of the incense, the Sages <b>considered it like a sacred vessel</b> with regard <b>to</b> its ability to bestow an elevated, <b>consecrated</b> status upon its contents. Apparently, Shmuel holds that the incense must be prepared in a sacred vessel. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Bun, said: Rabbi Ḥuna said</b> this <i>halakha</i> of Shmuel <b>in the presence of Rabbi Yosei</b> and asked: May <b>an item that was sanctified in a sacred vessel be redeemed?</b> Since placing an item in a sacred vessel elevates its sanctity, ordinarily it would not be possible to transfer its sanctity onto money. Yet Shmuel insists that the incense be prepared in a sacred vessel, and the leftover incense was desacralized by transferring its sanctity onto money. Rabbi Yosei <b>said to him: Isn’t that</b> precisely the opinion <b>of Shmuel? As Shmuel said: This is a leniency with regard to leftover</b> communal offerings, including incense. They can, in fact, be desacralized, despite their elevated level of sanctity. The Gemara cites the source of Shmuel’s opinion: The <i>amora’im</i> <b>disagreed</b> with regard to <b>unblemished</b> lambs that had been set aside for the daily offerings and <b>remained</b> unused at the end of the year. They may not be sacrificed in the coming year, as they were not purchased with funds collected for that year. <b>Shmuel said:</b> Although in general one may redeem such animals only after they develop a blemish, in this case <b>they</b> may be <b>redeemed</b> even <b>as unblemished</b> animals. The Temple treasury could then purchase them anew from the newly collected funds, rendering them suitable as offerings for the new year. The same holds true for remaining incense; it too may be desacralized in a more lenient manner. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> Although they are animals remaining from the previous year, they still must be <b>redeemed</b> in the regular manner, <b>like</b> any other <b>consecrated animals that have been disqualified.</b> One must wait until they develop a blemish; only at that point may they be redeemed. The Gemara raises a similar issue: If <b>goats</b> that were set aside for communal sin-offerings <b>were left over</b> at the end of the year, according to <b>Shmuel</b> they may be redeemed in their unblemished state. <b>If a burnt-offering</b> may be <b>redeemed</b> unblemished, <b>all the more so</b> it should be permitted to redeem an animal set aside as <b>a sin-offering.</b> With regard to an animal set aside for a burnt-offering, if it was not redeemed in its unblemished state, but became blemished and was subsequently redeemed, the funds used for its redemption would go to purchase another burnt-offering for the repletion of the altar. The value set aside as burnt-offering thus would remain dedicated to that purpose. In the parallel case of a sin-offering, the funds used to redeem a blemished leftover communal sin-offering did not go to the purchase of a new sin-offering but to the purchase of a burnt-offering for the repletion of the altar as well. Since the animal’s sanctification as a sin-offering is effectively nullified in any case, it is easier in such a case to be lenient and permit the redemption of the animal even before it has become blemished. <b>Rabbi Ze’eira said in accordance with Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion:</b> Even in the case of leftover communal sin-offerings, the animals must <b>graze</b> until they develop a blemish; only then may they be redeemed. The funds used to redeem the animals became sanctified and were allocated for the purchase of burnt-offerings for the repletion of the altar. <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak said: They,</b> i.e., the leftover sin-offerings, are <b>themselves</b> used as burnt offerings to <b>replete the altar</b> without being redeemed. The Gemara asks: <b>But this is difficult: Is there a sin-offering that</b> may be <b>brought</b> as <b>a burnt-offering? Rabbi Yosei said: This</b> case <b>is different,</b> as it is dealing with a communal offering; <b>communal offerings are designated</b> as a particular type of offering <b>only with</b> their <b>slaughter,</b> and not when they are consecrated in the first place. <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya</b> offers a different explanation and says: <b>It is a condition of the court,</b> which stipulates <b>with regard to remaining</b> animals <b>that</b> even if they have been set aside as communal sin-offerings <b>they</b> may be <b>sacrificed as burnt offerings.</b>

Halakhah 4

<sup><b>[12a]</b></sup> <strong>Halakha 4 · MISHNA</strong> <b>One who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions</b> without specifying for what purpose, his possessions are consecrated for Temple maintenance. <b>And</b> if <b>among them there are items</b> that are <b>suitable for</b> use as <b>communal offerings,</b> which may not be used for the maintenance of the Temple but only for sacrificial purposes, what is done with those items to remove their consecration for Temple maintenance, in order that they may be reconsecrated for sacrificial use? <b>They are given to</b> Temple <b>artisans as their wages,</b> and thereby they are desacralized; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. Ben Azzai said to him: This is not the method</b> to be used. <b>Rather,</b> the same method that is used to desacralize the leftover incense, as is described in the previous mishna, should also be used here, i.e., <b>they set aside from</b> the consecrated items the equivalent of the value owed to the <b>artisans for their wages, and they desacralize them</b> by transferring their sanctity <b>onto</b> the <b>money</b> allocated <b>for the artisans’</b> wages, <b>and</b> then <b>they give those</b> items, which are no longer consecrated, <b>to the artisans as their wages.</b> According to both opinions, once the desacralized items are in the possession of the artisans, one of Temple treasurers <b>should repurchase those</b> items using money <b>from</b> that year’s <b>new collection of half-shekels,</b> consecrating them for sacrificial use during the coming year. In the case of <b>one who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions</b> without specifying for what purpose, <b>and among them there is an animal that is suitable</b> to be sacrificed <b>on the altar, male</b> or <b>female,</b> what should be done with it? <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> Since he did not specify otherwise, everything is consecrated for Temple maintenance. Therefore, any <b>males</b> should <b>be sold for the needs of burnt-offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals who will sacrifice them as such. <b>And</b> any <b>females,</b> since they cannot be brought as burnt-offerings, should <b>be sold for the needs of peace-offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals who will sacrifice them as such. <b>And their monetary value</b> that is received from their sale is <b>allocated with the rest of his property for Temple maintenance.</b> <b>Rabbi Yehoshua says:</b> Although he did not specify for what purpose he consecrated his possessions, it may be assumed that he intended the animals to be consecrated as burnt-offerings. Therefore, any <b>males</b> should <b>themselves be sacrificed as burnt-offerings, and</b> any <b>females,</b> since they cannot be brought as burnt-offerings, should <b>be sold for the needs of peace-offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals who will sacrifice them as such, <b>and their monetary value</b> that is received from their sale should be used to purchase and <b>bring burnt-offerings.</b> According to both opinions, <b>the rest of the possessions,</b> which are not suitable for sacrificial use, <b>are allocated for Temple maintenance.</b> <b>Rabbi Akiva said: I see the statement of Rabbi Eliezer</b> as more correct <b>than the statement of Rabbi Yehoshua, since Rabbi Eliezer applied his method equally</b> to both animals and other possessions in treating both as consecrated for Temple maintenance, whereas <b>Rabbi Yehoshua made a distinction</b> between them. <b>Rabbi Papeyyas said: I heard the statements of both of them</b> applied to different situations: <b>One who consecrates</b> all his possessions and <b>explicitly</b> states that his animals are to be included, clearly intends to equate his animals with the rest of his possessions, that both should be consecrated for the same purpose, i.e., for Temple maintenance. Therefore, he should act <b>in accordance with the statement of Rabbi Eliezer.</b> However, with regard to <b>one who consecrates</b> all his possessions <b>without</b> explicitly <b>specifying</b> that this includes his animals, since there is no reason to presume that he wishes them all to be consecrated for the same purpose, it is presumed that each item is consecrated for the purpose most suited to it. Therefore, he should act <b>in accordance with the statement of Rabbi Yehoshua.</b> In the case of <b>one who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions, and among them there were items that are suitable to</b> be sacrificed <b>on the altar,</b> such as <b>wines</b> for libations, <b>and oils</b> for meal-offerings, <b>and birds,</b> e.g., turtledoves or young pigeons, <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: They are sold for the needs of that kind</b> of item, i.e., to individuals who will use them as such. <b>And he should bring with their monetary value</b> that is received from their sale <b>burnt-offerings. And the rest of the possessions are allocated for Temple maintenance.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that there is a dispute with regard to the case of <b>one who consecrates</b> all his possessions, and among them there were items that are suitable for use as <b>communal offerings. Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The mishna is referring to one whose possessions include ingredients used to prepare the <b>incense. Rabbi Hoshaya says:</b> If so, the mishna <b>should be explained</b> as referring specifically <b>to an artisan of the house of Avtinas, who would receive incense as his wages.</b> There is a dispute about whether the incense may be prepared by an individual in a non-sacred vessel and then dedicated to the Temple. Explaining the mishna as referring to an artisan who received the incense from the Temple means that the mishna holds true for both sides of the dispute. § The mishna teaches that Rabbi Akiva holds that incense consecrated for Temple maintenance may be desacralized simply by giving it to the artisans as their wages, but ben Azzai insisted the incense must be desacralized by transferring its sanctity to money. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reasoning of ben Azzai?</b> He holds <b>that consecrated</b> property <b>cannot be desacralized upon labor</b> since it is intangible. <b>Rather,</b> it can be desacralized only <b>upon</b> a tangible object, such as <b>money.</b>
The Gemara notes: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Temura</i> 31b): <b>There are</b> certain <i>halakhot</i> that apply to <b>items consecrated for Temple maintenance</b> that do not apply to animals consecrated for the altar. They are: <b>That unspecified consecrated</b> items are consecrated <b>for Temple maintenance.</b><br><b>Consecration for Temple maintenance takes effect on all</b> types of objects, even wood, stones, and blemished animals; while consecration for the altar takes effect only on objects that may be brought as offerings upon the altar.<br><b>One</b> violates the prohibition against <b>misuse</b> of consecrated objects by deriving benefit <b>from that which grows from</b> items consecrated for Temple maintenance, such as an egg of a chicken or milk of a domesticated animal. One who derives benefit is liable to pay a fifth of the principle amount in addition to sacrificing the relevant guilt-offering.<br><b>And</b> unlike with animals consecrated to the altar, <b>there is no benefit from them for the priests.</b> Priests sometimes benefit from animals consecrated to the altar by eating portions of the offerings or receiving their hides. <b>Rabbi Ḥananya said</b> that this mishna is in accordance with the opinion <b>of Rabbi Eliezer, as we learned in the mishna:</b> In the case of <b>one who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions</b> without specifying for what purpose, <b>and there was among them an animal that is suitable</b> to be sacrificed <b>on the altar,</b> whether <b>males</b> or <b>females, Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> Any <b>males</b> should <b>be sold for the needs of burnt-offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals who will sacrifice them as such; <b>and</b> any <b>females,</b> since they cannot be brought as burnt-offerings, should <b>be sold for the needs of peace-offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals who will sacrifice them as such. <b>And their monetary value</b> that is received from their sale is <b>allocated with the rest of his property for Temple maintenance.</b> Unlike Rabbi Yehoshua, who argues with him in the mishna, Rabbi Eliezer assumes that since he did not specify otherwise, all one’s possessions, including his animals, are consecrated for Temple maintenance and must therefore be desacralized. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: The source for Rabbi Eliezer’s</b> opinion is the verse: <b>“And when a man shall consecrate his house to be holy to the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 27:14). <b>To what</b> case <b>are we referring</b> in the verse? <b>If</b> you suggest this verse is referring to <b>a dwelling place,</b> that cannot be correct because <b>it is already written: “And if the one who consecrated it will redeem his house”</b> (Leviticus 27:15). <b>Rather, we are referring to one who,</b> without specifying for what purpose, <b>consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions. From here</b> it is derived <b>that unspecified consecrated</b> property is allocated <b>for Temple maintenance.</b>
The Gemara cites differing opinions concerning which cases Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua agree and disagree upon: <b>Rabbi Ze’eira said</b> that <b>Rav Ḥuna</b> said <b>in the name of Rav: With regard to what</b> case <b>do they disagree?</b> They disagree with regard to <b>one who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions</b> without specifying for what purpose. <b>However, with regard to one who consecrates</b> the animals in <b>his flock</b> without specifying for what purpose, <b>everyone agrees that they are</b> consecrated <b>for the altar.</b> Since the consecration includes only items that are suitable to be sacrificed on the altar, even Rabbi Eliezer concedes that it is assumed that one consecrated them for the altar and not for Temple maintenance. <b>Rabbi Ba</b> said that <b>Rav Ḥuna</b> said <b>in the name of Rav: With regard to what</b> case <b>do they disagree? With regard to one who consecrates</b> the animals in <b>his flock</b> without specifying for what purpose. <b>However, with regard to one who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions</b> without specifying for what purpose, including animals that are unsuitable to be sacrificed on the altar, <b>everyone agrees that they are</b> consecrated <b>for Temple maintenance.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>According to the opinion of Rabbi Ze’eira, it works out well.</b> It is understandable that all agree that when one consecrates only animals, his intention is that they be used as offerings on the altar, for why else would he consecrate them? <b>However, there is a difficulty with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ba,</b> who maintains that even in such a case Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua disagree. <b>Isn’t an animal</b> suitable <b>for</b> the <b>altar?</b> Why then would Rabbi Eliezer disagree and say that the animals are consecrated for Temple maintenance? The Gemara answers: Indeed, <b>an animal is</b> suitable <b>for</b> the <b>altar. Why</b> then <b>did this man</b> decide to consecrate his animals <b>without specifying</b> that this was his intent? Apparently, his intention was not for them to be used on the altar; rather, <b>he is like one who says</b> that these animals should <b>be</b> consecrated <b>only for Temple maintenance.</b> There is a third opinion concerning the dispute: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: There is no difference</b> between the two cases. <b>Both this</b> case of <b>one who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions</b> and <b>that</b> case of <b>one who consecrates</b> the animals in <b>his flock</b> are alike. In both cases, <b>the disagreement</b> applies.
It was taught in the mishna that Rabbi Eliezer holds that animals that were consecrated for Temple maintenance are sold to those who need to bring them as offerings. What is the status of such animals after being sold? <b>Rav Ḥuna</b> said <b>in the name of Rav</b> that <b>Rabbi Abbahu</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> Animals that were <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance that were redeemed</b> by being sold, while still <b>unblemished</b> and therefore still suitable to be used as an offering, are nevertheless <b>transferred to non-sacred</b> status. The buyer must then consecrate the animals anew for use as offerings. The Gemara brings a proof. <b>The mishna</b> (<i>Ḥullin</i> 130a) <b>said that</b> as well: If animals are consecrated after already having developed a blemish, only their inherent value is sanctified. Therefore, <b>their offspring and their milk are permitted</b> to be used for mundane purposes <b>after their redemption,</b> since their sanctity is entirely removed upon their redemption. So too, the sanctity of animals that are consecrated for Temple maintenance is entirely removed upon their being redeemed, even if they are unblemished, as their sanctity inheres only in their value. <sup><b>[12b]</b></sup> The Gemara disarms this proof. <b>Rav Ḥizkiya</b> said <b>in the name of Rav Ḥisda:</b> The comparison to the mishna <b>should be explained</b> as referring to a case where the animals that were consecrated for Temple maintenance <b>were redeemed</b> while still <b>unblemished,</b> but they then <b>developed a blemish.</b> Once an animal has developed a blemish, its sanctity ceases to be inherent and inheres only in its monetary value. As long as the animal remains unblemished, it retains some inherent sanctity that is not removed upon redemption. <b>Rabbi Yosei</b> said <b>in the name of Rav Ḥisda: The mishna</b> (<i>Temura</i> 31b) <b>said that</b> as well: If animals consecrated for the altar subsequently develop a blemish, <b>it is prohibited</b> to use <b>their offspring or their milk</b> for any mundane purpose, even <b>after their redemption.</b> The mishna continues: However, if an animal consecrated for Temple maintenance subsequently develops a blemish, and is redeemed, it is permitted to use its offspring or milk for mundane purposes. The sanctity is fully removed only if the animal has a blemish. This implies that even animals that are consecrated for Temple maintenance retain some sanctity after redemption so long as they are unblemished. The Gemara cites support for the opposing view of Rabbi Yoḥanan that even if the animal did not develop a blemish, it loses all its sanctity upon its redemption: <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yosei:</b> Animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance that were redeemed</b> while still <b>unblemished are</b> entirely <b>transferred to non-sacred</b> status; they retain no sanctity at all. This must be correct, for <b>if you say they are not</b> entirely <b>transferred to non-sacred</b> status but retain some of the sanctity of their original consecration for Temple maintenance, <b>how can</b> the sanctity of <b>items consecrated to the altar take effect upon items consecrated for Temple maintenance?</b> In the mishna, it is clear that when an unblemished animal consecrated for Temple maintenance is redeemed by being sold, the buyers may consecrate the animals to the altar. But if the animals still retain the sanctity of Temple maintenance, this would not be possible, due to the principle that one may not change the status of an item from one type of consecrated status to another (see <i>Temura</i> 32b). Perforce, all their sanctity is removed upon redemption, despite the fact that they are still unblemished.
The Gemara accepts the preceding argument as compelling. Perforce, Rav Ḥisda should accept it. Why, then, does he claim that it is prohibited to use for any mundane purpose animals that are redeemed before developing a blemish? The Gemara explains: Rav Ḥisda holds that only animals that are <b>blemished</b> will become entirely non-sacred upon redemption. He holds that, by rabbinic decree, <b>the sanctity of the altar takes effect upon them.</b> With regard <b>to what matters</b> does it take effect? With regard <b>to</b> the prohibition against <b>shearing</b> them <b>and</b> using them for <b>labor.</b> However, he agrees that according to Torah law, they have no sanctity of the altar, since they were not consecrated for that purpose. Therefore, their sanctity is removed entirely upon redemption. § It was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who set aside a female</b> animal and consecrates it <b>for his burnt-offering,</b> or <b>his Paschal lamb,</b> or <b>his guilt-offering,</b> although these offerings may be brought only from a male animal, the consecration is still effective in endowing the female animal with inherent sanctity, such that <b>it makes a substitute;</b> if he tries to substitute another animal in place of the first, both animals are consecrated. <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> If one consecrated a female animal for <b>his burnt-offering, it makes a substitute;</b> however, if he consecrated it for <b>his Paschal lamb</b> or <b>his guilt-offering, it does not make a substitute. Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda said in the name of Rabbi Shimon:</b> In all cases, whether one consecrated it for <b>his burnt-offering,</b> or for <b>his Paschal lamb,</b> or for <b>his guilt-offering, it does not make a substitute.</b> The Gemara explains the basis for the different opinions in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: The reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon,</b> as cited by the first <i>tanna</i>, is <b>that we find</b> a case in which <b>a female bird is valid to be brought as a burnt-offering.</b> It is therefore clear that sanctity of a burnt-offering can be applied to a female animal, and consequently, a substitute can be made from it. However, this is not true with regard to a Paschal lamb or a guilt-offering, which are never brought from a female animal. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said: The reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda</b> in the name of Rabbi Shimon is based on the following <i>a fortiori</i> argument: <b>If</b> two animals are <b>of the same kind,</b> i.e., the same gender, and <b>one is distinct from</b> the other in a small, invalidating detail, that distinction is sufficient to prevent the animal from attaining any sanctity. This is <b>all the more so</b> in the case of two animals of <b>different kinds,</b> i.e., of different genders, which is a distinction of greater import. If being of one gender invalidates the animal, it will certainly prevent the invalid animal from attaining any sanctity. <b>What is the</b> case of two animals <b>of the same kind,</b> where <b>one is distinct from</b> the other in a small, invalidating detail? <b>Like that which was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to one who is obligated to sacrifice <b>a guilt-offering</b> from an animal less than <b>one year old and brings</b> an animal that was <b>two years old,</b> or one who is obligated to sacrifice <b>a guilt-offering</b> from an animal that was <b>two years old and brings</b> an animal that was <b>three years old, he does not fulfill his obligation.</b> In the first case, the animal is endowed with sanctity. However, in the second case, the animal remains non-sacred, despite the fact that the two animals are of the same kind and are distinct only in regard to one detail, their age. § <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Rabbi Shimon,</b> as cited by Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda, <b>and Rabbi Yehoshua,</b> in the mishna, <b>both said the same idea. Just as Rabbi Yehoshua said</b> that <b>a female</b> animal that was consecrated without a specific purpose <b>is not consecrated</b> with inherent sanctity <b>as a burnt-offering,</b> but <b>rather it is consecrated</b> only <b>with sanctity</b> that inheres in the animal’s <b>monetary value, so too, Rabbi Shimon said</b> that <b>a female</b> animal that was consecrated to be brought as a burnt-offering <b>is not consecrated</b> with inherent sanctity <b>as a burnt-offering,</b> but <b>rather it is consecrated</b> only <b>with sanctity</b> that inheres in the animal’s <b>monetary value,</b> which is why one cannot make a substitute from it. It is clear that Rabbi Yehoshua holds that the animal does not have inherent sanctity. <b>If you say</b> he holds that one <b>consecrated</b> the female animal <b>with inherent sanctity,</b> then it would not be permitted to sell it for the needs of peace-offerings, as Rabbi Yehoshua indeed rules in the mishna. Rather, <b>it</b> should have to <b>graze</b> until it develops a blemish, since animals with inherent sanctity may not be sold until they develop a blemish. The Gemara cites the remainder of the <i>baraita</i> already cited above: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said: I</b> do not <b>see the statement of Rabbi Shimon</b> as correct <b>with regard to</b> a female animal consecrated for <b>a Paschal lamb.</b> Rabbi Shimon said that the animal does not attain any sanctity, since a Paschal lamb is brought only from male animals. I disagree, <b>because a leftover Paschal lamb,</b> a Paschal lamb that was not slaughtered on Passover, <b>is brought as a peace-offering,</b> which can be brought from female animals. It is therefore appropriate for the consecration for a Paschal lamb to take effect upon a female animal and endow it with inherent sanctity, in a manner similar to Rabbi Shimon’s own argument concerning a burnt-offering. <b>But,</b> according to this line of logic, <b>let</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi also <b>say: I do not see the statement of Rabbi Shimon with regard to</b> a female animal consecrated for <b>a guilt-offering</b> as correct. Rabbi Shimon said that the animal does not attain any sanctity, since a guilt-offering is brought only from male animals. However, based on Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s reasoning in the case of a Paschal lamb, he should also disagree here, <b>since a leftover guilt-offering,</b> a guilt-offering that was not sacrificed as such because its owner achieved atonement with a different guilt-offering, <b>is brought as a burnt-offering,</b> and as Rabbi Shimon himself explained that the consecration of a female animal for a burnt-offering takes effect. <b>Rabbi Avin said:</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s argument for a Paschal lamb does not extend to a guilt-offering, because there is a crucial distinction between the cases: <b>If one consecrates</b> an animal as <b>a Paschal lamb,</b> and it is not used as such, and it is therefore <b>brought as a peace-offering,</b> the animal <b>itself is sacrificed as a peace-offering.</b> However, <b>if one consecrates</b> an animal as <b>a guilt-offering,</b> and it is not used as such, and it is therefore <b>brought as a burnt-offering,</b> the animal <b>itself is not sacrificed as a burnt-offering.</b> Rather, it is left until it develops a blemish, then it is sold, and the proceeds are used for the purchase of a burnt offering. <b>What is</b> behind <b>this</b> disagreement between Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi and Rabbi Shimon concerning a female animal consecrated as a Paschal lamb? <b>This one,</b> Rabbi Shimon, <b>says:</b> Surely <b>he consecrated</b> the animal only <b>with sanctity</b> that inheres in the animal’s <b>monetary value,</b> since he knows that it cannot actually be brought as a Paschal lamb. <b>And this one,</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, <b>says:</b> We do not make that assumption. Rather, <b>he consecrated</b> the animal to have <b>inherent sanctity,</b> and it takes effect because a leftover Paschal lamb can be brought as a peace-offering.
§ Rabbi Yehoshua taught in the mishna that if one consecrates animals without specifying for what purpose, the animals are endowed with the sanctity of burnt-offerings. <b>Rabbi Ze’eira</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish: The source for Rabbi Yehoshua’s</b> opinion is: <b>“Speak to Aaron, and to his sons, and to all the children of Israel, and say unto them: Any man of the house of Israel,</b> or of the strangers in Israel, <b>that brings</b> his offering, whether it be any of their vows, or any of their free-will offerings, which are brought <b>to the Lord as a burnt-offering”</b> (Leviticus 22:18). The verse is taken to mean that <b>all</b> vows or free-will offerings that can be brought to the Lord, i.e., animals, are consecrated to be <b>sacrificed as burnt-offerings</b> unless otherwise specified. The next verse states: <b>“That you may be accepted, you shall sacrifice a male without blemish”</b> (Leviticus 22:19). This verse would seem to limit to male animals the principle learned in the preceding verse. <b>From where</b> is it derived that <b>even females</b> that are consecrated without a specific purpose are also endowed with the sanctity of a burnt-offering? <b>The</b> continuation of the <b>verse states: “Of the cattle”</b> (Leviticus 22:19), which is a general term that comes <b>to include females.</b> <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak, son of Rabbi Elazar, asked:</b> Although <b>it is</b> explicitly <b>written “male.” You said</b> that the phrase <b>“of the cattle”</b> comes <b>to include females.</b> If this is true, then <b>in a similar manner,</b> one could make the following claim: Although <b>it is</b> explicitly <b>written “without blemish,” you</b> could <b>say</b> that the phrase <b>“of the cattle”</b> comes <b>to include blemished</b> animals.
For <b>what is</b> the difference <b>between them?</b> Why should one assume that the phrase “of the cattle” includes only female animals but not blemished animals? <b>Rav said:</b> The difference between them is so clear it is as if <b>the weaver’s rod [<i>shivta dekhadkada</i>] is between them:</b> Female animals are suitable to be brought on the altar in some form, whereas blemished animals are never suitable to be brought on the altar.
§ The mishna teaches: In the case of one who consecrates all his possessions without specifying for what purpose, and among them are wines, oils, and birds, which are suitable to be sacrificed on the altar, <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: They are sold</b> for the needs of that <b>kind</b> of item, i.e., to individuals who will use them as such, and the proceeds are used to purchase burnt-offerings. Rabbi Eliezer holds that items suitable for use on the altar that are consecrated without specifying their purpose are consecrated with sanctity that inheres in the item’s value, yet they are designated as burnt-offerings. Therefore, everything, including the birds, must be sold to achieve that end. <b>Rabbi Abbahu</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish: The source of Rabbi Eliezer’s opinion</b> is: <b>“Speak to Aaron, and to his sons,</b> and to all the children of Israel, and say unto them: Any man of the house of Israel, or of the strangers in Israel, that brings his offering, whether it be any of their vows, or any of their free-will offerings, <b>which are brought to the Lord as a burnt-offering”</b> (Leviticus 22:18). The verse is taken to mean that <b>all</b> vows or free-will offerings that can be brought to the Lord, i.e., animals, are consecrated to be <b>sacrificed as burnt-offerings,</b> unless otherwise specified. The next verse states: <b>“That you may be accepted, you shall sacrifice a male without blemish”</b> (Leviticus 22:19). <b>I might</b> have thought this verse teaches that an unspecified consecration can be fulfilled with the sacrifice of any unblemished male, <b>even birds.</b> To reject this possibility, <b>the</b> continuation of the <b>verse states: “Of the cattle”</b> (Leviticus 22:19), which includes only domesticated animals <b>and not birds.</b> <b>Rabbi Yirmeya and Rabbi Bun bar Ḥiyya were sitting and saying: There,</b> in explanation of the <i>baraita</i> cited above concerning one who consecrated a female animal for a burnt-offering, <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said that the reason for Rabbi Shimon’s opinion,</b> as cited by the first <i>tanna</i>, that the female is consecrated with the inherent sanctity of a burnt-offering is <b>that</b> there is that there is a case of <b>a female bird that is valid to be brought as a burnt-offering.</b> Yet <b>here,</b> in this case concerning the consecration of a female bird itself, Rabbi Eliezer <b>said this.</b> Presumably, Rabbi Eliezer agrees with Rabbi Yoḥanan’s explanation of Rabbi Shimon’s opinion. If the argument for a female animal gaining inherent sanctity when consecrated for a burnt-offering is based on a case of a female bird, how can it be that when a bird itself is consecrated it does not attain inherent sanctity and can therefore be sold? <sup><b>[13a]</b></sup> The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Yosei said: I established</b> Rabbi Eliezer’s opinion as being <b>in accordance with that which Rabbi Shmuel said in the name of Rabbi Ze’eira: Anything that cannot be sacrificed</b> on the altar, <b>neither itself nor its monetary value,</b> as it cannot be redeemed, <b>is consecrated only with sanctity</b> that inheres in its <b>monetary value. And</b> therefore, <b>you,</b> Rabbi Eliezer, <b>said correctly: You are not able to sacrifice</b> this bird on the altar, <b>as it is written: “Of the cattle,” and not birds. You are not able to redeem</b> the bird were it to have inherent sanctity, <b>as there is no</b> concept of <b>redemption for birds. Therefore, you must say that it is consecrated only with sanctity</b> that inheres in its <b>value.</b>
§ In the <i>baraita</i> cited above, Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda said in the name of Rabbi Shimon that if one consecrates a female animal for a burnt-offering, a Paschal lamb, or a guilt-offering, it is endowed only with sanctity that inheres in the animal’s value, since a female animal is invalid for these offerings. Consequently, a substitute cannot be made from it. <b>The group</b> of students said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan, and Rabbi Ayyevu bar Nagri said in the presence of Rabbi Ila, in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan: The source</b> for the opinion of <b>this <i>tanna</i>,</b> Rabbi Shimon, is the verse in the passage about substitution: <b>“And if it be any unclean animal, of which they may not bring an offering to the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 27:11), which indicates that an unclean animal that was consecrated cannot make a substitution. <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states “unclean,”</b> as it certainly is not referring to an animal of an unclean species of animal, i.e., a non-kosher animal, since they are already disqualified by a different verse? <b>Rather,</b> it is referring <b>even</b> to an animal of a clean, kosher species that is only <b>unclean,</b> i.e., unsuitable, <b>for that</b> particular <b>category</b> of offering. The Gemara notes: <b>It is difficult</b> to justify this source as the basis for Rabbi Shimon’s opinion because, <b>with regard to this, it is written</b> in the continuation of the verse: <b>“Then he shall stand</b> the animal before the priest. <b>And</b> the priest <b>shall value</b> it” (Leviticus 27:11–12), indicating that before being redeemed, the animal must be stood before the priest for valuation. However, Rabbi Shimon himself holds that this process is necessary only for animals that have inherent sanctity. Perforce, this verse and the previous one are referring to cases where the animal has inherent sanctity. If so, these verses cannot be used as the basis for Rabbi Shimon’s opinion in the <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rabbi Ze’eira, in the name of Rabbi Elazar, did not say</b> that <b>this</b> verse is the basis of Rabbi Shimon’s opinion. <b>Rather,</b> he explained it as the basis for the opposing opinion: <b>“And if it be any unclean animal, of which they may not bring an offering to the Lord.”</b> The superfluous word “offering” indicates that <b>any</b> animal <b>that is not suitable to be sacrificed, neither here,</b> for the type of offering for which it was consecrated, <b>nor anywhere else,</b> as any other type of offering, is never endowed with inherent sanctity. Therefore, <b>a substitute cannot be made</b> from it. This principle <b>excludes</b> the case of <b>a female</b> animal that was consecrated <b>for a burnt-offering. Although it is not suitable to be sacrificed here,</b> as an animal burnt-offering, <b>it is suitable to be sacrificed in another place,</b> such as a bird burnt-offering. This verse therefore serves as a source for the opinion of the first <i>tanna</i> in that <i>baraita</i>, who holds that a female animal consecrated for a burnt-offering, a guilt-offering, or a Paschal lamb is endowed with inherent sanctity, and therefore a substitute can be made from it. The Gemara challenges this principle: <b>Rabbi Avun and Rabbi Bun asked in the presence of Rabbi Ze’eira:</b> But <b>there is</b> the case of an animal <b>that sodomizes</b> a person <b>or</b> an animal <b>that is sodomized, which are not suitable to be sacrificed, neither here,</b> for the kind of offering for which it was consecrated, <b>nor anywhere else</b> as any other kind of offering, <b>and</b> yet <b>a substitute can be made</b> from it. The Gemara answers: <b>He said to them: Even I said</b> this principle <b>only</b> with regard to an animal that is <b>actually unclean,</b> i.e., from a non-kosher species. But it does not apply to an animal of a kosher species that is disqualified for some other reason. The Gemara notes: <b>But it is difficult</b> to claim that “unclean” is referring to an animal of an unclean, non-kosher species, because <b>with regard to this, it is written</b> in the subsequent verses: <b>“Then he shall stand</b> the animal before the priest. <b>And</b> the priest <b>shall value</b> it” (Leviticus 27:11–12), indicating the procedure for redeeming the animal. This certainly cannot refer to an animal of a non-kosher species, since a verse later on (Leviticus 27:27) addresses that case. Rabbi Ze’eira’s derivation is therefore problematic.

Halakhah 5

<strong>Halakha 5 · MISHNA</strong> <b>Once every thirty days, the prices</b> at which the Temple supplies such as wine, flour, or oil will be purchased <b>are set for the</b> Temple <b>chamber.</b> This set price is implemented in the following way: <b>Any</b> merchant <b>who undertakes to provide fine flour</b> after the chamber set a price of <b>four</b> <i>se’a</i> per <i>sela</i>, even if the general market price rose and <b>stood at three</b> <i>se’a</i> per <i>sela</i>, <b>he must provide</b> fine flour based on the set price of <b>four</b> <i>se’a</i> per <i>sela</i>. However, if the chamber’s set price was <b>three</b> <i>se’a</i> per <i>sela</i>, and the general market price fell to <b>four</b> <i>se’a</i> per <i>sela</i>, <b>he must</b> now <b>provide</b> fine flour based on the new market price of <b>four</b> <i>se’a</i> per <i>sela</i>. This is in order <b>that the</b> Temple treasury of <b>consecrated</b> property always <b>has the upper hand.</b> <b>If the fine flour became wormy, it became wormy for</b> the merchant, i.e., he bears the loss of the ruined fine flour and must provide new fine flour in its place. Similarly, <b>if the wine turned to vinegar, it turned to vinegar for</b> the merchant. This is because the merchant <b>only receives,</b> i.e., earns, <b>his money once the altar is satisfied,</b> i.e., the transaction is only realized once the items have been sacrificed on the altar.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon: The</b> merchants <b>would immediately receive their money. And</b> there was no concern that the items would spoil, because <b>priests are vigilant</b> and ensure that no item has a chance to spoil. 

Chapter 5



Halakhah 1

<strong>Halakha 1 · MISHNA</strong> <b>These are the officials who served</b> in specific positions <b>in the Temple: Yoḥanan ben Pineḥas</b> was responsible <b>for the seals.</b> One who paid for a specific type of sacrificial item received a seal, which he presented to the Temple official in exchange for that item. <b>Aḥiyya</b> was responsible <b>for the libations,</b> i.e., the wine, oil, and flour prepared with the level of ritual purity necessary for the libation offerings and the meal-offerings, which accompanied many animal offerings. Aḥiyya supplied the libations to those who presented the appropriate seal. <b>Matya ben Shmuel</b> was responsible <b>for the lotteries,</b> which were used to select priests for the various Temple services each day. <b>Petaḥya</b> was responsible <b>for the pairs</b> of birds, i.e., the turtledoves or pigeons, brought by a <i>zav</i>, a <i>zava</i>, a woman after childbirth, and a leper. They placed the appropriate sum of money into the horn designated for this purpose, and each day Petaḥya oversaw the purchase of birds from that money and their sacrifice in the proper manner. Incidentally, the Gemara mentions: <b>Petaḥya is Mordecai</b> from the book of Esther. <b>And why was he called Petaḥya,</b> which resembles the word for opening [<i>petaḥ</i>]? The reason is <b>that he would open,</b> i.e., elucidate, difficult <b>topics and interpret them</b> to the people, <b>and</b> because <b>he knew</b> all <b>seventy languages</b> known at the time. The mishna resumes the list of officials. <b>Ben Aḥiyya</b> was responsible <b>for</b> the care of the priests who suffered from <b>intestinal disease. Neḥunya</b> was the <b>well digger</b> for pilgrims on their way to Jerusalem for the Festivals. <b>Gevini</b> was the Temple <b>crier</b> who would awaken the priests and the Levites for their Temple duties. <b>Ben Gever</b> was responsible <b>for locking the</b> Temple <b>gates</b> in the evening and for unlocking them in the morning. <b>Ben Bevai</b> was <b>appointed over the shreds</b> of garments, which were formed into wicks for the Temple candelabra. He also supervised the twisting of those wicks into the appropriate thickness for the various nights during the different seasons of the year. <b>Ben Arza</b> was responsible <b>for the cymbal,</b> which was rung as a signal that the Levites should commence their song. <b>Hugras ben Levi</b> was responsible <b>for the song.</b> He taught and conducted the singers in the Temple. <b>The house of Garmu</b> was responsible <b>for the preparation of the shewbread; the house of Avtinas</b> was responsible <b>for the preparation of the incense; and Elazar</b> was responsible <b>for</b> weaving <b>the</b> Temple <b>curtains; and Pineḥas</b> was <b>the valet,</b> who assisted the priests in fitting their clothes and dressing themselves for their Temple service.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna lists fifteen names of officials who filled specific positions in the Temple, despite the fact that during the many years of the Temple there must have been far more than fifteen officials who served those functions. <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiyah said</b> that <b>Rabbi Simon and the Rabbis</b> disagreed as to why the mishna mentions only these fifteen names. <b>One</b> of them <b>said:</b> The mishna <b>comes to enumerate those</b> who were the most <b>fit</b> of all the officials who served in each position, <b>from whatever generation</b> they were from. <b>And the other said:</b> The <i>tanna</i> <b>who was in that generation enumerated those</b> functionaries <b>who</b> served <b>in his generation.</b> Several of these officials are also mentioned in the mishna in Yoma, some for praise (37a), and others for censure (38a). The Gemara notes that this issue is related to the aforementioned dispute. <b>The one who said</b> that the mishna <b>comes to enumerate the</b> most <b>fit</b> of those who served <b>from whatever generation</b> claims that when the mishna in <i>Yoma</i> <b>says: “The memory of a righteous person shall be for a blessing”</b> (Proverbs 10:7) it is <b>in reference to all of them.</b> According to this opinion, the <i>tanna</i> in <i>Yoma</i> maintains that the reason these officials did not teach their specialties to others was because they wanted to prevent their knowledge from being used for idol worship. The only officials censured are those like ben Kamtzar, who refused to divulge their secrets for unworthy reasons, but these people are not mentioned in the mishna. Conversely, <b>the one who said</b> that the <i>tanna</i> <b>who was in that generation enumerated those</b> officials <b>who</b> served <b>in his generation,</b> he claims that the mishna in <i>Yoma</i> <b>says: “But the name of the wicked shall rot”</b> (Proverbs 10:7) <b>in reference to all of them. And with regard to whom is</b> the <i>tanna</i> in <i>Yoma</i> <b>speaking</b> when he cites the verse: <b>“The memory of a righteous person shall be for a blessing”?</b> He is referring only <b>to ben Katin,</b> who improved the basin for the priests in the Temple, <b>and his colleagues.</b>
<sup><b>[13b]</b></sup> § After recording the praises of certain historical figures, the Gemara continues in a similar vein. <b>Rabbi Yona said</b> that <b>it is written: “Therefore will I divide him a portion among the great, and he shall divide the spoil with the mighty;</b> because he bared his soul until death, and was numbered with the transgressors” (Isaiah 53:12). <b>This</b> verse <b>is</b> referring to <b>Rabbi Akiva, who</b> conveyed the Oral Law to the people by <b>arranging the halakhic and homiletic midrash.</b> <b>And some say</b> that <b>the members of the Great Assembly arranged these</b> compilations of Torah knowledge. <b>But</b> if so, <b>what did this</b> Sage, Rabbi Akiva, <b>arrange?</b> He arranged the interpretive method of <b>generalizations and details.</b>
<b>Rabbi Abbahu said:</b> The lineage of Salma, son of Caleb, son of Hur, is listed in the book of Chronicles. <b>It is written</b> there: <b>“And the families of scribes that dwelt at Jabez:</b> The Tirathites, the Shimeathites, the Sucathites; these are the Kenites that came of Hammath, the father of the house of Rechab” (I Chronicles 2:55). <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> the word <b>scribes,</b> literally, counters? It does not mean that they were scribes; <b>rather,</b> it means <b>that they crafted</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the Torah</b> into <b>numbered groups.</b> They categorized and brought together disparate <i>halakhot</i> into a mnemonic device to assist learners. The Gemara lists some examples of this numerical categorization: <b>Five</b> categories of people may <b>not separate <i>teruma</i>; five types</b> of grain <b>require</b> the separation <b>of <i>ḥalla</i></b> from their dough; <b>fifteen</b> categories of <b>women exempt their co-wives</b> from levirate marriage and <i>ḥalitza</i> if they were both married to the same man who died childless; <b>thirty-six</b> transgressions for which one is liable to receive <b><i>karet</i></b> are listed <b>in the Torah; thirteen matters are stated with regard to the unslaughtered carcass of a kosher bird;</b> there are <b>four primary categories of damages;</b> and the number of <b>primary categories of labor</b> prohibited on Shabbat <b>is forty-less-one.</b>
 The Gemara cites a different interpretation for the Hebrew word for scribe. <b>Rabbi Eliezer said: It is written:</b> “Now this is the copy of the letter that the king Artaxerxes gave <b>to Ezra the priest the scribe,</b> a scribe of the words of the mitzvot of the Lord, and of His statutes to Israel” (Ezra 7:11). <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> the word <b>scribe [<i>sofer</i>]</b> twice? “Scribe” can be interpreted as scholar. The verse is not redundant; <b>rather,</b> it means that <b>just as</b> Ezra <b>was a scholar in matters of Torah, so was he a scholar in matters of the Sages,</b> i.e., the Oral Law.
<b>Rabbi Ḥaggai</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥman: The former</b> Sages, i.e., the scholars of earlier generations, metaphorically <b>plowed and planted, weeded, cleared</b> thorns, <b>hoed, harvested, gathered</b> sheaves into a pile, <b>threshed</b> the sheaves, <b>winnowed</b> the threshed grain, <b>separated</b> the bad grain form the good, <b>ground</b> the remainder into flour, <b>sifted</b> the flour in a sieve, <b>kneaded</b> the dough, <b>smoothed</b> the surface of the unbaked loaves with liquid, <b>and baked</b> the bread. They prepared everything so that we should be able to grasp Torah concepts; <b>and</b> yet, after all that, <b>we have nothing to eat,</b> as we are still unable to understand the Torah properly. The Gemara cites another metaphor with regard to the relationship between the earlier and later generations. <b>Rabbi Abba bar Zemina</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ze’eira: If the former</b> generations were akin to <b>angels, we are</b> akin to <b>humans; and if they were</b> akin to <b>humans, we are</b> akin to <b>donkeys. Rabbi Mana said: At that hour,</b> when the previous statement was issued, <b>they</b> also <b>said: We are not even comparable to the female donkey of Rabbi Pineḥas ben Yair.</b>
§ The Gemara explains the reference to this particular donkey. <b>The donkey of Rabbi Pineḥas ben Yair was stolen by robbers one night. It was kept hidden by them for three days, and</b> yet <b>it did not eat anything. After three days, they reconsidered</b> and decided to <b>return it. They said: Let’s get it out of here, so that it shouldn’t die in our possession</b> and leave a stench in our cave. When <b>they set it free it went and stood by its master’s gate</b> and <b>began braying.</b> Rabbi Pineḥas <b>said to</b> the members of his household: <b>Open up for that poor</b> creature, <b>which has gone three days without eating anything. They opened</b> the gate <b>for it, and it entered</b> Rabbi Pineḥas’ courtyard. <b>He told them: Give it something to eat. They placed barley before it, but it would not eat. They said to him: Rabbi, it will not eat. He said to them: Has</b> the barley been tithed so that it is <b>fit</b> to eat? <b>They replied: Yes. He</b> then <b>asked them: And have you separated their doubtfully tithed produce?</b> Did you tithe the grain about which there is doubt as to whether it has been tithed properly? <b>They replied: Didn’t you teach us the following, Rabbi: One who purchases grain for</b> feeding <b>an animal,</b> or <b>flour for</b> processing animal <b>hides,</b> or <b>oil for lighting</b> a lamp, is <b>exempt from</b> separating <b>doubtfully tithed produce?</b> There is no need to separate tithes from doubtfully tithed produce to feed a donkey. <b>He said to them: What can we do for that poor</b> creature, <b>which is very strict with itself</b> and will not eat even from doubtfully tithed produce, despite this exemption? <b>And they</b> therefore <b>separated</b> tithes from the <b>doubtfully tithed produce, and</b> the donkey finally <b>ate</b> the barley grains.
§ The mishna states that <b>Petaḥya</b> was responsible <b>for the pairs</b> of birds. The Gemara mentions some of the talents of this Petaḥya. <b>Come and see how great was the skill of that man.</b> He could <b>open,</b> i.e., elucidate, difficult <b>topics and interpret them.</b> As mentioned in the mishna, <b>he understood</b> all <b>seventy languages.</b> The Gemara adds: <b>It was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: A Sanhedrin that includes two</b> members <b>who are able to speak</b> all the seventy languages, <b>and all</b> of its members <b>are</b> at least <b>capable of understanding</b> those languages, this court <b>is</b> minimally <b>fit to</b> serve as <b>a Sanhedrin.</b> If it has <b>three</b> who can speak those languages, <b>it is a medium</b> level Sanhedrin. If it has <b>four, it is a wise</b> Sanhedrin. <b>In Yavne,</b> the Sanhedrin <b>included four</b> who could speak all seventy languages: <b>Ben Azzai, ben Zoma, ben Ḥakhinai, and Rabbi Elazar ben Matya.</b>
The Gemara cites a story that exemplifies Petaḥya’s skills at understanding linguistic allusions. <b>Rabbi Ḥisda said: Once Eretz Yisrael dried up.</b> There was a drought, no grain grew there, <b>and</b> the Sages <b>did not know from where they could bring the <i>omer</i>,</b> the measure of barley brought as a communal offering on the sixteenth of Nisan. <b>And there was a mute</b> present <b>there who</b> knew where barley was growing that year. He came to the Sages <b>and</b> gestured to them with his hands. He <b>placed one of his hands on roofs [<i>gagot</i>] and the other hand on huts [<i>tzerifin</i>],</b> alluding to the name of the place where barley could be found. Since they did not understand his allusions, <b>they brought Petaḥya before him, who</b> observed the actions of the mute and <b>said to them: Is there a place called Gagot Tzerifin or Tzerifin Gagot?</b> They remembered that there was indeed such a place. <b>They went there and found</b> barley for the <i>omer</i> offering. The Gemara relates a similar story. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Bun, said: Once</b> all the grain in <b>the entire world was blighted, and</b> the Sages <b>did not know from where they could bring</b> barley for <b>the <i>omer</i>. And</b> again <b>there was a mute</b> present <b>there who</b> knew where barley was growing. He came before the Sages and gestured to them with his hands. He <b>placed one hand on his eye and the other hand on a doorjamb [<i>sokhara</i>].</b> Since the Sages were unable to understand his allusions, <b>they brought Petaḥya before him, who</b> observed his actions and <b>said to them: Is there a place called Ein Sokher or Sokher Ein?</b> They remembered that there was indeed such a place. <b>They went there and found</b> barley for the <i>omer</i> offering.
The Gemara cites yet another story that exemplifies Petaḥya’s deciphering skills. There were <b>three women</b> who <b>brought their</b> own <b>pair</b> of birds to the Temple. It was not clear which type of offering each of them intended to bring. <b>One</b> of the women <b>said:</b> This pair of birds is <b>for my fountain; and</b> the second <b>one said:</b> It is <b>for my sea; and</b> the third <b>one said:</b> It is <b>for my <i>ziva</i>.</b> With regard to <b>that</b> woman <b>who said: For my fountain,</b> the Rabbis <b>thought to say</b> that she meant that her post-menstrual blood was <b>flowing like a fountain.</b> She was therefore bringing the pair of birds of a <i>zava</i>, as a sin-offering and a burnt-offering for her ritual purity. However, Petaḥya <b>said to them: She was endangered in a fountain.</b> She has brought this pair of birds as a thanks-offering for her escape from that life-threatening situation. With regard to <b>that</b> woman <b>who said: For my sea,</b> the Rabbis <b>thought to say</b> that she meant that her post-menstrual blood was <b>flowing like the sea,</b> which likewise means that she was a <i>zava</i> bringing her offerings for ritual purity. But Petaḥya <b>said to them: She was endangered in the sea,</b> so she too has brought this pair of birds as a thanks-offering. Finally, with regard to <b>that</b> woman <b>who said: For my <i>ziva</i>,</b> the Rabbis <b>thought to say</b> that she meant that she was <b>an actual <i>zava</i>,</b> and she was therefore bringing an offering for ritual purity. But Petaḥya <b>said to them: A wolf [<i>ze’ev</i>],</b> a word that is similar to the one she used, <b>came</b> and attempted <b>to snatch her son,</b> but he was ultimately saved. She, too, has brought this pair of birds as a thanks-offering.
 § The mishna states that <b>ben Aḥiyya</b> was appointed to treat those priests who suffered from intestinal disease. The Gemara explains why priests were particularly susceptible to this ailment. <b>Since the priests would walk barefoot on the floor,</b> even when it was cold, as their feet had to be in contact with the stones of the Temple floor, <b>and</b> since <b>they would eat</b> a lot of <b>meat</b> from the offerings <b>and drink</b> a lot of <b>water, they would contract intestinal disease. And</b> ben Aḥiyya <b>knew</b> that <b>this</b> particular type of <b>wine was good for</b> healing <b>the intestines, and</b> that <b>this</b> other type of <b>wine was</b> effective <b>intestinal medicine.</b>
The mishna states that <b>Neḥunya</b> was <b>the well digger.</b> The Gemara explains <b>that he would dig wells and caves,</b> where rainwater would collect, for pilgrims to use on their way to Jerusalem for the Festivals. <b>And he knew</b> <sup><b>[14a]</b></sup> <b>which rock contains water, and which rock contains fissures</b> in which water may be found, <b>and how far those fissures extend.</b> This would enable him to calculate how deep he had to dig to reach water. <b>Rabbi Eliezer said: And his son died of thirst.</b> Since the previous passage mentioned God’s rigorously exacting attitude toward righteous people like Neḥunya, the Gemara discusses how God relates to sinners. <b>Rabbi Ḥanina said: Whoever says</b> that <b>the Merciful One overlooks</b> the punishment due to sinners, <b>his intestines will be overlooked,</b> i.e., cease functioning. The reason sinners often appear to go unpunished is <b>rather</b> that God <b>extends His patience</b> with evildoers to give them a chance to repent, <b>but</b> eventually He <b>collects His due</b> and punishes the wicked. The Gemara explains the harsh judgment inflicted on the righteous Neḥunya. <b>Rabbi Aḥa said</b> that <b>it is written:</b> “Our God comes and does not keep silence; a fire devours before Him, <b>and His surroundings storm [<i>nis’ara</i>] mightily”</b> (Psalms 50:3). The Hebrew spelling of the word <i>nis’ara</i> is very similar to the word <i>sa’ara</i>, hair. This hints that God <b>is exacting with</b> the righteous, who are close to Him and can be called His surroundings, <b>up to a hairsbreadth.</b> Even slight deviations from the proper path can elicit punishment. <b>Rabbi Yosei said:</b> This idea is derived <b>not through that source,</b> but <b>rather from that which is written</b> about God: <b>“And dreadful is He upon all of His surroundings”</b> (Psalms 89:8), which indicates that <b>His dread is upon those close</b> to Him, i.e., the righteous, <b>more than</b> upon <b>those distant</b> from Him, i.e., the sinful.
The Gemara cites a related story. <b>Rabbi Ḥaggai</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥman:</b> There was <b>an incident involving a certain pious man who would dig pits, wells, and caves</b> to collect water <b>for passersby. Once his daughter was passing over</b> a river <b>for</b> the purpose of <b>marriage, and</b> the <b>river washed her away. And all the people came to console him, but he refused to accept their condolences.</b> <b>Rabbi Pineḥas ben Yair came to</b> visit <b>him to console him, but he refused to accept condolences</b> even from Rabbi Pineḥas. Rabbi Pineḥas <b>said to</b> the people of that community: Is <b>this your righteous man,</b> who will not be consoled and accept God’s judgment? <b>They said to him: Rabbi, he would perform such and such</b> acts of righteousness, by supplying water, <b>and</b> yet <b>such and such</b> tragedy, the drowning of his daughter, <b>occurred to him.</b> Rabbi Pineḥas <b>said:</b> Is it <b>possible that he honors his Creator with water, and</b> yet his Creator <b>strikes him with water? Immediately</b> thereafter, <b>a report spread throughout the city: The daughter of that</b> righteous <b>man has arrived,</b> as she did not actually drown. <b>Some say she grasped a branch</b> and pulled herself out of the river, <b>and some say an angel in the form of Rabbi Pineḥas ben Yair descended</b> from heaven <b>and rescued her.</b>
 § The mishna states that <b>Gevini</b> was the Temple <b>crier.</b> The Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> that states <b>that</b> Gevini <b>would issue an announcement</b> every morning <b>in the Temple. What would he say</b> in his announcement? <b>Arise, priests, to service; and Levites to their platform</b> to sing; <b>and Israelites,</b> i.e., the group of Israelites who represented the entire community at the sacrifice, <b>to their watch.</b> Gevini’s voice was so powerful that <b>King Agrippa</b> once <b>heard his voice as far</b> away <b>as eight parasangs, and</b> Agrippa <b>gave him many gifts</b> as a sign of his appreciation.
The mishna states that <b>ben Gever</b> was responsible <b>for locking</b> the Temple <b>gates</b> in the evening and opening them in the morning. The Gemara cites an incident involving ben Gever. It is taught in a mishna (<i>Yoma</i> 20a): On each day, the ashes are removed from the altar at the calling of the <i>gever</i> or near this time. The Sages dispute whether the term <i>gever</i> in this context means man or rooster. The Gemara relates that when Rav went to visit Rabbi Sheila, he would serve as his interpreter, explaining his lectures to the public. On one occasion, after Rabbi Sheila had explicitly stated that <i>gever</i> means a rooster, <b>Rav</b> nevertheless <b>interpreted in the</b> study <b>hall of Rabbi Sheila</b> that the phrase: <b><i>Gever</i> cried out,</b> means: <b>The crier announced.</b> Rabbi Sheila <b>said to him: Say</b> that this expression means: <b>The rooster cried out.</b> Rav <b>said to him: But we learned</b> in a mishna above (13a) that there is a man called <b>ben Gever. Could you</b> possibly <b>say</b> that his name means <b>son of a rooster?</b> In this context, <i>gever</i> must mean man, not rooster. Therefore, in <i>Yoma</i> as well it must indicate that a man issued an announcement.
The mishna states that <b>ben Bevai</b> was responsible <b>for the shreds</b> of garments. The Gemara explains <b>that he would braid</b> shreds together to prepare <b>wicks</b> for the candelabrum that were of the appropriate thickness, so that they would burn the entire night during each period of the year. The Gemara relates that <b>Rabbi Yosei came to</b> the town of <b>Kufra</b> and <b>wanted to appoint</b> community <b>leaders for them</b> to care for the needs of the community and the provisions of the poor. <b>However,</b> those who were selected <b>would not accept</b> the appointment <b>from them,</b> i.e., from Rabbi Yosei, because they deemed this job beneath their dignity. Rabbi Yosei <b>came and said to them: Ben Bevai,</b> was responsible <b>for</b> the lowly function of <b>the shreds</b> of garments, <b>and if this</b> man, <b>who was appointed</b> to deal <b>with the wicks, merited to be listed with the greatest of that generation, you, who are appointed for life-sustaining</b> matters, <b>all the more so</b> are you <b>not</b> honored by the position? You should therefore accept the request without hesitation.
 The mishna further taught that <b>ben Arza</b> was responsible <b>for the cymbal.</b> The Gemara explains that this is referring to <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Tamid</i> 33b): When the High Priest bent over to pour the wine libation, the <b>deputy</b> High Priest <b>waved the scarves</b> as a signal <b>and ben Arza beat upon the cymbal.</b>
 The mishna states that <b>Hugras ben Levi</b> was responsible <b>for the song. Rabbi Aḥa said: He knew an extraordinary manner of singing. And they said of Hugras ben Levi that he would make his voice pleasant in song, and when he would stick his thumb into his mouth he could produce several types of music</b> simultaneously, <b>and all of his fellow priests would lurch toward him all at once,</b> from the intensity and the charm of the sound.
§ The mishna mentioned that <b>the house of Garmu</b> was responsible <b>for the preparation of the shewbread,</b> which is the bread baked each week in a special form and displayed for a week on the designated table in the Sanctuary. The Gemara relates a story from a <i>baraita</i> about the house of Garmu and their talent in baking. <b>The house of Garmu was proficient in the preparation of the shewbread and in its removal</b> from the oven without ruining it, <b>but they did not want to teach</b> these skills to others. Therefore, <b>the Sages sent</b> messengers <b>and brought in craftsmen from Alexandria, who were</b> also <b>proficient in the preparation of the shewbread,</b> in an attempt to replace the house of Garmu. <b>However, they were not as proficient as</b> the house of Garmu <b>in its removal</b> from the oven. The complex form of the shewbread rendered it very difficult to remove from the oven without its breaking. The Gemara clarifies the difference between the two manners of preparation. <b>The house of Garmu would ignite</b> the fire <b>within</b> the oven, bake the shewbread in a mold, <b>and remove</b> the bread from the mold only <b>outside</b> the oven, after it was fully baked. <b>And,</b> as they would remove it from the mold at this late stage, <b>it would not spoil. Whereas these</b> Egyptian craftsmen <b>would ignite</b> the fire <b>within</b> the oven and bake the shewbread in the mold, <b>but,</b> out of fear that it would break when removed from the mold, they would <b>remove</b> it from the mold <b>inside</b> the oven before it was fully baked. <b>And</b> the result was that the bread <b>would spoil.</b> <b>Once the Sages realized this matter,</b> that their replacement shewbread was flawed, <b>they said: Everything that the Holy One, Blessed be He, created, He created for His glory, as it is stated: “The Lord has made everything for His own purpose”</b> (Proverbs 16:4). Since spoiled shewbread does not promote the glory of God, we must forgo our honor and request the services of the house of Garmu once again. Therefore, the Sages <b>sent for</b> the house of Garmu, <b>but they did not want to come</b> back to bake <b>until</b> the Sages <b>doubled their compensation. They had been receiving twelve <i>maneh</i>, and</b> now <b>they gave them twenty-four. Rabbi Yehuda says: They had been receiving twenty-four</b> <i>maneh</i>, <b>and</b> now <b>they gave them forty-eight.</b> The Sages <b>said to them: For what</b> reason <b>don’t you want to teach</b> the art of shewbread baking to others? The house of Garmu <b>said to them: We have a tradition from our fathers that this House,</b> the Temple, <b>will be destroyed.</b> We do not want to teach our method, so <b>that others should not learn</b> this art and unscrupulously <b>perform</b> it <b>for their idolatry.</b> Although their reason for refraining from teaching their method to others was not accepted, <b>on this</b> other <b>matter</b> the Sages <b>did mention them with praise: That refined bread was never found in their children’s possession at all,</b> so <b>that</b> people <b>should not say</b> that <b>they feast upon their shewbread labor.</b>
The mishna states that <b>the house of Avtinas</b> was responsible <b>for the preparation of the incense.</b> The Gemara relates a similar story from a <i>baraita</i> concerning the house of Avtinas and their knowledge of the incense preparation: <b>The house of Avtinas was proficient in the preparations of the incense mixture,</b> which included grinding the incense herbs and blending them together. And they were also expert in identifying the so-called <b>smoke raiser,</b> a plant that caused the incense smoke to rise upward in a straight line to the ceiling, <b>but they did not want to teach</b> these skills to others. Therefore, the Sages <b>sent</b> out messengers <b>and brought in craftsmen from Alexandria in Egypt. And</b> these craftsmen <b>were proficient in the incense mixture, but they were not proficient in</b> identifying <b>the smoke raiser.</b> The incense smoke <b>of the house of Avtinas would rise straight up like a staff</b> to the ceiling, <b>and</b> from there it would <b>spread out</b> and <b>descend, whereas</b> the incense smoke <b>of these</b> Egyptian craftsmen <b>would</b> not rise but <b>would</b> simply <b>spread out immediately.</b> <b>Once the Sages realized this matter,</b> that this incense smoke was flawed, <b>they said: Everything that the Holy One, Blessed be He, created, He created for His glory, as it is stated: “Everything that is called by My name, and I have created for My glory,</b> I have formed it and even made it” (Isaiah 43:7). Since incense smoke that rises straight up is superior, we must forgo our honor and request the services of the house of Avtinas once again. The Sages <b>sent for them, but they did not want to come back until they doubled their compensation. They had been receiving twelve <i>maneh</i>, and</b> now <b>they gave them twenty-four. Rabbi Yehuda says: They had been receiving twenty-four</b> <i>maneh</i>, <b>and</b> now <b>they gave them forty-eight.</b> The Sages <b>said to them: For what</b> reason <b>don’t you want to teach</b> the art of incense preparation to others? The house of Avtinas <b>said to them: We have a tradition from our fathers that this House,</b> the Temple, <b>will be destroyed.</b> We do not want to teach our method, so <b>that others should not learn</b> these skills <b>and prepare this</b> incense <b>for their idolatry.</b> Although their reason for refraining from teaching their method to others was not accepted, <b>on these</b> other <b>matters</b> the Sages <b>did mention them with praise: That never did the wife of</b> any <b>one of them leave</b> her house <b>perfumed; and not only that, but when one of them would marry a woman from elsewhere, he would stipulate with her</b> that the marriage was <b>on condition that she</b> may <b>not perfume herself,</b> so <b>that</b> people <b>should not say</b> that <b>they perfume themselves from the preparation of the incense mixture.</b> They did so <b>to fulfill that which is stated: “Then you shall be clear before the Lord and before Israel”</b> (Numbers 32:22).
<b>Rabbi Yosei said: Once I was standing in Jerusalem, and I found a certain child from the house of Avtinas. I said to him: My son, from which family are you? He said to me:</b> I am <b>from a particular family,</b> i.e., the house of Avtinas. <b>I said to him: My son,</b> I will say this about <b>your forefathers: Since they intended to increase their own glory</b> by ensuring that none were as proficient as they at preparing incense and by demanding double their previous wages, <b>and</b> they sought <b>to reduce</b> of <b>the glory of Heaven</b> by taking money from the Temple coffers, <b>therefore</b> they were punished and ultimately <b>their glory was diminished,</b> as after the destruction of the Temple they lost their importance. <b>But the glory of Heaven increased,</b> for God’s honor is not dependent upon the existence of the Temple. <b>Rabbi Akiva said: Shimon ben Loga told me:</b> Once <b>I and a certain child from the house of Avtinas were collecting herbs, and I saw him crying, and</b> later <b>I saw him laughing. I said to him: My son, why did you cry? He said to me:</b> I cried <b>for the glory of</b> my <b>father’s house, which has been diminished</b> after the destruction of the Temple. I subsequently asked him: <b>And why did you laugh? He said to me:</b> I laughed with joy <b>over the glory prepared for the righteous in the future,</b> when my family will have its role restored to them in the rebuilt Temple. Shimon ben Loga added that he asked that child further: <b>And what did you see</b> that brought these things to mind?
