He replied: I saw the <b>smoke raiser before me,</b> among the herbs we were collecting. <b>I said to him: My son, show it to me,</b> and I will keep its identity secret so that no one will be able to use it for idolatry. <b>He said to me: Rabbi, I have a tradition from my forefathers not to show it to a soul.</b> Similarly, <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri said:</b> Once <b>I and a certain elderly man were collecting herbs. I encountered a certain elderly man from the house of Avtinas with a scroll in his hand</b> with instructions on how to identify <b>herbs</b> for the Temple incense and how to prepare it. That elderly man <b>said to me: Rabbi, in the past</b> the members of my <b>father’s house were discreet [<i>tzenu’in</i>]</b> and cautious. <b>And they would</b> <sup><b>[14b]</b></sup> <b>transfer this scroll</b> only <b>to one another,</b> so that it would not come into the possession of anyone who might misuse its information. <b>And now that</b> the members of my <b>father’s house are not reliable,</b> I am concerned that they might pass the scroll to the wrong person. I will not give it to one of them but only to someone whom I judge to be completely trustworthy. Therefore, <b>here</b> it is <b>for you,</b> as I can trust you to take <b>the scroll and be careful with it,</b> so that it will not reach anyone who might misuse its information. Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri continued: <b>And when I came and recounted these matters to Rabbi Akiva, his eyes shed tears, and he said: From now on, we should not mention</b> the members of the house of Avtinas <b>unfavorably,</b> as it is evident that the only reason they did not teach their skills to others was due to the fear that someone might misuse them for idolatrous purposes.
 The mishna states that <b>Elazar</b> was responsible <b>for the</b> Temple <b>curtains.</b> The Gemara explains <b>that</b> this means <b>he was</b> the <b>supervisor of</b> the Temple <b>curtain weavers.</b> The mishna further teaches that <b>Pineḥas</b> was <b>the valet.</b> The Gemara states <b>that he would dress</b> the High Priest in <b>the High Priestly vestments.</b> The expertise of those appointed to this position was so great that there was <b>an incident involving a certain</b> valet <b>priest who dressed a certain</b> Roman <b>army officer [<i>isteratiyot</i>], and</b> the officer was so impressed with the manner in which he dressed him that <b>he gave him eight gold coins. And some say he gave him twelve</b> gold coins.

Halakhah 2

 <strong>Halakha 2 · MISHNA</strong> <b>There must be no fewer than seven trustees [<i>amarkolin</i>] and three treasurers</b> appointed over the Temple administration. <b>And we do not appoint an authority over the public</b> comprised <b>of fewer than two</b> people, <b>except for ben Aḥiyya, who</b> was responsible <b>for</b> healing priests who suffered from <b>intestinal disease, and Elazar, who</b> was responsible <b>for</b> the weaving <b>of the</b> Temple <b>curtains.</b> The reason for these exceptions is <b>that the majority of the public accepted these</b> men <b>upon themselves</b> as officials who served without the assistance of even a single partner.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna states that <b>there must be no fewer three treasurers and seven trustees.</b> The Gemara states that <b>it was</b> likewise <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that there must be no fewer <b>than two executive supervisors [<i>katalikin</i>].</b> <b>This is as it is written</b> in the verse that lists the men who supervised the receipt of <i>teruma</i> and tithes from the public and their distribution to the priests and the Levites, as well as the receipt of items dedicated to the Temple: <b>“And Jehiel, and Azaziah, and Nahath,”</b> these were the <b>treasurers; “and Asahel, and Jerimoth, and Jozabad, and Eliel, and Ismachiah, and Mahath, and Benaiah,”</b> these were the <b>trustees; “overseers under the hand of Conaniah and Shimei his brother,”</b> these were <b>executive supervisors; “by the appointment of Hezekiah the king and Azariah the ruler of the House of God,”</b> they were <b>the king and</b> the <b>High Priest,</b> the most senior officials (II Chronicles 31:13).
These officials participated in the supervision of the Temple assets, according to the level and status of their positions. <b>When he would seal</b> an item to store in the treasury until it would be needed, the <b>treasurer</b> would first <b>seal</b> it with his own seal; <b>and</b> subsequently <b>give it to a trustee;</b> the <b>trustee</b> would <b>seal</b> it <b>and give it to an executive supervisor;</b> the <b>executive supervisor</b> would <b>seal</b> it <b>and give it to the High Priest;</b> and the <b>High Priest</b> would <b>seal</b> it <b>and give it to the king,</b> who would place the final seal. <b>And when</b> a treasurer wanted to <b>release</b> an item for usage, the reverse order was followed: First <b>the king</b> would <b>examine his seal and release</b> the restriction on the item; the <b>High Priest</b> would <b>examine his seal and release</b> it; the <b>executive supervisor</b> would <b>examine his seal and release</b> it; the <b>trustee</b> would <b>examine his seal and release</b> it; and finally the <b>treasurer</b> would <b>examine his seal and release</b> it.
 The mishna states that <b>we do not appoint an authority over the public, in monetary matters,</b> comprised <b>of fewer than two</b> appointees. <b>Rav Naḥman</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Mana:</b> This principle is derived <b>on the basis of</b> a verse that is referring to those engaged in building the Tabernacle and weaving the priestly vestments, who received the community’s donations: <b>“And they shall take the gold, and the sky blue wool, and the purple wool”</b> (Exodus 28:5). The plural “they” indicates that this authority must consist of at least two people.
 <b>Rabbi Ḥama, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, said:</b> Although Moses supervised the building of the Tabernacle, he took nothing for himself from the community donations, as <b>Moses grew wealthy</b> only <b>from the shavings</b> that broke off during the chiseling process <b>of</b> the second <b>tablets. This is as it is written: “Hew for yourself two tablets of stone”</b> (Exodus 34:1). “<b>Hew [<i>pesol</i>] for yourself”</b> indicates that <b>the shavings [<i>pesolet</i>] shall be yours. Rabbi Ḥanin said: The Holy One, Blessed be He, created a quarry of precious stones and pearls for</b> Moses <b>inside his tent, and Moses grew wealthy from that</b> source.
<b>It is written:</b> “And it came to pass when Moses went out to the Tent, that all the people rose up, and stood, every man at his tent door, <b>and looked after Moses, until he had gone into the Tent”</b> (Exodus 33:8). <b>Two <i>amora’im</i></b> interpreted this verse with regard to Moses’ wealth, but in opposite ways. <b>One said:</b> The people looked at Moses <b>unfavorably; while the other said:</b> The people looked at Moses <b>favorably.</b> <b>He who said</b> that the people looked at Moses <b>unfavorably</b> maintains that they said: <b>Look at</b> his <b>legs, look at</b> his <b>feet, look at</b> his <b>flesh.</b> See how healthy they are. All that <b>he eats</b> comes <b>from</b> us <b>Jews,</b> all that <b>he drinks</b> comes <b>from</b> us <b>Jews,</b> and <b>all</b> of <b>his assets</b> are taken <b>from</b> us <b>Jews. And the one who said</b> that they looked at Moses <b>favorably</b> maintains that the people were not responding to Moses’ wealth but simply wanted to see the righteous man, saying: We desire to <b>view the righteous, and one who merits to view</b> Moses <b>earns great</b> rewards.

Halakhah 3

<strong>Halakha 3 · MISHNA</strong> This mishna provides details of the functions performed by Yoḥanan ben Pineḥas and Aḥiyya, the officials mentioned in the first mishna of this chapter, which concerns the seals and libations. <b>There were four seals in the Temple</b> that confirmed that the bearer had paid for the libations that accompanied his offering. <b>And</b> one of the following inscriptions was <b>written on them: Calf; male,</b> i.e., a ram; <b>kid; and sinner,</b> i.e., a leper, as leprosy is a punishment for one of seven sins (see <i>Arakhin</i> 16a). Conversely, <b>ben Azzai says: There were five</b> seals, <b>and</b> the following was <b>written upon them</b> in <b>Aramaic,</b> not Hebrew: <b>Calf, male, kid, poor sinner, and rich sinner.</b> The mishna explains the significance of each of the four aforementioned seals. The <b>calf</b> seal <b>serves</b> as a payment receipt <b>for libations of cattle</b> offerings, whether they are <b>large or small, male or female,</b> as all offerings from the cow family are accompanied by the same libation. The <b>kid</b> seal <b>serves for libations of sheep</b> or goat offerings, whether <b>large or small, male or female, except for those of rams</b> aged thirteen months and older. The <b>ram</b> seal, which was earlier called the male seal, <b>serves exclusively for ram libations.</b> The <b>sinner</b> seal <b>serves for libations of the three animal</b> offerings <b>of a leper,</b> for the completion of his purification. <b>One who seeks libations</b> for his offering <b>goes to Yoḥanan,</b> the official <b>who was responsible for the seals, and gives him</b> the appropriate sum of <b>money and receives a seal from him.</b> With that seal <b>he</b> subsequently <b>comes to Aḥiyya, who was responsible for the libations, and gives him</b> Yoḥanan’s <b>seal and receives</b> his <b>libations from him.</b> <b>In the evening,</b> Yoḥanan and Aḥiyya <b>would get together</b> to reconcile their accounts, <b>and Aḥiyya would take out the seals</b> he had received <b>and accept the money</b> Yoḥanan had received <b>in exchange for them. If</b> the money <b>was less</b> than the value of the seals, <b>they were less to him,</b> i.e., Yoḥanan would bear the loss, <b>and Yoḥanan would</b> have to <b>pay</b> the difference to the Temple treasury <b>from his own</b> property. <b>And if</b> there was some money <b>left over,</b> i.e., the total money was greater than the value of the seals, <b>they</b> were <b>left over to</b> the benefit of <b>the Temple treasury of consecrated property, as the Temple treasury</b> always <b>has the upper hand.</b> With regard to <b>one who lost his seal</b> that he purchased from Yoḥanan, Yoḥanan and <b>Aḥiyya would wait to</b> resolve <b>his</b> problem <b>until the evening. And</b> when they added their accounts in the evening, <b>if they found for him</b> a surplus of money <b>equivalent to</b> the value of <b>his seal, they would give him</b> the corresponding libations. <b>And if not, they would not give him</b> libations. <b>And the name of the day</b> of the week <b>was written upon</b> the seals <b>because of the cheats.</b> They might try to use old seals that had been lost by the Temple officials or by someone who had brought an offering at an earlier date, so as to receive libations in a deceitful manner.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>ben Azzai, why</b> was it necessary to have a seal labeled <b>poor sinner?</b> The Gemara answers: Ben Azzai maintains that a poor leper <b>would</b> have to <b>bring his <i>log</i></b> of oil <b>with him.</b> This is referring to the additional oil required for the leper’s purification (Leviticus 14:15–18), some of which is sprinkled on the altar while the rest is applied to the leper’s ear, thumb, big toe, and head. According to ben Azzai, a leper would purchase this additional oil from Aḥiyya, and therefore he required a separate seal for each <i>log</i> of oil. <b>However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> a poor leper <b>brings</b> only the <b>kid</b> seal purchased from Yoḥanan, by which he receives the oil for his meal offering, and he must use his own private oil for the additional <i>log</i>. The mishna mentions only the libations for kids, referring to both sheep and goats that are less than one year old, and for rams, which are adult male sheep. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> amounts are brought for <b>the libations of a lamb,</b> i.e., a female sheep in its second year? Are they the same as those of a kid or a ram? The Gemara answers that this <i>halakha</i> may be inferred <b>from that which we learned</b> in the mishna, that the <b>kid</b> seal <b>serves for libations of sheep</b> offerings, whether <b>large or small, male or female. That</b> ruling <b>says,</b> i.e., indicates, that <b>the libations of a lamb are like the libations of a kid.</b> The Gemara adds that this is derived from a verse that equates the two libations, <b>as it is written: “Thus shall it be done for each bull, or for each ram, or for each of the lambs, or of the kids”</b> (Numbers 15:11). The Gemara inquires further: <b>Why is it stated: For each bull?</b> The verse <b>teaches that</b> The Torah <b>does not differentiate between the libations of a calf,</b> which is less than a year old, <b>and the libations of a bull,</b> which is at least one year old. The Gemara explains: This is <b>because it was fitting</b> to differentiate between them, in light of the fact that both <b>the offspring of a sheep requires libations and the offspring of a cow requires libations. If we find that</b> the Torah <b>differentiates between the libations of a sheep</b> in its first year, whose meal offering is one <i>isaron</i> of fine flour, <b>and the libations of a ram,</b> a male sheep thirteen months old and older, whose meal-offering is two <i>isaron</i>, <sup><b>[15a]</b></sup> <b>then we</b> should likewise <b>differentiate between the libations of a calf and the libations of a bull.</b> In order to indicate that this is not the case, <b>the</b> aforementioned <b>verse states: “Thus shall it be done for each bull,”</b> which <b>teaches that</b> the Torah <b>does not differentiate between the libations of a calf and the libations of a bull.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Why does</b> the verse <b>state: “Or for</b> each <b>ram,”</b> as the details of the ram libations were specified in the previous verses (Numbers 15:6–7)? The Gemara explains that this verse is necessary <b>because it would be fitting</b> to argue as follows: <b>If we have found that</b> the Torah <b>differentiated between the libations of a one-year-old</b> sheep <b>and the libations of a two-year-old</b> sheep, i.e., a sheep in its second year, which is already called a ram, <b>then we</b> should <b>differentiate between the libations of a</b> two-year-old <b>and the libations of a</b> three-year-old ram. In order to indicate that this is not the case, <b>the verse states: “Or for each ram,”</b> which teaches that the libations for rams of all ages are identical. The Gemara similarly asks: <b>Why does</b> the verse <b>state: “Or for each of the lambs,”</b> as the details of the lamb libations were provided in the previous verses (Numbers 15:4–5)? The Gemara explains that this phrase is necessary <b>because it would be fitting</b> to argue as follows: <b>If we have found that</b> the Torah <b>differentiated between the libations of a lamb</b> in its first year <b>and the libations of ram</b> that is thirteen months or older, <b>then we</b> should likewise <b>differentiate between the libations of</b> a full-grown <b>ewe</b> in its second year <b>and the libations of a female lamb</b> in its first year. In order to indicate that this is not the case, <b>the verse states: “Or for each of the lambs,”</b> which teaches that the libations for female sheep of all ages are identical. The Gemara continues: <b>Why does</b> the verse <b>state: “Or of the goats,”</b> which are also part of the sheep family? The Gemara explains that this phrase is necessary <b>because it would be fitting</b> to argue as follows: <b>If we have found that</b> the Torah <b>differentiated between the libations of a lamb</b> in its first year <b>and the libations of a ram</b> that is thirteen months or old, <b>then we</b> should likewise <b>differentiate between the libations of a kid,</b> a young goat, <b>and the libations of a full-grown goat.</b> In order to indicate that this is not the case, <b>the verse states: “Or of the kids,”</b> and thereby <b>juxtaposes</b> the libations for <b>the youngest of the kids and</b> those of <b>the oldest of the goats: Just as</b> the libations of <b>this</b> young kid consist <b>of three <i>log</i></b> of wine, <b>so too,</b> the libations of <b>that</b> full-grown goat consist <b>of three <i>log</i>.</b>
The mishna states: <b>And the name of the day</b> of the week <b>was written</b> upon the seals, to deter cheats. The Gemara points out a problem: <b>Consider</b> the following situation <b>yourself.</b> Suppose <b>that</b> a cheat found a seal and circumvented this precaution by waiting until the same day the next week, at which point he could <b>match up [<i>ziyyeig</i>]</b> his seal with <b>that day.</b> He would be able to collect the libations deceitfully a week later. The Gemara answers: <b>The name of</b> the priestly <b>watch</b> that served in the Temple that week <b>was</b> also <b>written on it,</b> as each watch serves in the Temple only twice a year. The Gemara retorts that even this is not a sufficient precaution. After all, <b>consider</b> for <b>yourself that</b> a cheat can wait half a year and <b>match up</b> with <b>that watch</b> and collect the libations. The Gemara explains that <b>the name of the day; the name,</b> i.e., the number, <b>of the week</b> within the month; and <b>the name of the month were</b> all <b>written on it.</b> Consequently, <b>even if</b> a cheat <b>wanted to match up</b> the seal with a similar day, <b>there is no</b> identical day to be <b>found</b> with which <b>to match</b> it <b>up.</b>

Halakhah 4

<strong>Halakha 4 · MISHNA</strong> <b>There were two</b> special <b>chambers in the Temple, one</b> called <b>the chamber of secret</b> gifts <b>and</b> the other <b>one</b> called <b>the chamber of vessels.</b> The mishna explains the purpose of these chambers. In <b>the chamber of secret</b> gifts, <b>sin-fearing people put</b> money <b>secretly and poor people of noble descent support themselves from it secretly.</b> With regard to <b>the chamber of vessels, anyone who donates a vessel</b> to the Temple <b>drops it inside</b> that chamber, <b>and once every thirty days the treasurers open it. And any vessel that they found for it a use for Temple maintenance, they leave it</b> for that purpose, <b>and the rest are sold, and their monetary</b> value is <b>allocated to Temple maintenance.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> Apropos the topic of charity given anonymously, the Gemara cites several stories on that theme and the mitzva of charity in general. <b>Rabbi Ya’akov bar Idi and Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Naḥman were supporters</b> of the indigent, i.e., they were responsible for a charity fund. <b>And</b> so as not to embarrass the poor, <b>they would give a dinar to Rabbi Ḥama, father of Rabbi Yehoshaya, and he would give it to</b> impoverished <b>others</b> surreptitiously, so that neither the givers nor the receivers were aware of one another’s identity. The Gemara similarly relates with regard to <b>Rabbi Zekharya, son-in-law of Rabbi Levi, that everyone would malign him. They would say that he does not need</b> charity, <b>and</b> yet <b>he takes</b> money from the charity fund. <b>After he died, they investigated and found that he had</b> discreetly <b>been distributing</b> all the charity money he received <b>to others</b> in actual need.
<b>Rabbi Ḥinnana bar Pappa would distribute mitzva</b> money, i.e., charity, <b>at night. Once the leader of</b> the evil <b>spirits encountered him.</b> The evil spirit <b>said to him: Didn’t the rabbi,</b> i.e., you, <b>teach</b> that the verse: <b>“You shall not trespass upon your neighbor’s border”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:14) indicates that one should not enter the domain of another, and the night belongs to evil spirits? Rabbi Ḥinnana, <b>said to him: And isn’t it written</b> elsewhere: <b>“A gift in secret pacifies anger”</b> (Proverbs 21:14)? Since I am busy giving secret charity, I have no fear that the Divine Wrath will permit evil spirits to harm me. <b>And</b> upon hearing this, the evil spirit <b>grew afraid of him and fled from his presence.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yona said: Happy is he who gives to the poor, is not written here; rather: “Happy is he who considers the poor”</b> (Psalms 41:2) is written, which indicates that one must consider his actions carefully and act wisely in giving charity. <b>This</b> is referring to one <b>who scrutinizes the mitzva</b> of charity and considers <b>how to perform it</b> in the most appropriate manner to avoid embarrassing the poor. <b>How would Rabbi Yona</b> himself <b>behave when he would see a poor person of noble descent, who</b> was once wealthy but <b>had lost his assets?</b> Rabbi Yona wanted to assist him but feared he would be too ashamed to accept charity. <b>He would say to him: My son, since I heard that you have come into an inheritance in another place, take</b> this money now <b>and you</b> can <b>pay</b> it back when you receive the inheritance. <b>When</b> the poor person <b>would take</b> the money from him <b>he would tell him:</b> This money <b>is a gift to you,</b> and you do not have to repay it.
 The Gemara cites a story involving people who did not want to receive charity from others, but preferred to rely on God’s bounty. <b>Ḥiyya bar Adda</b> related: <b>In our days, there were</b> some <b>elderly people</b> who acted as follows. If <b>someone would give them</b> charity <b>between Rosh HaShana and the Great Fast,</b> i.e., Yom Kippur, <b>they would accept</b> it, but <b>after that period they would not accept</b> it. <b>They would say:</b> The money <b>that</b> is <b>for this year</b> is already <b>with us,</b> i.e., our income for the entire year has already been decreed between Rosh HaShana and Yom Kippur (see <i>Beitza</i> 16a).
 <b>Neḥemya the well digger encountered a certain Jerusalemite,</b> who <b>said to him: Give me one chicken</b> as charity. Neḥemya <b>said to him: Here is the</b> monetary <b>value of a piece of meat for you,</b> as meat was cheaper than chicken. <b>And</b> the beggar <b>bought meat</b> with the money he accepted from Neḥemya, <b>and ate</b> it, <b>and died</b> because his constitution was extremely sensitive to meat. <b>And</b> Neḥemya <b>cried out and said: Come and eulogize the one killed by Neḥemya,</b> as he felt guilty over the beggar’s demise.
<b>Naḥum of Gam Zo was bringing a gift to his father-in-law’s house</b> when a man <b>afflicted with boils met him</b> on the way. <b>He said to</b> Naḥum: <b>Give me</b> charity <b>from that which</b> you have brought <b>with you.</b> Naḥum <b>said to him: When I return,</b> I will give you something. After visiting his father-in-law’s house, Naḥum <b>returned and found</b> the man <b>dead.</b> He realized that when he had first met him, this boil-afflicted man must have been on the verge of death from starvation. <b>And</b> in his guilt over failing to provide immediate relief for his hunger, Naḥum <b>said about</b> the boil-afflicted man: <b>The eyes that saw you,</b> i.e., my own eyes, <b>and did not give you</b> food <b>should be blinded; the hands that did not stretch forth to give you</b> food <b>should be cut off; the feet that did not run to give you</b> food <b>should be broken. And</b> later all of <b>these</b> calamities actually <b>befell him.</b> <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> Naḥum’s disciple, <b>came to</b> visit and console <b>him.</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>said to him: Woe is me, that I see you like this.</b> Naḥum <b>said to him: Woe is me, that I do not see you like this.</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>said to him: Why do you curse me?</b> Naḥum <b>said to him: And why do you reject suffering?</b> You should not do so, as the Sages have said that suffering atones for sins like the sacrifice of offerings (<i>Mekhilta</i> of Rabbi Yishmael).
<b>Rabbi Hoshaya the Great was the master,</b> i.e., the teacher, <b>of the son of a certain blind man and was accustomed to eat with</b> the blind man <b>every day. Once</b> Rabbi Hoshaya <b>had guests, and he</b> did not <b>invite</b> the blind man to <b>eat with him. In the evening,</b> Rabbi Hoshaya <b>went up to</b> visit the blind man and <b>said to him:</b> I request that <b>my master,</b> i.e., you, <b>not be angry with me, as I had guests today. I</b> therefore <b>said</b> to myself that I will not invite you <b>today,</b> so <b>as not to demean my master’s dignity.</b> You sometimes spill food on yourself and they might have mocked you. <b>For this reason, I did not eat with my master today.</b> The blind man <b>said to him:</b> Since <b>you appeased one who is seen but does not see, that</b> Holy <b>One, Who sees but is not seen, should accept your appeasement,</b> i.e., God should accept your prayers. Rabbi Hoshaya <b>said to him: From where</b> did <b>you</b> hear <b>that</b> metaphorical statement? The blind man <b>replied:</b> I learned that idea <b>from</b> a story involving <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov</b> and a blind man. <b>As</b> it is told <b>of Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov that a blind man</b> once <b>came to his city,</b> and <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov sat below him,</b> so <b>that</b> the people of the city <b>would say that if he,</b> i.e., the blind man, <b>were not a great man, Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov would not have sat below him.</b> When the people of the city saw this, <b>they gave</b> the blind man a very <b>respectable livelihood.</b> The blind man <b>asked them: What is this</b> situation? Why are you paying me such a high salary? <b>They said to him:</b> Since <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov sat below you,</b> we honor you. <b>And</b> the blind man <b>prayed this prayer with regard to</b> Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov: <b>You rendered kindness to one who is seen but does not see.</b> Therefore, <b>that</b> Holy <b>One, Who sees but is not seen, should render kindness to you.</b>
<b>Rabbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina and Rabbi Hoshaya were</b> once <b>touring the synagogues of Lod. Rabbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina said to Rabbi Hoshaya: How much money my forefathers invested</b> in building synagogues <b>here!</b> Rabbi Hoshaya <b>said to him: How many souls your forefathers invested</b> in building synagogues <b>here!</b> The money they spent actually harmed people spiritually rather than helping them. Rabbi Hoshaya explained his comment: <b>Weren’t there people who would have exerted themselves in</b> the study of <b>Torah</b> if only the money used for the construction of these buildings had been donated to them instead?
In another case, <b>Rabbi Avun</b> donated money for <b>building the gates</b> <sup><b>[15b]</b></sup> <b>of the great study hall.</b> When <b>Rabbi Mana came to</b> visit <b>him,</b> Rabbi Avun, proud of his donation for this building, <b>said to him: Look at what I did</b> and the greatness of my contribution. Rabbi Mana <b>said to him:</b> Your attitude reminds me of the verse: <b>“For Israel has forgotten his Maker, and built palaces”</b> (Hosea 8:14). How could you focus upon the gates? <b>Weren’t there people who would have exerted themselves in</b> the study of <b>Torah</b> if they only had the money? You could have given them that money instead of using it to build structures.
§ With regard to the vessels donated for the Temple maintenance, which are mentioned in the mishna, the Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> in which <b>it was taught:</b> If the shekels collected for the purchase of articles <b>consecrated for the altar,</b> i.e., communal offerings, are not enough to purchase them and the items that accompanied them, e.g., their libations, the funds <b>required for</b> purchasing the remainder <b>are taken from the funds consecrated for Temple maintenance.</b> However, the converse is not the case: If there is a need for <b>funds consecrated to Temple maintenance, that which is required for it is not taken from funds consecrated for the altar.</b> The reason for this difference is that an item may be changed from a lesser to a greater sanctity, but not vice versa. The Gemara notes an apparent contradiction. <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna: <b>A vessel that they found for it a use for Temple maintenance, they leave it</b> for that purpose; <b>and the rest are sold, and their monetary</b> value <b>is allocated to Temple maintenance.</b> This statement indicates that this money could be used only for Temple maintenance, not for items consecrated for the altar. This contradicts the <i>baraita</i> cited above. In resolution of this contradiction, <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya said: This is</b> the intent of <b>the mishna:</b> Donated items and, likewise, any money received as payment for sale of an item are physically taken <b>to the chamber for the Temple maintenance.</b> From there they may be removed for any Temple purpose, either for the maintenance of the Temple or for articles consecrated for the altar. 

Chapter 6



Halakhah 1

<strong>Halakha 1 · MISHNA</strong> <b>There were thirteen</b> collection <b>horns,</b> narrow at the top and wide at the bottom, into which were placed the shekels that were collected for the various needs of the Temple. There <b>were</b> also <b>thirteen tables</b> for various purposes, and <b>thirteen prostrations in the Temple.</b> The members <b>of the household of Rabban Gamliel and</b> the members <b>of the household of Rabbi Ḥananya, the deputy High Priest, would prostrate</b> themselves <b>in fourteen</b> places. <b>And where was</b> this <b>extra</b> location? It was <b>facing the wood depository, as there was a tradition handed</b> down <b>to them from their fathers that the Ark was sequestered there.</b> The mishna relates that there was <b>an incident involving a certain priest who was going about</b> his duties <b>and saw</b> a certain <b>flagstone that was different from the others.</b> He noticed that one of the stones was slightly raised above the others, indicating that it had been removed and returned to its place. The priest understood that this was the opening to an underground tunnel where the Ark was concealed. <b>He came and said to his fellow</b> that he had noticed this deviation in the floor. <b>He did not manage to conclude</b> relating <b>the incident before his soul left him,</b> i.e., he died. Following this event, <b>they knew with certainty that the Ark was sequestered there</b> and that God had prevented that priest from revealing its location.
 <strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Those</b> collection <b>horns were asymmetrical.</b> They were <b>narrow at the top and wide at the bottom.</b> Why were they shaped like that? It was <b>due to cheaters,</b> to prevent them from inserting their hands on the pretense of adding shekels and removing them instead.
The Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> that expounds a tannaitic dispute with regard to the sequestering of the Ark, a topic mentioned in the mishna: <b>It was taught in the name of Rabbi Elazar: The Ark was exiled with</b> the Jews <b>to Babylonia.</b> When the Jews were exiled to Babylonia, they took the Ark with them. <b>What is the source</b> for this statement? When Isaiah prophesied about the exile before King Hezekiah, he stated: “Behold, the days come, that all that is in your house, and that which your fathers have stored up until this day, shall be carried to Babylon; <b>no item shall be left, said the Lord”</b> (II Kings 20:17). The <b>item [<i>davar</i>]</b> referred to in the verse can <b>only be</b> an item <b>that contains the commandments [<i>dibrot</i>].</b> That is the Ark, which contains the two tablets upon which the Ten Commandments are carved. <b>And, so</b> too, <b>it says: “And at the return of the year, King Nebuchadnezzar sent, and brought him to Babylon, with the desired vessels of the House of the Lord,</b> and made Zedekiah his brother king over Judah and Jerusalem” (II Chronicles 36:10). <b>What is</b> referred to by the phrase <b>“the desired vessels of the House of the Lord”? This is the Ark,</b> in which the Tablets of the Law were placed. The Torah is a desirable object for the Jews, as it is stated, “More to be desired are they than gold, indeed, than much fine gold” (Psalms 19:11). <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish</b> differs and <b>says: The Ark</b> was not sent into exile, but rather <b>was sequestered in its place,</b> i.e., buried in the Holy of Holies. <b>This is as it is written: “And the staves were so long that the ends of the staves were seen from the holy place, even before the Sanctuary; but they could not be seen without;</b> and there they are until this day” (I Kings 8:8). The phrase “until this day” means forever, as “this day” can refer to any point in time. Consequently, this verse indicates that the Ark was sequestered in its place. Having cited this verse, the Gemara proceeds to further explain it, by pointing out an internal contradiction in the verse. Initially, <b>it is written:</b> “The ends of the staves <b>were seen,” and you say,</b> in the continuation of the verse, <b>“but they could not be seen.”</b> How can that be? <b>Rather,</b> the verse should be understood as follows: The staves <b>could be seen and</b> they <b>could not be seen, as they protruded outward</b> through the curtain <b>like the two breasts of a woman.</b> The staves themselves could not be seen, but their position was discernible from the protrusion of the curtain.
 The Gemara returns to the discussion of the location of the Ark during the Second Temple period. <b>And the Rabbis say: The Ark was sequestered in the wood depository chamber.</b> The Gemara relates that there was <b>an incident involving a certain blemished priest who was standing and splitting wood in the wood depository chamber</b> in order to verify that the wood was not infested with worms. <b>And he saw the flagstone that was different from the others. He came and said to his fellow: Come and see that this flagstone differs from the others. He did not manage to conclude</b> relating <b>the incident before his soul left him. And they knew with certainty that the Ark was sequestered there. Rabbi Hoshaya taught</b> a slightly different version of the story in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>He tapped on</b> the stone <b>with a mallet [<i>kurenas</i>]</b> to determine if it was hollow underneath the stone, <b>and fire came out and burned him.</b>
The Gemara elaborates on the topic of the Ark of the Covenant. <b>It was taught</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda ben Lakish said: Two Arks would travel with Israel in the desert: One, in which the Torah,</b> i.e., the second tablets that were given to Moses after he broke the first ones, <b>was placed, and the other, in which the shards of</b> the first <b>tablets were placed. That in which the Torah was kept,</b> was the Ark that <b>was placed in the Tent of Meeting,</b> in the Holy of Holies. <b>This is as it is written: “And the Ark of the Covenant of the Lord, and Moses, departed not out of the camp”</b> (Numbers 14:44). <b>The one in which the shards of</b> the <b>tablets were placed would embark and return with them</b> when they went to war. Consequently, <b>there were times that it would be seen among them.</b> <b>And the Rabbis say: There was</b> only <b>one Ark,</b> not two, kept in the Holy of Holies. <b>And one time it went out</b> with the nation to battle <b>in the days of Eli and it was captured.</b> The Gemara notes that <b>a verse supports</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis:</b> When the Philistines who captured the Ark saw it, they said: <b>“Woe unto us! Who shall deliver us out of the hand of these mighty gods?”</b> (I Samuel 4:8). It is clear that they were very frightened, and it would seem that this was due to the fact that the Ark was <b>something that they had never seen in all their days.</b> There is also <b>a verse</b> that <b>supports</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yuda ben Lakish</b> that there were two Arks, one that they took with them into battle and one that remained in its place. Apropos the battle of King Saul with the Philistines, the verse states: <b>“And Saul said unto Ahijah: Bring here the Ark of God,</b> for the Ark of God was there at that time with the children of Israel” (I Samuel 14:18). This is puzzling. <b>Wasn’t the Ark in Kiryat Ye’arim</b> at the time (see I Samuel 7:1–2)? Therefore, it is clear that there were two Arks, one that resided in Kiryat Ye’arim and a different one that they took with them to battle. <b>What do the Rabbis do with this</b> verse so that it does not contradict their opinion? The Rabbis would interpret the verse as follows: Saul said, <b>“Bring here”</b> the container that contains the eight priestly garments, among them <b>the frontplate.</b> The verse is referring to this container, not the Ark. A different <b>verse supports</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yuda ben Lakish</b> that there were two Arks: When Uriah explains to King David why he will not return to his house while there is a war with the children of Ammon, he says: <b>“The Ark, and Israel, and Judah, abide in booths;</b> and my lord Joab, and the servants of my lord, are encamped in the open field; shall I then go into my house, to eat and to drink, and to lie with my wife?” (II Samuel 11:11). <sup><b>[16a]</b></sup> <b>But wasn’t the Ark in Zion,</b> i.e., Jerusalem, at the time, and not in a booth at the battlefield? Rather, there must have been two Arks; one remained in Jerusalem and the other went out with the Jews to the battlefield. <b>What do the Rabbis do with this</b> verse? How would they interpret it according to their opinion that there was only one Ark, and it remained in Jerusalem? The word for booth, <i>sukka</i>, is related to the word <b><i>sekhakh</i>, which is</b> temporary <b>roofing</b> for a structure. The verse, in mentioning that the Ark was in a booth, is not implying that it was with the army at the battlefield, but merely that the Ark was in a temporary structure, <b>as the Temple had not yet been built.</b>
§ As the mishna mentions the subject of concealing the Ark, the Gemara cites a further <i>baraita</i> on this topic: <b>When the Ark was sequestered, sequestered along with it was the canister of manna</b> that Moses placed before God to be kept throughout the generations (see Exodus 16:32–34); <b>the flask of the anointing oil; Aaron’s staff with its blossoms and berries,</b> which were placed before the Ark (see Numbers 17:23); <b>and the box that the Philistines returned</b> together with the Ark that was captured during the war, which they filled with golden vessels <b>as a guilt-offering to the God of Israel,</b> as the verse says: “And put the jewels of gold, which you return Him for a guilt-offering, in a coffer by the side thereof” (I Samuel 6:8). <b>Who sequestered</b> the Ark? <b>Josiah,</b> king of Judah, sequestered it. <b>Since he saw that it is written, “The Lord will bring you and your king, whom you shall set over you, to a nation that you have not known, you nor your fathers”</b> (Deuteronomy 28:36), <b>he arose and sequestered it,</b> so that it would not be sent into exile with the Jews. <b>This is as it is written: “And he said unto the Levites who taught all Israel, who were holy unto the Lord: Put the Holy Ark in the house which Solomon the son of David king of Israel did build; there shall no more be a burden upon your shoulders;</b> now serve the Lord your God, and His people Israel” (II Chronicles 35:3). The Gemara explains: Josiah <b>said to</b> the Levites: <b>If</b> the Ark <b>is exiled with you to Babylonia,</b> there is a real concern that <b>you will never return it to its place. Rather,</b> I am concealing it so you will no longer need to carry it. <b>“Now serve the Lord your God, and His people Israel”</b> (II Chronicles 35:3) in the other tasks that are incumbent upon you.
§ Apropos the anointing oil mentioned among the items sequestered with the Ark, the Gemara elaborates: The types and quantities of spices that were used in the <b>blending of the anointing oil</b> are detailed in the verses: <b>“Take you also unto yourself</b> the chief spices, of flowing myrrh five hundred shekels, and of sweet cinnamon half so much, even two hundred and fifty, and of sweet calamus two hundred and fifty, <b>and of cassia [<i>kidda</i>] five hundred,</b> after the shekel of the Sanctuary” (Exodus 30:23–24). All together, <b>they</b> total <b>1,500 <i>maneh</i>.</b> The Gemara explains in a <i>baraita</i> how this was done: <b>“And of olive oil a <i>hin</i>”</b> (Exodus 30:24), this <i>hin</i> equals <b>twelve <i>log</i></b> of oil <b>in which they would cook the roots</b> of the plants mentioned in the verse above, so that the oil would absorb their fragrance; <b>this is the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b> <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> That is not how it was prepared. Rather, the priest who prepared the oil <b>would cook</b> the roots <b>in water,</b> such that the fragrant essence would rise to the top, <b>and</b> he would then <b>place oil on top of</b> the water. <b>When it had</b> completely <b>absorbed the fragrance, he would remove</b> the oil from the water, <b>in the manner that the perfumers prepare</b> fragrant oils. The accepted way of extracting fragrances was to boil the plants in water and then place the oil on top to absorb the fragrance essences. <b>This is as it is written:</b> “And you shall make it <b>a holy anointing oil,</b> a perfume compounded after the art of the perfumer; it shall be a holy anointing oil” (Exodus 30:25). “After the art of the perfumer” indicates that it should be prepared in the manner of those who make such perfumes.
Continuing on the subject of the anointing oil, <b>Rabbi Yehuda, son of Rabbi Elai, taught: The anointing oil that Moses prepared in the desert was a miraculous feat from beginning to end. At the outset, there were only twelve <i>log</i></b> of oil, <b>as it is stated: “And of olive oil a <i>hin</i>,”</b> and a <i>hin</i> is twelve <i>log</i>. <b>If there</b> initially <b>wasn’t</b> even <b>enough</b> oil <b>to pour over the roots, all the more so after the fire absorbs</b> some oil, as it reduces the amount of oil through cooking, <b>and the wood,</b> i.e., the roots from which the fragrant essence was extracted, <b>absorbs</b> some oil, <b>and the pot</b> in which the roots were cooked absorbs some oil as well. <b>Yet from this</b> small amount <b>were anointed the Tabernacle and all its vessels, the table and all its vessels, and the candelabrum and all its vessels. And from that</b> oil <b>Aaron the High Priest and his sons were anointed during all the seven days of consecration, and from that</b> oil <b>the High Priests and kings</b> throughout all the generations <b>were anointed.</b> The <i>baraita</i> goes on to discuss those who were anointed with the anointing oil. <b>A king at the outset,</b> i.e., when his appointment as king begins a dynasty, <b>requires anointing</b> with the anointing oil. <b>A king</b> who is the <b>son of a king,</b> who stands to reign in his father’s place, <b>does not require anointing. What is the source</b> for this ruling? God instructed Samuel with regard to David: <b>“Arise, anoint him; for this is he”</b> (I Samuel 16:12). Only <b>“this”</b> one, i.e., David, <b>requires anointing,</b> as his monarchy begins a dynasty, <b>but his son does not require anointing.</b> In contrast to the anointing of kings, with regard to <b>a High Priest, son of a High Priest, even up to ten generations</b> or more of High Priests of fathers and sons, each one <b>requires anointing,</b> as the position of High Priest is not passed as an inheritance from father to son like the monarchy. Rabbi Yehuda concludes his statement about the miraculous nature of the anointing oil, adding that despite the reduction in the amount of oil during its preparation process, as well as its multiple uses throughout history, <b>it all</b> will remain <b>intact in the future</b> that will surely <b>come. This is as it is written: “This [<i>zeh</i>] shall be a holy anointing oil unto Me throughout your generations”</b> (Exodus 30:31). The word <i>zeh</i> has a numerical value of twelve, which teaches that the original twelve <i>log</i> of oil that existed at the outset will remain throughout all the generations.
§ The Gemara continues the discussion of anointing kings, citing an additional <i>baraita</i> on the subject. <b>One</b> may <b>anoint kings only next to a spring.</b> It is a fortuitous sign that their monarchy should continue uninterrupted just as the waters of the spring flow uninterrupted throughout the year, <b>as it is stated</b> with regard to the coronation of Solomon in the days of King David: “And the king said unto them: Take with you the servants of your lord, <b>and cause Solomon my son to ride upon my own mule, and bring him down to Giḥon. And let Zadok the priest and Nathan the prophet anoint him there king over Israel”</b> (I Kings 1:33–34). From this verse, the Sages learned that all kings should be anointed near a spring, just as David instructed that the anointing of Solomon take place near the Giḥon spring. Another <i>baraita</i> states: <b>One</b> only <b>anoints a king, son of a king, due to a dispute</b> over the monarchy. For example, <b>why was Solomon anointed,</b> despite the fact that he was the son of a king? It was <b>because of the dispute</b> over the throne instigated <b>by</b> his older brother <b>Adonijah,</b> who attempted to usurp the monarchy. Similarly, <b>Joash,</b> son of Ahaziah, was anointed as king (see II Kings 11:12) <b>due to</b> the threat of <b>Athaliah,</b> his paternal grandmother, who attempted to seize the monarchy for herself (II Kings 11:1–3). <b>Jehoahaz,</b> son of Josiah, was anointed (II Kings 23:30) <b>due to</b> the competition from <b>Jehoiakim, his brother, who was older than him by two years.</b> Ordinarily, the older brother should have succeeded their father, but Jehoahaz was more worthy of the throne. <b>Jehu,</b> son of Jehoshaphat, was anointed <b>due to Joram,</b> son of Ahab, who was the incumbent king, and Jehu rebelled against his rule (see II Kings 9:6). The Gemara presents a difficulty concerning the latter example cited previously: Why was Jehu anointed as king? <b>Isn’t it written</b> with regard to the anointing of David: <b>“Arise, anoint him; for this is he”</b> (I Samuel 16:12)? <b>This</b> king, i.e., any king from the house of David, <b>requires anointing, but the kings</b> from the kingdom <b>of Israel,</b> who were not descendants of the house of David, <b>do not require anointing.</b> Jehu, a king of Israel, should not have required anointing. The Gemara presents a counter-question: According to that line of reasoning, there is a further difficulty in the <i>baraita</i>. How can one explain that <b>Jehoahaz</b> was anointed <b>due to</b> the competition from <b>Jehoiakim his brother, who was older than him by two years? Didn’t Josiah,</b> their father, <b>sequester</b> the anointing oil as was explained previously? How then was there oil available to anoint Jehoahaz, his son? Rather, when the <i>baraita</i> says that Jehoahaz was anointed, <b>that is to say</b> that <b>he was anointed with balsam</b> oil and not with the anointing oil. Similarly, one can say that Jehu was also anointed with balsam oil and not with the anointing oil. It was further taught that <b>one</b> may <b>anoint kings only</b> with anointing oil that is poured <b>from a horn,</b> and not any other vessel. <b>Saul and Jehu were anointed from</b> oil poured from an earthenware <b>cruse,</b> and <b>their monarchy was a temporary monarchy. David and Solomon were anointed</b> with oil poured <b>from a horn,</b> and <b>their monarchy was a monarchy that was established</b> for many generations. <b>One</b> may <b>not anoint priests</b> to serve <b>as kings</b> <i>ab initio</i>. <b>Rabbi Yuda Antondraya said</b> that this rule is <b>on account of</b> the verse that states: <b>“The scepter shall not depart from Judah”</b> (Genesis 49:10), i.e., the scepter of monarchy will forever belong to the tribe of Judah and not to any other tribe. Therefore, the priests who are from the tribe of Levi may not be appointed as kings. <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Adda said</b> that this rule is derived from a different verse, as it is written: <b>“To the end that he may prolong his days in his kingdom, he and his children, in the midst of Israel”</b> (Deuteronomy 17:20). <b>What is written</b> in the <b>subsequent</b> verse? <b>“The priests the Levites, even all the tribe of Levi, shall have no</b> portion nor inheritance with Israel” (Deuteronomy 18:1). This implies that the priests will have no part in that which is referred to previously, i.e., the monarchy.
It was taught previously that Jehoahaz was anointed king, despite the fact that his brother Jehoiakim was older. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The one who is called <b>Johanan</b> in the verse, “And the sons of Josiah: The firstborn Johanan, the second Jehoiakim, the third Zedekiah, the fourth Shallum” (I Chronicles 3:15), <b>he</b> is the same as the one called <b>Jehoahaz. But isn’t it written</b> in that verse <b>“the firstborn Johanan,”</b> and it was just stated that Jehoiakim was two years older than Jehoahaz? The Gemara explains that in calling Jehoahaz firstborn, the verse means that he was the <b>first</b> to rise <b>to the monarchy. Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> continued and <b>said: He</b> who is called <b>Shallum</b> is the same as <b>he</b> who is called <b>Zedekiah.</b> The Gemara challenges this statement: <b>But isn’t it written: “The third Zedekiah, the fourth Shallum,”</b> indicating that these are two different people? The Gemara explains the verse is as follows: He was the <b>third in birth position, but he was fourth to</b> achieve <b>kingship,</b> as his two brothers, Jehoahaz and Jehoiakim, as well as his nephew, Jehoiachin, all ruled before him. The Gemara cites an additional <i>baraita</i> that discusses these names: He was called <b>Zedekiah because he accepted [<i>tzideik</i>] upon himself the attribute of judgment</b> when the king of Babylonia had Zedekiah’s sons slaughtered in front of him and then proceeded to have his eyes gouged out. Zedekiah justified the punishment that God had inflicted upon him. He was called <b>Shallum</b> because <b>in his days the kingdom of the house of David was completed [<i>shalma</i>],</b> as he was the last king in King David’s dynasty. <b>Reish Lakish said: His name was not Shallum and his name was not Zedekiah; rather,</b> his name was <b>Mattaniah. This is as it is written: “And the king of Babylon made Mattaniah, his father’s brother, king in his stead and changed his name to Zedekiah”</b> (II Kings 24:17). This verse indicates that the king of Babylonia changed his name to Zedekiah, but it was not his original name.
§ The Gemara turns to a discussion of the measurements of the Ark. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: The Ark was constructed with a cubit of six handbreadths.</b> The unit of measurement used in the Torah to describe the Ark, the cubit, equaled six handbreadths. <b>Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> that holds that the Ark was constructed <b>using cubits of six handbreadths? It is Rabbi Meir, as we learned</b> in a mishna: <b>Rabbi Meir says: All the cubits that</b> are mentioned in the Torah <b>were medium-</b>sized cubits six handbreadths in length. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that <b>cubits</b> used for measuring <b>the building,</b> such as the walls or the gates, were of <b>six</b> handbreadths. However, cubits used for measuring <b>the vessels,</b> such as the Ark, were of <b>five</b> handbreadths. <b>According to the opinion of Rabbi Meir, who said</b> that <b>the Ark was constructed using a cubit of six handbreadths, the length of the Ark was fifteen handbreadths, as it is written: “Two cubits and a half shall be the length thereof”</b> (Exodus 25:10). <b>A cubit is six</b> handbreadths, <b>and</b> another <b>cubit is six</b> handbreadths, <b>and a half-cubit is three</b> handbreadths. This totals fifteen handbreadths. <b>And four tablets were</b> placed <b>in</b> the Ark, <b>two</b> tablets were <b>whole and two</b> tablets were <b>broken, as it is written:</b> “And I will write on the tablets the words that were on the first tablets <b>which you did break, and you shall put them in the Ark”</b> (Deuteronomy 10:2). The juxtaposition of these phrases teaches that the broken tablets were also placed in the Ark. <b>The length of each one of the tablets was six handbreadths, and its width was six handbreadths. Lay the length of the tablets across the length of the Ark,</b> and <b>three handbreadths remain there.</b> The length of the Ark was fifteen handbreadths, and the length of the two tablets together was twelve handbreadths; there were three remaining handbreadths available at the end of the Ark. <sup><b>[16b]</b></sup> <b>Give,</b> i.e., subtract, <b>from</b> those remaining three handbreadths <b>a half-handbreadth for</b> the width of <b>each wall</b> of the Ark. <b>There remain two handbreadths</b> in which to place <b>the Torah scroll</b> that Moses wrote (see <i>Bava Batra</i> 14a). <b>The width of the Ark was nine handbreadths, as it is written: “And a cubit and a half the breadth thereof”</b> (Exodus 25:10). <b>A cubit is six</b> handbreadths, <b>and a half-cubit is three</b> handbreadths. Therefore, the total width of the Ark is nine handbreadths. <b>And four tablets were</b> placed <b>in</b> the Ark, <b>two</b> tablets were <b>whole and two</b> tablets were <b>broken.</b> What is the source for the fact that the broken tablets were placed in the Ark? <b>As it is written:</b> “And I will write on the tablets the words that were on the first tablets <b>which you did break, and you shall put them in the Ark”</b> (Deuteronomy 10:2). <b>The length of each one of the tablets was six handbreadths and their width was six handbreadths.</b> If one <b>lays the width of the tablets across the width of the Ark, three handbreadths remains there: A half-handbreadth from here,</b> on one side, <b>for</b> the thickness of <b>this wall, and a half-handbreadth from here,</b> on the other side, <b>for</b> the thickness of <b>that wall, and two handbreadths for handling</b> [<b><i>shilut</i></b>] <b>in the place where the Torah scroll was placed.</b> It was necessary to leave this space around the Torah scroll in order to allow the insertion and removal of the Torah scroll with ease. This is the opinion of Rabbi Meir that is adopted by Rabbi Yoḥanan.
<b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish</b> disagrees with Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion. He <b>said: The Ark was constructed using a cubit of five handbreadths. Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> that holds that the Ark was constructed <b>using a cubit of five handbreadths?</b> It is <b>Rabbi Yehuda. As we learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Kelim</i> 17:10): <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> The <b>cubit</b> used as the unit of measurement for <b>the construction</b> of the walls and the gates was of <b>six</b> handbreadths; the cubit for <b>the vessels</b> was of <b>five</b> handbreadths. <b>And this Ark is a vessel.</b> Therefore, <b>it</b> was constructed with a cubit of five handbreadths. <b>According to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, who said</b> that unit of measurement for <b>the Ark was a cubit of five handbreadths, it was constructed to a length of twelve and a half handbreadths, as it is written: “Two cubits and a half shall be the length thereof”</b> (Exodus 25:10). <b>A cubit is five</b> handbreadths, <b>and</b> another <b>cubit is five</b> handbreadths, <b>and a half-cubit is two and a half</b> handbreadths, which totals twelve and a half handbreadths. <b>And four tablets were</b> placed <b>in</b> the Ark, <b>two</b> tablets were <b>whole and two</b> tablets were <b>broken, as it is written:</b> “And I will write on the tables the words that were on the first tables <b>which you did break, and you shall put them in the Ark”</b> (Deuteronomy 10:2). <b>Each one of the tablets’ length was six handbreadths, and its width was six handbreadths.</b> If one <b>lays the length of the tablets across the length of the Ark, a half-handbreadth remains there.</b> This leaves <b>a fingerbreadth,</b> equal to a quarter of a handbreadth, <b>for the wall from here,</b> on one side, and <b>a fingerbreadth for the wall from there,</b> on the other side. According to this opinion, the Torah scroll was not placed in the Ark. <b>The width of the Ark was seven and a half handbreadths, as it is written: “And a cubit and a half the breadth thereof”</b> (Exodus 25:10). <b>A cubit is five</b> handbreadths, <b>and a half-cubit is two and a half</b> handbreadths, which totals seven and a half handbreadths. <b>And four tablets were</b> placed <b>in</b> the Ark, <b>two</b> tablets were <b>whole and two</b> tablets were <b>broken, as it is written:</b> “And I will write on the tables the words that were on the first tables <b>which you did break, and you shall put them in the Ark”</b> (Deuteronomy 10:2). <b>The length of each one of the tablets was six handbreadths, and their width was six handbreadths.</b> If one <b>lays the width of the tablets across the width of the Ark, one and a half handbreadths remains there: A fingerbreadth for the wall from here,</b> on one side, <b>and a fingerbreadth for the wall from there,</b> on the other side, as well as <b>a half-handbreadth from here,</b> on one side, <b>and a half-handbreadth from here,</b> on the other side, <b>for handling</b> the tablets.
The Gemara continues its discussion of the Ark. <b>How did Bezalel construct the Ark? Rabbi Ḥanina said: He constructed it of three boxes,</b> each one larger than the previous one (see <i>Yoma</i> 72b). <b>Two</b> of them were made <b>of gold</b> and <b>one</b> was made <b>of wood. He placed</b> the smallest box, which was made <b>of gold, in</b> the box <b>of wood, and</b> he placed the box <b>of wood in</b> the box <b>of gold,</b> the largest box. <b>And</b> he then <b>overlaid</b> the lip of the box of wood with gold as well, <b>as it is written: “And you shall overlay it with pure gold, within and without</b> shall you overlay it” (Exodus 25:11). The verse already stated: “You shall overlay it.” <b>What</b> is the added meaning when <b>the verse states: “Shall you overlay it?”</b> The added words <b>convey</b> that <b>the upper lip</b> of the wooden box was also covered in gold. Alternatively, Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish said: <b>He constructed one box and overlaid it</b> on all sides with gold, <b>as it is written: “And you shall overlay it with pure gold, within and without</b> shall you overlay it” (Exodus 25:11). According to this explanation, <b>what</b> is the added meaning when <b>the verse states: “Shall you overlay it”? Rabbi Pineḥas said:</b> The added words <b>convey</b> that the area <b>between each board</b> needed to be covered in gold as well, to insure that no part of the Ark lacked a gold covering.
The Gemara continues with discussion of the two tablets. <b>How were the tablets written? Rabbi Ḥanina ben Gamliel says: Five on this tablet and five on that tablet. This is as it is written: “And He wrote them upon two tablets of stone”</b> (Deuteronomy 4:13), i.e., <b>five</b> of the Ten Commandments <b>on this tablet and five on that tablet. But the Rabbis say:</b> All of the <b>Ten</b> Commandments were written <b>on this tablet and</b> the same <b>ten were written on that tablet. This is as it is written: “And He declared unto you His covenant, which He commanded you to perform, even the ten words”</b> (Deuteronomy 4:13). This teaches that there were <b>ten on this tablet and ten on that tablet. Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai says: Twenty on this tablet and twenty on that tablet, as it is written: “And He wrote them upon two tablets of stone”</b> (Deuteronomy 4:13). This teaches that there <b>were twenty on this tablet and twenty on that tablet. Rabbi Simai said: Forty on this tablet and forty on that tablet, as it is written: “On the one side and on the other were they written”</b> (Exodus 32:15), as <b>a cube</b> [<b><i>tatroga</i></b>]. <b>Ḥananya, nephew of Rabbi Yehoshua, says: Between each and every statement</b> that was written on the tablets, <b>its precise</b> details <b>and</b> the explanation of <b>its letters</b> were written as well, <b>as it is written: “Filled with beryl [<i>tarshish</i>]”</b> (Song of Songs 5:14). <i>Tarshish</i> is the name of a sea, or more likely, an area of the Mediterranean. The verse is indicating that the Torah is filled with all of these details, <b>like the great sea</b> is filled with waves. <b>When Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish would reach this verse,</b> “Filled with beryl,” <b>he would say:</b> The parable that <b>Ḥananya, nephew of Rabbi Yehoshua, taught me is appropriate. Just as between</b> one <b>large wave and</b> another <b>large wave there are smaller waves</b> in <b>this sea, so too, the precise</b> details <b>and</b> the explanations of <b>the letters of the Torah</b> were written <b>between each and every commandment.</b>
Due to the differing opinions with regard to the length of the cubit, there are differing opinions with regard to the size of the Ark. It follows that, according to the opinion of Rabbi Meir, the Torah scroll was placed in the Ark, while according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, it was not placed there. <b>Rabbi Tanḥuma said: I raised the</b> following <b>difficulty in the presence of Rabbi Pineḥas.</b> The next verse <b>conforms to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda and does not conform to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir. What is the source for</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda? As it is written: “Take this book of the law, and put it by the side of the Ark of the Covenant of the Lord your God”</b> (Deuteronomy 31:26). This verse indicates that the Torah scroll was placed next to the Ark and not inside it. This is <b>in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, who said: Where was the Torah scroll placed? A box-like</b> container [<b><i>gluskiyya</i></b>] <b>was made for it on the outside, and the Torah scroll was placed inside it.</b> <b>What is the source for</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir</b> that the Torah scroll was placed inside the Ark? It is <b>as it is written, “And you shall put the Ark cover upon the Ark from above; and in the Ark you shall put the testimony that I shall give you”</b> (Exodus 25:21). This verse indicates that first the Ark cover was placed on the Ark, and only subsequently was the testimony, i.e., the tablets, placed inside. Since a previous verse (Exodus 25:16) already commands to put the tablets in the Ark, this second instruction to place the testimony must be referring to the Torah scroll rather than the tablets. This reading is supported by the fact that the instruction to place the testimony follows the instruction to place the Ark cover. The Ark cover must have been put in place first, as the complete Torah scroll did not exist until the end of the fortieth year in the desert, long after the assembly of the Tabernacle and the Ark. <b>According to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, who stated</b> that <b>there is no absolute chronological order in the Torah,</b> the verse can be understand that testimony always refers to the tablets. <b>Therefore,</b> the verse may be understood that first it states: <b>“And in the Ark you shall put the testimony that I shall give you,” and</b> only <b>afterward</b> it states: <b>“And you shall put the Ark cover upon the Ark from above.”</b> Apropos the discussion of the tablets and the Torah, the Gemara cites a further statement on the subject. <b>Rabbi Pineḥas</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish: The Torah that the Holy One,</b> Blessed be He, <b>gave to Moses</b> on Mount Sinai <b>was given to him</b> as <b>white fire engraved with black fire. It,</b> the Torah itself, <b>is fire mixed with fire, carved from</b> beneath the throne of glory, which is <b>fire, and given from</b> the One who is <b>fire, as it is written: “At His right hand was a fiery law unto them”</b> (Deuteronomy 33:2).

Halakhah 2

<strong>Halakha 2 · MISHNA</strong> The previous mishna mentioned that there were thirteen prostrations in the Temple. <b>Where were these prostrations?</b> There were <b>four in the north</b> of the courtyard, <b>four in the south, three in the east and two in the west,</b> as the thirteen prostrations were <b>facing the thirteen gates</b> of the Temple courtyard. The thirteen gates were as follows: <b>The southern ones,</b> listed in order, beginning with the one <b>adjacent to the western</b> side, were <b>the Upper Gate,</b> and the topography of the courtyard was such that there was an incline on the east-west plane, therefore the gate farthest to the west was higher than the other gates; <b>the Gate of Kindling,</b> through which the priests would bring the wood for the arrangement of fire on top of the altar; <b>the Gate of the Firstborn,</b> through which priests would bring the ritually pure firstborn animals to be sacrificed, as it is permitted to slaughter firstborn animals on the southern side of the courtyard; and <b>the Gate of Water.</b> The mishna elaborates: <b>And why was it named the Gate of Water? Since through it they would bring in the vial</b> <sup><b>[17a]</b></sup> <b>of</b> water for <b>the water libation on the festival</b> of <i>Sukkot</i>, as they would ceremoniously draw the water from the Pool of Siloam and bring it to the altar through this gate. <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says:</b> There was a different reason for this name. It was called the Gate of Water because <b>through it the water would trickle [<i>mefakim</i>], and in the future</b> this water will increase and <b>go out from under the threshold of the House.</b> <b>Facing these</b> gates were the ones <b>in the north,</b> listed in order from the one <b>closest to the west: The Gate of Jeconiah; the Gate of the Offering,</b> through which they would bring the offerings of the most sacred order, as these could be slaughtered only in the northern part of the courtyard; <b>the Gate of Women,</b> where women would enter the courtyard to place their hands on the heads of their offerings; and <b>the Gate of Song,</b> through which they would bring the musical instruments into the courtyard. The mishna asks: <b>And why was it called the Gate of Jeconiah?</b> The reason is <b>that through it Jeconiah went out to his exile.</b> Before Jeconiah was exiled by Nebuchadnezzar to Babylon he came to take leave of the Temple, and he left through this gate. The mishna resumes the list with the gates <b>that</b> are <b>in the east: the Gate of Nicanor,</b> which was named after Nicanor, who brought the doors of this gate from Egypt (see <i>Yoma</i> 38a). <b>And</b> the Gate of Nicanor <b>had two wickets [<i>pishpeshin</i>], one on its right and one on its left. And</b> there were <b>two</b> gates <b>in the west that did not have a name,</b> making a total of thirteen gates.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that there were thirteen gates in the courtyard. The Gemara notes that <b>the mishna is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Abba Yosei ben Yoḥanan, who said</b> in <i>Middot</i> 2:6 that the thirteen prostrations were performed <b>opposite</b> the <b>thirteen gates. However, according to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> in <i>Middot</i> 1:4, <b>there were</b> only <b>seven gates in the courtyard.</b> Consequently, <b>according to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, where were these</b> thirteen <b>prostrations</b> performed, if there weren’t a corresponding number of gates? The Gemara answers that the prostrations correspond <b>to that which we learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (see <i>Middot</i> 2:3): <b>There were thirteen breaches</b> in the <i>soreg</i>, the lattice fence between the walls of the courtyard and the Temple Mount, <b>that the kings of Greece breached.</b> They did this to allow foreigners to enter the courtyard and to demonstrate that this area was not reserved exclusively for priests. <b>And the sons of the Hasmonean</b> dynasty <b>returned and fenced in</b> those breaches, <b>and decreed</b> that <b>thirteen prostrations</b> should be performed <b>opposite them.</b> Anyone who encircled the courtyard and passed by one of the places where a breach had been sealed would prostrate in gratitude to God for removing Greek control and the decrees against the Jews of Eretz Yisrael.
The Gemara expands upon the idea expressed by Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov that in the future the water from the Gate of Water will increase and emerge from under the threshold of the House: It is written in a prophecy concerning the end of days: <b>“And it shall come to pass in that day, that living waters shall go out from Jerusalem:</b> Half of them toward the eastern sea, and half of them toward the western sea; in summer and in winter shall it be” (Zechariah 14:8), and <b>it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>From the house of the Holy of Holies to the curtain,</b> the stream of water will be as thin <b>as the antennae of <i>silai</i> and <i>khilai</i>,</b> two types of snails. <b>From the veil to the golden altar</b> the stream of water will increase slightly until it is <b>like the antennae of grasshoppers. From</b> the place of <b>the golden altar to the courtyards</b> it will further increase to the thickness <b>of a thread of the warp</b> of a loom. <b>From the courtyards to the threshold of the House</b> the stream will widen to the size <b>of a thread of the woof,</b> which was thicker than the thread of the warp. <b>From here onward</b> the stream will reach the width of <b>a stream of liquid poured from the mouth of a cruse.</b> The Gemara cites another <i>baraita</i> on this topic. <b>It is written: “And, behold, there trickled forth waters on the right side. When the man went forth eastward with the line in his hand, he measured a thousand cubits, and he caused me to pass through the waters, waters that were to the ankles”</b> (Ezekiel 47:2–3). This verse teaches that at a distance of a thousand cubits the height of the water was <b>up to the ankles.</b> The next verse states: <b>“Again he measured a thousand, and caused me to pass through the waters, waters that were to the knees,”</b> which indicates that after an additional thousand cubits the water was <b>up to the knees.</b> The verse continues: <b>“Again he measured a thousand, and caused me to pass through waters that were to the loins”</b> (Ezekiel 47:4), which teaches that after the next thousand cubits the height of the water rose <b>until the loins. From here onward</b> the water continued to rise, until <b>“he measured a thousand; and it was a river that I could not pass through”</b> (Ezekiel 47:5). After the last thousand cubits the water was so high that <b>even a large Liburnian ship,</b> a type of a warship, <b>could not pass through</b> the current. <b>What is the explanation</b> of the verse: <b>“Neither shall gallant ship pass thereby”</b> (Isaiah 33:21). <b>Why? “For the waters were risen, waters to swim in [<i>mei saḥu</i>],</b> a river that could not be passed through” (Ezekiel 47:5). <b>What is</b> the meaning of the phrase <b><i>mei saḥu</i>?</b> It means that the water flowed so fast and high it prevented one <b>from swimming.</b> Similarly, <b>Rav Ḥuna said</b> to clarify the meaning of the word “<i>saḥu</i>”: <b>In our locale they call swimming <i>saḥona</i>,</b> as in the verse: <b>“And when he shall spread forth his hands in its midst, as he who swims spreads forth his hands to swim [<i>hasoḥeh lisḥot</i>]”</b> (Isaiah 25:11). The Gemara presents an alternate explanation for this verse. <b>What is</b> the meaning of <b>“waters to swim in [saḥu]”? Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Bun, said: Water that is discussed in the world.</b> The term <i>saḥu</i> is associated with <i>siḥa</i>, which means discussion or conversation. The verse indicates that this river will be the topic of every discussion, due to its high waters and strong current. The Gemara continues to discuss this river. It is written: <b>“In that day there shall be a fountain opened to the house of David and to the inhabitants of Jerusalem, for purification and for sprinkling”</b> (Zechariah 13:1). This river will be used for the purification of the house of David and the inhabitants of Jerusalem. The Gemara asks: Since this river can serve to purify any member of the Jewish people, why did the verse single out the house of David and the inhabitants of Jerusalem? The Gemara answers: The verse is not listing those who are fit to be purified by this river. Rather, it is delineating the various parts of this river that serve different functions. As <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Naḥman</b> explained <b>in the name of Rabbi Yonatan: From</b> the dwelling place of <b>the house of David until</b> the residences of <b>the inhabitants of Jerusalem,</b> the water in this river is considered running spring water, which <b>is fit for</b> the purpose of purifying <b>a menstruating woman,</b> as well as for use in the waters of <b>purification,</b> i.e., spring water that was mixed with the ashes of the Red Heifer. <b>From here onward it is</b> considered <b>combined water,</b> a mixture of spring and rain water, which is <b>fit for</b> the purification of <b>a menstruating woman but unfit for the waters of purification.</b> The Gemara presents an alternate explanation of the verse. <b>Rabbi Elazar said: From</b> the palace of <b>the house of David until</b> the residences of <b>the inhabitants of Jerusalem,</b> the water is <b>fit for</b> the purification of <b>a menstruating woman</b> as well as for the waters of <b>purification. From here onward, it is</b> considered <b>water</b> running down <b>slopes [<i>ketafrisot</i>],</b> and <b>is unfit</b> both <b>for</b> the purification of <b>a menstruating woman and for</b> the waters of <b>purification.</b>
In Ezekiel’s prophecy concerning this river, <b>it is</b> further <b>written: “Then he said to me: These waters issue forth toward the eastern region,</b> and shall go down into the Arabah; and when they shall enter into the sea, into the sea of the putrid waters, the waters shall be healed” (Ezekiel 47:8). <b>This</b> eastern region mentioned in this verse <b>is</b> the <b>Sea of Samkhu,</b> which is the modern Hula Lake in the northern Galilee. <b>“And shall go down into the Arabah,” this</b> phrase refers to <b>the Sea of Tiberias,</b> i.e., the Sea of Galilee. <b>“And when they shall enter into the sea,” this is the Dead Sea. “Into the sea of the putrid waters [<i>mutza’im</i>],” this is the Great Sea,</b> the Mediterranean Sea. <b>And why was it called <i>mutza’im</i>,</b> which literally means taken out? This <b>corresponds to the two occasions that</b> the sea <b>went out</b> and overran the dry land, <b>once in the Generation of Enosh,</b> when idolatry proliferated, <b>and once in the Generation of the Dispersion,</b> i.e., that of the Tower of Babel. Until where did the sea overrun the dry land? <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥanina: In the first instance,</b> the Generation of Enosh, it <b>went out until Calabria,</b> in southern Italy, <b>and in the second instance,</b> the Generation of Dispersion, it <b>went out until the rocks of Barbary</b> in North Africa. <b>Rabbi Aḥa</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥanina: In the first instance</b> the sea <b>went out until the rocks of Barbary, and in the second instance</b> it <b>went out until Akko and until Jaffa.</b> The Gemara adds that there is support for this claim from the verse: <b>“Thus far [<i>ad po</i>] you shall come, but no further,</b> and here your proud waves shall be stayed” (Job 38:11). The phrase <i>ad po</i> is similar in meaning to <i>ad ko</i>, which alludes to the city of Akko. The verse is saying that <b>until Akko you shall come, but no further.</b> Likewise, in the last part of the verse: <b>“And here [<i>ufo</i>] your proud waves shall be stayed,”</b> the similarity between the words <i>ufo</i> and <i>Yafo</i>, Jaffa, hints that <b>until Jaffa your proud waves shall be stayed.</b> The Gemara stated above that the waters of the river that emerge from the Temple Mount will reach four seas: The Sea of Samkhu, the Sea of Tiberias, the Dead Sea, and the Mediterranean Sea. The Gemara asks: <b>It works out well</b> that the river will reach the <b>Great Sea,</b> i.e., the Mediterranean Sea, and the <b>Dead Sea, in order to sweeten them,</b> and render them drinkable, as they contain saltwater. However, with regard to <b>the Sea of Tiberias, and the Sea of Samkhu,</b> whose water is already sweet, for what purpose will this river reach them? The Gemara responds that when the water of this river reaches these seas, their waters will be blessed <b>by an increase</b> in the number of <b>their fish, as it is written with regard to</b> this river: <b>“Their fish shall be after their kinds”</b> (Ezekiel 47:10). This verse teaches that <b>their fish will be of multiple kinds,</b> great in number and variety. <b>It was</b> likewise <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel said:</b> There was <b>an incident in which I went to Sidon,</b> north of the Sea of Galilee, <b>and they brought before me more than three hundred kinds of fish in one pot.</b>
 The Gemara continues to interpret the verses in Ezekiel: <b>“And the waters shall be healed…But its miry places and its marshes shall not be healed; they shall be given for salt”</b> (Ezekiel 47:8–11). The Gemara points out an apparent contradiction: It is written: <b>“And the waters shall be healed,” and</b> yet <b>you say that the waters shall not be healed?</b> The Gemara responds that <b>it is a place called Velo Yerafu,</b> which means: They will not be healed. This verse is detailing the locations where the waters will heal, namely the miry places, the marshes, and a place called Velo Yerafu.
<b>It is</b> further <b>written</b> there: <b>“And by the river upon its bank, on this side and on that side, shall grow every tree for food, whose leaf shall not wither, neither shall its fruit fail; it shall bring forth new fruit every month,</b> because its waters issue out of the Temple; and its fruit shall be for food, and its leaf for healing” (Ezekiel 47:12). What is the meaning of the phrase “it shall bring forth new fruit every month”? <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda said:</b> The meaning of the verse is <b>that in this world, grain produces</b> a crop <b>six months</b> after it is planted, <b>and a tree produces fruit twelve months</b> after its fruit is harvested. <b>However, in the future, grain will produce</b> crops <b>in one month, and a tree will produce</b> fruit <b>in two months.</b> <b>What is the explanation</b> of the words in the verse: <b>“It shall bring forth new fruit every month [<i>ḥodashav</i>]?”</b> The term <i>ḥodashav</i>, which incorporates the plural of the word month, indicates that the fruit will grow in two months. As the time required for grain to ripen is half the time of that of fruit, grain will ripen in a single month.
