This Gemara presents a dissenting opinion. <b>Rabbi Yosei said:</b> The meaning of the verse is <b>that in this world grain produces a</b> crop <b>six months</b> after it is planted, <b>while a tree produces fruit twelve months</b> after its fruit is harvested. <b>However, in the future, grain will produce</b> crops <b>in fifteen days, and a tree will produce</b> fruit <b>in one month, as we found that grain produced</b> a crop <b>in the days of Joel</b> that ripened <b>in fifteen days, and the <i>omer</i></b> offering <b>was brought from</b> that grain on the sixteenth of Nisan. In that year they brought the <i>omer</i> offering from grain fifteen days after it was sown. <b>What is the explanation</b> of the verse: <b>“Be glad then, you children of Zion, and rejoice in the Lord your God; for He gives you the first rain in just measure, and He causes the rain to come down for you, the first rain and the latter rain, at the first”</b> (Joel 2:23)? The Sages explain that there was a severe drought that year, and the first rains fell only on the first of Nisan (see <i>Ta’anit</i> 5a). The prophet commanded the people to plant their fields, and they found sufficient seeds to sow the fields. The second rains fell on the fifth of Nisan, and miraculously the grain ripened so quickly that they were able to bring the <i>omer</i> offering from that grain on the sixteenth of the month. In the future the grain will ripen as quickly as it did then. The Gemara asks: <b>And how</b> does <b>Rabbi Yosei establish</b> the meaning of the verse: <b>“It shall bring forth new fruit every month,”</b> which apparently indicates that fruit will grow in two months? He explains that the verse means <b>that each and every month the tree will bring forth fruit.</b> The aforementioned verse states: <b>“And its leaf for healing [<i>terufa</i>]”</b> (Ezekiel 47:12). The Sages dispute the meaning of this phrase. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The word <i>terufa</i> with a <i>tav</i> is similar to <i>terufa</i> with a <i>tet</i>, which means food. Therefore the verse means: For <b>its food he sucks the leaves</b> of the tree that grows on the banks of that river, <b>and ingests their sustenance,</b> as the leaves of this tree nourish as much as its fruit. <b>Rav and Shmuel</b> both interpreted <i>terufa</i> as an allusion to <i>lehatir pe</i>, to release the mouth, but differed over the exact meaning. <b>One said</b> it means <b>to release the upper mouth,</b> i.e., to open the mouth of a mute person, such that he will be able to speak if he eats from the fruit of this tree. And the other <b>one said</b> it means <b>to release the lower mouth,</b> a reference to the womb; a barren woman who eats from this tree will be able to conceive. <b>Rabbi Ḥanina and Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> disagree in a similar manner. <b>One said</b> the verse means <b>to release the mouth of infertility, and</b> the other <b>one said</b> it means <b>to open the mouth of the mute.</b>
In its list of the gates in the Temple, the mishna stated: <b>Facing</b> the gates in the south were additional gates <b>in the north, etc.</b> One of the gates in the north was the Gate of Jeconiah, through which this king departed for exile. The Gemara relates that <b>you find at the time that Nebuchadnezzar ascended to</b> Eretz Yisrael to remove Jeconiah, Jehoiachin, from his throne, three months after he had crowned him instead of his father Jehoiakim. <b>He came and settled in Dofnei of Antioch,</b> where he set up his camp. <b>And the Great Sanhedrin came out to greet him and said to him: Has the time come for this House to be destroyed?</b> Is that the purpose for which you have arrived? <b>He said to them: The king that I crowned</b> to rule <b>over you, give him to me</b> as a captive <b>and I will go.</b> <b>They came and said to Jehoiachin, king of Judea: Nebuchadnezzar requires</b> that <b>you</b> be taken into captivity to Babylon. <b>When he heard this from them, he took the keys of the Temple</b> and <b>ascended to the roof of the Sanctuary and said before Him: Master of the Universe, in the past we were faithful to You and Your keys were handed over to us. Now that we are not faithful, Your keys are handed over to You. Two <i>amora’im</i></b> dispute what happened next: <b>One said</b> that Jehoiachin took the keys and <b>threw them</b> up to the heavens <b>and they have not yet descended</b> from there. <b>And one said</b> that <b>the likeness of a hand came and took them from his hand. When all</b> <sup><b>[17b]</b></sup> the <b>nobles of [<i>ḥorei</i>] Judea saw</b> what had occurred, <b>they went up to the top of their roofs and fell and died. This is as it is written: “The burden concerning the Valley of Vision. What ails you now, that you have wholly gone up to the housetops, you that are full of uproar, a tumultuous city,</b> a joyous town? Your slain are not slain with the sword, nor dead in battle” (Isaiah 22:1–2). Isaiah prophesies about Jerusalem, the Valley of Vision, which will be conquered by Nebuchadnezzar, crying: What ails you, Jerusalem, that your nobles will go up to the rooftops and fall to their deaths? Your dead will not be slain with the sword or in battle; they will die by falling from the rooftops.

Halakhah 3

<strong>Halakha 3 · MISHNA</strong> This mishna details the exact location and purpose of the thirteen tables in the Temple. There <b>were thirteen tables in the Temple. Eight</b> of them were made <b>of marble</b> and were located <b>in the slaughtering area,</b> north of the altar, where the priests would slaughter the offerings of the most sacred order. <b>Upon these</b> tables <b>they would wash the innards</b> of the offerings, as the marble was cool and preserved the freshness of the meat. <b>And</b> there were <b>two</b> more tables <b>on the western</b> side <b>of the ramp,</b> south of the altar, <b>one of marble and one of silver.On</b> the table <b>of marble they would place the limbs</b> before they were sacrificed, and from there the priests would bring them up to the altar. <b>On</b> the table made <b>of silver</b> they would place the ninety-three <b>sacred vessels</b> brought out from the Chamber of Vessels each morning for the services of that day. <b>And</b> there were <b>two</b> tables <b>in the Entrance Hall</b> to the Sanctuary, <b>on the inside</b> of the Entrance Hall, <b>near the opening to the Temple, one of marble and one of gold. On</b> the table <b>of marble they would put the shewbread before its entrance</b> to the Sanctuary after it was baked on the eve of Shabbat. <b>And</b> they would place the old shewbread <b>on</b> the table <b>of gold upon its exit</b> from the Sanctuary, to be divided among the priests. The reason the shewbread was placed on a marble table before being brought into the Sanctuary and on a golden one upon when removed from there is <b>that one elevates</b> to a higher level <b>in</b> matters of <b>sanctity and one does not downgrade.</b> Since it had been placed on the golden Shewbread table all week inside the Sanctuary, upon its removal it could not be derogated to a marble table and so was placed on a different golden table in the Entrance Hall. Finally, there was <b>one</b> table <b>of gold inside</b> the Sanctuary, i.e. the Shewbread table, <b>upon which the shewbread</b> was placed <b>always.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna stated that the shewbread was placed on a marble table when it was brought into the Sanctuary. The Gemara cites a different opinion: As it <b>was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: When the shewbread was brought into the sanctuary it was placed <b>on</b> a table <b>of silver.</b> In accordance with the opinion stated in the mishna, that the bread was placed on a marble table, <b>Rabbi Yosei</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak,</b> who said that <b>Rabbi Ḥananya cites this</b> tradition <b>in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan: There was no</b> table <b>of silver here, as it boils,</b> i.e., it heats articles placed upon it, which could cause the shewbread to grow moldy. The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>Wasn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>as follows: This was one of the miracles that were performed in the Temple, that just as they would place</b> the shewbread <b>hot</b> as it came out of the oven, <b>so too they would remove it hot, as it is stated: “To put hot bread in the day when it was taken away”</b> (I Samuel 21:7). When they removed the shewbread after seven days on the golden table, it was as hot as it was when they originally placed it there. Why, then, were they concerned about placing the shewbread on a silver table, if it would remain warm in any event? The Gemara answers that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: One does not mention miraculous events.</b> Despite the fact that miracles occur, one should not rely on them, but rather act according to the laws of nature. Therefore the priests did not place the bread on a silver table that could have spoiled the shewbread through natural causes. <b>They raised a dilemma before Rabbi Ila:</b> If one week <b>there was no</b> new <b>bread</b> to replace the old bread on the shewbread table, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>leaving</b> the old shewbread <b>until the next Shabbat?</b> On the one hand, as the time has arrived for the bread to be removed and divided among the priests, if it remains after this time it could be considered <i>notar</i>, left over, and disqualified by remaining overnight. On the other hand, as there is no new bread to replace it, perhaps this bread would still be considered the shewbread of the table and would not be disqualified until new bread would be brought. Rabbi Ila <b>said to them</b> that <b>it is written: “And you shall set upon the table shewbread before Me always”</b> (Exodus 25:30). There should always be <b>shewbread</b> on the table, <b>even if it is disqualified.</b>
The Gemara cites a <i>baraita</i> that discusses the shewbread table: <b>Solomon built ten tables</b> for the Temple, modeled after the shewbread table. <b>As it is written: “He made also ten tables, and placed them in the Temple, five on the right side and five on the left”</b> (II Chronicles 4:8). The Gemara asks: <b>If you say</b> that right and left refer to the sides of the entrance to the Sanctuary, which means that Solomon set up <b>five</b> tables <b>in the southern</b> side of the Sanctuary to the left of its entrance, <b>and five in the northern</b> side of the Sanctuary to the right, this is difficult. The Gemara explains the difficulty: <b>Isn’t the table fit only</b> if it is <b>in the northern</b> part of the Sanctuary, <b>as it is stated: “And you shall put the table on the north side”</b> (Exodus 26:35)? If so, <b>what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “Five on the right side and five on the left”? Rather,</b> it must mean that the table prepared by Moses was positioned in the proper place in the Sanctuary, and <b>five</b> of the tables made by Solomon were placed <b>to the right of the table of Moses, and five to its left.</b> <b>Even so,</b> despite the fact that there were so many tables in the Sanctuary, the priest <b>would arrange</b> the shewbread <b>only on the table of Moses alone, as it is stated:</b> “And Solomon made all the vessels that were in the House of the Lord, the golden altar, <b>and the table whereupon the shewbread was,</b> of gold” (I Kings 7:48). The use of the definite article “the” to describe the table indicates that there was one unique table upon which the shewbread was arranged, the table of Moses. Conversely, <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says: He would arrange</b> the shewbread <b>on all of</b> the tables, each one in turn, <b>as it is stated: “And the tables upon which was the shewbread”</b> (II Chronicles 4:19). This verse indicates that the shewbread was placed on all the tables. The Gemara continues to discuss the shewbread table. <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: All the tables, both of Moses and Solomon, <b>were positioned</b> from <b>east to west,</b> in the northern part of the Sanctuary, so that their length ran parallel to the length of the Sanctuary. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, says: They were placed</b> from <b>north to south,</b> and their length was to the width of the Sanctuary. According to <b>the one who said</b> that the tables were placed from <b>east to west it works out well, as they are all suitable for service.</b> The tables were positioned in a manner that rendered them all fit for service, as the table could be used only in the northern part of the Sanctuary, in accordance with the verse: “And you shall put the table on the north side” (Exodus 26:35). However, according to <b>the one who said</b> that they were placed from <b>north to south, the table,</b> i.e., five of the tables, are <b>found in the southern</b> part of the Sanctuary, <sup><b>[18a]</b></sup> <b>and the candelabrum in the north</b> of the Sanctuary. <b>But wasn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The</b> shewbread <b>table was situated from</b> the <b>halfway</b> point <b>of the House and inward,</b> in the inner half of the Sanctuary, <b>drawn two and a half cubits</b> away <b>from the wall to the north.</b> The priests who arranged the shewbread on the table would stand in this space. <b>And the candelabrum</b> was situated <b>opposite it, in the south</b> of the Sanctuary, likewise two and a half cubits from the wall. <b>The golden altar was situated in the middle of the House, dividing the House</b> from its <b>halfway</b> point <b>and inward,</b> i.e., equidistant from the north and south walls, <b>slightly drawn</b> back from the place of the table and the candelabrum <b>toward the outside.</b> Since the length of the entire area was sixty cubits, twenty for the Holy of Holies, and forty for the Sanctuary, <b>all</b> the vessels <b>were situated from a third of the</b> entire <b>House and inward,</b> i.e., the vessels were all contained in the second third of the entire area.
King <b>Solomon constructed ten candelabra,</b> modeled after the one that Moses crafted, <b>as it is stated: “And he made the ten candlesticks of gold according to the ordinance concerning them; and he set them in the Sanctuary, five on the right, and five on the left”</b> (II Chronicles 4:7). Before completing its citation of the <i>baraita</i>, the Gemara asks: <b>If you say</b> that right and left refer to the sides of the entrance to the Sanctuary, which would mean that Solomon set up <b>five</b> candelabra <b>in the north</b> of the Sanctuary, to the left of its entrance, <b>and five in the south</b> of the Sanctuary, to the right of its entrance, this is difficult. The Gemara explains the difficulty: <b>Isn’t the candelabrum fit only</b> if it is <b>in the south of the Sanctuary, as it is stated: “And the candelabrum over against the table on the side of the Tabernacle toward the south”</b> (Exodus 26:35). <b>What,</b> then, is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “Five on the right, and five on the left”? Rather,</b> it must mean that the candelabrum that Moses made was located in the Sanctuary, while <b>five</b> of the candelabra that Solomon crafted were placed <b>to the right of the candelabrum of Moses, and five to its left.</b> <b>Even so,</b> i.e., despite the fact that there were so many candelabra in the Sanctuary, the priest <b>would kindle only</b> the candelabrum <b>of Moses alone, as it is stated: “And the candelabrum of gold with its lamps, to burn every evening”</b> (II Chronicles 13:11). The singular form indicates that only one candelabrum was lit. Conversely, <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says: He would kindle all</b> eleven candelabra, one of Moses and ten of Solomon, each one in turn, <b>as it is stated: “And the candelabra with their lamps, that they should burn according to the ordinance before the Sanctuary, of pure gold”</b> (II Chronicles 4:20). The plural “candelabra” indicates that they were all lit. The <i>baraita</i> analyzes the subsequent verse: <b>“And the flowers, and the lamps, and the tongs, of gold, and that perfect gold [<i>mikhelot zahav</i>]”</b> (II Chronicles 4:21). This means that the candelabrum completely <b>depleted [<i>killu</i>] the gold of Solomon.</b> As the gold was repeatedly purified until it reached the required level of purity, a large quantity of gold evaporated. The Gemara cites a related <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rav Yehuda taught in the name of</b> the Sage <b>Asi: Solomon would take a thousand talents of gold and insert them into the crucible and remove them.</b> He repeated this process <b>until</b> the gold was so refined that <b>he established</b> its weight <b>as one</b> talent of gold, <b>to fulfill that which is stated</b> about the original candelabrum in the Tabernacle: <b>“Of a talent of pure gold made he it, etc.”</b> (Exodus 37:24). <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, said:</b> There was <b>an incident involving the candelabrum of gold that Moses made in the desert, which exceeded</b> the weight set by the Torah, one talent, <b>by one dinar of gold. And they inserted it into the crucible eighty times,</b> to further refine it and reduce its weight, <b>but it was not reduced</b> by <b>anything.</b> <b>And this is proper,</b> for <b>as long as it had not achieved its correct</b> state of purity, <b>it would decrease greatly,</b> as its refinement in the crucible removed all the impurities, thereby reducing its weight. However, <b>from when it had achieved its correct</b> state of purity, <b>it was not reduced at all.</b> Unlike the gold of Moses, the gold Solomon used was not originally free of impurity and therefore required refinement.

Halakhah 4

<strong>Halakha 4 · MISHNA</strong> <b>There were thirteen</b> collection <b>horns in the Temple, and</b> the intended use of the funds <b>was written upon</b> each one, as follows: <b>New shekels, old shekels, pairs</b> of birds, <b>fledglings</b> designated for <b>burnt-offerings, wood</b> for the arrangement on the altar, <b>frankincense</b> that accompanied meal-offerings, and <b>gold</b> donated <b>for the Ark cover.</b> The remaining <b>six horns</b> were designated <b>for</b> communal <b>free-will offerings.</b> The horn labeled <b>new shekels</b> was designated for the half-shekel donation <b>that</b> was brought <b>every year</b> for the needs of that year. The horn labeled <b>old shekels</b> was for <b>one who did not bring</b> his half-shekel <b>the previous year,</b> who would <b>contribute his shekel for the following year.</b> The funds in the horn labeled <b>pairs</b> of birds <b>are</b> designated for the <b>turtledoves</b> used for bird-offerings, and the one labeled <b>fledglings</b> for <b>burnt-offerings are</b> used to purchase <b>young pigeons</b> as burnt-offerings. <b>All of these,</b> i.e., the funds in both horns, were used exclusively for voluntary <b>burnt-offerings.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b> <b>And the Rabbis say:</b> The funds in both the horn labeled pairs of birds and the horn labeled fledglings were for young pigeons and turtledoves. The distinction between them is that the funds in the horn labeled <b>pairs</b> of birds were designated for the obligatory offerings of a <i>zav</i>, a <i>zava</i>, a woman after childbirth, and a leper. These offerings included a pair of birds, <b>one</b> brought for <b>a sin-offering, and</b> the other <b>one</b> brought for <b>a burnt-offering.</b> Conversely, the funds in the horn labeled <b>fledglings</b> for <b>burnt-offerings</b> were <b>all</b> used exclusively for voluntary <b>burnt-offerings.</b> <b>One who says:</b> It is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to donate <b>wood</b> to the Temple, must donate <b>no fewer than two logs</b> for the arrangement on the altar. One who says: It is incumbent upon me to donate <b>frankincense,</b> must donate <b>no less than a handful</b> of frankincense, the amount brought with a meal-offering. One who says: It is incumbent upon me to donate <b>gold,</b> must donate <b>no less than a dinar of gold.</b> It was stated that <b>six horns</b> were designated <b>for</b> communal <b>free-will offerings.</b> The Mishna asks: With regard to the money designated for communal <b>free-will offerings, what would they do with this</b> money? The Mishna answers that they used <b>it to purchase</b> animals for <b>burnt-offerings,</b> as <b>the meat</b> from these offerings was offered on the altar <b>to God and the hides</b> were given <b>to the priests.</b> <b>This midrash was taught</b> by <b>Jehoiada the High Priest:</b> There is an apparent contradiction between two verses. With regard to the guilt-offering, the verse states: <b>“It is a guilt-offering; he is certainly guilty before the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 5:19). This verse indicates that the guilt-offering goes to God, not the priests. However, a different verse states: “As is the sin-offering, so is the guilt-offering; there is one law for them; the priest who makes atonement with it, he shall have it” (Leviticus 7:7). This verse indicates that the offering is designated for the priests alone. How can these two verses be reconciled? The Mishna explains that <b>this is the principle: Any</b> funds <b>that come due to a sin-offering or due to a guilt-offering,</b> i.e., leftover coins designated for one of these offerings, <b>they</b> should be used for the <b>purchase of animals for a</b> voluntary <b>burnt-offering,</b> as <b>the meat</b> will be offered on the altar <b>to God, and the hides</b> will go <b>to the priests.</b> In this manner <b>the two verses are found</b> to be <b>fulfilled,</b> as it is both <b>a guilt-offering to God as well as a guilt-offering to the priest.</b> <b>And</b> this <i>halakha</i> also explains the verse that <b>says: “The guilt-offering money and the sin-offering money was not brought into the House of the Lord; it was for the priests”</b> (II Kings 12:17). This verse is understood to refer to the hides given to the priests.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: There was no</b> collection <b>horn for pairs</b> of birds in the Temple in <b>Jerusalem, due to the mixtures.</b> The concern was that <b>perhaps</b> one of the women obligated to bring a pair of birds <b>would die</b> after putting her money in the horn. <b>And</b> if that happens, it would <b>turn out</b> that <b>the funds for sin-offerings left to die are mixed with</b> the rest of the money in the horn. When the owner of funds designated for a sin-offering passes away, the designated money must be destroyed. Since there is no way to distinguish between the coins, all the money is prohibited. The Gemara raises a difficulty against this conclusion. <b>But wasn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that with regard to <b>a woman who said:</b> It is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring <b>a pair</b> of birds, that <b>she brings the funds for</b> the purchase of <b>a pair</b> of birds to the Temple <b>and places them in the</b> collection <b>horn.</b> <sup><b>[18b]</b></sup> <b>And</b> after she has ritually immersed for her purification and the sun has set, <b>she</b> may <b>eat consecrated</b> foods. <b>And she need not be concerned that perhaps</b> the priest <b>was lazy</b> and neglected to sacrifice her offering, which would cause her to lack atonement and be prohibited from eating sacrificial food. <b>And the priest need not be concerned that perhaps</b> one of the women who placed funds in this horn has died, which would mean that <b>the funds for sin-offerings left to die are mixed with</b> the rest of the coins. Since this ruling is undisputed, it is evidently accepted by all the Sages, including Rabbi Yehuda. If so, how can it be claimed that Rabbi Yehuda said there were no horns for obligatory pairs of birds due to the concern that one of the women who placed money there had died? The Gemara answers: <b>When we say</b> this opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, we are referring to a case where it is known that one of the women had died, which means that the funds she gave have the status <b>of a sin-offering whose owner had certainly died.</b> In this case the coins definitely cannot be used for an offering, and they invalidate the other coins with which they are mingled in the horn. To avoid this scenario, Rabbi Yehuda maintains that there was no horn for obligatory pairs of birds. The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> even <b>if we say</b> that there is a concern that the horn contains invalidated coins, the following remedy can be applied: <b>Let us select four <i>zuz</i>,</b> the value of one pair of birds (see <i>Keritot</i> 1:7), and declare that these are the invalidated coins, <b>and throw them into the river</b> so that they will be destroyed, <b>and the remainder will be permitted.</b> If so, even according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, there should be no problem with a horn for pairs of birds. The Gemara answers: This is not an effective solution according to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, because <b>we generally say: Rabbi Yehuda does not accept</b> the principle of <b>retroactive designation.</b> According to Rabbi Yehuda, a doubtful state of affairs cannot be clarified retroactively. Consequently, one cannot resolve this problem by setting aside four <i>zuz</i>, and he maintains that there was no horn for obligatory pairs of birds.
§ It was taught in the mishna that if one said: It is incumbent upon me to donate wood, he must donate no fewer than two logs. The Gemara addresses a similar case: <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Bun, said that Rabbi Ba bar Memel raised a dilemma:</b> What is the ruling if <b>one said:</b> It is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to donate a log of <b>wood?</b> Does <b>he bring</b> only <b>one log?</b> <b>Rabbi Elazar said: The Mishna says so;</b> i.e., a mishna provides a solution to our dilemma. We learned that the two logs added each day to the arrangement on the altar, before the daily morning offering and before the daily afternoon offering, were not considered one offering, <b>as this</b> log <b>was an offering to itself and that</b> log <b>was an offering to itself.</b> Since each log was a separate offering, evidently one may donate even a single log. Where was this ruling taught? <b>As we learned in a mishna there,</b> with regard to the order of the daily afternoon offering: <b>Two</b> priests ascended to the altar, and <b>in their hands</b> were <b>two logs of wood</b> for the arrangement, each holding one log in his hand. This statement indicates that each log was a separate offering, as each was held by a different priest. The Gemara asks: From where is it derived that even an individual can pledge wood for the arrangement? From the verse: “And when any one brings a meal-offering to the Lord” (Leviticus 2:1). The word <b>offering</b> comes <b>to include the wood,</b> which is also referred to as an offering in the verse: “And we cast lots, the priests, the Levites, and the people, for the wood-offering” (Nehemiah 10:35). Furthermore, the phrase “any one” teaches that even an individual can pledge wood.
As the Gemara is discussing the donation of logs for the arrangement, it mentions their measurements. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: The thickness</b> of this wood <b>was a cubit of the expanded cubits, and their length</b> was measured <b>by the shortened cubits. Rabbi Ḥoni</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ammi:</b> The measurement of their thickness was <b>as on a scale [<i>turtanei</i>],</b> i.e., it was exactly one cubit. <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak</b> explained why a shortened cubit was used for the measurement of the wood and <b>said: As the area of the arrangement</b> on the bronze altar built by Moses <b>was only one cubit by one cubit, therefore,</b> the length of the log <b>was only a shortened cubit,</b> as it would otherwise protrude from the arrangement. <b>And it was likewise taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The breadth of <b>the base</b> of the altar made by Moses was <b>a cubit, the surrounding ledge</b> was <b>a cubit, the rim a cubit, the corners a cubit,</b> and the breadth of the place of the <b>arrangement</b> was likewise <b>a cubit.</b>
§ It was taught in the mishna that if one pledges <b>frankincense</b> without specifying an amount, he must donate <b>no less than a handful.</b> The Gemara cites the verbal analogy that is the source for this <i>halakha</i>. <b>It is stated here:</b> “And you shall put pure frankincense with each row, that it may be to the bread for a <b>memorial-part”</b> (Leviticus 24:7). <b>And it is stated there,</b> with regard to the meal-offering of a sinner: “And the priest shall take his handful of it as its <b>memorial-part”</b> (Leviticus 5:12). The Gemara interprets the verbal analogy: <b>Just as</b> the term <b>“memorial-part” that is stated below</b> must be <b>a handful, so too,</b> the term <b>“memorial-part” that is stated here,</b> with regard to the frankincense brought with the shewbread, must be <b>a handful.</b> This shows that when an unspecified amount of frankincense is pledged as an independent free-will offering, the obligation is to bring a handful. The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> so, one can derive the opposite conclusion from the same verbal analogy: <b>Just as</b> the <b>memorial-part that is stated below,</b> by the shewbread, must be <b>two handfuls,</b> one for each arrangement of bread, <b>so too</b> the term <b>memorial-part stated here,</b> with regard to the free-will offering of frankincense, should be a measure of <b>two handfuls.</b> <b>Rabbi Ila said</b> in response: <b>Didn’t we</b> derive the measurement of a handful of frankincense <b>only from the meal-offering of a sinner? Just as there,</b> in the case of the meal-offering of a sinner, <b>an incomplete handful is invalid, so too, here,</b> with regard to frankincense of the shewbread, <b>an incomplete handful</b> should be <b>invalid.</b> It is possible to invalidate the frankincense of the shewbread only if each handful is considered an independent mitzva. If the two handfuls together were considered one mitzva, they would remain valid even if one of them were incomplete, as they contain a total amount greater than a handful. This proves that each handful is an independent mitzva, which means that if one of them is incomplete, the second does not complete it and it is invalidated. From here it can be inferred that every measurement of the frankincense, including a free-will offering of frankincense, requires a handful. <b>Rabbi Yosei said:</b> From the <b>statement of Rabbi Ila,</b> that the measurement of a handful of frankincense for a free-will offering is derived from the handful that came with the shewbread, which is measured according to the handful of the largest of the priests, it can be <b>said</b> that <b>one who donates incense brings it</b> according to the measure <b>of the handful of a priest</b> who has the <b>largest</b> hands, not the handful of the one who brings it. <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yirmeya:</b> One who donates incense can approximate the handful of any priest, <b>and even</b> use <b>the handful of the owners.</b>
 The mishna taught that one who pledges <b>gold</b> without specifying the amount must donate <b>no less</b> than a dinar of gold. <b>Rabbi Elazar said: And this is</b> the case <b>if he mentioned a form</b> by saying: It is incumbent upon me to bring a gold coin. <b>However, if he did not mention a form,</b> but merely said: It is incumbent upon me to bring gold, <b>he</b> may <b>bring even a</b> small <b>hook</b> of any size.
The mishna states that there were <b>six</b> collection horns <b>for</b> communal <b>free-will offerings,</b> in which the remainder of the funds for sin-offerings and burnt-offerings were placed, for the purchase of free-will offerings. <b>Ḥizkiya said:</b> There six horns <b>correspond to the six</b> extended <b>patrilineal families</b> of priests who served each week in the Temple. There was one collection horn for each family, to prevent quarrels among the priests. <b>Bar Pedaya said:</b> The six horns <b>correspond to</b> the <b>six</b> types of <b>animals</b> from which burnt-offerings can be brought: <b>Bulls, calves, goats, rams, kids, and lambs.</b> Each collection horn was for the funds designated for a particular type of animal. <b>Shmuel said:</b> They <b>correspond to the six</b> obligatory <b>offerings</b> whose leftover funds were used for the purchase of burnt-offerings: (1) <b>Pairs</b> of birds offered in the purification ritual <b>of a <i>zav</i>, pairs</b> of birds <b>of a <i>zava</i>;</b> (2) <b>pairs</b> of birds <b>of a woman after childbirth;</b> (3) <b>sin-offerings;</b> (4) <b>guilt-offerings;</b> (5) <b>meal-offerings</b> of a sinner; <b>and</b> (6) <b>the tenth part of an ephah</b> brought each day by the High Priest as a meal-offering. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Since the free-will offerings are plentiful,</b> i.e., much money was donated for this purpose, there was a concern that if too many coins were placed in one horn, only the uppermost coins would be taken and the bottom ones would deteriorate. Therefore <b>they instituted many horns,</b> so that each horn contained fewer coins and the funds would be preserved properly. The Gemara discusses the midrash of Jehoiada the High Priest mentioned in the mishna. <b>It is written: “And when they had made an end, they brought the rest of the money before the king and Jehoiada,</b> from which they made vessels for the house of the Lord, even vessels used in the Temple service” (II Chronicles 24:14). In explanation of this verse, <b>Rabbi Shimon bar Naḥman said in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan: Jehoiada made two</b> chests for two different types of <b>voluntary donations,</b> one for maintaining the Temple and the other for free-will offerings. <sup><b>[19a]</b></sup> A dissenting opinion <b>was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>of the school of Rabbi Yishmael:</b> Jehoiada prepared a single chest for <b>one free-will offering, as it is written: “So the king commanded, and they made a chest”</b> (II Chronicles 24:8). The verse specifically states that Jehoiada made only one chest. This is the same chest mentioned elsewhere: <b>And he placed it in the Sanctuary of the House of God, “and he bored a hole in its lid”</b> (II Kings 12:10). The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it written: “So the king commanded, and they made a chest, and set it without, at the gate of the House of the Lord”</b> (II Chronicles 24:8)? This indicates that that there was an additional chest that was placed outside the Temple. <b>Rav Ḥuna said:</b> There was only one chest, which was originally located inside the Temple by Jehoiada’s command. Afterward, however, he instructed that it should be placed outside the Temple <b>because of</b> those people who were <b>ritually impure</b> by contact with a corpse or a dead creeping animal. These people were unable to enter the Temple to insert their money for free-will offerings. <b>Rabbi Ḥanina</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yosef:</b> There were two free-will offerings in the Temple, as it is written: <b>“But there were not made for the House of the Lord cups of silver, snuffers,</b> basins, trumpets, any vessels of gold, or vessels of silver, of the money that was brought into the House of the Lord; for they gave that to them who did the work, and repaired with it the House of the Lord” (II Kings 12:14). These were for the repairs of the Temple, not for making sacred serving vessels. Conversely, the verses in II Chronicles discuss the construction of the vessels, as it is written: “From which they made vessels for the House of the Lord” (II Chronicles 24:14). As this verse refers to a different chest and a separate free-will offering, it shows that there were in fact two chests. 

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>Halakha 1 · MISHNA</strong> If <b>money was found</b> on the floor of the Temple <b>between</b> one of <b>the</b> collection horns marked <b>shekels and the</b> collection horn marked <b>free-will offerings,</b> that is to say, between the first and the thirteenth collection horns, in which funds contributed to the Temple were stored, the following distinctions apply: If the money was found <b>closer to the</b> horn marked <b>shekels, it is allocated to the shekels;</b> if it was found <b>closer to the</b> horn marked <b>free-will offerings, it is allocated to free-will offerings;</b> and if it was <b>equidistant</b> from the horn marked shekels and the horn marked free-will offerings, <b>it is allocated to free-will offerings.</b> If the money was found <b>between</b> the horn marked <b>wood and</b> the horn marked <b>frankincense,</b> that is, between the fifth and sixth horns, if it was <b>closer to the</b> horn marked <b>wood, it is allocated to wood;</b> if it was closer to <b>the</b> horn marked <b>frankincense, it is allocated to frankincense;</b> and if it was found <b>equidistant</b> from both, <b>it is allocated to frankincense.</b> If the money was found <b>between</b> the horn marked <b>pairs</b> of bird-offerings <b>and</b> the horn marked <b>doves for burnt-offerings,</b> i.e., between the third and the fourth horns, if it is <b>closer to the</b> horn marked <b>pairs</b> of bird-offerings, <b>it is allocated to pairs</b> of bird-offerings; if it was found <b>closer to</b> the horn marked <b>doves for burnt-offerings, it is allocated to doves for burnt-offerings;</b> and if it was found <b>equidistant</b> from both, <b>it is allocated to doves for burnt-offerings.</b> And similarly, if money was found anywhere else <b>between</b> a container for ordinary, <b>non-sacred</b> money <b>and</b> one containing <b>second-tithe</b> money, the following distinctions apply: If the money was found <b>closer to the non-sacred</b> money, <b>it is allocated to the non-sacred</b> money; if it was found closer to <b>second-tithe</b> money, <b>it is allocated to second-tithe</b> money; and if it was found <b>equidistant</b> from both, <b>it is allocated to second-tithe</b> money. <b>This is the principle:</b> In cases of doubt, the ruling <b>follows whichever is closer,</b> even if this involves <b>being lenient,</b> but if the money was found <b>equidistant</b> from both, the ruling follows whichever allocation involves <b>being stringent.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught the <i>halakha</i> governing the case where money was found between the horn marked shekels and a horn marked free-will offerings. The Gemara asks: Addressing this case <b>was not necessary,</b> as these horns were not adjacent to one another; the one marked shekels was the first horn and the last six were marked free-will offerings. Why should it be presumed that money found in the middle belongs to one or the other and not to one of the horns in between? <b>Rather [<i>dela</i>],</b> the mishna should have addressed the case where money was found <b>between</b> the horn marked old <b>shekels and</b> the horn marked <b>pairs</b> of bird-offerings, as they are adjacent to one another. <b>Rabbi Avun</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Pineḥas:</b> The contribution horns in the Temple <b>were arranged like a circle [<i>bukhliyar</i>].</b> The horns did not stand in a straight row, but rather in a circle, such that the horn marked new shekels and the horn marked free-will offerings were adjacent to one another from the other side. The mishna teaches that if money was found <b>equidistant</b> from the horn marked shekels and the horn marked free-will offerings, <b>it is allocated to free-will offerings.</b> The Gemara asks: The mishna <b>should not have</b> said that the money is allocated to free-will offerings. <b>Rather,</b> it should have said that when money is found <b>equidistant</b> from both, <b>it is allocated to shekels.</b> For the <i>halakha</i> governing the money in the horn marked shekels is more stringent, as the regular communal offerings are bought with that money, whereas the money in the horn marked free-will offerings is used only to buy offerings for those times when there are no regular offerings being sacrificed. The Gemara answers: <b>Some wish to say</b> that the <i>halakha</i> governing the money in the horn marked shekels is less stringent because <b>perhaps</b> the money allocated would not be used for the purchase of offerings, and consequently <b>it would be allocated to the remainders of the chamber</b> funds, i.e., those left over after the public offerings have been bought with the half-shekels collected in the chamber. Such money would be used for repairing the walls and towers of Jerusalem, and not for offerings. It is consequently preferable that the money be allocated to free-will offerings, and therefore be used for offerings. And <b>some wish to say</b> that money that is found <b>equidistant</b> from both is treated like money that has no owner, i.e., <b>like</b> money that was set aside by <b>someone who</b> subsequently <b>died,</b> and the Sages said that such money is allocated to free-will offerings. As <b>Rabbi Yesa said: While I was there</b> in Babylonia, <b>I heard the voice of Rav Yehuda who asked Shmuel</b> the following question: If someone <b>set aside his shekel and</b> subsequently <b>died,</b> what is to be done with the money? Shmuel <b>said to him: It is allocated to free-will offerings.</b> The Gemara now asks a similar question: The High Priest could set aside money to be used for the purchase of <b>his</b> daily griddle-cake offering made from <b>one-tenth of an ephah</b> of flour, half in the morning and half in the evening. If he died before the flour had been bought, what is to be done with the money? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The money must be <b>cast into the Dead Sea,</b> i.e., so that it is destroyed. <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> disagreed and <b>said:</b> The money <b>is allocated to free-will offerings.</b>
The mishna taught that if the money was found equidistant from the horn marked pairs of bird-offerings, i.e., one burnt-offering and one sin-offering, and the horn marked doves for burnt-offerings, it is allocated to doves for burnt-offerings. <b>And</b> this is <b>difficult: Is a sin-offering brought as a burnt-offering?</b> Surely it is possible that the money fell from the horn marked pairs of bird-offerings, and is therefore partially designated for the purchase of sin-offerings. <b>Ḥizkiya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish: A condition of the court stipulates with regard to the remainder</b> of the money originally consecrated for sin-offerings <b>that it is brought</b> as <b>burnt-offerings.</b> Even if the one who consecrated the money did not state such a condition explicitly, he is regarded as having done so, as it is presumed that the money was consecrated in accordance with the court’s stipulation. The Gemara asks: If so, <b>with what does this woman,</b> who had placed the money for her offering in the horn marked pairs of bird-offerings, <b>achieve atonement?</b> A woman who had given birth or is a <i>zava</i> must bring a pair of bird-offerings, one as a burnt-offering and the other as a sin-offering, as part of her purification process. If that money has been allocated to doves for burnt-offerings, she has not completed the process. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak said: A condition of the court stipulates: Whoever supplies</b> the Temple with <b>the pairs</b> of birds, <b>he</b> also <b>supplies</b> extra birds to replace those birds that are <b>disqualified.</b> If one of the birds is disqualified or missing, he provides another bird in its place, and so in any event the woman will have brought both a burnt-offering and a sin-offering. The mishna did not consider all the possible places that money could be found between different horns, and so the Gemara asks: <b>Shouldn’t</b> the mishna <b>also</b> discuss additional cases, like where the money was found between the fourth and fifth horns, i.e., <b>between</b> the horn marked <b>incense and</b> the horn marked <b>wood,</b> or between the sixth and seventh horns, i.e., between the horn marked <b>frankincense and</b> the horn marked <b>gold for the Ark cover?</b> The Gemara answers: In fact, this mishna addresses these cases as well, <b>as it teaches at its end: This is the principle:</b> In cases of doubt, the ruling <b>follows whichever is closer,</b> even if this involves being lenient; but if the money was found <b>equidistant</b> from both, the ruling follows whichever attribution involves <b>being stringent.</b>

Halakhah 2

<strong>Halakha 2 · MISHNA</strong> This mishna considers other situations in which something is found and its source is unknown. <b>Money found before animal merchants</b> in Jerusalem is <b>always</b> presumed to be second-<b>tithe</b> money. The presumption is based on the fact that in Jerusalem, most of the animals are bought with second-tithe money and sacrificed as peace-offerings. <b>And</b> money found <b>on the Temple Mount</b> is presumed to be <b>non-sacred</b> money. <b>And</b> with regard to money found <b>in</b> the rest of <b>Jerusalem,</b> the following distinction applies: If it was found <b>during the rest of the days of the year,</b> it is presumed to be <b>non-sacred</b> money, <b>but</b> if it was found <b>during the time of a pilgrim Festival,</b> it is <b>all</b> presumed to be second-<b>tithe</b> money, because most of the money found in Jerusalem at the time of a Festival is second-tithe money. The mishna continues: With regard to <b>meat that was found in the</b> Temple <b>courtyard,</b> and it is not known from whence it came, the <i>halakha</i> is as follows: If it is whole <b>limbs</b> of the animal, in the manner that burnt-offerings are brought to the altar, it is presumed to be <b>burnt-offerings. And</b> if it is in small <b>pieces,</b> it is presumed to be <b>sin-offerings. And</b> if the meat, in whatever form, is found <b>in</b> the city of <b>Jerusalem,</b> as opposed to the courtyard, it is presumed to be the meat of <b>peace-offerings,</b> as most of the meat in Jerusalem is the meat of peace-offerings. Since it is possible that the time during which it is permitted to eat any of it has already passed, both <b>this and that,</b> whether it is determined to be the meat of burnt-offerings or the meat of peace-offerings, <b>its form</b> must be allowed <b>to decay,</b> i.e., it must be left until it is definitely disqualified, <b>and</b> then <b>it must be taken out to the place of burning,</b> where offerings that have become disqualified are burned. With regard to meat <b>found in the outlying areas,</b> outside of Jerusalem, if it is in the form of whole <b>limbs,</b> the meat presumably comes from <b>carcasses</b> of animals that were not properly slaughtered, for meat unfit for eating was generally cut up into full limbs, to be fed to dogs or sold to gentiles. <b>But</b> if it is in small <b>pieces,</b> it is presumably kosher and <b>permitted</b> to be eaten, as kosher meat was ordinarily cut up into small pieces. <sup><b>[19b]</b></sup> <b>And</b> if meat is found <b>at the time of a Festival, when meat is plentiful,</b> so that it is generally not cut up into small pieces, then <b>even</b> whole <b>limbs are permitted</b> to be eaten.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that money found on the Temple Mount is presumed to be non-sacred money. The Gemara asks: <b>Shouldn’t the mishna</b> have said that the money found <b>on the Temple Mount</b> is treated as <b>consecrated</b> money? Most of the money on the Temple Mount is consecrated money, and even if it were not, one ought to be stringent and regard any found money as such. The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Ba</b> said that <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> There is <b>a presumption that the priest</b> who is in charge <b>does not remove money from the chamber until he desacralizes it</b> by transferring its consecrated status <b>onto the animals</b> that he purchases for offerings. Therefore, any money that is found on the Temple Mount is presumably non-sacred money.
The mishna teaches that <b>meat found</b> in the rest of the city of Jerusalem, cut into whole limbs or small pieces, is presumably from a peace-offering. However, it may not be eaten and its form must be allowed to decay, after which it is burned. The Gemara now cites what <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Hoshaya</b> and a proof for his opinion from the mishna: If <b>one diverted</b> his <b>attention</b> from sacrificial meat such that it was not protected from ritual impurity, even though the meat is disqualified, it is not burned immediately. Rather, it <b>must</b> be left over past the time permitted for its consumption in order that its <b>form decays.</b> <b>Rabbi Hoshaya said: The mishna says like that</b> with regard to meat that was found in Jerusalem, i.e., that <b>its form</b> must first be allowed <b>to decay and</b> then <b>it is taken out to the place of burning.</b> As the meat was lost, clearly its owner’s attention was diverted from it, and the meat became disqualified. The mishna rules that nevertheless it must be kept left over before it is burned, therefore supporting Rabbi Hoshaya’s ruling that meat from which one’s attention was diverted is not burned immediately but must be left over. The disqualification stems from the uncertainty whether the meat became impure, and the meat cannot be burned until its disqualification is certain. <b>Rabbi Yosei said:</b> The <i>halakha</i> of the mishna is indeed <b>correct,</b> but not for this reason. With regard to the meat that one found in Jerusalem, the reason <b>you may not eat it</b> is that <b>perhaps its form had</b> already <b>decayed.</b> Perhaps before it was found the time permitted for eating it had already elapsed. <b>Therefore, one must say</b> that <b>its form must</b> be allowed to <b>decay,</b> lest the allotted time has not elapsed, <b>and</b> only then <b>is it taken out to the place of burning.</b> The requirement that its form decay has nothing to do with the fact that the meat might have become impure. Since the meat was lost in Jerusalem, which is a public domain, it cannot become disqualified because of doubtful ritual impurity, as the principle is that any doubt about ritual impurity that arises in a public domain is declared ritually pure (<i>Teharot</i> 4:7).
The mishna taught that with regard to meat <b>found in the outlying areas,</b> outside of Jerusalem, if it was whole limbs, the meat is presumably that of carcasses and therefore forbidden, but if it was found in small pieces it is presumably kosher and permitted to be eaten. <b>Rabbi Krispa</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina:</b> When the mishna says that <b>whole limbs are</b> presumed to come from <b>carcasses,</b> that presumption is determinate, to the extent that one who eats this meat <b>receives lashes for</b> eating the meat of unslaughtered <b>carcasses.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna</b> itself <b>says this,</b> i.e., the wording of the mishna implies this, for the mishna teaches: With regard to meat found in the outlying areas, outside of Jerusalem, if it is in the form of whole <b>limbs,</b> the meat presumably comes from <b>carcasses. And</b> if it is in small <b>pieces,</b> it is presumably kosher and <b>permitted</b> to be eaten. When the mishna says that <b>small pieces are permitted,</b> does it <b>not</b> mean that they are <b>actually</b> permitted, i.e., the mishna permits the eating of the small pieces of meat and does not regard it as an uncertainty? <b>In a corresponding manner,</b> when the mishna says that meat found in the form of <b>limbs</b> is presumably that of <b>carcasses,</b> this is regarded as a certainty, such that one who eats this meat <b>is flogged for</b> having violated the prohibition against eating the meat of <b>a carcass</b> that was not properly slaughtered.
<b>Rabbi Krispa</b> said further <b>in the name of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> with regard to meat that was found in the form of whole limbs: <b>If</b> the meat <b>was</b> found strung together on strings like <b>beads,</b> it may be presumed that the meat is kosher, and it is therefore <b>permitted.</b> It was further taught in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to meat of an unknown source: If one bought meat in a town that has ten stores, <b>nine stores selling</b> the non-kosher <b>meat of a carcass and one</b> store <b>selling the meat of a</b> properly <b>slaughtered</b> animal, <b>and he was confused about them,</b> not remembering from which store he made his purchase, <b>he must be concerned</b> that perhaps he bought non-kosher meat. However, the meat is forbidden only because of an uncertainty, and if he ate it he is not liable to receive lashes. <b>And</b> if a piece of meat <b>was found</b> in the marketplace and it is not known from which store the meat came, <b>one follows the majority</b> of meat stores. Since in this scenario the majority of stores sell non-kosher meat, the found meat is presumed to be non-kosher. As for the reverse case, if <b>nine stores were selling the meat of a</b> properly <b>slaughtered</b> animal <b>and one</b> store <b>was selling</b> the non-kosher <b>meat of a carcass, and</b> one bought meat in one of the stores but <b>was confused about them,</b> not remembering from which store he made his purchase, <b>he must be concerned</b> that perhaps he bought non-kosher meat. <b>And</b> if a piece of meat <b>was found</b> in the marketplace and it is not known from which store the meat came, <b>one follows the majority</b> of meat stores, and presume that the meat is kosher.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> Meat <b>that is found in the hand of a gentile</b> is regarded <b>as if it were found in a large public square [<i>pelatya</i>],</b> and so its status is determined in accordance with the majority of stores in the town. It is related that <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Ḥaggai, was supporting Rabbi Mana,</b> helping him walk, when <b>he saw a certain Roman [<i>Armai</i>] lopping off</b> a piece of flesh <b>from his horse and taking it out</b> for sale. Rabbi Elazar <b>said to</b> Rabbi Mana: <b>Is this what Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> meant when he <b>said:</b> Meat <b>that is found in the hand of a gentile</b> is regarded <b>as if it were found in a large public square,</b> and so one follows the majority of stores in the town? If so, then if the majority of the town’s stores are owned by Jews, this meat should be treated as kosher. But surely it isn’t, as we saw where it came from. Rabbi Mana <b>said to</b> Rabbi Elazar: <b>Thus said Rabbi Yosei my teacher</b> about Rabbi Yoḥanan’s ruling: <b>This</b> ruling of Rabbi Yoḥanan’s applies only <b>where they saw</b> the gentile <b>exiting from a butcher shop belonging to a Jew</b> with a piece of meat in his hand. It is further related that <b>a certain man in Tzippori was walking</b> and <b>he wanted to buy a piece of meat [<i>kufad</i>] from the butcher, but</b> the butcher <b>did not give him</b> the meat, as they were at odds with each other. The man <b>asked a certain Roman</b> to buy the meat on his behalf, <b>and he brought him</b> the meat. The man later <b>said to</b> the butcher: <b>Did I not</b> in the end <b>take</b> the meat from you <b>against your will?</b> The butcher <b>said to him: Did I not give him the</b> non-kosher <b>meat of a carcass,</b> and so the meat you received from him was not kosher? <b>Rabbi Yirmeya</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥanina: An incident</b> like this <b>came before Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, who <b>said</b> about this: <b>It is not within his power to prohibit all the</b> butcher <b>shops [<i>makkulin</i>] of Tzippori</b> on that day; since the majority of meat shops in that city belong to Jews, the butcher’s claim that he gave the Roman non-kosher meat is not to be believed.
It is related that <b>Rav went down there</b> to Babylonia and <b>saw that they were lenient</b> about meat of unknown origin, <b>and he was stringent with them.</b> The Gemara now reports what it is that Rav saw: <b>A certain man went</b> <sup><b>[20a]</b></sup> and <b>wanted to rinse his hat [<i>askuptei</i>] in the river.</b> While doing so, the piece of kosher meat he was carrying <b>fell and</b> the man <b>went</b> on his way. Afterward, <b>he returned</b> to that very place and found a piece of meat identical to the one that he had dropped, <b>and he wanted to take it. Rav,</b> however, <b>said to him:</b> This meat <b>is forbidden for you</b> to eat, <b>as I</b> can <b>say:</b> It is possible that <b>the river swept away that</b> piece of meat, <b>and brought in its place a different</b> piece of <b>non-kosher meat</b> that had fallen into the water somewhere else. The Gemara relates another incident where Rav was stringent about meat of doubtful provenance. <b>A certain man was walking in the market, carrying a piece of</b> kosher <b>meat. A kite</b> bird <b>came and snatched it from him, and</b> later <b>cast it</b> away elsewhere. The man <b>returned</b> and <b>wanted to take</b> the piece of meat from the place where he saw the bird drop it. <b>Rav,</b> however, <b>said to him:</b> This meat <b>is forbidden for you</b> to eat, <b>as I</b> can <b>say:</b> It is possible the kite <b>was carrying non-kosher meat and</b> that is what <b>it cast</b> away in this place. It then <b>took away the other</b> piece of meat that it snatched from you.
 It is further related that the <b>Gina’ei</b> river once <b>swept away wineskins</b> belonging to Jews, and the wineskins were later found along the riverbank. <b>The incident came before Rabbi Yitzḥak, son of Rabbi Elazar,</b> who <b>said: Let the</b> Jewish <b>wine-dealers</b> come and <b>examine the knots</b> on the wineskins. If they recognize the knots as theirs, the wineskins are presumably theirs, and the wine and the wineskins are permitted.
 It once happened that <b>a</b> wine <b>container [<i>nekonika</i>] was found in the synagogue of the</b> city <b>council [<i>bulei</i>],</b> and <b>the incident came before Rabbi Yirmeya</b> for a ruling. <b>He said: Let the</b> Jewish <b>markers,</b> i.e., those who paint the markings on the containers, <b>examine their work.</b> Only if they recognize it as theirs, is the wine permitted.
It is further related that <b>a roasted goat was</b> once <b>found in the street</b> in the town of <b>Gufta, and</b> the Rabbis <b>permitted it</b> to be eaten <b>based on two considerations:</b> First, <b>because of found property;</b> that is to say, they allowed the finder to keep it. Secondly, <b>because of the majority of traffic,</b> i.e., the majority of people passing through that street were Jews. The Gemara explains: <b>Because of found property, as it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One</b> who rescues an article from a lion, from an enemy army [<i>gayyis</i>], from rocks in the <b>sea [<i>shunit hayam</i>], or from rocks in a river, or from a large</b> public <b>street [<i>isratya</i>], or from a large</b> public <b>square;</b> in all these cases, <b>these</b> articles <b>belong to him,</b> even if they have an identifying mark on them, <b>because the owners have given up hope of</b> ever recovering <b>them.</b> For this reason the Rabbis ruled that the goat belonged to its finder, as it was found on the street. The Rabbis also permitted the goat <b>because of the majority of traffic.</b> Since most of the people who passed through that street were Jews, it stands to reason that a Jew had lost the roast goat, and the Rabbis were not concerned <b>that</b> it was the product of <b>gentile slaughter</b> and therefore not kosher. The Gemara adds that in the end, the goat <b>was found</b> to be <b>from the house of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, and indeed kosher.
The Gemara relates that <b>a wheel of cheese was</b> once <b>found in an inn [<i>pundaka</i>]</b> belonging <b>to Levi, and</b> the Rabbis <b>permitted it</b> to be eaten <b>based on two considerations: Because of found property</b> and <b>because of the majority of traffic.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>Because of found property, as it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who rescues</b> an article <b>from an</b> enemy <b>army, from a lion, from rocks in the sea, or from rocks in a river, from a large</b> public <b>street, or from a large</b> public <b>square;</b> in all these cases, <b>these</b> articles <b>belong to him,</b> even if they have an identifying mark on them, <b>because the owners have given up hope of</b> ever recovering <b>them.</b> The same applies to something that was found in an inn, which is also a public area; its owner presumably gave up hope of getting it back, and so the finder is entitled to keep it for himself. The problem that was overcome <b>because of the majority of traffic,</b> was that perhaps the cheese should have been prohibited <b>because</b> it was <b>the cheese of a gentile.</b> Since the majority of the guests at the inn were Jews, this was not a concern. The Gemara notes that, in the end the wheel of cheese <b>was found</b> to be <b>from</b> the house of <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Yosei,</b> and was indeed kosher.
 <b>Rabbi Mana said in the presence of Rabbi Yosei: But I saw the Rabbis announcing</b> that they had found lost property, even when the item was found in a public area. Rabbi Yosei <b>said to him: If you were to find</b> an article in a public area, <b>you would not take</b> it either. <b>Rabbi Yona, your father, did not say this, but said: If only that when we find</b> some item, <b>we should find it</b> in a place that will not require us to announce its discovery, e.g., <b>from the gate outward,</b> in a public area. The Gemara notes that <b>even so,</b> when Rabbi Yona <b>found</b> a lost article in a crowded public place, <b>he did not take</b> it for himself, but rather announced that he had found it, so that he could locate its original owner.

Halakhah 3

<strong>Halakha 3 · MISHNA</strong> If <b>an animal</b> that is fit for the altar <b>was found</b> straying, <b>from Jerusalem and as far as Migdal Eder, and</b> similarly if it was found <b>within that distance</b> from Jerusalem <b>in any</b> other <b>direction,</b> it is presumed that the animal came from Jerusalem. Most of the animals in Jerusalem were designated for offerings, and presumably this one was as well. <b>Males</b> are presumed to be <b>burnt-offerings,</b> as only males are brought as burnt-offerings. <b>Females</b> are presumed to be <b>peace-offerings,</b> as it is permitted to bring a female peace-offering. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> An animal <b>that is fit for the Paschal offering,</b> i.e., a one-year-old male lamb or kid, is presumed to be <b>a Paschal offering,</b> provided that it was found <b>within thirty days before the Festival</b> of Passover. <b>Originally,</b> the court <b>would seize collateral from one who found</b> such an animal, as security <b>until he would bring with it the libations</b> associated with this offering, as if the found animal were his own and he had committed himself to bring the libations. This brought about a situation in which those who found the animals <b>began leaving them</b> where they found them, <b>and absconding,</b> so as not to become liable for the libations. <b>The court</b> therefore <b>instituted that the libations</b> accompanying these offerings <b>would come from public</b> funds, that is, from the Temple treasury. <b>Rabbi Shimon said: The court instituted seven ordinances</b> with regard to the financial aspects of offerings and consecrations. <b>And this</b> ordinance, namely, that the cost of the libations accompanying the sacrifice of a found animal is borne by the public, <b>is one of them.</b> These are the other ordinances: If <b>a gentile sent his burnt-offering from abroad,</b> outside Eretz Yisrael, <b>and he sent with it</b> money for the purchase of the <b>libations</b> that must accompany it, the libations <b>are offered at his</b> expense. <b>And if</b> the gentile <b>did not</b> cover the cost of the libations, it is a condition of the court that the libations are <b>sacrificed at the public’s</b> expense, with funds taken from the Temple treasury. <b>And likewise,</b> in the case of <b>a convert who died</b> without heirs <b>and left</b> animals that he had designated as <b>offerings. If he has the libations,</b> i.e., if he also had set aside libations or money for that purpose, the libations <b>are sacrificed from his</b> estate. <b>And if</b> he did <b>not</b> do so, the libations <b>are sacrificed from public</b> funds. <b>And</b> another ordinance: <b>It is a condition of the court with regard to a High Priest who died,</b> and a new High Priest had not yet been appointed in his place, <b>that his meal-offering,</b> i.e., the griddle-cake offering that the High Priest would bring each day from one-tenth of an ephah of flour, would be <b>sacrificed from public</b> funds. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> It was brought <b>from</b> the property of the High Priest’s <b>heirs,</b> i.e., his estate, and not from public funds. In any event, the offering was not brought as it would have been brought by the High Priest himself were he still alive, half in the morning and half in the evening, <b>but</b> rather <b>it was sacrificed</b> all at once, from a <b>whole</b> one-tenth of an ephah. The fourth ordinance was <b>about the salt</b> in the Temple that was designated for salting the offerings, <b>and</b> the fifth was <b>about the wood</b> that was used for the burning of the offerings. These ordinances decreed <b>that the priests may use them</b> also to prepare the meat of the offerings that they eat. <b>And</b> the sixth ordinance <b>concerned the</b> red <b>heifer: that</b> deriving benefit <b>from its ashes is not considered misusing</b> consecrated property. <b>And</b> the seventh ordinance was <b>about disqualified pairs</b> of bird-offerings: It ruled <b>that</b> their replacements <b>should come from public</b> funds. <b>Rabbi Yosei</b> disagreed and <b>says:</b> The expense does not fall upon the public, but rather upon <b>whoever supplies</b> all <b>the pairs</b> of bird-offerings to the Temple; <b>he must</b> also <b>supply,</b> at no additional charge, the replacements for <b>the disqualified</b> birds.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that if a male animal was found near Jerusalem, it is presumed to have been consecrated as a burnt-offering. Given that a male animal could just as easily be a peace-offering, and as such, most of its meat is meant to be eaten and it is prohibited to burn it on the altar, how can the found animal be sacrificed as a burnt-offering? In answer to this question, <b>Rabbi Hoshaya Rabba said: They taught</b> the <i>halakha</i> that it is presumed to be a burnt-offering only <b>with regard to one who comes with its money,</b> i.e., the money with which the animal was redeemed. One who finds such an animal near Jerusalem may not bring it as a burnt-offering, as it might be a peace-offering. He must redeem the animal for its monetary value, and in addition he must consecrate the same sum again, from his own money. He then stipulates the following: If the animal is a burnt-offering, let it be redeemed on the first sum, and let the second sum be used for a free-will peace-offering. If this animal is a peace-offering, let it be redeemed on the second sum, and let the first sum be used for a free-will burnt-offering. He then purchases a burnt-offering with the first sum and a peace-offering with the second sum. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said to him: Does one say to a person</b> that he should <b>go out and misuse consecrated animals?</b> If one redeems a consecrated animal that is still fit for the altar, the redemption is valid; the animal is no longer consecrated and the money is now sacred in its place and must be used to buy other offerings. This redemption, however, is valid only after the fact; it is considered misuse to redeem an unblemished animal and prohibited to do so <i>ab initio</i>. <b>Rather,</b> the mishna must be understood differently. <b>In their regard, follow the majority. If the majority</b> of the animals found <b>are males,</b> they are determined to be <b>burnt-offerings,</b> for most male animals brought as offerings are burnt-offerings. And <b>if the majority</b> of the animals found <b>are females,</b> they are determined to be <b>peace-offerings,</b> for most female animals brought as offerings are peace-offerings. The Gemara asks: <b>But are peace-offerings not brought</b> both <b>from males and from females?</b> Therefore, it is possible that the male animals that were found are not burnt-offerings, but rather peace-offerings. Rather, Rabbi Yoḥanan’s words must be understood as follows: If one finds animals near Jerusalem, even though they are presumed to be offerings, he cannot sacrifice the animals themselves on the altar. <b>What</b> then <b>does he do? He must</b> wait until the animals develop blemishes and only then redeem them, and thereby <b>remove</b> their consecrated status and restore them <b>to their non-sacred state. He then goes back and</b> ensures that he makes them <sup><b>[20b]</b></sup> <b>burnt-offerings.</b> He does this by taking two unblemished animals and stipulating as follows: If the animal that was found was a burnt-offering, let the first animal be a burnt-offering in its place, and let the second be a peace-offering. And if the animal that was found was a peace-offering, let the second animal be peace-offering, and let the first be a free-will burnt-offering. <b>Rabbi Zeira</b> offers a different explanation of the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna: Male animals found near Jerusalem are sacrificed as burnt-offerings, even though they may have been designated as peace-offerings. He <b>says: Just as you say there</b> (19a) in the name of Reish Lakish that <b>it is a condition of the court</b> that stipulates <b>with regard to funds left over</b> from what had originally been consecrated for sin-offerings that they <b>are brought as burnt-offerings; so too, you say here</b> that <b>it is a condition of the court with regard to</b> animals that were <b>lost</b> and later found, that even though they may be peace-offerings, they <b>are brought as burnt-offerings.</b> <b>Rabbi Yosei said to Rabbi Yaakov bar Aḥa: Is this not</b> a case of <b>intentional</b> change of an offering’s purpose? A condition of the court can allocate leftover money that had originally been consecrated for sin-offerings to the purchase of a burnt-offering, but that does not mean that one can intentionally change the sanctity of the animal itself from that of a peace-offering to that of a burnt-offering. Rabbi Yaakov bar Aḥa <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yosei: <b>Since it is a condition of the court, it is not</b> considered a case of <b>intentional</b> change of an animal’s purpose. Even if the animal had initially been consecrated as a peace-offering, that consecration is regarded as conditional in accordance with the court’s stipulation. Consequently, the animal can now be sacrificed as a burnt-offering <i>ab initio</i>.
§ Since the mishna mentioned the griddle-cake offering of a High Priest who had died, the Gemara records what <b>Rabbi Yesa said</b> about similar matters: <b>While I was there</b> in Babylonia, <b>I heard the voice of Rav Yehuda asking Shmuel:</b> If one <b>set aside his shekel and</b> then <b>died</b> before handing it over to the treasurer, what is to be done with the shekel? Shmuel <b>said to him: It is allocated to free-will offerings</b> and is used for the purchase of free-will burnt-offerings. It was further asked: What is to be done with <b>leftover</b> money that a High Priest set aside for the purchase of his griddle-cake offering made from <b>one-tenth of an ephah</b> of flour? If a High Priest designated money for the purchase of flour for his meal-offering and it turns out that he set aside too much money, what is to be done with the extra money? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The money <b>goes to the Dead Sea,</b> meaning, it is destroyed. <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> disagreed and <b>said:</b> The money <b>is allocated to free-will offerings.</b>
The Gemara continues its discussion of the High Priest’s griddle-cake offering. The mishna in <i>Menaḥot</i> 4:5 teaches that it was divided in two; half of it was sacrificed in the morning and half in the evening. The Gemara records a dispute among the <i>amora’im</i>: With regard to <b>the High Priest’s one-tenth of an ephah, Rabbi Yoḥanan said: He divides it</b> into two equal portions in two non-sacred vessels, <b>and afterward he consecrates</b> each half by itself before it is sacrificed. <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish said: He must</b> first <b>consecrate</b> the entire one-tenth of an ephah, <b>and</b> only <b>afterward does he divide it</b> into two equal portions. The Gemara comments: <b>A mishna</b> seems to <b>disagree with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> as the mishna (<i>Menaḥot</i> 4:5) teaches that if the High Priest sacrificed half of his griddle-cake offering in the morning and then died, and another High Priest was appointed in his place, the successor does not bring the second half that evening. Instead <b>he</b> consecrates a whole one-tenth of an ephah and divides it into two equal portions. He then <b>sacrifices</b> one <b>half, and the</b> second <b>half is destroyed.</b> According to Rabbi Yoḥanan, who says that the High Priest consecrates each half by itself, why must the second half be destroyed? It had never been consecrated for the altar, and so it can still be redeemed. Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>solves it,</b> i.e., resolves the difficulty: The mishna maintains that even though the second half is consecrated only for its value and not for the altar, it still must be destroyed. <b>As even</b> what is left over from the <b>money</b> that a High Priest set aside for the purchase of his griddle-cake offering <b>goes to the Dead Sea,</b> although it had never been consecrated for the altar. The Gemara notes that <b>the</b> same <b>mishna</b> also seems to <b>disagree with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish.</b> The mishna teaches that in the aforementioned case where the High Priest dies before sacrificing the second half of his griddle-cake offering and is succeeded on the same day, <b>it turns out that two halves</b> of the griddle-cake offering <b>are sacrificed,</b> i.e., the first half from the deceased High Priest and the first half from his successor, <b>and two halves are destroyed,</b> i.e., the second half of each offering. <b>And it was taught about</b> this mishna in a <i>baraita</i>: The <b>half of the first</b> High Priest <b>and the half of the second</b> High Priest that are destroyed, i.e., that which is left over once each of them has sacrificed half of one-tenth of an ephah, <b>their form</b> must first be allowed <b>to decay,</b> i.e., they must be left overnight until they are disqualified, <b>and</b> only then <b>are they taken out to the place of burning.</b> According to the opinion of Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish that the High Priest consecrates for the altar the entire one-tenth of an ephah before dividing it, there is no reason to wait for the form to decay. The principle is that if anything becomes intrinsically disqualified such that it cannot be sacrificed, it is burned immediately (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 34b). Therefore, it should be possible to immediately burn each disqualified, leftover half from each High Priest, and not wait for their forms to decay. Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish <b>solves</b> this difficulty by arguing that the mishna, and his own opinion, which is an interpretation of the mishna, does not accord with the <i>baraita</i> but rather <b>with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael</b> (<i>Menaḥot</i> 90a), <b>who says</b> that <b>the one-tenth</b> of an ephah <b>measure</b> is regarded as a sacred service vessel that <b>consecrates</b> its contents. The flour is therefore consecrated for the altar at the original measuring of the one-tenth of the ephah. Accordingly, the remaining half of each one-tenth of an ephah is destroyed immediately without decay of form. The <i>baraita</i>, on the other hand, does not consider the one-tenth of an ephah measure to be a sacred service vessel, and therefore the measure does not consecrate the flour. As long as the remaining halves have not been consecrated for the altar, they are consecrated only for their value and require decay of form before they are burned. § As the topic of the griddle-cake offering brought each day by the High Priest has been raised, the Gemara elaborates on it and on the inaugural meal-offering brought by each and every priest the first time that he serves in the Temple. It was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>When a priest approaches</b> the altar <b>for the first time to</b> perform <b>the service, he brings his</b> meal-offering from <b>one-tenth of an ephah</b> of flour <b>and he performs the service himself,</b> and in this way he inaugurates himself for service. Nevertheless, for <b>both a High Priest and an ordinary priest who performed the</b> sacrificial <b>service before bringing their</b> meal-offerings from <b>one-tenth of an ephah</b> of flour as their inaugural meal-offerings, <b>their service is valid,</b> as the absence of a priest’s inaugural meal-offerings does not invalidate his service. <b>Rabbi Mana wanted to say</b> the following <i>halakha</i>: If <b>on the same day that</b> a priest <b>approached</b> the altar <b>to</b> perform <b>the service for the first time, on that same day he was</b> also <b>appointed to be the High Priest, he must bring two</b> meal-offerings, each one-tenth of an ephah of flour; <b>one for his inaugural</b> meal-offering <b>and one for the obligation of the day,</b> i.e., for the griddle-cake offering that the High Priest must bring every day.
The Gemara records another <i>halakha</i> with regard to the daily griddle-cake offering brought by the High Priest. Concerning this offering, the verse states: “On a griddle it shall be made with oil; when it is soaked, you shall bring it in; and <b>baked pieces</b> of the meal-offering shall you offer for a sweet savor unto the Lord” (Leviticus 6:14). The juxtaposition of the words “you shall bring it in” to the words “and baked pieces” teaches that <b>at the time</b> that the meal-offering <b>is brought,</b> it is made into <b>baked pieces,</b> i.e., during that same day, <b>and not before daybreak</b> is it made into <b>baked pieces.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Tamid</i> 1:3) that every day before the removal of the ashes from the altar, which was before daybreak, <b>they assigned whoever made the griddle-cake offerings to make,</b> i.e., bake, <b>griddle-cakes? Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Aḥa said:</b> All that they assigned him to do was <b>to heat water for the boiling</b> of the dough as part of the preparation of the griddle-cakes for the day. Having mentioned the baking of the High Priest’s griddle-cake offering, the Gemara addresses the various stages in the preparation of the offering. The verse implies that the offering must be fried, as it says: “On a griddle it shall be made with oil”; it also indicates that it must be scalded in water, as it says: “When it is soaked”; and it further teaches that it must be baked, as it says: “And <b>baked pieces</b> of the meal-offering shall you offer for a sweet savor unto the Lord.” As for the order of these procedures, <b>Rabbi Yesa</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥanina: He</b> first <b>fries it, and afterward he bakes it.
