Rabbi Aḥa</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥanina: He</b> first <b>bakes it, and</b> only <b>afterward does he fry it.</b> The Gemara notes that this dispute depends on another tannaitic dispute, as it was taught in a <i>baraita</i> that says with regard to the High Priest’s griddle-cake offering: “And <b>baked pieces [<i>tufinei</i>]</b> of the meal-offering” (Leviticus 6:14). This word is interpreted as <b>it shall be baked when</b> it is already <b>half done [<i>te’afena na</i>],</b> meaning that it is fried before it is baked. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> The word for baked pieces <b>[<i>tufinei</i>]</b> should be understood as meaning <b>it must be baked when</b> still <b>beautiful [<i>te’afena na’a</i>],</b> i.e., before it is fried. <b>Rabbi Dosa says: It shall be baked in an amplified</b> manner, i.e., more than once. The Gemara explains: <b>These disputes follow,</b> i.e., match, <b>those disputes</b> mentioned earlier. <b>The one who says,</b> i.e., understands <i>tufinei</i> to mean that <b>it must be baked when</b> still <b>beautiful [<i>te’afena na’a</i>] is like the one who says</b> that <b>he</b> first <b>bakes it, and afterwards fries it. And the one who says</b> <i>tufinei</i> should be interpreted as baked <b>when half done [<i>te’afena na</i>] is like the one who says</b> that <b>he</b> first <b>fries it, and</b> only <b>afterward does he bake it.</b>
The Gemara above cited the mishna in tractate <i>Menaḥot</i> that teaches that in the event of the death of the High Priest after having brought his morning griddle-cake offering, if another High Priest is appointed that same day, the successor brings one-tenth of an ephah of his own, divides it into two, and offers one half for the evening griddle-cake offering. The Gemara clarifies this matter: <b>Is this not the end of it,</b> that is to say, is the <i>halakha</i> not restricted to the case <b>where</b> the High Priest <b>died? Rather, even if</b> he <b>became</b> ritually <b>impure</b> after having brought the morning griddle-cake offering, another High Priest must bring a whole one-tenth of an ephah, divide it into two equal portions, and then sacrifice one half as the evening griddle-cake offering. And all the more so is this true, in accordance with this ruling, if the first High Priest was permanently disqualified because he became disfigured by a blemish. The Gemara suggests an alternative: The mishna’s ruling extends <b>even</b> to cases where the first High Priest is found <b>unfit because of a</b> permanent <b>blemish.</b> This scenario would require his successor to bring a new one-tenth of an ephah. However, in accordance with this ruling, temporary disqualification due to ritual impurity would not require the temporary High Priest to bring a separate meal-offering. <b>Rabbi Yehuda bar Pazzi of Bar Delaya taught: Even</b> the first High Priest being found <b>unfit because of a</b> permanent <b>blemish</b> would require his successor to bring a new one-tenth of an ephah. However, if the first High Priest became ritually impure and only temporarily suspended from his position, the second High Priest may use the first High Priest’s leftover half-tenth of an ephah for the evening griddle-cake offering. The mishna records a dispute among <i>tanna’im</i> with regard to the case of a High Priest who died. The first opinion is that until a new High Priest has been appointed, the griddle-cake offering is brought from public funds. Rabbi Yehuda disagrees, and argues that it should come from the property of the High Priest’s heirs. The Gemara recounts a <i>baraita</i> about this issue: <b>From where is it derived that</b> if <b>the High Priest died and another</b> High Priest <b>had not</b> yet <b>been appointed in his place, that his meal-offering should be brought from</b> the property of <b>the</b> High Priest’s <b>heirs? The verse states</b> with regard to this offering: “And the anointed priest that shall be in his stead <b>from among his sons shall bring it”</b> (Leviticus 6:15). That is to say, he sacrifices it from the property of the sons of the High Priest. I <b>might</b> have thought that the heirs <b>should bring</b> this meal-offering <b>in halves,</b> half in the morning and half in the evening, as it is ordinarily brought by the High Priest. Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “Shall bring <b>it”</b> (Leviticus 6:15); <b>I said</b> that he should bring <b>all of it</b> as a single offering, and not in halves. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b> <b>Rabbi Shimon says: It comes only from public</b> funds collected in the Temple treasury, <b>as it is stated:</b> “It is a <b>statute forever</b> to the Lord” (Leviticus 6:15), to be brought from <b>those with whom a covenant was made,</b> i.e., from the community with whom God entered into a covenant. The verse continues: <b>“It shall be wholly burnt,”</b> from which it may be derived that the griddle-cake offering is <b>to be burnt in its entirety.</b> In contrast to most meal-offerings, a handful of flour is not separated to be burned on the altar and no part of it is eaten by the priests, but rather the entire offering is burned on the altar.
Returning to the dispute with regard to a <b>High Priest who died, etc.,</b> whether the griddle-cake offering is brought from the his estate or comes from public funds, <b>Rabbi Ba bar Memel,</b> in the context of a parallel discussion (<i>Menaḥot</i> 51b), <b>raised a difficulty:</b> It would seem that the attribution of <b>the opinion of Rabbi Shimon is reversed. There,</b> that is, according to the mishna in <i>Shekalim</i>, <b>he says</b> that the original, Torah law was that the griddle-cake offering should be brought <b>from</b> the property of the late High Priest’s <b>heirs.</b> This is indicated by the fact that according to Rabbi Shimon, the griddle-cake offering is brought from public funds only by rabbinic ordinance as a condition of the court, implying that prior to the institution of the ordinance, the <i>halakha</i> was that it be brought from the late High Priest’s estate. <b>Whereas here,</b> that is, in the <i>baraita</i> above and in tractate <i>Menaḥot</i>, Rabbi Shimon <b>says</b> that by Torah law it comes <b>from public</b> funds, as according to his opinion it is derived from the wording of the Torah: “It is a statute forever.” <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya</b> <sup><b>[21a]</b></sup> <b>bar Ba said: Isn’t the difficulty</b> raised by Rabbi Ba bar Memel <b>legitimate?</b> The Gemara answers that when <b>Rabbi Yaakov bar Aḥa came,</b> he said that <b>Rabbi Abbahu</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> In the case where the High Priest has died and not yet been replaced, <b>by Torah law,</b> the griddle-cake offering <b>comes from public</b> funds, as is derived from the words: “It is a statute forever.” Rabbi Shimon did not mean to imply otherwise. As it is not a public offering but the High Priest’s personal one, <b>I might have said</b> that it should be <b>collected from the heirs’</b> property. Therefore, to ensure compliance with the Torah law, the Sages <b>instituted that it should come from the collection of the</b> Temple treasury <b>chamber.</b>
§ The mishna taught that a griddle-cake offering that was brought in the interim between the tenures of two High Priests was not brought in halves, morning and evening. Instead, a whole one-tenth of an ephah was sacrificed. <b>Rabbi Yosei said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan raised a dilemma: What</b> does this mean? Is a <b>whole</b> one-tenth of an ephah brought <b>in the morning, and</b> then a <b>whole</b> one-tenth of an ephah again <b>in the evening? Or</b> was a <b>whole</b> one-tenth of an ephah brought <b>in the morning,</b> and since they already brought one-tenth of an ephah that day, it <b>was canceled in the evening?</b> <b>When</b> that question <b>was resolved for him,</b> it gave rise to other questions. The original question was resolved by reference to the verse, <b>as it is written:</b> “This is the offering of Aaron and of his sons, which they shall offer unto the Lord in the day when he is anointed: The tenth part of an ephah of fine flour for a <b>meal-offering perpetually,</b> half of it in the morning, and half thereof in the evening” (Leviticus 6:13). The word “perpetually” indicates that the griddle-cake offering is always brought both in the morning and in the evening. A further question: <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the <b>three <i>log</i></b> of oil that the High Priest would bring every day together with his griddle-cake offering, half in the morning and half in the evening? Is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the oil the same, i.e., that <b>three <i>log</i></b> of oil were brought in the <b>morning and three <i>log</i></b> of oil were brought again <b>in the evening? Or</b> was only <b>a <i>log</i> and a half</b> brought in the <b>morning and</b> another <b><i>log</i> and a half</b> brought <b>in the evening?</b> <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya said</b> that Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>also wonders about this: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the <b>two handfuls</b> of frankincense that were ordinarily brought every day together with the High Priest’s griddle-cake offering, one with the morning offering and the other together with the evening offering? Is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the frankincense the same as that of the griddle-cake offering, i.e., that <b>two handfuls</b> of frankincense were brought <b>in the morning and two handfuls</b> of frankincense were brought again <b>in the evening? Or</b> was only <b>one handful</b> brought <b>in the morning and one handful</b> brought <b>in the evening?</b> <b>Rabbi Yosei said: Didn’t they learn</b> (18b) that the amount of frankincense brought along with a meal-offering must be <b>a handful</b> by way of analogy <b>to</b> the handful of flour that must be taken <b>from a sinner’s meal-offering?</b> This being the case, the <i>halakha</i> in the issue under discussion should also be learned from the <i>halakha</i> governing meal-offerings in general. <b>Just as there,</b> when the quantity of flour is doubled <b>two handfuls</b> of frankincense are required, <b>here too,</b> since two-tenths of an ephah are brought, the measure of the frankincense must also be doubled, that is to say, <b>two handfuls</b> are required. The Gemara rejects this proof: Whether the measure of frankincense is doubled when a meal-offering is doubled is not completely clear: <b>Just as there,</b> with regard to meal-offerings in general, <b>he requires</b> a resolution, i.e., he does not know the answer as to whether a double measure of frankincense is required when the quantity of flour is doubled, <b>so too here,</b> with regard to the High Priest’s griddle-cake offering, <b>he requires</b> a resolution as to whether a double measure is required. <b>Rabbi Ḥizkiya</b> addresses the previous question about the three <i>log</i> of oil brought as part of the High Priest’s griddle-cake offering. He <b>said: Didn’t they learn</b> that <b>three <i>log</i></b> of oil must be brought along with the griddle-cake offering <b>from the daily evening offering,</b> as this is the measure of oil that must be brought as part of this offering? Accordingly, <b>just as there,</b> in the case of the daily evening offering the measure of oil is <b>three <i>log</i></b> per one-tenth of an ephah of flour, <b>so too</b> in the case where the griddle-cake offering is brought from a whole one-tenth of an ephah, it is accompanied by <b>three <i>log</i></b> of oil both in the morning and in the evening. The Gemara rejects this proof as well: <b>And just as there,</b> when the daily meal offering is doubled <b>he requires</b> a resolution with regard to the measure of oil, <b>here too</b> with regard to the High Priest’s griddle-cake offering, <b>he requires</b> a resolution as to whether only the original three <i>log</i> are brought, or whether three <i>log</i> are brought for each measure of one-tenth of an ephah of flour.
§ The mishna taught that one who derives benefit from the ashes of a red heifer <b>is not</b> liable for misusing property consecrated to the Temple. <b>Rabbi Shimon bar Naḥman</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yonatan: By</b> Torah <b>law</b> one <b>should be liable for misusing it,</b> i.e., the ashes of a red heifer, as it is consecrated to the Temple, <b>but</b> the Sages <b>decreed that one is not liable.</b> This, however, is difficult, <b>as it was taught</b> otherwise in the following <i>baraita</i>: With regard to a red heifer, the verse states: “It is <b>a sin-offering”</b> (Numbers 19:9), which <b>teaches</b> that a red heifer is treated like a sin-offering in that <b>one is liable for misusing</b> consecrated property <b>with it,</b> i.e., the animal itself. Only <b>with it,</b> the animal itself, is one <b>liable for misusing</b> consecrated property, <b>but he is not liable for misusing</b> consecrated property <b>with its ashes.</b> Therefore, it is clear from the <i>baraita</i> that by Torah law one is not liable for misusing the ashes of a red heifer. <b>Rabbi Abbahu said:</b> Indeed, there is no liability by Torah law for misusing the ashes of a red heifer. <b>At first,</b> however, the priests would treat the sanctity of the ashes lightly and <b>would clean themselves [<i>mishtakshekin</i>] with them and</b> even <b>apply them to their wounds</b> as medicine. <b>And</b> the Sages therefore <b>decreed that one becomes liable for misusing</b> consecrated property <b>with them,</b> the ashes. But <b>when</b> they saw that the priests <b>restrained themselves</b> and no longer used the ashes for anything except purification water, the Sages reverted to the Torah law and <b>decreed that one does not become liable for misusing</b> consecrated property if he derives benefit from a red heifer’s ashes.
§ The mishna taught, <b>with regard to</b> the replacements for <b>disqualified pairs</b> of bird-offerings, that they should come from public funds. Rabbi Yosei disagreed and said that whoever supplies all the pairs of bird-offerings to the Temple must also supply, at no extra charge, the replacements for the disqualified birds. Having mentioned the pairs of bird-offerings, the Gemara repeats the discussion from earlier in the chapter. The mishna there (19a) taught that if money was found between the horn marked pairs of bird-offerings and the horn marked doves for burnt-offerings, it is allocated to doves for burnt-offerings, and is used exclusively for that purpose. The Gemara asks: If so, <b>how does this woman,</b> who had placed the money for her offering in the horn marked pairs of bird-offerings, <b>achieve atonement?</b> A woman who gave birth or is a <i>zava</i> must bring a pair of bird-offerings, one as a burnt-offering and the other as a sin-offering, as part of her purification process. If that money has now been allocated to doves for burnt-offerings, she has not completed the process. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak said: It is a condition of the court that</b> whoever <b>supplies</b> the Temple with <b>the pairs</b> of birds, <b>he</b> also <b>supplies</b> extra birds to replace those birds that are <b>disqualified or lost.</b> Therefore, in any event the woman will have brought both a burnt-offering and a sin-offering. 

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>Halakha 1 · MISHNA</strong> The mishna discusses the ritual purity of items found either in the Temple or in Jerusalem and its environs, in continuation of the previous chapter’s discussion of found money, animals, or meat. <b>All the spittle that is found in Jerusalem is ritually pure.</b> Since neither ritually impure people nor gentiles were commonly present in Jerusalem, the Sages decreed an exception to the rule that spittle that is found is ritually impure since it presumably comes from one of those groups. This is the case <b>except for</b> spittle found <b>in the upper marketplace,</b> where gentiles and ritually impure Jews were likely to be present. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b> <b>Rabbi Yosei says: On all the other days of the year,</b> i.e., any day that is not on one of the three pilgrim Festivals, Passover, <i>Shavuot</i>, and <i>Sukkot</i>, spittle <b>that</b> is found <b>in the middle</b> of the street is <b>ritually impure,</b> and spittle <b>that</b> is found on <b>the sides</b> of the street is <b>ritually pure.</b> According to Rabbi Yosei, it was common for people who were ritually impure to be present in the streets of Jerusalem. They would be careful to walk in the middle of the street, while the ritually pure who wished to remain so would walk on the sides. Therefore, it is reasonable to presume that spittle found in the middle of the street is from one who is impure, while spittle found on the side of the street is from one who is pure. <b>But during the time of the Festival,</b> when most of the people in Jerusalem were there for the Festival and were ritually pure, the spittle found <b>in the middle</b> of the street was <b>ritually pure,</b> and that found on <b>the sides</b> of the street was <b>ritually impure.</b> The difference is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> at the time of the Festival, <b>the</b> ritually impure <b>minority moves to the sides</b> of the streets. The mishna continues: <b>All the vessels that are found in Jerusalem</b> on the <b>way down into the bathhouse,</b> wherein one purifies vessels in a ritual bath, are <b>ritually impure, and</b> those that are found on the <b>way up</b> are <b>ritually pure.</b> The mishna explains: <b>Their descent</b> into the bathhouse <b>is not</b> by the same route <b>as their ascent</b> out of it, and it can be assumed that those found on the way down have not yet been immersed, while those found on the way up have been. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b> However, <b>Rabbi Yosei says: They are all ritually pure, except for the basket, and the shovel, and the <i>meritza</i>, which are specifically used for graves,</b> to gather up the bones of the dead. These tools must be presumed to be ritually impure, but in general, vessels are presumed to be pure. The mishna continues with another ruling about ritual purity: One may <b>slaughter immediately with a knife that was found on the fourteenth</b> of Nisan, i.e., the day the Paschal lamb is slaughtered, and need not be concerned that it is ritually impure. Presumably it was immersed the day before so that it could be used to slaughter the Paschal offering. If he found it <b>on the thirteenth</b> of Nisan, he <b>immerses</b> it <b>again.</b> Perhaps its owners had not yet immersed it, since they still had time to do so before the evening. If one finds a <b>cleaver [<i>kofitz</i>],</b> which is used to slaughter an animal and break its bones, whether it was <b>on this</b> day, i.e., the fourteenth, or <b>on that</b> day, i.e., the thirteenth, <b>he immerses</b> it <b>again</b> out of doubt. Since breaking the bones of the Paschal lamb is prohibited, its owners would have no need for it on the fourteenth, and it cannot be presumed that it has already been immersed to make it ritually pure. However, if the <b>fourteenth occurs on Shabbat,</b> he may <b>slaughter with</b> the cleaver <b>immediately.</b> Since immersing a vessel is prohibited on Shabbat, and presumably the owner of the cleaver wants it to be ritually pure on the fifteenth, one can assume that he immersed it already on Friday, the thirteenth of Nisan. It is therefore ritually pure. <sup><b>[21b]</b></sup> If the cleaver was found <b>on the fifteenth</b> of Nisan, i.e., if it was found on the Festival itself, he may <b>slaughter with it immediately.</b> The owners of the cleaver would have immersed it so that they could use it on the fifteenth to cut up the bones of a Festival peace-offering. If the cleaver <b>was found attached to a knife, it is like a knife,</b> i.e., if it was found on the thirteenth of Nisan it is presumed impure, and if it was found on the fourteenth he may slaughter with it immediately, as it was certainly immersed on the day before.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna taught that according to Rabbi Meir, all spittle that is found in Jerusalem is ritually pure, except if it were found in the upper marketplace. With regard to this, <b>Rabbi Avin in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> explains: <b>A fortress [<i>katzran</i>] of gentiles was there</b> in the upper marketplace. The Sages decreed that gentiles have the ritual impurity of a <i>zav</i> (see <i>Shabbat</i> 17b and <i>Nidda</i> 34a), therefore their spittle is impure. The Roman soldiers were gentiles, so any spittle found in the upper marketplace was presumably theirs. The Gemara recounts an incident about this fortress. <b>Rabbi Ḥanina said:</b> Once <b>they were killing wild donkeys in Jerusalem,</b> to feed the lions in the circus (see <i>Menaḥot</i> 103b), <b>and the pilgrims</b> coming to Jerusalem to celebrate <b>the Festival were wading in blood up to their ankles</b> from the large amount of blood coming from the wild donkeys. <b>And they came before the Sages</b> to find out if they had been rendered ritually impure, <b>and they,</b> the Sages, <b>did not say anything,</b> as the blood of an animal carcass does not render one ritually impure, even though the carcass itself does. Where did this story take place? <b>Rabbi Shimon bar Abba</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Ḥanina: There was a gentile,</b> i.e., Roman, <b>fortress there,</b> in the upper marketplace of Jerusalem, and that was where they were killing the wild donkeys.
Apropos the ritual impurity of blood from an animal carcass, the Gemara recounts that. <b>Rabbi Simon</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi:</b> There was <b>an incident</b> with regard to <b>a mule from Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi’s <b>household that died, and</b> the Sages <b>ruled its blood to be ritually pure</b> and not subject to the impurity imparted by an animal <b>carcass.</b> On this topic, <b>Rabbi Elazar asked Rabbi Simon: Up to how much</b> blood from an animal carcass does not render one ritually impure? <b>And he did not answer him. He</b> then went and <b>asked Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi.</b> Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi, <b>said to him: Up to</b> the size of a <b>quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>is ritually pure; more than that is ritually impure. And Rabbi Elazar was annoyed that Rabbi Simon did not respond with the <i>halakha</i></b> that the minimal size for ritual impurity is a quarter-<i>log</i>, as he certainly heard it from Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi. The Gemara continues: <b>Rav Beivai was sitting</b> and <b>teaching this story</b> of the mule from Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s household, in which the Sages ruled that the blood of a carcass does not render one impure. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Bisna said to him: Up to how much</b> blood from an animal carcass does not render one ritually impure? Rav Beivai <b>said to him: Up to</b> a <b>quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>is ritually pure; more than that is impure. And</b> then <b>he kicked him</b>. <b>Rabbi Zerika said to</b> Rav Beivai: <b>You kicked him because he asked you a question? He said to</b> Rabbi Zerika: <b>Because my mind was unsettled,</b> and not because he did anything wrong. <b>As Rabbi Ḥanin said</b> in expounding the verse: “And your life shall hang in doubt before you; and you shall fear night and day, and shall have no assurance of your life” (Deuteronomy 28:66). <b>“And your life shall hang in doubt before you”; this is one who buys for himself wheat for a year,</b> who has no financial security with regard to the following year. <b>“And you shall fear night and day”; this</b> is one who is so poor that <b>he buys</b> wheat <b>from the storekeeper</b> a bit at a time, with the attendant concern that he might not have enough for the morrow. <b>“And shall have no assurance of your life”; this is one who</b> is so poor <b>he buys from the baker</b> and cannot afford to buy wheat in advance to assure even one future meal. <b>And I rely on the baker,</b> i.e., I am on this lowest level of poverty, and therefore I do not have the presence of mind to answer his questions. The Gemara asks: <b>What then</b> is the halakhic ruling regarding the ritual impurity of the blood of an animal carcass? In response, the Gemara quotes a mishna (<i>Eduyyot</i> 8:1): <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Petora testified that the blood of animal carcasses is ritually pure,</b> which implies that it is ritually pure regardless of the amount, even more than a quarter-<i>log</i>. The Gemara rejects this answer: <b>What</b> does the mishna mean by <b>ritually pure?</b> That such blood is <b>ritually pure</b> in that it does not <b>render</b> an item <b>susceptible</b> to impurity. Even though blood is one of the seven liquids that render an item susceptible to ritual impurity, the blood of an animal carcass is not deemed blood for this purpose. <b>Nevertheless, with regard to conferring impurity,</b> such blood <b>does render something ritually impure.</b> The Gemara asks: Didn’t <b>we learn</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Makhshirin</i> 6:5): <b>The blood of a creeping animal confers ritual impurity, as does its flesh? It confers impurity, but does not render an item susceptible</b> to impurity, <b>and we have nothing</b> else <b>like it</b> that confers impurity but does not render something susceptible to impurity. The implication is that the blood of an animal carcass would either both confer impurity and render an item susceptible to impurity or do neither. The Gemara answers: When the mishna said that we have nothing else like it, it meant that <b>we have nothing else like it with regard to the measure</b> required for this substance <b>to confer impurity.</b> The measure of both the blood and the flesh of a creeping animal required for conferring impurity is a lentil-bulk. Creeping animals are unique in this regard, since the measures of blood and flesh that confer impurity from an animal carcass are different. An olive-bulk of flesh is sufficient to confer impurity, while a quarter-<i>log</i> of blood is required. <b>However,</b> the Gemara concludes, <b>its blood,</b> i.e., that of an animal carcass, nevertheless <b>confers impurity like its flesh.</b> <b>Rabbi Yosei said: Two <i>amora’im</i> disputed this</b> point, whether the blood of an animal carcass confers ritual impurity. <b>One said</b> a quarter-<i>log</i> of this blood renders one <b>ritually impure, and one said</b> that even after contact with this blood, one remains <b>ritually pure.</b> <b>The one who said ritually impure</b> holds <b>like the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda</b> as it appears in a mishna (<i>Eduyyot</i> 5:1). Rabbi Yehuda asserts there that this issue is the subject of a dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel. The <i>halakha</i> would follow the opinion of Beit Hillel, which is that carcass blood does confer impurity. <b>And the one who said ritually pure</b> holds <b>like</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Petora,</b> who testified (<i>Eduyyot</i> 8:1) that the blood of an animal carcass is ritually pure. It appears that this <i>amora</i> interprets Rabbi Yehoshua ben Petora’s statement literally, implying that even more than a quarter-<i>log</i> of blood does not confer impurity. <b>Rav Avduma of the descenders,</b> i.e., travelers from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, bringing with them the Torah taught in Eretz Yisrael, <b>said to him: And it is right,</b> that which you explained is correct, <b>as Rabbi Yehuda was the halakhic authority for the house of the <i>Nasi</i>.</b> Rabbi Yehuda ruled then that the blood of the dead mule was pure only because there was less than a quarter-<i>log</i> of it.
§ The mishna states that according to Rabbi Meir’s opinion, <b>all the spittle</b> found in Jerusalem is pure, except for spittle found in the upper marketplace. The Gemara asks: <b>Didn’t Rabbi Abbahu say this in the name of Rabbi Yosei ben Ḥanina:</b> The Sages <b>did not decree the spittle</b> found <b>in Jerusalem</b> to be ritually impure? If so, why is the spittle found in the upper marketplace ritually impure? The Gemara answers: <b>But wasn’t it stated with regard to</b> that marketplace that <b>Rabbi Avin</b> said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi: A fortress of gentiles was there,</b> and since the Sages decreed that gentiles have the ritual impurity of a <i>zav</i>, any spittle found there must be from them and therefore is ritually impure. The mishna also states Rabbi Yosei’s opinion that for most of the year, all spittle found in the middle of the street was impure and spittle found on the sides was pure; during the pilgrim Festival, the spittle in the middle was pure and that on the sides was impure. The Gemara discusses a <i>baraita</i> that explains this opinion: <b>On the rest of the days of the year, the ritually impure proceed</b> down the middle of the street <b>in a group, and the pure proceed on the side</b> to avoid contact that would render them impure. <b>And the ritually pure proceed ordinarily</b> and don’t warn the ritually impure not to touch them, while the <b>impure</b> are the ones who <b>say to</b> those who are ritually pure: <b>Stay away. During the period of the pilgrim Festival the ritually pure proceed in a group</b> in the middle of the street <b>and the impure proceed on the side</b> of the street. <b>The impure proceed ordinarily</b> and do not warn the ritually pure not to touch them, <b>and the ritually pure</b> are the ones who <b>say to</b> those who are impure: <b>Stay away,</b> i.e., be careful not to touch us and render us impure.
 § The mishna further states: <b>And all the vessels that are found</b> in Jerusalem, if they are found on the way down to the <b>bathhouse,</b> where one purifies vessels in a ritual bath, they are ritually impure, and if they are found on the way up, they are ritually pure. The Gemara asks: <b>Didn’t Rabbi Abbahu say this in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> The Sages <b>did not decree</b> ritual impurity <b>with regard to the vessels</b> found in <b>Jerusalem?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Since they were found on the way down to the bathhouse, it becomes</b> as if it had been conclusively <b>demonstrated</b> that they are ritually impure, for one does not bring a vessel to be immersed if it is not ritually impure.
 The mishna also states that Rabbi Yosei is of the opinion that all vessels that are found in Jerusalem are ritually pure, except for the basket, the shovel, and the <i>meritza</i>, which are specifically used for graves. The Gemara recounts that <b>Abba Shaul,</b> who was a gravedigger and regularly used such tools, <b>would call them,</b> the tools referred to by the name <i>meritza</i>, <b>fingernails</b> [<b><i>tzipporin</i></b>]. The Gemara explains: <b>The one who said</b> to call it <b><i>tzipporin</i></b> did so because this tool <b>was similar</b> in appearance to a <b>fingernail,</b> with a sharp point. <b>The one who said</b> to call it a <b><i>meritza</i>,</b> literally, a runner, did so because it is a tool with which <b>one runs,</b> i.e., moves <b>the stone</b> used to close the entrance to the burial cave <b>to the cemetery.</b>
 The mishna also states that if the <b>cleaver</b> is found tied to a knife, it has the same rule as the knife with regard to ritual impurity. The Gemara quotes: <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that disagrees with the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna, and in its view, if <b>the knife were tied to</b> the cleaver, <b>it has the same</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>as</b> the cleaver, and if the cleaver is ritually impure, the knife is also ritually impure.

Halakhah 2

<strong>Halakha 2 · MISHNA</strong> With regard to <b>a curtain that became ritually impure from a secondary source of impurity,</b> since its ritual impurity is by rabbinic law and not Torah law, there is no need to remove it from the Temple. Rather, <b>it is immersed inside</b> the Temple. <b>And</b> if it were removed to outside the courtyard in order to immerse it, <b>it</b> can <b>be brought</b> back into the courtyard <b>immediately.</b> Since it is ritually impure only by rabbinic law, there is no need to wait until sunset before returning it. But <b>if it became impure from a primary source of impurity,</b> e.g., it came into contact with the carcass of one of the eight creeping animals that confer impurity by Torah law, <b>it is immersed outside</b> the courtyard <b>and is spread out</b> to dry <b>on the rampart.</b> This is the low wall surrounding the Temple courtyard and the buildings within it, which has a lower level of holiness than the courtyard. The reason for this policy is <b>because the sun needs to set on it.</b> Immersion does not confer ritual purity on an item that became impure by Torah law until after the sun has set. And <b>if</b> this curtain <b>were new, it is spread out</b> to dry <b>on top of the bench [<i>itztabba</i>],</b> a prominent place on the Temple Mount, <b>so that the people</b> will <b>see its craftsmanship and perceive its beauty.</b> The Gemara discusses the aforementioned curtain that separated the Holy of Holies from the Sanctuary. <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says in the name of Rabbi Shimon the son of the deputy</b> High Priest: The <b>curtain</b> has the <b>thickness of a handbreadth,</b> and <b>it is woven from seventy-two strands</b> of yarn. And <b>each and every strand</b> from those seventy-two is made from <b>twenty-four threads.</b> The curtain was made from four materials: Sky-blue wool, purple wool, scarlet wool, and fine linen, and a strand was made up of six threads of each type of material. And with regard to the dimensions of the curtain, <b>its length</b> was <b>forty cubits,</b> as the height of the ceiling of the Sanctuary; <b>and its width</b> was <b>twenty</b> cubits, to match the width of the entrance; <b>and</b> it <b>was made from eighty-two ten-thousands,</b> i.e., 820,000 golden dinar. <b>And they</b> used to <b>make two</b> new curtains <b>every year. And</b> the curtain was so heavy that they needed <b>three hundred priests</b> to carry it when they would <b>immerse it.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna stated that every one of the strands of yarn used to weave the curtain was made of twenty-four threads. The Gemara explains the biblical source for this: The verse says: “And you shall make a curtain of sky-blue wool, purple wool, scarlet wool, and fine twined linen” (Exodus 26:31). <b>Had</b> the verse about the curtain <b>said a thread,</b> it would mean <b>one</b> thread. Had it said <b>doubled,</b> it would be expanded <b>to two</b> threads. <b>Twisted</b> would be increased <b>to three</b> threads. <b>Twined,</b> which is what the verse in fact says, must mean double that, i.e., <b>to six</b> threads. Since the curtain was woven from <b>four</b> types of thread, i.e., sky-blue wool, purple wool, scarlet wool, and fine linen, <b>from here this</b> count tells us that each strand used in the curtain was made from <b>twenty-four</b> threads. Another opinion was <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Each strand of the curtain was made from <b>thirty-two</b> threads. This is how that <i>tanna</i> expounds the verse: <b>Had</b> the verse about the curtain <b>said a thread,</b> it would mean <b>one</b> thread. Had it said <b>doubled,</b> it would be expanded <b>to two</b> threads. <b>Twisted</b> would be increased <b>to four</b> threads. <b>Twined,</b> which is what the verse in fact says, must mean double that, i.e., <b>to eight</b> threads. Since the curtain was woven from <b>four</b> types of thread, <b>from here this</b> count tells us that each strand used in the curtain was made from <b>thirty-two</b> threads. Another <i>baraita</i> <b>taught:</b> Each strand of the curtain was made from <b>forty-eight</b> threads. That <i>tanna</i> expounds the verse as follows: <b>Had</b> the verse about the curtain <b>said a thread,</b> it would mean <b>one</b> thread. Had it said <b>doubled,</b> it would be expanded <b>to two</b> threads. <b>Wound</b> would be increased <b>to three</b> threads. <b>Twisted</b> would be increased <b>to six</b> threads. <b>Twined,</b> which is what the verse in fact says, must mean double that, i.e., <b>to twelve</b> threads. Since the curtain was woven from <b>four</b> types of thread, <b>from here this</b> count tells us that each thread used in the curtain was made from <b>forty-eight</b> threads.
The Gemara compares the workmanship of the curtain, which separated the Holy of Holies from the Sanctuary, with that of the screen, which was hung at the entrance to the Sanctuary: <b>One verse,</b> about the screen, <b>says</b> that it is <b>“the work of the embroiderer”</b> (Exodus 26:36), <b>and one verse,</b> about the curtain, <b>says</b> that it is <b>“the work of the skillful workman”</b> (Exodus 26:31). The Gemara explains: <b>“The work of the embroiderer”</b> means <b>one face</b> was on both sides of the curtain; <b>“the work of the skillful workman”</b> means there were <b>two</b> different <b>faces,</b> one on each side. <b>Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Neḥemya</b> disagreed about this: <b>One said</b> that <b>“the work of the embroiderer”</b> means that there was an image embroidered that could be seen from both sides, e.g., <b>a lion from here,</b> on one side, <b>and a lion from there,</b> on the other side. <b>“The work of the skillful workman”</b> means that the <b>lion</b> could be seen <b>from here,</b> from one side, <b>and</b> it was <b>smooth</b> with no image <b>from there,</b> on the other side. <b>And the other said: “The work of the embroiderer”</b> means that there was an image like a <b>lion from here,</b> on one side, <b>and smooth</b> without an image <b>from there. “The work of the skillful workman”</b> means that from each side a different image could be seen, e.g., <b>a lion from here and an eagle from there.</b>
 The mishna stated that the curtain <b>was made from eighty-two ten-thousands,</b> i.e., 820,000 golden dinar, and that it needed three hundred priests in order to immerse it. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak bar Bizna</b> said <b>in the name of Shmuel:</b> Three hundred priests is an <b>exaggeration [<i>guzma</i>],</b> and really a much smaller number of priests were needed. Similarly, <b>we learned</b> in a mishna <b>there</b> (<i>Tamid</i> 28b) with regard to the pile of ash on the altar, that <b>at times there was on it as</b> much as <b>three hundred <i>kor</i>.</b> Regarding this, <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Bun,</b> said <b>in the name of Shmuel:</b> This is an <b>exaggeration.</b>

Halakhah 3

<strong>Halakha 3 · MISHNA</strong> With regard to <b>the flesh of offerings of the most sacred order that became impure, whether</b> it became impure <b>from a primary source of impurity or from a secondary source of impurity, whether</b> it became impure <b>inside</b> the courtyard <b>or outside,</b> it must be burned. There is a dispute among the <i>tanna’im</i> with regard to where it is burned. <b>Beit Shammai say: It all should be burned inside</b> the Temple courtyard, <b>except for that which became impure from a primary source of ritual impurity outside,</b> as under such circumstances, it is not appropriate to bring it inside the Temple. <b>Beit Hillel say: It all should be burned outside</b> the Temple courtyard, <b>except for that which became ritually impure by a secondary source of impurity inside.</b> In such a case the flesh need not be removed from the courtyard and is burned there. <sup><b>[22a]</b></sup> <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> Flesh from offerings of the most sacred order <b>that became</b> ritually <b>impure from a primary source of ritual impurity, whether</b> it became so <b>inside</b> the courtyard <b>or outside, is burned outside.</b> Since its ritual impurity is of the most stringent type, it is not to be brought into the courtyard, or allowed to remain there. However, an item <b>that became ritually impure from a secondary source of ritual impurity, whether</b> it became ritually impure <b>outside or inside, is burned inside.</b> Since its ritual impurity is of a lenient type, it can be brought into the courtyard in order to be burned. <b>Rabbi Akiva says: The place of its impurity is where its burning should occur.</b> Therefore, regardless of whether the source is primary or secondary, such flesh is burned wherever it presently is.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The opinions in the mishna, with the exception of that of Rabbi Akiva, distinguish between impurity conferred by a primary source and that conferred by a secondary source. The Gemara presents a dispute as to the nature of this distinction. <b>Bar Kappara said: The primary source of ritual impurity</b> mentioned in the mishna is referring to a scenario where the flesh of the offering became impure by <b>Torah law,</b> and <b>the secondary source of ritual impurity</b> mentioned in the mishna is referring to impurity <b>by</b> rabbinic <b>ordinance. Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Both this and that,</b> both the primary and secondary sources of ritual impurity mentioned, refer to cases of impurity by <b>Torah law.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> there is <b>a difficulty</b> with <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan’s</b> opinion that arises from <b>Beit Shammai’s</b> opinion in the mishna. It was taught in the mishna that <b>Beit Shammai say: It all should be burned inside</b> the courtyard of the Temple, <b>except for that which became impure from a primary source of impurity outside.</b> In accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan, <b>what is</b> the distinction <b>between a primary source of impurity outside</b> and <b>a secondary source of impurity outside?</b> After all, are not <b>this and that,</b> both the flesh that became impure from the primary source and that which became impure from the secondary source, impure by <b>Torah law?</b> <b>And is this not difficult even according to</b> the opinion <b>of Beit Hillel?</b> As the mishna stated <b>that Beit Hillel said: It all should be burned outside</b> the courtyard of the Temple, <b>except for what became ritually impure by a secondary source of impurity inside.</b> In accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan, <b>what is</b> the distinction <b>between</b> consecrated flesh that became ritually impure from a <b>secondary source of impurity inside</b> and consecrated flesh that became ritually impure from a <b>primary source of impurity inside?</b> Are <b>not this and that Torah law,</b> and shouldn’t the principle be the same? The Gemara proceeds to ignore these questions with regard to Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion, explaining that <b>the Sages did not discuss</b> the mishna except <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>bar Kappara. And</b> there is also <b>a difficulty in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>bar Kappara with</b> the statement of <b>Beit Shammai.</b> For it was taught in the mishna that <b>Beit Shammai say: It all should be burned inside</b> the courtyard of the Temple, <b>except for that which became impure from a primary source of ritual impurity outside.</b> In accordance with the opinion of bar Kappara, <b>what is</b> the distinction, with regard to flesh that became impure by a <b>primary source of impurity, between whether</b> it occurred <b>outside</b> the Temple courtyard <b>or inside?</b> In accordance with the opinion of bar Kappara, are <b>not this and that Torah law</b> and shouldn’t the rule be the same? Accordingly, why distinguish between them with regard to where the flesh must be burned? The Gemara answers: The reason that Beit Shammai distinguish between impurity that occurred inside the Temple courtyard and that which occurred outside is <b>because</b> they accepted the reasoning of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> who <b>said</b> in the mishna: <b>The place of its impurity is where its burning should occur.</b> Beit Shammai, however, do not completely agree with Rabbi Akiva. With regard to items that became impure by Torah law, their opinion is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Akiva, meaning that the items should be burned wherever they are. However, if an item became impure by rabbinic ordinance, Beit Shammai maintain, against the opinion of Rabbi Akiva, that it is brought into the courtyard to be burned. The Gemara continues to question the opinion of bar Kappara: <b>And is this not difficult even according to</b> the opinion <b>of Beit Hillel?</b> As the mishna stated <b>that Beit Hillel said: It all should be burned outside</b> the courtyard of the Temple, <b>except for that which became ritually impure by a secondary source of impurity inside.</b> According to bar Kappara <b>what is between,</b> why is there a distinction, with regard to flesh that became impure by a <b>secondary source of impurity between whether</b> it occurred <b>outside</b> the Temple courtyard <b>or inside? Aren’t</b> both <b>this and that</b> instances where the impurity <b>is by rabbinic ordinance?</b> The Gemara answers: Beit Hillel distinguish between impurity that occurred inside the Temple courtyard and impurity that occurred outside <b>due to that</b> which <b>Rabbi Shimon said: The food and drink of a leper is banished from all three</b> of the <b>camps</b> in which the Children of Israel lived in the desert. These are the camp of the Divine Presence, i.e., the Tabernacle; the camp of the Levites, who were encamped around the Tabernacle; and the camp of Israel, where the rest of the nation lived. Once food and drink has become impure through contact with a leper, it is banned from all three camps, like the leper himself. If it became impure outside the camps, it should not be brought inside. Beit Hillel follow the same rationale, that an item rendered impure outside, even if by rabbinic ordinance, may not be brought into the Temple even for the purpose of burning it.

Halakhah 4

<strong>Halakha 4 · MISHNA</strong> The <b>limbs of the daily offering</b> were not placed directly on the altar fire. Instead, after cutting up the offering, its limbs <b>were placed</b> first on the <b>ramp</b> of the altar, <b>from</b> the <b>halfway</b> point <b>and below,</b> on the lower sixteen cubits of the ramp, <b>on</b> its <b>western</b> side. Limbs <b>of</b> the <b>additional</b> offerings of the Shabbat and Festivals <b>were placed on the ramp from the halfway point and below on</b> its <b>eastern</b> side. <b>Additional New Moon offerings were placed on top of the upper part of the edge [<i>karkov</i>] of the altar.</b> Another law: The obligation to give <b>half-shekels</b> each year <b>and</b> to offer <b>the first fruits is practiced only in the presence of the Temple,</b>as fulfillment of these mitzvot is only possible then. <b>But</b> the mitzvot of produce tithes and <b>grain tithes and</b> of <b>animal tithes and of</b> the sanctified <b>firstborn</b> animals <b>are practiced whether</b> one is <b>in the presence of the Temple, or</b> one is <b>not in the presence of the Temple.</b> Although animal tithes and firstborn cannot be sacrificed without a Temple, once they develop a blemish, they may be eaten by their owners. If, in the present time when there is no Temple, <b>one consecrates shekels</b> for the mitzva of the half-shekel <b>or</b> fruits for the mitzva of <b>first fruits, they are consecrated,</b> and it is prohibited to derive benefit from them. <b>Rabbi Shimon says: One who declared first fruits to be consecrated,</b> in the present time, does not give them that status and they are <b>not consecrated.</b>
 <strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna states that additional New Moon offerings were placed on the <i>karkov</i>. <b>What is the <i>karkov</i> of the altar?</b> It is the <b>cubit</b>-wide area <b>between one horn and the other</b> on the top surface of the altar, and <b>the place allocated for the priests’ passage</b> when they move about the altar performing their duties.
While on the topic, the Gemara asks: When the New Moon falls on Shabbat and both the <b>additional Shabbat offerings</b> and the <b>additional New Moon offerings</b> must be offered, <b>which</b> of them <b>takes precedence? Rabbi Yirmeya thought to say</b> that when <b>additional Shabbat offerings and additional New Moon offerings</b> are both sacrificed, the <b>additional New Moon offerings take precedence</b> and are offered first. The Gemara comments: <b>The strength</b> of, or support for, <b>Rabbi Yirmeya’s</b> opinion comes <b>from that</b> which was taught in a <i>baraita</i>: When the New Moon falls on Shabbat, <b>the song</b> sung by the Levites in the Temple <b>of Shabbat and the song of</b> the <b>New Moon</b> both need to be sung; <b>the song of</b> the <b>New Moon takes precedence,</b> and likewise, the additional offering of the New Moon is offered first. <b>Rabbi Yosei said: The</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>is different there</b> in the case of the Levites’ song, as <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya said in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> The reason that the song of the New Moon takes precedence over the song of Shabbat is <b>in order to publicize</b> the occasion <b>and to inform</b> everyone <b>that it is</b> the <b>New Moon.</b> Since the New Moon originally depended upon the Sanhedrin establishing its exact date, there was a need to publicize which day was the first of the month. <b>How</b> precisely <b>would it be done?</b> When would they sing the song for the New Moon, while preserving the precedence of the additional Shabbat offerings? The priest would <b>slaughter</b> the <b>additional Shabbat offerings and</b> the Levites would then <b>sing the song for</b> the <b>New Moon. However [<i>beram</i>] here,</b> with regard to <b>the additional Shabbat offerings and</b> the <b>additional New Moon offerings</b> rather than the songs, the <b>additional Shabbat offerings take precedence, following</b> the principle: When <b>a frequent</b> practice and <b>an infrequent</b> practice clash, <b>the frequent practice takes precedence</b> over <b>the other,</b> the infrequent, practice. Therefore, the Sages said to slaughter the additional Shabbat offering before the additional New Moon offering.
§ The mishna stated that the obligation to give half-shekels each year and to offer the first fruits is only practiced in the presence of the Temple. However, if one consecrates shekels or first fruits in the present day, they are consecrated, and it is prohibited to derive benefit from them. Rabbi Shimon disagrees and says such an attempt to consecrate the first fruits would not be effective. The Gemara points out: Even according to Rabbi Shimon, <b>this</b> indicates that <b>shekels would be consecrated</b> if one did so in the present day. <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda in the name of Rabbi Shimon</b> says, contrary to this implication: <b>Both these,</b> first fruits, <b>and those,</b> shekels, <b>are not consecrated.</b> The Gemara continues with a discussion of consecrated items in the present day, in the absence of the Temple. <b>It was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: A <b>convert</b> who converts <b>in the present day is obligated to set aside,</b> in lieu of <b>his pair</b> of doves, <b>a quarter-</b>dinar <b>of silver,</b> for that is the cheapest price at which one can purchase them. A convert at the time of the Temple was obligated to offer two doves or pigeons as the conclusion of the conversion process. <b>Rabbi Shimon said: Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Zakkai voided</b> that obligation <b>due to the</b> potential for a <b>mishap,</b> since that money would be consecrated and there is a high risk that it would be misused. <b>What</b> is meant by: <b>Due to the</b> potential for a <b>mishap?</b> Where can it be seen that the Sages were concerned about the possibility of such mishaps? <b>Like that</b> which <b>was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One may not consecrate</b> an item, <b>or take a valuation vow,</b> i.e., vow that one’s value is dedicated to the Temple, <b>or consecrate</b> objects for use by the priests or the Temple, <b>or separate <i>terumot</i></b> or <b>tithes in the present day</b> when there is no Temple, lest there be a mishap and one derive prohibited benefit from any of those items. <b>And if</b> he violated this rule, and <b>consecrated</b> an item, <b>or took a valuation vow, or consecrated</b> an object for use by the priests or the Temple, <b>or separated</b> <i>terumot</i> or tithes, <b>the clothing</b> that was consecrated <b>is burnt, the animal is destroyed. How</b> is it destroyed? The door of its stall is locked <b>in front of it and it dies by itself,</b> of hunger, as it is prohibited to actively destroy consecrated items. <b>And the money</b> that was consecrated <b>goes to the Dead Sea,</b> i.e., is abandoned where no one will ever find it. Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Zakkai voided the convert’s obligation to set aside money for a pair of doves. The Gemara asks: If the convert <b>transgressed</b> and ignored the instructions of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Zakkai <b>and consecrated</b> money for a pair of doves, is it effective? The answer to this question can be inferred <b>from that which Rabbi Shimon said</b> in that same <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Zakkai voided</b> that obligation <b>due to the</b> potential for a <b>mishap. That is to say,</b> from the fact that the potential for a mishap exists, one can infer that if one <b>transgressed and consecrated</b> the money, <b>it is</b> nevertheless <b>consecrated.</b> <b>Rabbi Yodeh Antodarya asked in the presence of Rabbi Yosei: Here</b> in the <i>baraita</i> <b>you</b> <sup><b>[22b]</b></sup> <b>say</b> that the money <b>has been consecrated; and here,</b> in the <i>baraita</i> mentioned before, <b>you say</b> that Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Shimon that if one consecrates shekels in the present day, they <b>are not consecrated.</b> He <b>said to</b> him: <b>There,</b> regarding the shekels, <b>one does not consecrate <i>ab initio</i> because the mitzva</b> is <b>to bring</b> the communal <b>offerings</b> each year <b>from</b> the <b>new collection</b> of half-shekels, those collected for that year, <b>and these</b> shekels, those consecrated in the present day, will necessarily be from the <b>old</b> collection, whenever the Temple is rebuilt, and as such have no use. For this reason, even if he consecrated shekels, they remain non-sacred. <b>And here,</b> however, with regard to the convert’s money set aside for his pair of doves, <b>what have you to say?</b> In what sense is <b>this</b> consecrated money <b>old?</b> The disqualification of the old collection only applies to shekels used to purchase communal offerings, <b>and other</b> private <b>consecrated items and a convert’s pair</b> of doves do <b>not need</b> to be purchased from a <b>new</b> collection. Money consecrated now can be used to acquire a convert’s pair of doves. <b>And</b> therefore, <b>if</b> he went ahead and <b>consecrated</b> a quarter-dinar of silver in order to acquire a pair of doves, it is <b>consecrated,</b> and he <b>should set it aside</b> until the Temple is rebuilt. According to Rabbi Yosei’s reasoning, the shekels are not consecrated because they have no use, as long as the Temple is not rebuilt this year. The Gemara asks: Why are they not consecrated? <b>Perhaps</b> the <b>Temple will</b> be <b>rebuilt</b> again <b>as at first, and the collection of the</b> Temple Treasury <b>chamber will be collected from the new</b> collection <b>in its</b> proper <b>time on the first of Nisan.</b> If that comes to pass, his shekels will be from the current year, and their consecration would have a point. <b>And here</b> too, <b>what have you</b> to say? What are the grounds for a distinction between the convert’s offering whose illicit consecration is effective and the shekels whose illicit consecration is ineffective? The Gemara does not offer an answer. Apropos the dispute in the mishna about someone who went ahead and consecrated first fruits in the present day, <b>Rav Hamnuna and Rav Adda bar Ahava</b> said <b>in the name of Rav: The <i>halakha</i> is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon</b> that they are not consecrated.
